# NIST SP 800-53 5.2.0 — Access Control Solution Plan — 2026-06-18 22:24

**Standard:** NIST SP 800-53 5.2.0
**Family:** AC — Access Control
**Generated:** 2026-06-18 22:42 UTC
**Model:** N/A

## Executive Summary

- **Total Solutions:** 131
- **Manual (Policy/Process/Documentation):** 7
- **Software (Development/Configuration):** 124
- **Critical:** 122
- **High:** 9
- **Medium:** 0
- **Low:** 0

## Application / System Context

Enterprise business application operating within a security-conscious environment that processes, stores, and transmits confidential organizational information. The application supports critical business functions and is considered important to the organization's operational, regulatory, and security objectives.

Technology Environment:
- Enterprise-managed application hosted within approved infrastructure environments (cloud, on-premises, hybrid, or SaaS)
- Integrated with enterprise identity, access management, monitoring, logging, backup, and security services
- May include web, mobile, API, client-server, microservices, or traditional enterprise application architectures
- Utilizes enterprise-approved databases, storage platforms, and network services

Architecture:
- Multi-tier architecture with logical separation between presentation, application, data, and administrative functions
- Production, test, development, and disaster recovery environments are segregated and managed separately
- Security controls are implemented using defense-in-depth principles across infrastructure, applications, networks, identities, and data
- Administrative functions are isolated from standard user activities where feasible

Users and Access:
- Users include employees, contractors, partners, service accounts, administrators, and privileged operators
- Authentication is integrated with centralized enterprise identity services
- Authorization is governed through role-based and least-privilege access models
- Privileged access is restricted, monitored, approved, and periodically reviewed
- Access to data and functionality is granted based on business need, job responsibilities, and data sensitivity

Data Classification and Sensitivity:
- The application may store, process, or transmit confidential, sensitive, proprietary, regulated, or business-critical information
- Information assets are classified according to enterprise data classification standards
- Data may include business records, customer information, employee information, financial data, operational data, intellectual property, or regulated information
- Data confidentiality, integrity, and availability requirements are considered high

Security Requirements:
- The application must comply with organizational security policies, standards, and governance requirements
- Security is incorporated throughout the system lifecycle, including design, development, testing, deployment, operation, and decommissioning
- Strong authentication, authorization, encryption, logging, monitoring, vulnerability management, and incident response capabilities are required
- Security controls are implemented according to risk assessments and regulatory obligations
- Secure configuration baselines are established and maintained
- Security events and administrative activities are logged, monitored, and retained according to enterprise requirements

Integration Points:
- Enterprise identity and access management services
- Security information and event management (SIEM) platforms
- Monitoring and observability platforms
- Backup and recovery services
- Enterprise databases, APIs, messaging systems, and shared services
- Third-party and vendor-managed systems where business requirements necessitate integration

Operational Environment:
- Managed by designated business, application, infrastructure, and security teams
- Formal change management, configuration management, and release management processes are followed
- Systems are subject to vulnerability scanning, patch management, security assessments, and periodic audits
- Business continuity, disaster recovery, and incident response processes are established and tested
- Security responsibilities are shared among business owners, application owners, administrators, developers, and cybersecurity personnel

Compliance and Governance:
- The application must satisfy applicable regulatory, contractual, legal, privacy, and organizational requirements
- Controls should align with recognized security frameworks and standards, including NIST SP 800-53, NIST Cybersecurity Framework, ISO 27001, CIS Controls, SOC 2, and other applicable requirements
- Control implementations should be scalable, risk-based, auditable, and suitable for enterprise-wide adoption
- Solutions should favor centralized enterprise capabilities, standardization, automation, and repeatable governance processes wherever possible

Assumptions for Compliance Planning:
- Confidentiality, integrity, and availability are all important objectives
- The organization maintains centralized security services and governance functions
- Enterprise security policies and standards take precedence over application-specific implementations
- Controls should be written to accommodate a broad range of technologies and deployment models while maintaining compliance with the selected framework
- Recommendations should emphasize sustainable operational processes, measurable control effectiveness, and audit-ready evidence generation

## Solutions by Control

### ac-1 — Policy and Procedures (Control)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure everyone in the organisation knows the rules for who is allowed to access systems and information, and how those rules are applied in practice. Without clear, documented guidance, the wrong people could gain access, access might not be approved or removed when it should be, and sensitive information could be misused or exposed, leading to financial loss, disruption, and legal or regulatory breaches. The organisation must create and publish an organisation-wide access policy and supporting procedures, written in plain terms for all relevant roles, including all personnel, contractors, and privileged users, and it must cover the purpose, what it includes and excludes, roles and responsibilities, leadership commitment, how different parts of the organisation coordinate, and how compliance will be ensured, while also matching applicable laws and organisational rules. The Chief Information Security Officer must be responsible for producing, maintaining, and sharing these documents. The policy and procedures must be reviewed at least every year and updated after any material change, security incidents or breaches, audit findings, major organisational changes, or changes to relevant laws and regulations.

#### CISO-owned access control policy & procedures with review triggers

- **Category:** Manual
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Create and maintain an organisation-level **Access Control Policy** and supporting **Access Control Procedures** that define how AC-family requirements are implemented across the organisation and systems.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-1 requires that the organisation has **access control policy and procedures** that address the controls in the AC family implemented within systems and organisations. The guidance also requires that the **risk management strategy** informs the policy, that **security and privacy programs collaborate**, and that the policy/procedures are **owned, disseminated to defined roles, and kept current**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Appoint ownership**: designate the **CISO** as the official responsible for managing the access control policy and procedures.
2. **Develop the policy (organisation-level)**: publish an organisation-level Access Control Policy (or a small set of linked policies) that includes, at minimum, purpose, scope, roles/responsibilities, management commitment, coordination between security and privacy, and compliance approach. Ensure it is consistent with applicable laws/regulations and organisational standards.
3. **Develop the procedures**: document Access Control Procedures describing how the policy is implemented in practice (e.g., identity/access lifecycle, privileged access handling, access request/approval, exception handling, and access review execution). Procedures must be written for the roles that execute them.
4. **Define dissemination audiences**:
   - Disseminate the **policy** to **all personnel, contractors, and privileged users**.
   - Disseminate the **procedures** to **system owners, network administrators, OT/ICS engineers, and IAM administrators**.
5. **Set review/update cadence and event triggers**:
   - Review/update the **policy** **annually and after any material change**, and also after events such as **security incidents/breaches, audit findings, major organisational changes, or changes to applicable laws/regulations**.
   - Review/update the **procedures** **annually and after any material change**, and also after the same event types.
6. **Establish change governance and evidence**: store documents in a controlled repository with versioning, approval workflow, and an auditable record of dissemination and review outcomes (including who approved updates and when).

## WHO (role responsible)
- **CISO**: accountable for managing (developing, documenting, disseminating, and maintaining) the access control policy and procedures.
- **Security programme** and **Privacy programme**: collaborate on development and review/sign-off.
- **IAM administrators, system owners, network administrators, OT/ICS engineers**: provide procedure input and execute procedure steps.
- **Legal/Compliance** (as needed): validate alignment with applicable laws/regulations.

## Acceptance criteria
- An organisation-level Access Control Policy exists, is versioned, and has a named **CISO** owner.
- Access Control Procedures exist (one or more documents) and clearly describe implementation steps for the defined executing roles.
- Evidence exists that the policy was disseminated to **all personnel, contractors, and privileged users**, and procedures were disseminated to **system owners, network administrators, OT/ICS engineers, and IAM administrators**.
- The policy and procedures have documented **review/update frequency** (**annually and after material change**) and **event triggers** (incidents/breaches, audit findings, major organisational changes, changes in laws/regulations).
- Documented collaboration/sign-off exists between **security** and **privacy** programmes.
- The policy does not merely restate AC controls; it explains how AC-family requirements are implemented across the organisation/systems.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Appoint the CISO as the official responsible for managing the access control policy and procedures
- [ ] Draft and publish an organisation-level Access Control Policy with purpose, scope, roles/responsibilities, coordination, and compliance alignment
- [ ] Create Access Control Procedures that describe how the policy is implemented for identity/access lifecycle and privileged access handling
- [ ] Disseminate the policy to all personnel, contractors, and privileged users via controlled channels with acknowledgement records
- [ ] Disseminate the procedures to system owners, network administrators, OT/ICS engineers, and IAM administrators via role-based access to the controlled repository
- [ ] Set documented review/update schedules (annually and after material change) and event-driven triggers (incidents, breaches, audit findings, major changes, law/regulation changes)
- [ ] Implement document control with versioning, approval workflow, and auditable evidence of security/privacy collaboration and updates

### ac-2 — Account Management (Control)

> **Overview**
>
> This expectation is about making sure every person’s (and approved service’s) access to your system is properly set up, clearly documented, and promptly removed when it’s no longer needed. Without this, the wrong people could keep access after leaving or changing roles, accounts could be created without proper checks, and privileges could be broader than necessary, increasing the risk of unauthorised access and data misuse. The organisation must define and record which account types are allowed and specifically forbid shared, group, emergency, anonymous, temporary and guest accounts, while assigning an account manager and recording who is authorised, what groups or roles they belong to, and the exact permissions for each account, including which parts of the system or data they can use, whether extra identity checks are required, and any time limits. New accounts must be approved by the system owner and the senior information security officer, created and changed only through a documented process that includes a request record, approval, and audit trail, and any account changes triggered by events must be disabled within 24 hours. Account use must be monitored, reviewed at least quarterly, and the account manager and system owner must be notified within 24 hours when accounts are no longer required, when staff are terminated or transferred, or when someone’s need to know changes; if shared or group accounts are used, login details must be changed when members leave, and account actions must be aligned with termination and transfer processes.

#### IAM-enforced account lifecycle with prohibited account types

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement an account management lifecycle for the application that (1) restricts account types to **individual and service accounts only** and **prohibits** shared, group, emergency, anonymous, temporary, and guest accounts; (2) defines and enforces **account-specific attributes** (least privilege, access scope, MFA requirement, and time-of-day/day-of-week restrictions where applicable); and (3) ensures all account create/enable/modify/disable/remove actions occur via an **approved, auditable workflow** with **timely disablement (within 24 hours)** and **notifications (within 24 hours)** when accounts are no longer required, when users are terminated/transferred, or when need-to-know changes.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-2 requires the organisation to identify authorised system users and specify access privileges, define account types and membership/authorisation conditions, and establish procedures for creating, enabling, modifying, disabling, and removing accounts. It also calls for additional scrutiny/approval for administrative privileges and timely disabling and notification when accounts are no longer required or when personnel status changes.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Enforce allowed/prohibited account types in the IAM integration**: configure the application’s identity/authorisation integration so that only **individual** and **service** accounts can be provisioned for user access; block provisioning for prohibited account types (shared/group/emergency/anonymous/temporary/guest) and record any attempted requests as exceptions.
2. **Define per-account authorisation attributes** in the IAM entitlement model and map them to application roles/permissions:
   - least privilege based on role, system function, and approved access authorisation
   - access scope (systems/data) per account
   - MFA requirement for interactive access
   - time-of-day/day-of-week restrictions where the organisation’s policy requires them
3. **Implement an approval-backed provisioning workflow** for account creation and privileged access:
   - require approvals from the **system owner** and the **senior agency information security officer** for account creation (and any elevation to administrative/sensitive privileges)
   - ensure the workflow is ticket-driven and produces immutable audit records (request, approvals, provisioning outcome)
4. **Automate lifecycle triggers and enforce the 24-hour disablement SLA**:
   - integrate HR/identity lifecycle events (termination, transfer, role change, need-to-know change) to automatically disable accounts within **24 hours** of the trigger
   - ensure disablement precedes or accompanies removal where required by operational constraints, but the disablement SLA is met
5. **Automate 24-hour notifications** to the **account manager** and **system owner** when accounts are no longer required, when users are terminated/transferred, and when need-to-know/usage changes occur.
6. **Set up quarterly account review reporting** from the IAM/application entitlement data to support recertification of account access and privileges.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Account manager / IAM administrator**: owns the provisioning workflow configuration, entitlement templates, and notification rules.
- **System owner**: approves account creation and privileged access requests; validates quarterly review outputs.
- **Senior agency information security officer**: provides required approvals for account creation and privileged/sensitive access.
- **Security engineering/compliance**: verifies evidence generation (audit logs, disablement/notification SLAs, quarterly review reports).

## Acceptance criteria
- The application cannot be provisioned with prohibited account types for access (attempts are blocked and logged as exceptions).
- For all in-scope accounts, the IAM/application integration enforces: least-privilege entitlements, defined access scope, MFA requirement (for interactive access), and time-of-day/day-of-week restrictions where configured.
- Account creation and any administrative/sensitive privilege elevation require approvals from the **system owner** and **senior agency information security officer**, with auditable ticket/workflow evidence.
- For termination/transfer/need-to-know change triggers, accounts are disabled within **24 hours**, and notifications are sent within **24 hours** to the defined roles.
- Quarterly account review evidence is produced from authoritative IAM/application entitlement data, including account inventory, assigned roles/privileges, and reviewer/attestation records.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Configure the IAM-to-application provisioning integration to allow only individual and service accounts and to block prohibited account types (shared/group/emergency/anonymous/temporary/guest) with exception logging
- [ ] Create entitlement templates that define per-account privileges, access scope (systems/data), MFA requirement, and time-of-day/day-of-week restrictions and map them to application roles
- [ ] Implement a ticket-driven approval workflow requiring system owner and senior agency information security officer approvals for account creation and any administrative/sensitive privilege elevation
- [ ] Integrate HR/identity lifecycle events (hire/transfer/termination/need-to-know change) and enforce automatic account disablement within 24 hours of triggers
- [ ] Configure automated notifications to the account manager and system owner within 24 hours when accounts are no longer required, when users are terminated/transferred, and when need-to-know/usage changes occur
- [ ] Enable quarterly account review reporting from authoritative IAM/application entitlement data and store reviewer/attestation evidence for audit

### ac-2.1 — Automated System Account Management (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure system accounts are created, changed, paused, and removed in a controlled, automatic way, with the right people being told immediately. Without this, staff could accidentally leave accounts active after someone changes job or leaves, or unauthorised access could go unnoticed for too long, and unusual account use could be missed. The organisation is expected to use built-in system features to manage the full account life cycle (create, enable, modify, disable, remove), automatically monitor how those accounts are used, and automatically send email notifications to the account managers whenever an account is created, enabled, modified, disabled, or removed, and when a user is terminated or transferred. It should also automatically flag unexpected account activity and report it to the responsible account managers, keep records showing what happened and when, and document the procedures, responsibilities, system design, and settings that make these automated actions and notifications work.

#### Automated system account lifecycle with event notifications

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement automated mechanisms to manage **system accounts** across the application’s environments (create, enable, modify, disable, remove), and automatically **notify account managers** for each lifecycle event and for **user termination/transfer**. Also implement automated **monitoring of system account usage** and **reporting of atypical usage**.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-2(1)** enhancement requires support for system account management using the organisation-defined **automated mechanisms** (param **ac-02.01_odp**), including:
- automated create/enable/modify/disable/remove
- automated notifications to account managers on lifecycle events and on user termination/transfer
- monitoring of system account usage and reporting of atypical usage

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define system-account ownership and mapping
- Create a maintained mapping between each system account (or system-account group) and its **account manager(s)** (e.g., directory attribute, CMDB owner, or application ownership group).
- Ensure the mapping is used by the automation workflow to determine notification recipients.

### 2) Implement event-driven lifecycle automation (internal system functions)
- Use the enterprise identity/access platform integration (e.g., IdP/Directory + workflow engine) to drive system-account lifecycle actions via **internal system functions**.
- Configure automation to perform, at minimum, the following lifecycle actions for system accounts in the relevant target systems:
  - **create** (provision account, set initial state, attach to correct groups/roles)
  - **enable** (activate only when prerequisites are met)
  - **modify** (change attributes/roles/entitlements)
  - **disable** (pause access without deleting audit history)
  - **remove** (delete/deprovision after retention requirements)

### 3) Configure automated notifications per lifecycle event
- Subscribe to lifecycle/audit events from the identity platform and/or target systems.
- For each event (created/enabled/modified/disabled/removed), trigger an automated notification workflow that sends **templated email** to the mapped account manager(s).
- Additionally, trigger notifications when a user is **terminated or transferred** (even if the system account is indirectly affected via group membership/role changes).

### 4) Implement automated monitoring and atypical-usage reporting
- Ingest system-account authentication/usage logs (from the identity platform and application access logs) into the SIEM/monitoring stack.
- Define detection rules for atypical system-account usage (e.g., impossible travel/time-of-day anomalies, unusual source IP/device, sudden privilege/role changes, repeated failed logins followed by success, access outside approved maintenance windows).
- Route alerts to the account manager(s) (and optionally SOC) with enough context to investigate.

### 5) Ensure auditable linkage between lifecycle actions and notifications
- Ensure the automation workflow writes a correlation record (e.g., account ID + event type + timestamp + notification ID) into an auditable store (ticketing/audit log) so auditors can prove that lifecycle actions and notifications occurred.
- Retain logs/audit records according to enterprise logging retention standards.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Identity & Access Management (IAM) engineer**: implement lifecycle workflows, event subscriptions, and notification templates.
- **Application owner / service owner**: maintain system-account ownership mapping and approve atypical-usage alert routing.
- **Security operations (SOC/SecOps) analyst**: validate detection rules and triage/feedback atypical-usage alerts.
- **Compliance/audit lead**: verify evidence sufficiency (audit trail + notification evidence + monitoring configuration).

## Acceptance criteria (evidence to demonstrate)
1. For system accounts, the organisation can show automated execution of **create, enable, modify, disable, and remove** actions (screenshots/config exports and/or workflow run logs) with no manual steps required for routine lifecycle changes.
2. For each lifecycle event, there is evidence that the **mapped account manager(s)** received an automated email notification (sample notifications for at least 3 event types, including disable or remove).
3. When a user is terminated or transferred (or when equivalent identity events occur), the workflow triggers the required notification and corresponding system-account entitlement changes.
4. Atypical system-account usage detections are implemented in the SIEM/monitoring stack and generate alerts routed to the account manager(s) (provide at least one alert example and the rule configuration).
5. An auditable correlation exists linking lifecycle events to notification events (correlation IDs/timestamps) and logs are retained per enterprise policy.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define and maintain a system-account-to-account-manager ownership mapping used by the automation workflow
- [ ] Implement event-driven automation to create, enable, modify, disable, and remove system accounts using internal system functions
- [ ] Configure automated email notifications for each system-account lifecycle event and for user termination/transfer events
- [ ] Integrate system-account usage logs (identity sign-in/audit logs and application access logs) into the SIEM/monitoring platform
- [ ] Create and tune atypical-usage detection rules for system accounts and route alerts to the mapped account manager(s)
- [ ] Enable auditable correlation records linking lifecycle actions to notification events and retain logs per enterprise retention policy

### ac-2.2 — Automated Temporary and Emergency Account Management (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure short-term and emergency user accounts do not stay active longer than intended. If temporary or emergency access is left on past its agreed end time, it increases the risk that someone could keep using that access by mistake or misuse it, even after the original need has passed. To meet this expectation, the organisation must automatically disable any temporary and emergency accounts after 24 hours, using built-in automated account-management features rather than waiting for an administrator to manually switch them off. The organisation should have clear account-management rules and procedures, documented system settings and design, and be able to show system-generated records listing which temporary and emergency accounts were disabled and when, supported by audit records and relevant internal documentation. People responsible for account management and system administration should ensure the automated process is in place and operating as intended.

#### Automate 24-hour disablement for temporary/emergency accounts

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement an automated mechanism that **automatically disables temporary and emergency accounts after the configured time period** (default **24 hours**) rather than relying on administrator action.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-2(2)** enhancement requires that temporary and emergency accounts are **automatically removed or disabled** after **{{ param, ac-02.02_odp.02 }}**. The guidance emphasises consistent implementation by removing/disabling accounts **after a predefined time period**.

## HOW (specific approach)
### 1) Standardise temporary/emergency access as time-bound identity assignments
- Ensure temporary and emergency access is represented in the identity system as either:
  - an **expiring account** (account lifecycle), or
  - an **expiring privileged role / group membership** that results in effective access being disabled.
- Capture the **enablement timestamp** at the moment the account/assignment becomes active.

### 2) Configure the identity lifecycle automation to disable at expiry
- In the enterprise IdP/IGA platform, configure lifecycle rules so that when a temporary/emergency account (or privileged assignment) is created/enabled, the system sets **expiry = enablement time + 24 hours**.
- Configure the platform to **disable the account and/or revoke sign-in** automatically at expiry (i.e., no manual ticket closure required to complete disablement).

### 3) Ensure auditable evidence is generated automatically
- Enable and retain system-generated audit logs/events for:
  - account/assignment creation and enablement,
  - expiry timestamp calculation,
  - disablement/revocation action at the 24-hour mark.
- Provide a reporting view (exportable for audit) that lists temporary/emergency accounts disabled and the disablement time.

### 4) Operationalise with monitoring and exception handling
- Add a scheduled verification job that checks for any temporary/emergency accounts whose expiry time has passed but are still enabled.
- Route exceptions to the appropriate operations/IAM team with enough context to remediate, while still keeping the primary control action automated.

### 5) Apply consistently across environments
- Apply the same automation configuration to production and any other in-scope environments (e.g., test/UAT if used for real access), ensuring environment segregation does not break lifecycle rules.

## WHO
- **IAM/Identity Engineer**: implements IdP/IGA lifecycle automation and expiry logic.
- **Security Operations / SOC Analyst**: monitors automated disablement verification and investigates exceptions.
- **System Owner / Application Owner**: confirms that temporary/emergency access types map correctly to the automated disablement mechanism.

## Acceptance criteria
- For temporary and emergency accounts created/enabled through the approved workflow, the identity platform **automatically disables or revokes sign-in exactly at enablement + 24 hours** (within an agreed operational time tolerance, e.g., minutes).
- Evidence exists showing, for a sample of accounts, the **enablement timestamp, calculated expiry, and disablement/revocation event**.
- A verification check demonstrates **no accounts remain enabled beyond expiry** for the configured period.
- The automation is implemented via **system configuration/workflow/lifecycle policy**, not manual administrator disablement.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define temporary and emergency access types in the IAM/IGA platform and map them to expiring accounts or expiring privileged assignments
- [ ] Configure lifecycle automation to set expiry to enablement time plus 24 hours (ac-02.02_odp.02) and to disable or revoke sign-in at expiry (ac-02.02_odp.01)
- [ ] Enable audit logging for account/assignment enablement, expiry calculation, and disablement/revocation events and ensure logs are retained per policy
- [ ] Implement an automated post-expiry verification job that flags any temporary/emergency accounts still enabled after the 24-hour window
- [ ] Create an audit-ready report/export that lists disabled temporary/emergency accounts with disablement timestamps
- [ ] Test the end-to-end flow in a non-production environment using time-bounded test accounts and confirm disablement occurs automatically at the expected time

### ac-2.3 — Disable Accounts (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure old or suspicious access accounts are switched off quickly, so they can’t be used after they should no longer be active. If expired accounts, accounts that are no longer linked to a real person, accounts found to be in breach of organisational rules, or accounts that have been unused for 90 days are left running, they can become an easy route for unauthorised access or misuse, even if nobody is actively using them. To meet this expectation, the organisation must identify these accounts and disable them within 24 hours of the trigger being confirmed, whether that trigger is the account expiry date, the point it becomes unlinked from a user or person, the point a policy breach is identified, or the point 90 days of inactivity is reached. The organisation should also keep clear records showing when accounts were disabled and why, so it can demonstrate that the 24-hour timing was consistently followed.

#### Automate account disablement within 24h for expiry, orphan, anomaly, inactivity

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement an automated identity lifecycle workflow that disables accounts within **{{ insert: param, ac-02.03_odp.01 }} (default: 24 hours)** when accounts are **expired, inactive for {{ insert: param, ac-02.03_odp.02 }} (default: 90 days), unlinked/orphaned, or otherwise anomalous/policy-violating**.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-2(3) enhancement requires that accounts matching the defined conditions are **disabled within the defined time period**. This reduces the attack surface by removing access from accounts that should no longer be active.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define authoritative triggers and timestamps** for each condition:
   - Expiry date reached (from HR/IGA/IdP authoritative attributes)
   - Inactivity reached at **90 days** (from IdP sign-in logs and/or authoritative inactivity telemetry)
   - Orphan/unlinked (from IGA/HR-to-IdP reconciliation showing no linked person/role)
   - Anomalous/policy-violating (from IGA policy exception records or security policy violation flags)
2. **Configure IdP/IGA lifecycle automation** so that each trigger enqueues a disable action with an enforced **24-hour SLA** from trigger confirmation time.
3. **Ensure disablement is effective (least functionality)** by revoking active access artefacts as part of the workflow (e.g., invalidate sessions, revoke refresh tokens, force re-authentication; for federated access, ensure downstream relying parties are signalled as required by the IdP).
4. **Generate auditable evidence** for every case: record (a) trigger type, (b) trigger timestamp, (c) workflow/run identifier, and (d) disablement timestamp from IdP/IGA logs.
5. **Operationalise exception handling**: if a disable action cannot complete within 24 hours (e.g., dependency outage), automatically create an incident/ticket and retain evidence of the delay reason and remediation outcome.
6. **Apply the same workflow to the application’s account types** (employees, contractors, partners, service accounts where applicable) and ensure the application relies on the IdP/central identity state for authentication/authorisation decisions.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **IAM/IGA Engineer**: implement and tune IdP/IGA lifecycle rules and inactivity detection.
- **Application Security/Platform Engineer**: ensure the application honours IdP disablement (session/token handling) and that logs are retained.
- **Security Operations (SOC)**: monitor workflow failures/exception tickets and validate evidence completeness.

## Acceptance criteria
- For a sampled set of test accounts covering all trigger types (expired, orphaned/unlinked, policy-violating/anomalous, and 90-day inactivity), the **disablement timestamp is within 24 hours** of the trigger confirmation timestamp.
- Each disablement event has complete, assessor-ready evidence (trigger type, trigger timestamp, workflow/run ID, disablement timestamp) in IdP/IGA logs.
- Disabled accounts cannot successfully authenticate to the application (including active sessions/tokens being revoked or rendered ineffective per the workflow design).
- Workflow failures create traceable exception records and are remediated with evidence.


**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Identify and document authoritative data sources and timestamp semantics for expiry, inactivity (90 days), orphan/unlinked, and anomalous/policy-violating triggers
- [ ] Configure IdP/IGA lifecycle rules to disable accounts automatically with an enforced 24-hour SLA from trigger confirmation
- [ ] Implement session and token revocation as part of the disable workflow so disabled accounts cannot remain authenticated
- [ ] Enable and retain audit logging that records trigger type, trigger timestamp, workflow/run ID, and disablement timestamp for every disablement
- [ ] Create automated exception handling that opens a ticket/incident on disablement SLA breach and preserves evidence of delay and remediation
- [ ] Validate end-to-end behaviour with test accounts for each trigger type across production and at least one non-production environment

### ac-2.4 — Automated Audit Actions (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement ensures that whenever a person’s account is created, changed, turned on, turned off, or removed, the system automatically records what happened, when it happened, and who or what made the change. This matters because account changes are a common way for unauthorised access to be gained or for legitimate access to be misused, and without reliable records it becomes difficult to spot mistakes, investigate suspicious activity, or prove what was done and by whom. To meet this expectation, your organisation must make sure the system automatically produces audit records for all account-management actions in a consistent format, keeps those records for the period your organisation has decided, and then regularly reviews, analyses, and reports on them according to your internal audit review and reporting procedures.

#### Automate audit events for account lifecycle actions to SIEM

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Automatically generate audit records for **account creation, modification, enabling, disabling, and removal** events, and ensure these records are **captured in a consistent format** and are available for **review, analysis, and reporting**.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-2(4) (Enhancement)** requires that the system **automatically audit** account lifecycle actions (creation, modification, enabling, disabling, removal). Guidance ties account management audit records to the organisation’s audit record definition (**AU-02**) and to subsequent **review/analysis/reporting** (**AU-06**).

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Standardise account lifecycle operations through the enterprise IAM/IdP** (e.g., provisioning APIs/workflows) so the application does not perform ad-hoc account changes outside the central audit trail.
2. **Enable and verify audit event generation** for all required lifecycle actions in the IAM/IdP and any downstream account stores that the application affects (e.g., directory/DB/service account registry). Confirm that each event includes: **event type** (created/modified/enabled/disabled/removed), **timestamp**, **target account identifier**, and **actor identity** (human admin, service principal, or system process).
3. **Implement an automated log normalisation pipeline** that maps IAM/IdP account lifecycle events into the organisation’s **AU-02 audit record format/content** (field names, required attributes, and consistent schema). Ensure the pipeline preserves original event identifiers and correlation IDs where available.
4. **Forward events to the SIEM automatically** using an ingestion connector/agent (no manual steps). Configure **tamper-evident/append-only storage** and ensure time synchronisation (NTP) across log sources and collectors.
5. **Create automated AU-06 review/analysis hooks**: detection rules for account lifecycle anomalies (e.g., mass enable/disable, deletion followed by re-creation, privileged role assignment changes) and routing to the organisation’s case management workflow for investigation.
6. **Validate retention and retrieval**: configure SIEM retention/archival to meet the organisation’s AU-02 retention period, and test that audit records for each lifecycle action can be retrieved by time window and event type.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **IAM/Identity Engineering Lead**: owns IdP/IAM configuration for audit event enablement and lifecycle workflow standardisation.
- **Security Engineering (SIEM/Logging)**: owns the normalisation pipeline, SIEM ingestion, integrity/tamper-evidence configuration, and detection/triage rules.
- **Application Owner / Platform Engineer**: ensures the application uses the approved IAM provisioning pathways for account lifecycle changes.

## Acceptance criteria
- For each lifecycle action (**create, modify, enable, disable, remove**), the system produces an **audit record automatically** with consistent AU-02-aligned fields.
- Audit records are **ingested into the SIEM without manual intervention**, and can be searched/retrieved by **event type, target account, and time window**.
- Each audit record includes **actor attribution** (admin/service principal/system process) and a reliable **timestamp**.
- Audit records are retained for the organisation-defined period (per **AU-02**) and are available for **review/analysis/reporting** workflows (per **AU-06**).
- Evidence includes sample SIEM events for all five lifecycle actions from at least one production-like environment and screenshots/configuration evidence of the ingestion and normalisation pipeline.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Route all account lifecycle changes affecting the application through the enterprise IAM/IdP provisioning workflows (no direct/manual account edits where feasible)
- [ ] Enable audit logging for account creation, modification, enabling, disabling, and removal in the IAM/IdP and any connected account stores
- [ ] Implement a log normalisation pipeline that maps IAM/IdP lifecycle events into the organisation’s AU-02 audit record schema
- [ ] Configure automated SIEM ingestion for the normalised audit events and verify actor attribution and target identifiers are present
- [ ] Configure tamper-evident/append-only storage and validate time synchronisation across log sources and collectors
- [ ] Create AU-06-aligned detection/triage rules for account lifecycle anomalies and link them to the organisation’s case workflow
- [ ] Test retention by retrieving sample events for each lifecycle action after the configured retention window and document evidence

### ac-2.5 — Inactivity Logout (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure people do not leave their accounts logged in when they have stepped away for a while. If a user’s session stays open after 15 minutes of no activity, someone else could potentially use that unattended access to view or change information, causing financial loss, data exposure, or disruption to services. To meet this expectation, the organisation must clearly define and communicate a simple rule to users: after 15 minutes of inactivity, they must log out (sign out) if they expect they will be inactive for longer than 15 minutes, and they should do this using the normal log-out option rather than relying on automatic timeouts. The organisation must also include this rule in its account and user guidance, ensure the system is set up to support user log-out, document the approach, and keep records showing that logout happened when users followed the 15-minute expectation.

#### Expectation-based user logout guidance and reliable sign-out flow

- **Category:** Manual
- **Priority:** High

## WHAT
Implement an organisation-defined rule that users must log out when they expect inactivity to exceed the defined period (ac-02.05_odp), and ensure the application/IdP provides a reliable user-initiated sign-out path that actually terminates the user session. Communicate the rule to users in onboarding and in-product help.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-2(5) enhancement requires: “Require that users log out when {{ insert: param, ac-02.05_odp }}.” The guidance clarifies this is **behaviour- or policy-based** and that **automatic inactivity logout is addressed by AC-11**, so this enhancement must ensure users understand and can perform the expected logout action when they anticipate being inactive longer than the threshold.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the expectation-based wording** using the organisation parameter: *“After 15 minutes of inactivity, users must log out when they expect to be inactive longer than this period.”* Publish it in the access control/acceptable use policy and user-facing guidance.
2. **Implement/verify user-initiated logout** so that clicking “Log out/Sign out” from the application triggers the IdP sign-out flow (e.g., OIDC/SAML logout) and invalidates relevant tokens/session state so the user cannot continue using the application as authenticated.
3. **Add in-product and onboarding communication**: include a short “Step away safely” reminder (15-minute expectation) in onboarding materials and in-app help/footer; ensure it is visible at the point users can act (near the logout control or in the help menu).
4. **Centralise audit evidence**: ensure IdP sign-out events and application logout events are forwarded to the SIEM with consistent fields (user, application, timestamp, event type) to provide auditability that logout occurred when users followed the expectation.
5. **Operationalise exceptions**: create a helpdesk/SOC workflow to record and investigate cases where users report logout did not terminate access (treat as a security-relevant incident/defect ticket).

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Security/Compliance owner**: approves the policy wording and user expectation content.
- **Identity & Access Management (IAM) engineer**: implements and validates IdP/app sign-out behaviour.
- **Application owner/engineering lead**: ensures the application logout button triggers the correct sign-out and clears local session state.
- **SOC/SIEM engineer**: ensures logout events are logged and routed to the SIEM.
- **Service desk/helpdesk manager**: manages the intake and tracking of logout failures.

## Acceptance criteria
- The organisation can show a published rule stating: *after 15 minutes of expected inactivity, users must log out when they expect to be inactive longer than 15 minutes* (ac-02.05_odp).
- The application’s “Log out/Sign out” action reliably terminates the authenticated session (validated in a test environment for the supported login method(s)).
- Users receive the expectation-based instruction via onboarding and in-product help (evidence: screenshots/records of materials).
- Logout/sign-out events are present in audit logs/SIEM for the relevant application and IdP (evidence: sample log queries/events).
- Documented workflow exists for handling and tracking reported logout failures, with tickets/incidents created and reviewed.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Publish the expectation-based logout rule using ac-02.05_odp (15 minutes) in the access control/acceptable use policy and user guidance
- [ ] Update onboarding materials and in-product help to instruct users to log out when they expect inactivity to exceed 15 minutes
- [ ] Verify the application logout button triggers the IdP sign-out flow and clears local session state for supported login methods
- [ ] Configure/confirm SIEM forwarding of IdP sign-out and application logout events with consistent user/app/timestamp fields
- [ ] Create and document a helpdesk/SOC workflow to log, triage, and track reported logout failures as security-relevant defects/incidents

### ac-2.6 — Dynamic Privilege Management (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure people’s access rights change instantly when their role or circumstances change, rather than staying the same until they log out and back in. Without this, someone who is reassigned, working outside permitted hours, or should no longer have access could keep using privileges they no longer deserve, and in urgent situations the organisation may be slow to reduce access when it matters most. The organisation is expected to run access checks that are based on up-to-date information about the person and the situation, continuously re-checking the rules during use and immediately blocking or adjusting access when the rules say it should change, including when communications encryption keys are updated so the new key state is reflected right away. It must document how this works, keep clear procedures for updating the information that drives access, configure the systems to support these dynamic changes, and keep audit records showing when access was changed and why, including the impact of key updates.

#### Implement ABAC with real-time privilege revocation and key-epoch context

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT must be done
Implement dynamic privilege management so that authorisation decisions are made using up-to-date user and context attributes (ABAC), with immediate revocation/adjustment of privileges during active sessions. The mechanism must also support time- and function/job-based rules, emergency/duress-triggered privilege reduction, and propagation of communications encryption key changes into access decisions.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 AC-2(6) enhancement requires implementing **dynamic privilege management capabilities** (organisation-defined parameter **ac-02.06_odp**). Guidance explicitly calls for runtime access control decisions, **immediate revocation without requiring session restart**, dynamic rule evaluation (e.g., time/function changes, emergency/duress), and **effects of privilege changes**, including when **encryption keys used for communications are changed**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define an authoritative attribute model** for the application’s authorisation decisions, including: job function/assignment, time-of-day/work-time eligibility, emergency/duress status, and a **communications key epoch/version** (or equivalent key-state indicator) sourced from the organisation’s key management/orchestration system.
2. **Implement ABAC policy-as-code** in the central authorisation decision point (e.g., IdP/authorisation gateway/policy engine). Express rules for:
   - time-based eligibility,
   - function/job/assignment-based eligibility,
   - emergency/duress-triggered privilege reduction/removal,
   - and key-epoch/version alignment (deny or restrict access when the active key epoch/version in the request/session context does not match the current approved key state).
3. **Enforce dynamic decisions during active use** by making the application route authorisation through the decision point on every request (preferred) or via short-lived authorisation decisions/tokens with a documented maximum staleness window (fallback). Ensure that when attributes change, the next authorisation decision reflects the change without requiring user logout/restart.
4. **Implement real-time attribute propagation** from authoritative systems (HRIS/IAM/assignment systems and incident/duress systems) to the decision point using event-driven updates (e.g., webhook/event bus). Validate that attribute changes take effect within the organisation’s defined maximum propagation delay.
5. **Integrate key-change propagation** by emitting key-rotation/key-epoch events to the decision point and ensuring the authorisation context includes the current key epoch/version (or that the decision point independently evaluates current key state). Confirm that communications governed by the new key state are allowed and those tied to an old key state are denied/restricted immediately (or per documented expectation).
6. **Produce audit evidence**: log (a) attribute changes that affect authorisation, (b) privilege changes/revocations, (c) emergency/duress triggers, and (d) key-epoch/version changes and their impact on authorisation outcomes. Ensure logs include correlation identifiers for user/session/request.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Owner / Security Architect**: owns the attribute model, policy design, and enforcement approach.
- **IAM/IdP/Authorisation Gateway Team**: implements ABAC policy-as-code, decision-point integration, and token/decision staleness controls.
- **Platform/DevOps Team**: implements request-time enforcement wiring, event-driven attribute propagation, and key-epoch context integration.
- **Security Operations (SOC)/Incident Response**: provides/maintains emergency/duress attribute triggers and validates operational effectiveness.

## Acceptance criteria
- Active sessions cannot retain privileges after relevant attributes change (role/function, time eligibility, emergency/duress) without the next authorisation decision reflecting the change.
- Time- and function/job-based rules are enforced by ABAC policies evaluated at runtime.
- Emergency/duress triggers result in immediate privilege reduction/removal according to the defined policy.
- Communications encryption key changes (key epoch/version) are reflected in authorisation decisions without requiring session restart, and access tied to an outdated key state is denied/restricted.
- Audit logs demonstrate: who/what attribute changed, when it changed, what authorisation outcome changed, and how key-epoch/version changes impacted access.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define authoritative ABAC attributes including job/function, time eligibility, emergency/duress, and communications key epoch/version
- [ ] Implement ABAC policies as version-controlled policy-as-code in the central authorisation decision point
- [ ] Configure the application to enforce authorisation via request-time decisions or short-lived decisions with documented maximum staleness
- [ ] Implement event-driven propagation of attribute changes from IAM/HRIS/assignment and duress/incident systems to the decision point
- [ ] Integrate key-rotation/key-epoch events into the authorisation context and deny/restrict access when key state is outdated
- [ ] Enable and verify audit logging for attribute changes, privilege revocations, emergency triggers, and key-epoch impacts with correlation IDs

### ac-2.7 — Privileged User Accounts (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure only the right people have the extra, high-impact access needed to run and protect key parts of the organisation, and that this access is kept under close watch. Without it, a person could keep powerful permissions after their job changes, or the wrong permissions could be granted and go unnoticed, making it easier for mistakes or misuse to cause serious damage. To meet this expectation, the organisation must set up and manage privileged user accounts using its role-based access scheme, so privileged access is granted according to defined roles. It must continuously monitor which privileged roles (or role characteristics) each person has, track any changes to those roles or characteristics, and regularly check whether the privileged access is still appropriate. When it is no longer appropriate, the organisation must promptly remove that privileged access, and keep clear records showing what was changed and when, supported by audit and monitoring information that can be reviewed.

#### RBAC-governed privileged access with continuous role-change monitoring

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT must be done
Implement privileged user account administration so that privileged access is granted only through the organisation-defined **role-based access scheme**, and ensure privileged role/attribute assignments are **continuously monitored for changes**. When privileged access is no longer appropriate, it must be **promptly revoked** and the revocation must be **recorded with audit/monitoring evidence**.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-2(7) enhancement requires privileged access to be managed via an approved scheme (organisation-defined parameter: **role-based access scheme**) and supported by ongoing monitoring and timely revocation when access is no longer appropriate.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define privileged roles in RBAC** aligned to the application’s administrative functions (e.g., application admin, database admin, network/admin console access, key management, web administration) and map each role to authoritative identity groups/entitlements.
2. **Make the identity provider (IdP) the authoritative source** for privileged role membership. Configure downstream authorisation (admin consoles, cloud resources, databases, CI/CD admin endpoints) to rely on IdP-issued group/role claims rather than manual local assignments.
3. **Enforce privileged access via an approval workflow** (e.g., identity governance/entitlement management) with time-bounded elevation where feasible, ensuring privileged roles are not granted directly outside the workflow.
4. **Continuously monitor privileged role membership and changes** by streaming IdP audit logs and group/entitlement change events to the SIEM/monitoring platform, with detections for:
   - new privileged role assignments,
   - privileged role removals,
   - role membership changes for existing users,
   - anomalous patterns (e.g., off-hours, new admin accounts, unexpected role combinations).
5. **Maintain a system-generated privileged account list** by periodically (and on-change) reconciling the IdP’s privileged role/group membership against the application’s privileged access surfaces, producing an auditable “current state” report.
6. **Automate prompt revocation** when access is no longer appropriate using HR lifecycle signals (joiner/mover/leaver) and/or role-change triggers from the governance workflow; ensure revocation actions record: user, role, timestamp, approver/automation actor, and reason.
7. **Produce review-ready evidence**: retain audit logs and monitoring outputs that demonstrate (a) role-based assignment, (b) continuous monitoring of changes, and (c) timely revocation and recording.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **IAM/Identity Governance Lead**: owns RBAC role definitions, IdP configuration, and entitlement workflows.
- **Application Owner / Security Architect**: validates that privileged roles map correctly to administrative functions and that downstream systems honour IdP roles.
- **SOC/SIEM Engineer**: implements detections, alerting, and evidence retention.
- **HR/Workforce Systems Integrations Owner**: ensures joiner/mover/leaver signals drive revocation.

## Acceptance criteria
- Privileged access to application administrative functions is granted only via IdP-backed RBAC roles (no direct/manual privileged assignments bypassing the workflow).
- The organisation can produce a **system-generated list** of current privileged users/roles from the IdP within the defined reporting interval.
- Privileged role membership changes generate monitored events in the SIEM (additions/removals/updates) with alerting/detection coverage.
- When a user’s privileged role is no longer appropriate (e.g., leaver/mover), the privileged role is revoked within the organisation’s operational target time and the revocation is recorded with auditable metadata.
- Audit evidence exists showing role-based assignment, monitoring of changes, and revocation actions (including timestamps and responsible actor).

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define privileged RBAC roles for the application’s administrative functions and map each role to IdP groups/entitlements
- [ ] Configure the IdP as the authoritative source for privileged role membership and disable direct/manual privileged assignments where feasible
- [ ] Implement an approval-based entitlement workflow for privileged role grants (with time-bounded elevation where supported)
- [ ] Stream IdP privileged role/group change events and audit logs to the SIEM/monitoring platform and create detections for privileged role additions/removals/changes
- [ ] Build and schedule a system-generated privileged account list by reconciling IdP privileged memberships with application privileged access surfaces
- [ ] Automate privileged role revocation on HR lifecycle events and record revocation metadata (user, role, timestamp, reason, actor)
- [ ] Verify audit evidence retention and produce a review pack demonstrating role-based assignment, continuous monitoring, and timely revocation

### ac-2.8 — Dynamic Account Management (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about automatically creating and switching on, updating, and switching off the special accounts that on-prem systems and integrations use to do their work, exactly when they are needed. Without this, accounts can be set up late, left active after they are no longer required, or given more access than they should have, increasing the risk of unauthorised access, misuse, and lingering access that is hard to trace. The organisation is expected to define which service and privileged system accounts are in scope, document how this dynamic account lifecycle works, and include it in its security planning and day-to-day procedures. It must use automated mechanisms to create, activate, manage, and deactivate these accounts at runtime, including for previously unknown integration entities, but only after checking that the request is properly authorised and that only the approved level of access is granted or removed. It should also keep an up-to-date list of such accounts and record what the automation did, so actions are auditable.

#### Automated JIT provisioning for in-scope system accounts

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement an automated, runtime “just-in-time” (JIT) lifecycle for the organisation-defined **system accounts** (service accounts and privileged system accounts) used by on-prem applications and integrations: **create, activate, manage (update), and deactivate** dynamically.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-2(8)** enhancement requires the organisation to **create, activate, manage, and deactivate system accounts dynamically**. Guidance expects runtime provisioning for previously unknown entities, supported by **trust relationships, business rules, and mechanisms** to validate authorisations and privileges.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define the in-scope dynamic account lifecycle model
- Create a documented account lifecycle specification covering: create → activate → manage/update → deactivate, including triggers (e.g., first-seen integration entity, entitlement change, expiry, integration disabled, partner removed).
- Define required metadata for each dynamic account (e.g., integration entity identifier, environment, owner/approver, expiry, privilege template/version).

### 2) Establish an authoritative entity registry and trust validation
- Maintain an **authoritative registry** of allowed integration entities/partners (and their allowed access attributes) that automation can query.
- Implement a trust/authorisation validation step that checks:
  - the runtime entity identifier is present in the registry,
  - the requested operation (create/activate/update/deactivate) is permitted for that entity,
  - the requested privilege template is permitted for that entity type,
  - required approval evidence (or pre-authorised rule match) is present and valid.

### 3) Implement policy-as-code privilege mapping
- Encode business rules as policy-as-code (or equivalent policy engine) to map entity attributes → **least-privilege** roles/groups/entitlements.
- Ensure “manage” operations only adjust privileges according to the policy outcome (no manual privilege drift).

### 4) Automate runtime provisioning and lifecycle transitions
- When an integration event occurs (including previously unknown entities), the integration platform/workflow must call a provisioning API (or emit an event to a workflow engine) with:
  - entity identifier,
  - requested lifecycle operation,
  - requested privilege template/version,
  - correlation identifiers.
- The provisioning workflow must only proceed after authorisation validation succeeds.
- Activation must be time-bounded where feasible (expiry/lease) and deactivation must revoke access and disable the account in all target systems.

### 5) Ensure auditable lifecycle evidence
- For every lifecycle action (create/activate/manage/deactivate), write structured audit records that include:
  - automation/workflow identifier,
  - runtime trigger/correlation ID,
  - entity identifier,
  - account identifier created/changed,
  - privilege template/version applied or removed,
  - authorisation decision outcome (allow/deny) and reason codes.
- Forward audit events to the organisation SIEM/logging platform with retention aligned to enterprise logging requirements.

### 6) Maintain a system-generated list of dynamic accounts
- Treat the IGA/provisioning directory (or equivalent authoritative store) as the source of truth for dynamic accounts.
- Continuously reconcile the list of active dynamic accounts against the registry and lifecycle rules (including expiry).

## WHO
- **Security architect / IAM lead**: defines lifecycle model, registry model, privilege templates, and policy-as-code rules.
- **Application/integration platform owner**: implements runtime triggers and provisioning API/event integration.
- **Identity & Access Management (IGA) engineers**: implement workflow automation, authorisation validation, and audit logging.
- **Approvers / security governance**: define approval rules and validate policy templates.

## Acceptance criteria
- Given a runtime integration event for an in-scope entity, the system automatically **creates and activates** the corresponding system account only after authorisation validation succeeds.
- Given an entitlement change or allowed update trigger, the system performs a **manage/update** action that results in privileges matching the policy outcome (no privilege escalation beyond the template).
- Given a deactivation trigger (expiry, integration disabled, partner removed), the system **deactivates** the account and revokes privileges in all configured target systems.
- For each lifecycle action, audit records exist and are queryable in SIEM, showing the runtime trigger, entity identifier, account identifier, privilege template/version, and authorisation decision outcome.
- A system-generated inventory/list of dynamic accounts exists and reflects current active accounts and their lifecycle metadata (including expiry/owner/approver reference).

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define and document the in-scope dynamic system account lifecycle (create/activate/manage/deactivate) including triggers and required metadata
- [ ] Create and maintain an authoritative entity/partner registry that automation can query for allowed access attributes
- [ ] Implement policy-as-code privilege mapping from entity attributes to least-privilege roles/groups/entitlements with versioning
- [ ] Build a provisioning workflow/API that performs authorisation validation before any lifecycle action and supports runtime unknown entities
- [ ] Configure target systems (directory/app/database/messaging) to support automated enable/disable and privilege assignment changes
- [ ] Implement structured audit logging for every lifecycle action including automation ID, correlation ID, entity ID, account ID, privilege template/version, and allow/deny decision
- [ ] Establish reconciliation/expiry handling so deactivation occurs automatically when lifecycle conditions are no longer met

### ac-2.9 — Restrictions on Use of Shared and Group Accounts (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure shared or group logins are only used when someone is clearly responsible and actions can still be traced back to a specific person. Without these safeguards, it becomes easy for the wrong person to use the account, for changes or mistakes to go unchallenged, and for investigations after an incident to stall because no one can be held accountable. To meet this expectation, the organisation must only allow shared or group accounts when a named custodian is assigned to each one and that custodian’s responsibility is recorded. Access must be limited to only the approved tasks the organisation has documented for these accounts. The organisation must also ensure individual accountability either by linking each user to the shared or group account in a way that produces clear evidence of who did what, or, if that linking is not used, by putting in place alternative measures that provide the same level of traceability. The organisation should consider and document the extra risk created by reduced accountability before allowing such accounts.

#### Custodian-governed shared accounts with per-user attribution

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Only permit shared and group accounts when (1) a **named custodian** is assigned and recorded, (2) access is limited to **approved functions**, and (3) **individual accountability** is enforced through **unique user-to-account mapping** (or equivalent compensating controls).

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-2(9) enhancement requires that shared/group accounts are permitted **only when they meet the organisation-defined conditions** (param: **ac-02.02.09_odp conditions**). The guidance highlights the increased risk of reduced accountability for shared/group accounts; therefore, the organisation must ensure actions remain traceable to individuals.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
Implement a custodian-governed shared/group account model integrated with the enterprise IAM and logging stack:
1. **Create an authoritative shared/group account registry** (IAM/IdP admin metadata store or ticket-integrated CMDB) that records, for each shared/group account: account identifier, role(s), **approved functions**, **named custodian**, environment (prod/test/DR), and lifecycle dates.
2. **Enforce custodian assignment as a creation/enablement gate**: block creation or enablement of any shared/group account unless the registry entry includes a named custodian and approved functions.
3. **Restrict access to approved functions only** by configuring the application/admin interfaces to use least-privilege roles and allow-lists aligned to the registry’s approved functions (e.g., RBAC/ABAC roles, constrained admin tooling, or command allow-lists).
4. **Enforce unique user-to-account mapping** using an enterprise pattern that produces auditable attribution:
   - Preferred: route access through a **PAM/access broker** or **privileged session** mechanism that records “user X used shared/group account Y for action Z” and binds sessions to the shared/group account.
   - If the shared/group account must be used directly by users, implement an **access delegation** pattern where the shared/group account is not directly used without an intermediary that logs the initiating user identity.
5. **Make audit evidence audit-ready**: ensure logs/SIEM events include (at minimum) initiating user identity, shared/group account identifier, session identifier, timestamp, and the executed action (or the closest available action granularity). Retain logs per enterprise retention requirements.
6. **Add a governance check for the increased risk**: document the risk acceptance rationale (why shared/group is necessary despite reduced accountability) and require periodic review of custodian assignment, approved functions, and mapping effectiveness.

## WHO
- **IAM/Identity Engineering Lead**: implements the registry, gating workflow, and mapping enforcement (PAM/access broker or equivalent).
- **Application Owner / Security Architect**: defines and enforces approved functions (RBAC/ABAC/allow-lists) and ensures admin actions are constrained.
- **Security Operations (SOC) / SIEM Engineer**: ensures audit fields and correlation rules are implemented and validated.
- **Account Custodian (named role)**: owns the approved functions list and validates periodic access reviews.

## Acceptance criteria
- Every shared/group account in the application environments has a **registry entry** with a **named custodian** and **approved functions**.
- No shared/group account is enabled without custodian metadata and approved-function constraints (verified by configuration/gating evidence).
- For every privileged action performed via a shared/group account, audit records show **initiating user identity** and the **shared/group account identifier** (via unique user-to-account mapping or equivalent attribution).
- SIEM correlation rules can produce an investigation-ready timeline linking user → session → shared/group account → action.
- Periodic review evidence exists showing custodian assignment and approved functions remain current.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define and implement a shared/group account registry schema including named custodian, approved functions, and lifecycle metadata
- [ ] Configure an enablement gate so shared/group accounts cannot be created or enabled without custodian assignment and approved-function definitions
- [ ] Implement approved-function restrictions in the application/admin interfaces using least-privilege RBAC/ABAC and allow-lists aligned to the registry
- [ ] Enforce unique user-to-account mapping via an access broker/PAM or equivalent session attribution mechanism that logs user→shared account→action
- [ ] Update logging and SIEM ingestion so audit events include initiating user identity, shared/group account identifier, session ID, and executed action
- [ ] Run a control validation test to confirm every shared/group account action is attributable to a named individual and produces investigation-ready evidence
- [ ] Establish a periodic review process for custodian assignment and approved functions, retaining evidence for audits

### ac-2.11 — Usage Conditions (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure special automated work accounts are only used in the right way, at the right time, for the right purpose. Without these limits, an account meant for routine system administration or automated batch jobs could be misused outside planned work periods, used as if a person were logging in directly, or left running too long, increasing the chance of unauthorised changes, hidden misuse, and hard-to-trace incidents. To meet this expectation, the organisation must set clear rules for every in-scope service account so it can be used only during approved maintenance windows, cannot be used for interactive logons (no direct user-style sessions), and can only run in time-bounded sessions that never exceed 8 hours. The system must enforce these rules, record what happened, analyse any misuse, and raise alerts when an account is used outside the approved conditions, with enough detail to identify the account and the specific rule that was broken.

#### Enforce service-account usage windows and block interactive logons

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT must be done
Enforce the organisation-defined usage conditions for the specified **system accounts**: **only during approved maintenance windows**, **no interactive logons**, and **time-bounded sessions not exceeding 8 hours**.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-2(11) (Enhancement) requires that the organisation **enforce** the defined **circumstances and/or usage conditions** for the defined **system accounts**, so that account monitoring can detect and alert on usage in violation of organisational parameters.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
Implement enforcement at the identity/access layer and the administrative access path so that service accounts cannot be used interactively and cannot run outside the approved maintenance windows.

1. **Create an authoritative mapping of in-scope system accounts** (service accounts used for on-prem infrastructure administration and automated batch processing) to a dedicated service-account group/attribute set (e.g., `svc-infra-admin`, `svc-batch`) in the identity system.
2. **Encode the usage conditions** as machine-enforceable policy inputs:
   - Maintenance-window schedule (days/times, timezone, and exception handling) sourced from the change/maintenance system.
   - “No interactive logons” rule (deny interactive sign-in flows for these accounts).
   - “≤ 8 hours” rule (session lifetime / token lifetime / re-authentication controls so sessions cannot exceed 8 hours).
3. **Enforce “no interactive logons”** by blocking interactive authentication methods for the service-account group(s) and ensuring administrative access to on-prem infrastructure is mediated via controlled tooling (e.g., bastion/PAM/jump host) that uses non-interactive or explicitly approved admin workflows.
4. **Enforce “maintenance-window-only”** by applying conditional access rules that allow the service-account group(s) only when the current time falls within the approved maintenance window; deny otherwise.
5. **Enforce “time-bounded sessions ≤ 8 hours”** by configuring session lifetime and/or token/session revalidation so that any authenticated session for these accounts is terminated or forced to re-establish within the 8-hour cap.
6. **Generate audit evidence and alerts**:
   - Ensure audit logs capture: account identifier, authentication attempt type (interactive vs non-interactive), start/end timestamps (or session duration), and the policy decision outcome.
   - Configure SIEM/SOAR detections to alert on: (a) any interactive logon attempt by these accounts, (b) any use outside maintenance windows, and (c) any session duration exceeding 8 hours.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Identity/IAM engineer**: implement conditional access/session controls and policy-as-code automation.
- **Security monitoring/SOC engineer**: implement SIEM detections and alert routing.
- **Infrastructure administrator / PAM owner**: ensure admin access is routed through mediated paths that comply with the “no interactive logons” requirement.
- **Change/maintenance process owner**: provide the authoritative maintenance-window schedule feed.

## Acceptance criteria
- For all in-scope system accounts, interactive logon attempts are denied and generate audit records.
- For all in-scope system accounts, authentication/usage outside approved maintenance windows is denied and generates audit records and alerts.
- For all in-scope system accounts, any authenticated session is prevented from exceeding 8 hours (terminated or forced to re-establish within the cap) and generates audit records.
- SIEM produces alerts for each violation type with sufficient detail to identify the account and the violated rule (maintenance-window-only, no interactive logons, or ≤8 hours).

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Identify and tag all in-scope system accounts (on-prem infrastructure administration and automated batch processing) in the identity system
- [ ] Define a machine-readable maintenance-window schedule and connect it to the conditional access policy inputs
- [ ] Configure conditional access to deny interactive sign-in for the service-account group(s)
- [ ] Configure conditional access to allow service-account usage only during approved maintenance windows
- [ ] Set session/token lifetime controls so service-account sessions cannot exceed 8 hours
- [ ] Enable and centralise audit logging for authentication attempts, session duration, and policy decision outcomes
