- [ ] Create SIEM/SOAR detections and alerting for interactive attempts, out-of-window usage, and sessions exceeding 8 hours

### ac-2.12 — Account Monitoring for Atypical Usage (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about keeping a close watch on system accounts so you can spot suspicious logins quickly, especially when they happen outside normal working hours or from places or network areas that are not approved for that system. Without this, a rogue insider, a stolen access, or an ongoing attack could go unnoticed while someone uses system accounts in ways that don’t match normal patterns. To meet this expectation, the organisation must define what counts as atypical use (out-of-hours and unapproved location or network segment), monitor all system accounts for those signs, and record enough detail to identify which account was used and when and from where. Every time atypical usage is detected, it must be reported to the Security Operations Centre incident response lead and the system owner. Because this monitoring can reveal sensitive behaviour patterns, the organisation must complete and keep up to date a privacy risk assessment and ensure the approach matches its privacy plan, including how data is limited and protected.

#### Atypical system-account login monitoring with dual escalation

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement continuous monitoring for **atypical usage of system accounts**, defined as **logins outside normal duty hours** and **logins from unapproved geolocations or network segments**, and ensure each detected event is **recorded with sufficient detail** and **escalated to both the SOC incident response lead and the relevant system owner**.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-2(12) enhancement guidance requires the organisation to: (1) **define atypical usage**, (2) **monitor accounts** for that atypical usage, (3) **record enough information** to identify the account and login context, (4) **report/escalate atypical usage** to the **SOC incident response lead** and **system owner**, and (5) complete and keep up to date a **privacy risk assessment** aligned with the **privacy programme plan**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define monitoring criteria per system/account group**: configure “normal duty hours” and “approved geolocations/network segments” for system accounts (e.g., by system owner group), using the organisation-defined parameters:
   - atypical usage = **out-of-hours logins** and **unapproved geolocations or network segments**.
2. **Ensure complete coverage of system accounts**: identify all system accounts and map each to its authentication/log source (IdP sign-in logs, VPN/jump host logs, local auth, service account auth). For any system accounts not covered by the IdP, integrate their authentication logs into the same monitoring pipeline.
3. **Implement detection in the SIEM (detection-as-code)**: create SIEM correlation rules that trigger when a system account login matches either atypical condition. Each alert must include at minimum: **system account identifier**, **timestamp**, **source IP**, and **derived/recorded geolocation and/or network segment** (as available), plus the **authentication method**.
4. **Escalate every atypical event to both parties**: use SOAR (or an equivalent workflow) to route each alert to:
   - the **SOC incident response lead** and
   - the **system owner**,
   using a routing map (system → owner) and ticket templates that include the minimum actionable fields from the SIEM alert.
5. **Privacy risk assessment and minimisation**: complete/maintain a privacy impact assessment for atypical usage monitoring and enforce privacy-plan-aligned controls, including:
   - minimising retained fields (store derived location/network segment rather than unnecessary raw identifiers where feasible),
   - restricting access to monitoring data to authorised roles, and
   - setting retention periods consistent with the privacy programme plan.

## WHO
- **SOC incident response lead**: receives and triages atypical usage alerts; initiates incident response actions.
- **System owner**: receives alerts for their systems; validates whether activity is expected and drives remediation.
- **Security engineering / IAM-SIEM integration team**: implements SIEM detections, log integrations, and SOAR escalation workflows.
- **Privacy officer / DPO (or equivalent)**: owns the privacy risk assessment and ensures alignment with the privacy programme plan.

## Acceptance criteria
- Atypical usage is defined and implemented exactly as: **out-of-hours logins** and **logins from unapproved geolocations or network segments**.
- All identified system accounts are covered by monitoring (via IdP and/or integrated authentication log sources).
- For each detected atypical event, an alert is generated containing the account identifier and login context (time + geolocation and/or network segment).
- Every alert is escalated to **both** the SOC incident response lead and the **system owner** within the organisation’s operational response window.
- A current privacy impact assessment exists for this monitoring approach, and monitoring data handling (collection, access, retention) matches the privacy programme plan.


**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define and publish per-system “normal duty hours” and “approved geolocations/network segments” for system accounts
- [ ] Inventory all system accounts and map each to its authentication/log source, integrating missing sources into the SIEM pipeline
- [ ] Implement SIEM correlation rules (detection-as-code) to detect out-of-hours and unapproved geolocation/network-segment logins for system accounts
- [ ] Configure alert enrichment so each atypical event includes account identifier, timestamp, and source context (geolocation and/or network segment)
- [ ] Deploy SOAR (or equivalent workflow) to escalate every atypical alert to both the SOC incident response lead and the relevant system owner
- [ ] Complete or update the privacy impact assessment and enforce privacy-plan-aligned minimisation, access restrictions, and retention for monitoring data

### ac-2.13 — Disable Accounts for High-risk Individuals (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about acting fast to stop dangerous access by switching off the accounts of people who are judged to be high risk. If an individual is credibly linked to an intention to cause harm using their authorised access, or if there is credible evidence that an attacker could cause harm through them, leaving their account active could allow unauthorised actions, damage to company systems and assets, harm to other organisations, or harm to individuals. To meet this expectation, the organisation must define what counts as credible evidence of this kind, identify the affected individuals, and disable their system accounts within 24 hours of discovering that evidence. The organisation must also keep clear, checkable records showing which accounts were disabled and when, maintain a list of risky user activities to support identification, and document the process in its account-management procedures and related system plans. Finally, it must coordinate promptly with system administrators, information security staff, human resources, legal advisers, and the authorising officials to ensure the right accounts are disabled correctly and with appropriate oversight.

#### 24-hour high-risk account disablement via IdP/PAM workflow

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement an auditable, time-bound workflow that disables system accounts for individuals identified as high-risk within **24 hours** of discovery, based on **credible evidence** (intent to cause harm via authorised access, or credible evidence an adversary can cause harm through the individual).

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST **AC-2(13)** enhancement requires: **“Disable accounts of individuals within {{time period}} of discovery of {{significant risks}}.”** The guidance further requires close coordination among **system administrators, legal staff, HR managers, and authorising officials** when disabling accounts for high-risk individuals.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define credible evidence criteria** in a controlled policy/workflow form with structured fields (e.g., insider-risk case type, threat intel source, HR incident linkage, legal assessment outcome) aligned to the organisation-defined parameter values:
   - **time period**: *within 24 hours* (ac-02.13_odp.01)
   - **significant risks**: *credible evidence of intent to cause harm via authorised access or evidence an adversary can cause harm through the individual* (ac-02.13_odp.02)
2. **Create a case-to-identity mapping**: when a high-risk case is raised (SOC/insider risk/HR/legal), automatically populate the affected identity attributes (e.g., user principal name, employee/contractor ID, groups, and any linked service accounts) and determine which accounts must be disabled (IdP user, federated apps, and any PAM-managed accounts).
3. **Automate disablement at the identity layer**: upon case approval (or emergency override with post-facto authorising official review), call IdP admin APIs to:
   - disable the user account
   - revoke active sessions/tokens where supported
   - enforce sign-in denial while deprovisioning propagates
4. **Extend disablement to downstream access paths** using existing enterprise integrations:
   - **SCIM/deprovisioning** for SaaS apps
   - **group-based access** for internal applications
   - **PAM** to immediately revoke/lock privileged credentials and sessions for the affected identity.
5. **Enforce the 24-hour SLA with measurable timestamps**: record (a) discovery time, (b) approval time, (c) disablement start/finish time, and (d) verification time. Generate an exception if disablement is not completed within 24 hours and route to the authorising official.
6. **Produce audit-ready evidence**: ensure the workflow retains immutable records linking the case ID to the identity changes, including IdP audit logs (account disabled, session revocation) and PAM/AD/LDAP change logs.
7. **Coordinate roles and approvals**: implement an approval path requiring involvement of **system administrators** (execution/technical validation), **HR** (employment/contractor context), **legal** (lawfulness/constraints), and **authorising official** (final approval/override). Require phishing-resistant MFA for approvers.

## WHO
- **Information Security / IAM team**: owns the workflow design, evidence criteria, and IdP/PAM integrations.
- **System administrators**: validate technical execution and downstream propagation.
- **HR managers**: provide identity/contract context and support case classification.
- **Legal staff**: confirm constraints and approval requirements for disabling.
- **Authorising official**: approves disablement decisions and emergency overrides.

## Acceptance criteria
- For test cases marked as “credible evidence” per the defined criteria, the affected identity is disabled (and sessions revoked where supported) in the IdP **within 24 hours of discovery**.
- The workflow produces auditable records showing discovery time, approval/override decision, disablement completion time, and verification evidence (IdP/PAM/AD logs).
- Downstream access is blocked (SaaS via SCIM/deprovisioning; internal apps via federation/group revocation; privileged access via PAM lock/revoke).
- Exceptions (missed SLA) are automatically raised to the authorising official with documented justification.
- Role-based approvals demonstrate coordination among system administrators, HR, legal, and the authorising official for each disablement case.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define and approve structured “credible evidence” criteria and map them to the workflow fields for AC-2(13) (intent via authorised access or adversary harm through the individual).
- [ ] Implement a case-to-identity mapping that identifies all affected accounts (IdP user, federated apps, and PAM/privileged identities) from the case record.
- [ ] Configure an IdP-driven disablement automation that disables the account and revokes sessions/tokens where supported, triggered by approved high-risk cases.
- [ ] Integrate SCIM/deprovisioning and group/federation revocation so downstream application access is blocked consistently after disablement.
- [ ] Integrate PAM to immediately revoke/lock privileged credentials and terminate active privileged sessions for the affected identity.
- [ ] Add SLA enforcement and timestamped evidence capture (discovery, approval, disablement completion, verification) and generate exceptions for any breach of the 24-hour window.
- [ ] Establish an approval path requiring system administrator execution, HR/legal input, and authorising official approval/override with phishing-resistant MFA for approvers.

### ac-3 — Access Enforcement (Control)

#### Enforce role-based authorisation at identity, app, and data layers

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement and continuously enforce approved authorisations for logical access to the application and its underlying resources (APIs, UI functions, services, and data) so that only identities and processes granted by the organisation’s access control policy can perform permitted actions.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-3 requires the organisation to **enforce approved authorisations for logical access** to information and system resources **in accordance with applicable access control policies**. This is satisfied when enforcement exists at the system/application level (not just documented policy) and is verifiable through configuration and audit evidence.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Map policy roles/permissions to concrete enforcement points** across the identity provider (e.g., Okta/Azure AD), application authorisation (RBAC/ABAC), and data access (database roles/row-level security), ensuring each permission in the policy has an implementation owner and target system.
2. **Centralise identity-to-role assignment** using the enterprise IAM platform (group/app assignments) and ensure the application consumes authorisation claims/groups from the identity provider rather than relying on local/manual role assignment.
3. **Implement server-side authorisation checks** for every protected endpoint/function (UI actions, API routes, background jobs, and service-to-service calls). Deny by default; authorise only when the caller’s role/claims satisfy the approved permission model.
4. **Enforce least-privilege at the data layer** using database roles and, where applicable, row/attribute-level controls so that even if an application bug occurs, data access remains constrained to approved authorisations.
5. **Handle service accounts and privileged functions explicitly**: use dedicated service identities, restrict scopes/roles, and ensure privileged operations require explicit approved authorisations (and are not reachable via standard user roles).
6. **Produce audit-ready evidence**: configure and retain logs showing authorisation decisions (e.g., denied/allowed outcomes), identity-to-role assignment changes, and application access enforcement behaviour; validate with automated tests that unauthorised requests are denied.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Security Lead / App Owner**: owns the permission model mapping and server-side enforcement implementation.
- **IAM Engineer**: owns identity provider role/group/app assignment configuration.
- **Database Administrator (DBA) / Data Platform Engineer**: owns database role/row-level enforcement.
- **Security Operations / SOC**: ensures logging, retention, and monitoring are aligned for audit evidence.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
- There is an approved access control policy that defines roles/permissions and approval expectations, and it is **mapped** to enforcement points (identity groups/app roles, application authorisation rules, and database roles).
- For the application’s protected endpoints and data operations, **unauthorised identities are denied** (documented test results or automated test evidence showing 401/403 or equivalent denial).
- Identity-to-role assignments are enforced via the enterprise IAM platform, and changes are auditable (audit logs or change records exist).
- Data access is constrained by database roles and/or row-level controls consistent with the approved permission model.
- Evidence exists that authorisation enforcement is active in production (configuration exports and logs demonstrating denied access for unauthorised roles).

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Create a role/permission matrix from the approved access control policy and map each permission to identity groups, application authorisation rules, and database roles
- [ ] Configure the enterprise identity provider to assign roles via approved group/app assignments and ensure role assignment changes are logged
- [ ] Implement deny-by-default server-side authorisation checks for every protected UI action and API endpoint using identity claims/roles
- [ ] Add automated tests that verify unauthorised requests are denied (e.g., 401/403) for each protected endpoint and privileged function
- [ ] Enforce least-privilege at the data layer using database roles and row/attribute-level controls aligned to the permission matrix
- [ ] Enable and retain application and identity audit logs that capture authorisation outcomes (allowed/denied) and correlate them to identity and permission changes

### ac-3.2 — Dual Authorization (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This expectation is about making sure that important, high-risk changes are never carried out by just one person acting alone: any privileged command or other action that changes who can access systems, alters security settings, or makes administrative changes to Secret/Confidential information must be approved and then carried out by two different authorised individuals. Without this “two-person” approach, a single insider, or a compromised account, could quietly change permissions or security settings and cause unauthorised access, data exposure, or other serious harm. To meet this expectation, the organisation must clearly define and document exactly which commands and actions require two approvals, keep up-to-date lists of them, and specify which people are allowed to provide those approvals. The system and its procedures must block execution unless two distinct authorised approvals are recorded before the change happens, and keep evidence of who approved what and when. The organisation should also consider how to reduce collusion risk and how to handle urgent situations where immediate action may be needed.

#### Dual-approval gate for privileged access/security/Secret changes

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement a two-person (dual authorization) mechanism so that any **privileged command and/or other action** in scope—specifically those that **change access control settings, security configurations, or execute administrative changes to Secret/Confidential data**—cannot be executed unless **two distinct authorised individuals** have approved the action **before** execution.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-3(2) Dual Authorization (enhancement)** requires enforcing dual authorization for the organisation-defined set of privileged commands/actions (AC-3.02_odp). This reduces risk from insider threats by ensuring no single person can unilaterally make high-impact changes.

## HOW (specific approach)
1. **Define the in-scope privileged actions** by maintaining an authoritative catalogue mapped to the three categories in the parameter guidance:
   - access control setting changes,
   - security configuration changes,
   - administrative changes to Secret/Confidential data.
2. **Create a dual-approval workflow** in the organisation’s change/ITSM or privileged-operations orchestration tool:
   - Require **two approvals**.
   - Enforce that the two approvers are **two different authorised individuals** (no same user twice; initiator cannot be an approver).
   - Record approver identity and timestamps.
3. **Gate execution technically**:
   - Integrate the workflow with the execution mechanism (e.g., CI/CD pipeline, infrastructure-as-code runner, admin console API, or runbook automation).
   - The execution step must **refuse to run** unless the workflow is in a state indicating **“approved by two distinct authorised individuals”**.
4. **Ensure audit-ready evidence**:
   - Store workflow approval events and execution events together (request ID, approver identities, timestamps, and the exact change payload/command identifier).
   - Forward logs to SIEM and retain per enterprise logging retention.
5. **Prevent bypass**:
   - Restrict direct administrative pathways (RBAC/ABAC, break-glass controls) so that in-scope actions can only be performed through the gated execution path.
6. **Handle emergencies with documented safety exceptions**:
   - Define an emergency process that is risk-assessed and time-bounded, with compensating controls (e.g., immediate post-action dual review, enhanced monitoring, and mandatory incident logging). Use this only where immediate response is necessary for public/environmental safety, as guidance suggests.

## WHO
- **Application/Platform Owner**: owns the in-scope actions catalogue and ensures mapping to the workflow.
- **Security/Compliance Lead**: validates scope, approval rules, and evidence requirements.
- **IAM/Privileged Access Administrator**: configures authorised approver roles/groups and enforces RBAC/ABAC to prevent bypass.
- **ITSM/Automation Engineer**: implements the dual-approval workflow and execution gating integration.
- **Approvers (two-person authorisation group members)**: provide approvals and are accountable for recorded decisions.

## Acceptance criteria
- For every in-scope privileged action (per **ac-03.02_odp** definition), execution is blocked unless **two distinct authorised individuals** have approved **before** the change occurs.
- System evidence exists showing: initiator, both approvers’ identities, timestamps, and the exact change payload/command identifier, correlated to the execution event.
- Attempts to execute in-scope actions outside the workflow (direct admin/API/script paths) are prevented or produce an auditable denial.
- Emergency/safety exceptions (if used) are documented, time-bounded, and followed by compensating dual review and incident logging.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Create and maintain an authoritative catalogue of in-scope privileged commands/actions mapped to ac-03.02_odp (access control, security configuration, Secret/Confidential admin changes).
- [ ] Configure an approval workflow requiring two distinct authorised approvers (no same-person approvals; initiator cannot approve).
- [ ] Integrate the workflow with the privileged execution mechanism so execution is refused unless the workflow state indicates two approvals completed before run.
- [ ] Implement audit logging that records approver identities, timestamps, and the exact change payload/command identifier correlated to execution.
- [ ] Enforce RBAC/ABAC and privileged access restrictions to prevent bypass of the gated execution path for in-scope actions.
- [ ] Define and document an emergency/safety exception process with compensating controls (post-action dual review, enhanced monitoring, and incident logging).

### ac-3.3 — Mandatory Access Control (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about stopping people and automated services from misusing information once they have access, by enforcing strict rules about who can receive it and what they can do with it. Without this, confidential material could be copied or shared into more sensitive areas, leaked to unauthorised colleagues, or used to quietly expand someone’s permissions, change how information is labelled, or even alter the rules that decide access—creating a serious risk of data breaches and improper access. To meet this expectation, the organisation must define a clear confidentiality policy that says information cannot be passed to anyone or anything with a lower security level (no write up and no read down), and that access privileges cannot be passed on, security labels cannot be changed, new or changed items cannot be given new labels, and the access rules cannot be altered. The system must apply these rules consistently across all covered users, service accounts, and trusted processes and across all covered resources labelled Confidential or Secret, with only explicitly approved trusted identities allowed the limited read, write, and execute exceptions defined by the policy.

#### Enforce Bell-LaPadula MAC with uniform label mediation

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement and operate a uniformly enforced Mandatory Access Control (MAC) mechanism that enforces the organisation-defined Bell‑LaPadula policy: **no write up** and **no read down** across all covered subjects and objects, with enforcement mediated by a reference-monitor style component.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-3(3) requires the system to **enforce the organisation-defined mandatory access control policy** over the set of covered subjects and objects specified in the policy. The guidance emphasises that enforcement must be **uniform** to prevent circumvention, and that the implementation should meet the **reference monitor concept** (AC-25). It also requires mandatory constraints to take precedence over less rigorous discretionary constraints (AC-3(4)).

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the MAC policy in policy-as-code** for the covered set:
   - Subjects: explicitly include authorised users, service accounts, and trusted system processes.
   - Objects: include all information assets under system control that are labelled **Confidential** or **Secret**.
   - Policy rules: enforce **no read down** and **no write up** based on the subject’s security level and the object’s security label.
2. **Implement label mediation at the system boundary** (reference-monitor style):
   - Use an OS/kernel-level MAC framework (or equivalent platform MAC capability) so that access decisions are mediated below normal application logic.
   - Ensure every access path (file/object access, inter-process communication, API/file streaming, database access where applicable) is mediated by the MAC layer for covered objects.
3. **Prevent label and rule manipulation by covered subjects**:
   - Configure the system so that covered subjects cannot change security labels/levels on existing objects, cannot assign labels to newly created/modified objects outside the policy, and cannot modify MAC policy/rules.
   - Ensure default label assignment for new objects is controlled by the MAC policy and subject context.
4. **Implement explicitly authorised trusted exceptions only**:
   - Identify trusted processes/service accounts that require exceptions (e.g., controlled data movement, backup/restore, indexing, controlled export).
   - Run these identities under dedicated security contexts that are explicitly permitted to perform the limited actions required, while still preventing privilege propagation and unauthorised information flow.
5. **Constrain propagation and privilege transfer**:
   - Ensure that when a subject accesses covered objects, the resulting data handling cannot be used to pass information to unauthorised subjects/objects at lower/higher levels.
   - Ensure subjects cannot grant/propagate access privileges to other subjects (enforced by the MAC layer and by restricting administrative capabilities).
6. **Bound the policy by system egress controls**:
   - Where information leaves the system boundary (e.g., exports, downloads, external integrations), enforce additional controls so the mandatory constraints remain effective after transfer (e.g., controlled export mechanisms that preserve/track labels and enforce destination constraints).

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Security architect / IAM & platform security engineer**: defines the covered subjects/objects and Bell‑LaPadula policy rules; designs the reference-monitor enforcement approach.
- **Platform/OS engineers**: implement and harden the MAC framework configuration and trusted exception contexts.
- **Application security engineer**: maps application access paths to covered objects and ensures all relevant access is mediated by the MAC layer.
- **Security operations / compliance**: validates evidence, monitors policy violations, and maintains policy-as-code change control.

## Acceptance criteria
- The MAC policy is implemented such that **no write up** and **no read down** are enforced for all covered subjects/objects labelled **Confidential/Secret**.
- Covered subjects cannot modify security labels/levels or MAC policy/rules; only explicitly authorised trusted identities can perform defined exceptions.
- Access attempts that violate the policy are denied and generate auditable events.
- All relevant access paths for covered objects are mediated by the MAC layer (no bypass via application logic, direct storage access, or unmanaged integration paths).
- Evidence exists (policy-as-code repository, configuration baselines, and audit logs) demonstrating uniform enforcement and reference-monitor style mediation.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Create a policy-as-code definition of the Bell-LaPadula MAC rules (no write up, no read down) for the covered subjects and Confidential/Secret objects
- [ ] Select and enable a reference-monitor style MAC enforcement point (e.g., OS/kernel MAC) and ensure all covered object access paths are mediated by it
- [ ] Configure label assignment so covered subjects cannot set or change labels on existing or newly created/modified objects
- [ ] Define and run explicitly authorised trusted exceptions under dedicated security contexts with narrowly scoped permissions
- [ ] Harden administrative and data-movement interfaces to prevent privilege propagation and to block unauthorised information flow across security levels
- [ ] Implement system-boundary egress controls for exports/integrations so mandatory constraints remain effective after information leaves the system

### ac-3.4 — Discretionary Access Control (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about stopping people from quietly spreading sensitive information or changing permissions in ways that move it into the wrong level of importance. Without it, someone who is allowed to access a document or system part could share it with others, hand over their access rights, or alter security settings so that higher-importance material leaks to the wrong audience, or so that access rules are weakened. To meet this expectation, the organisation must set and document a clear discretionary access policy that allows authorised users to pass information, grant their privileges, change security attributes, choose security settings for new or updated items, and even adjust access rules only when the sender and the receiver (or the target system part) are all authorised at the same impact and classification level. The organisation must apply this consistently across every relevant user and every relevant system resource, configure the systems to enforce it, and keep records showing when the system allowed or blocked these actions.

#### Enforce same impact/classification for discretionary sharing & grants

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement discretionary access control enforcement so that when the access policy permits a subject to (i) pass information to other subjects, (ii) pass information to other objects, (iii) grant privileges, and/or (iv) change security attributes (including choosing attributes for newly created/revised objects and changing access-control rules), those actions are only allowed when the acting subject and the target subject/object (or target of the attribute/rule change) are authorised at the **same impact and classification level**.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-3(4) enhancement requires enforcement of the organisation-defined discretionary access control policy over the covered subjects and objects, specifically constraining discretionary “passing” and related privilege/attribute changes to the same impact/classification level as defined in the policy (ac-3.4_prm_1).

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the authoritative level model** used by the application for both identities and resources (e.g., `impactLevel` and `classificationLevel`). Ensure it is derived from the organisation’s access policy and is available to the application at decision time.
2. **Implement an authorisation decision gate** at every discretionary action entry point (share, delegate, grant privileges, change security attributes, choose attributes for new/updated objects, and modify access-control rules). The gate must evaluate:
   - acting subject level == target subject level (for subject-to-subject passing), and/or
   - acting subject level == target object level (for subject-to-object passing), and/or
   - acting subject level == target level implied by the attribute/rule change target (for attribute changes and ACL/rule modifications).
   If levels do not match, the action must be denied.
3. **Integrate with the enterprise identity/access management layer** so the application receives trusted identity attributes (impact/classification) for the acting subject, and trusted resource attributes (impact/classification) for the target object/subject. Use a central policy engine (policy-as-code) or equivalent enforcement mechanism so the logic is consistent across UI, API, and background workflows.
4. **Enforce across all discretionary surfaces** within system boundaries:
   - UI sharing/delegation flows
   - API endpoints for share/grant/attribute-change
   - background jobs that perform sharing or provisioning
   - administrative consoles that allow discretionary changes (where within scope of covered subjects/objects)
5. **Add auditability for allow/deny decisions**: for each discretionary action attempt, log the acting subject, target, action type, evaluated levels, decision (allow/deny), and the policy reason (e.g., “denied: level mismatch”). Forward logs to the SIEM for retention and correlation.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Security Engineer / IAM Engineer**: implement the policy decision gate and integrations.
- **Application Owner**: confirm covered subjects/objects and ensure all discretionary entry points are in scope.
- **Security Operations / SOC**: validate logging, alerting, and evidence retention.

## Acceptance criteria
- For every covered discretionary action type (passing to subjects/objects, granting privileges, changing security attributes, selecting attributes for new/updated objects, changing access-control rules), the application **allows only** when acting and target are at the **same impact and classification level**.
- Attempts to share/grant/change across different impact/classification levels are **denied** and produce an audit record containing evaluated levels and the denial reason.
- Enforcement applies consistently across UI, API, and any background/distribution workflows that perform discretionary actions.
- Audit logs are generated for both successful and denied attempts and are available in the SIEM for review.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define and publish the authoritative `impactLevel` and `classificationLevel` attributes for covered subjects and covered objects used by the application
- [ ] Implement a policy decision gate that enforces `acting subject level == target subject/object level` for share, delegation, privilege grant, and security-attribute/ACL/rule change actions
- [ ] Integrate the application with the enterprise IdP/IAM to obtain trusted acting-subject levels and with the resource metadata service to obtain target-object levels at decision time
- [ ] Instrument and enforce all discretionary action entry points (UI, API, and background workflows) to call the same authorisation decision gate
- [ ] Add audit logging for every discretionary allow/deny decision including action type, acting subject, target, evaluated levels, and policy reason, and forward to the SIEM
- [ ] Test and validate denial behaviour for cross-level sharing/granting/attribute changes and confirm audit evidence is produced for each test case

### ac-3.5 — Security-relevant Information (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about keeping the most sensitive “security settings” locked away from everyone except when the system is deliberately not running normally. Those security settings include permission lists, the rules that decide what traffic is allowed or blocked, the configuration details for security services, and the information used to manage encryption keys. If unauthorised people could view or change these items during normal day-to-day operation, they could weaken protection, expose private information, or cause security safeguards to fail. To meet this expectation, the organisation must clearly define which information counts as security-relevant, identify where it is stored, and ensure access is blocked by default using appropriate access permissions and safeguards. Access must be allowed only during secure, non-operable periods such as maintenance, boot-up, troubleshooting, or shutdown, and only for authorised roles with a legitimate need. The organisation must also keep records showing who tried to access these items, when, what was targeted, and whether access was allowed or denied, and include the approach in its system security planning and supporting documentation.

#### State-gated access to security-relevant config and key info

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement a technical access control that **prevents access to security-relevant information** (ACLs, firewall/router filtering rules, security service configuration parameters, and cryptographic key management information) **except during secure, non-operable system states** (e.g., offline maintenance, boot-up, troubleshooting, shut down).

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-3(5)** (enhancement) requires: *“Prevent access to {{ insert: param, ac-03.05_odp }} except during secure, non-operable system states.”* The guidance clarifies that security-relevant information includes **access control lists, filtering rules, security service configuration parameters, and cryptographic key management information**, and that secure, non-operable states include periods when the system is **offline for maintenance, boot-up, troubleshooting, or shut down**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the security-relevant information set and map it to technical targets**
   - Create an inventory that explicitly lists each item in scope (e.g., firewall/router rule sets, ACL exports, security service config parameters, key-management/secret metadata and key-enrolment material references).
   - For each item, record the *system location* (file paths, repository objects, management endpoints, secrets/key-management views, CI/CD artefacts, etc.) and the *access method* (SSH/RDP, web console, API, config management tooling, secrets UI/API).
2. **Implement default-deny access to those targets during normal operation**
   - Remove direct read access to the mapped targets from normal privileged roles.
   - Enforce access only via a mediated privileged access workflow (e.g., PAM/privileged session broker) that can apply additional conditions.
3. **Enforce state-based gating for access release**
   - Define approved secure, non-operable states in operational terms (maintenance window approved; host marked offline/maintenance; boot/troubleshooting/shutdown mode).
   - Integrate the access broker/PAM with the maintenance/change workflow and host state signals so that access is granted **only when**:
     - a valid maintenance/troubleshooting ticket is approved, and
     - the target system is in an approved secure, non-operable state (e.g., “offline/maintenance” flag, or boot/troubleshooting mode indicator).
4. **Restrict to authorised roles and enforce least privilege**
   - Allow only authorised privileged operators (RBAC) to request access to each target category.
   - Use just-in-time authorisation with time-bounded sessions and require re-validation of the state at session start.
5. **Generate audit evidence for every attempt and outcome**
   - Ensure the access broker/PAM logs: user identity, target, requested action (read/view/export), ticket reference, system state at time of request, approval decision, and allow/deny outcome.
   - Forward logs to the enterprise SIEM for retention and correlation.
6. **Document the approach in system security planning and runbooks**
   - Update the system security plan and supporting procedures to describe: the defined security-relevant information set, the state definitions, the gating mechanism, and the operational steps for granting/revoking access during secure, non-operable states.

## WHO (roles responsible)
- **System Owner / Application Owner**: accountable for the security-relevant information inventory and ensuring the correct targets are in scope.
- **Security Engineering / IAM/PAM Team**: implements the mediated access workflow, RBAC, and state-gating integration.
- **Operations / OT/IT Operations (as applicable)**: maintains the maintenance workflow, host state signalling, and runbooks for boot/troubleshooting/shutdown modes.
- **Compliance / GRC**: validates that the documented approach and audit evidence meet AC-3(5) expectations.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
- During normal operation (system not in an approved secure, non-operable state), attempts to access any mapped security-relevant target are **denied** (no direct access path; only mediated access exists and is blocked by state).
- When the system is in an approved secure, non-operable state and an approved ticket exists, authorised users can access the mapped targets **successfully** for the time-bounded session.
- Every access attempt (allowed or denied) produces an auditable record containing: user, target, action, ticket reference, system state, and outcome.
- The system security plan and runbooks document the security-relevant information set, secure/non-operable state definitions, and the operational procedure for granting access.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Create and approve an inventory mapping each security-relevant information item to its technical storage/access target
- [ ] Remove direct read permissions to mapped targets from normal privileged roles and enforce mediated access only
- [ ] Define approved secure, non-operable states and implement state signals (maintenance/offline/boot/troubleshooting/shutdown) for each target host
- [ ] Integrate the privileged access workflow with the maintenance/change system so access is released only when an approved ticket and approved state both exist
- [ ] Configure RBAC and time-bounded just-in-time authorisation for authorised roles requesting access to each target category
- [ ] Enable and test audit logging for every access attempt (allowed/denied) including user, target, ticket reference, system state, and outcome
- [ ] Update the system security plan and operational runbooks to document the gating logic and the procedure for granting access during secure, non-operable states

### ac-3.7 — Role-based Access Control (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This expectation is about making sure people can only do what their job requires in the systems they use, by tying access to clearly defined job roles rather than giving permissions one person at a time. Without this, the wrong person could view or change sensitive information, or someone could take on a more powerful role without proper approval, increasing the risk of data misuse, mistakes, or unauthorised changes. To meet this expectation, the organisation must define which users are covered and which parts of the system are protected, create and document roles such as Information System Security Officer, Information System Owner, IAM Administrator, Data Steward, System Administrator, and Helpdesk Analyst, and list what each role is allowed to do. Users must be assigned to these roles, and any role assumption must be allowed only when the person’s approved role assignment is verified using authorised HR or contractor records. The organisation must keep up-to-date role and permission records, ensure the system enforces these rules for all covered users and functions, and retain audit records showing role changes and access decisions, with the approach included in security and privacy planning where relevant.

#### Enforce RBAC roles and role assumption via HR/contractor approval

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement an organisation-defined RBAC model that enforces access to defined application objects and functions based on role membership, and only allows role assumption when the user is authorised to assume the role per approved HR/contractor records.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-3(7) (Enhancement) requires the organisation to “enforce a role-based access control policy over defined subjects and objects and control access based upon {{ insert: param, ac-3.7_prm_1 }}.” The provided parameter specifies “RBAC roles for DoD system users (e.g., ISSO, IAM admin, data steward) with authorised role assumption via approved HR/contractor records.”

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define RBAC subjects and objects** in the application authorisation model: 
   - Subjects: the covered user population (employees/contractors/partners/service accounts) that may access the application.
   - Objects/functions: application endpoints, administrative consoles, APIs, data domains/records, and privileged operations (e.g., approve changes, manage users, export sensitive data).
2. **Create and document the RBAC roles** exactly as per the organisation-defined roles (e.g., ISSO, ISO, IAM Administrator, Data Steward, System Administrator, Helpdesk Analyst) and define **role-to-privilege mappings** (what each role can do on which objects/functions).
3. **Integrate with the enterprise IdP/authorisation layer** so that access decisions are made from role membership (not per-user grants):
   - Map IdP groups/roles to application entitlements/permissions.
   - Ensure all access checks in the application (including admin actions and API calls) rely on the role/entitlement claims issued by the IdP.
4. **Enforce authorised role assumption using approved HR/contractor records**:
   - Configure provisioning so that role membership is granted only when an HR/contractor approval reference exists (e.g., HR system assignment/approval ticket, contractor onboarding record).
   - Block or automatically remove role membership when the HR/contractor authorisation is absent, expired, or revoked.
5. **Implement auditability and evidence**:
   - Log role assignment/changes, role assumption events, and access denials (including the role/entitlement evaluated).
   - Retain logs centrally (e.g., SIEM) and ensure they can be correlated to the HR/contractor approval reference.
6. **Apply the model consistently across environments** (production/test/dev/DR) using the same role definitions and automated provisioning, with environment-specific object mappings where needed.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **IAM Administrator / Identity Engineer**: implement IdP role mapping, provisioning, and entitlement enforcement.
- **Application Owner / System Administrator**: define application objects/functions and validate role-to-privilege mappings.
- **ISSO / ISO**: approve the RBAC role definitions, role assumption rules, and evidence requirements.
- **HR/Contractor Management (process owner)**: maintain authoritative records and approvals used for role assumption.

## Acceptance criteria
- The application enforces access to defined objects/functions based on RBAC roles (no effective authorisation via ad-hoc per-user permissions for covered functions).
- For each organisation-defined role (ISSO, ISO, IAM Administrator, Data Steward, System Administrator, Helpdesk Analyst), there is a documented role-to-privilege mapping covering the defined objects/functions.
- Role membership/assumption is granted only when validated against approved HR/contractor records; expired/revoked authorisations result in role removal.
- Audit logs show: (a) role assignment/changes, (b) role assumption/entitlement evaluation, and (c) access denials, and can be correlated to the HR/contractor approval reference.
- The same RBAC model is applied across segregated environments with controlled, repeatable provisioning.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define RBAC subjects (covered users) and objects/functions (endpoints, APIs, admin actions, data domains) for the application
- [ ] Document RBAC roles and create role-to-privilege mappings for ISSO, ISO, IAM Administrator, Data Steward, System Administrator, and Helpdesk Analyst
- [ ] Configure the enterprise IdP to issue role/entitlement claims and map IdP roles/groups to application permissions
- [ ] Implement automated provisioning so role membership is granted only when an approved HR/contractor authorisation reference exists
- [ ] Add application-side authorisation checks that rely exclusively on RBAC role/entitlement claims for all privileged operations
- [ ] Enable and centralise audit logging for role assignment/changes, role assumption, and access denials with correlation to HR/contractor references
- [ ] Validate the RBAC model in production and non-production environments using test cases for authorised and unauthorised role assumption

### ac-3.8 — Revocation of Access Authorizations (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure that when someone’s permissions should change, the system stops them from accessing what they should no longer be able to reach. Without this, a person or an automated task could keep access for too long after their status changes, or after a file, folder, or record is reclassified, creating a real risk of unauthorised viewing, alteration, or misuse. To meet this expectation, the organisation must define and document clear rules for revoking access whenever either a person or a resource has its security attributes changed: if the change is a security-label change, access must be removed immediately; otherwise, access must be removed at the earlier of the next time the resource is opened or the next time the person tries to access it. The organisation must configure the systems to enforce this timing, ensure the relevant staff understand their responsibilities, and keep records showing that revocation happened at the correct moment, with enough detail to verify whether it was immediate or delayed.

#### Immediate revocation on security-label change; event-driven recheck otherwise

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement revocation timing rules for access authorisations when either **subject** or **object** security attributes change, enforcing:
- **Immediate revocation** when the change is a **security-label change**.
- Otherwise, revocation takes effect at the **earlier of**:
  1) the **next time the object is opened**, or
  2) the **next time the subject attempts to access** it.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-3(8)** enhancement requires the organisation to **enforce revocation of access authorisations** resulting from changes to security attributes of **subjects and objects**, using the organisation-defined timing rules in **param: ac-03.08_odp (rules)**: *“Immediate revocation for security-label changes; otherwise revoke at next object open or next subject access attempt, whichever occurs first.”* The guidance also notes that some revocations may be delayed until the next access boundary unless the system can make them immediate.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Classify attribute changes and define the trigger
- Implement a change classifier in the authorisation layer (or policy service) that tags each attribute update event as either:
  - **security-label change** (must be immediate), or
  - **non-label attribute change** (delayed until first access boundary).
- Ensure the classifier is based on the organisation’s security attribute taxonomy (e.g., data classification label changes vs. group membership/role changes).

### 2) Immediate revocation path for security-label changes
- On a security-label change event, enforce immediate revocation by:
  - invalidating/ending authorisation state that could permit access (e.g., revoke/deny active sessions or cached entitlements), and/or
  - forcing the next request to re-evaluate authorisation with **no reliance on stale cached decisions**.
- If the platform supports token/session revocation (e.g., IdP back-channel logout / token revocation), integrate it; otherwise, implement an application-side “authorisation version” check so that any request using a stale authorisation context is denied.

### 3) Delayed revocation path for non-label attribute changes
- For non-label attribute changes, implement enforcement at the **first relevant access boundary**:
  - **Object-open boundary**: when a user/process opens a protected object (e.g., document open, record view initialisation, file handle creation), re-evaluate authorisation using current subject/object attributes.
  - **Subject-access attempt boundary**: when the user/process attempts an access operation (e.g., API call to read/update/export), re-evaluate authorisation at the start of the operation.
- Ensure the application does not reuse previously authorised access decisions across these boundaries.

### 4) Auditability and evidence generation
- Log both:
  1) the **attribute-change event** (subject/object identifiers, attribute name(s), whether it was a security-label change, and timestamp), and
  2) the **first enforcement outcome** (denied/allowed) with timestamp and correlation identifiers.
- For immediate revocation, evidence must show the access attempt after the label change is denied (or the session is invalidated) without requiring logout.
- For delayed revocation, evidence must show denial occurs at the earlier of the next object-open or next subject access attempt.

### 5) Operational controls
- Provide runbooks for the application owner/admin team describing how attribute updates are propagated to the authorisation layer and how to troubleshoot cases where revocation appears delayed.
- Add automated regression tests that:
  - change a security label and verify immediate denial without re-authentication, and
  - change a non-label attribute and verify denial occurs at the correct first access boundary.

## WHO
- **Application Owner / Security Engineer**: implement authorisation enforcement logic, caching/session invalidation strategy, and audit logging.
- **Identity & Access Management (IAM) Administrator**: integrate with IdP/session revocation capabilities where available.
- **SOC/Monitoring Engineer**: ensure SIEM correlation rules and alerting for attribute-change-to-denial evidence.

## Acceptance criteria
1. **Security-label change**: after a label change event, an access attempt using an existing session is denied (or the session is invalidated) with evidence timestamps showing immediate effect.
2. **Non-label attribute change**: access is denied at the **earlier** of next object open or next access attempt; evidence shows the first enforcement boundary and timestamp.
3. **No stale authorisation**: the system re-evaluates authorisation at the defined boundaries and does not rely on long-lived cached decisions that bypass the timing rules.
4. **Audit evidence**: attribute-change and enforcement logs are present, correlated, and sufficient to verify immediate vs delayed timing.
5. **Test coverage**: automated tests exist for both label-change immediate revocation and non-label delayed revocation timing.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define and implement a security-attribute change classifier that marks events as security-label vs non-label changes
- [ ] Integrate immediate revocation for security-label changes by invalidating sessions/tokens or enforcing an authorisation-version recheck on every request
- [ ] Implement authorisation re-evaluation at the object-open boundary and at the start of each protected access operation for non-label attribute changes
- [ ] Disable or bound caching of authorisation decisions so stale decisions cannot bypass the timing rules across access boundaries
- [ ] Add correlated audit logging for attribute-change events and the first enforcement decision (including timestamps and correlation identifiers)
- [ ] Create automated tests that verify immediate denial after label changes without logout and correct boundary-based denial for non-label changes
- [ ] Publish a runbook for IAM/application teams describing how attribute updates propagate and how to troubleshoot revocation timing anomalies

### ac-3.9 — Controlled Release (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure information leaves your organisation only when the outside recipient can be trusted to protect it properly. Without this, sensitive details could be sent to the wrong place, handled in a weaker way than you expect, or exposed to unauthorised people, creating legal, financial, and reputational harm. To meet this expectation, the organisation must first decide which external systems or contractor-controlled endpoints are eligible to receive information. It must then release information only if the recipient provides security and privacy protections that are equivalent and suitable for how they are accredited and how they will handle the data. Before sending, the organisation must verify appropriateness using contractual commitments, periodic independent evidence such as inspection or test results, and a documented match between the information’s protection needs and the recipient’s safeguards. The organisation must keep records of the recipient’s safeguards, the checks performed, and the supporting agreements and assessment results.

#### Controlled release gate using recipient accreditation and attribute mapping

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement a controlled-release decision point that permits outbound sharing of information outside the system only when the external receiving system/contractor-controlled endpoint is eligible and provides security and privacy controls that are equivalent and appropriate for its accreditation and data handling. The decision must be validated prior to release and must be evidence-backed.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-3(9) (Enhancement) requires that release information outside of the system only occurs when the organisation validates the information’s appropriateness for the receiving external system, including ensuring exported security/privacy attributes are appropriate for the receiving system. Where adequacy of external protections cannot be determined directly, the organisation must procedurally determine adequacy using contractual assurance, periodic assessment evidence (inspection/test), and a documented match/attribute mapping.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define eligible external recipients**: Maintain an authoritative registry of external receiving systems and contractor-controlled endpoints (AC-03(09)_ODP[01]). Each entry must include accreditation/data-handling context and the endpoint’s eligibility status.
2. **Define required controls and validation criteria**: For each recipient entry, define the security and privacy controls the outside system is expected to provide (AC-03(09)_ODP[02]) and the validation controls used by your organisation to confirm appropriateness (AC-03(09)_ODP[03]).
3. **Create an attribute mapping model**: Build a mapping between your information release types (e.g., data classification, presence of personal data, retention constraints, encryption/logging/breach-notification expectations) and the required recipient protection attributes.
4. **Implement a release gate in the outbound workflow**: Integrate the release gate into the application’s data export/sharing workflow (e.g., API gateway, export service, integration service, or workflow engine). Before any outbound transmission, the gate must:
   - Verify the recipient is eligible.
   - Confirm contractual assurance exists for that recipient and data category.
   - Confirm periodic assessment evidence is current (e.g., inspection/test results) and record the evidence.
   - Perform attribute mapping validation: ensure the recipient’s recorded safeguards are equivalent and appropriate for the mapped security/privacy attributes of the specific exported information.
   - Allow release only if all checks pass; otherwise block and route to an exception process.
5. **Generate audit-ready evidence per release decision**: For each permitted release, store (a) recipient identifier, (b) contractual reference, (c) assessment evidence reference and expiry, (d) attribute mapping result, and (e) the final decision outcome.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Owner / Integration Lead**: implements the release gate integration into the outbound workflow.
- **Information Security & Privacy (GRC + Security Engineering)**: maintains the recipient accreditation registry, control equivalence criteria, and attribute mapping model.
- **Legal/Procurement**: ensures contractual assurance artefacts exist and are referenced.
- **Security Assurance/Compliance**: provides periodic assessment evidence and maintains its currency.

## Acceptance criteria
- Outbound releases to external recipients are blocked unless the recipient is present in the eligible registry and passes contractual assurance + current periodic assessment evidence checks.
- For each permitted release, the system records an attribute mapping validation result showing equivalence/appropriateness of security/privacy attributes.
- Evidence for each release decision is retrievable for audit (contract reference, assessment evidence reference/expiry, mapping outcome, and decision).
- Exceptions (where release is allowed despite gaps) are routed through a documented exception workflow and are still evidence-backed.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Create and populate an authoritative recipient accreditation registry for external receiving systems and contractor-controlled endpoints
- [ ] Define security and privacy control equivalence criteria and the validation controls used to assess appropriateness for each recipient category
- [ ] Develop an attribute mapping model from your information release types to required recipient protection attributes
- [ ] Integrate a pre-release policy gate into the application’s outbound export/sharing workflow to enforce eligibility, contractual assurance, periodic assessment currency, and attribute mapping validation
- [ ] Implement evidence capture for each permitted release decision (contract reference, assessment evidence reference/expiry, mapping result, decision outcome)
- [ ] Establish an exception workflow for blocked releases and ensure exceptions are still evidence-backed and time-bounded

### ac-3.10 — Audited Override of Access Control Mechanisms (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about allowing emergency access changes only when they are truly necessary, and making sure they are properly authorised and recorded. Without this, staff could bypass the usual access rules during a crisis for convenience or mistake, potentially exposing sensitive information or letting the wrong people into systems, which could seriously harm people or stop the organisation from carrying out critical work. The organisation must clearly define the only situations where an override is allowed: when there is a threat to human life, or when it is needed to prevent the loss of mission-critical capability during an active incident or declared emergency. Any such override must be approved before it happens by both the Information System Security Officer and the designated duty officer, and only they (or explicitly authorised people acting for them) should carry it out. Every override action must be written into the system’s audit records with enough detail to identify what was changed, who was involved, and when, so it can be reviewed afterwards.

#### Dual-approved break-glass access override with immutable audit

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement an emergency “break-glass” override for automated access control mechanisms that can only be used under the organisation-defined conditions (threat to human life, or to prevent loss of mission-critical capability during an active incident or declared emergency) and only by the organisation-defined roles (ISSO and designated duty officer). Every override must generate auditable records.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 AC-3(10) (Enhancement) requires an **audited override** of automated access control mechanisms **under defined conditions** by **defined roles**. The guidance specifies that override conditions are limited to limited emergency situations, and that **audit events** are defined in **AU-2** and **audit records** are generated in **AU-12**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define and encode override conditions** in the access-control enforcement layer (or IAM policy engine) so the override is only eligible when an incident/ticket indicates an **active incident** or **declared emergency**, and the justification explicitly maps to either **threat to human life** or **prevent loss of mission-critical capability**.
2. **Implement a dual-control workflow** in the privileged access/IAM layer requiring **two independent approvals**: first approval by the **ISSO**, second approval by the **designated duty officer**. All other users may only submit requests.
3. **Restrict execution authority** so the final “execute override” action can only be performed by the ISSO/duty officer (or explicitly configured delegates acting under their control). The workflow should enforce separation of duties (no single account can both approve and execute).
4. **Make the override scoped and time-bounded**: grant only the minimum required temporary access (specific application/resource/permission), with automatic expiry and optional re-approval if the emergency persists.
5. **Generate auditable, tamper-evident audit records** for every override execution, including: incident/ticket reference, override conditions selected, justification, approvers (ISSO + duty officer), executor identity, affected resource(s)/permission(s), start/end time, and outcome. Ensure logs are forwarded to the central SIEM in an append-only/tamper-evident manner consistent with AU-2/AU-12 expectations.
6. **Document the mechanism** in the system’s access control procedures and the system security plan so auditors can trace how AC-3(10) conditions and roles map to the implemented workflow and audit fields.

## WHO
- **Information System Security Officer (ISSO)**: approves override requests and authorises execution.
- **Designated duty officer**: provides the second independent approval and authorises execution.
- **IAM/Privileged Access Administrator (with security engineering oversight)**: implements workflow, policy-as-code/eligibility checks, scoping/time-bounds, and audit integration.
- **Security Operations / SIEM engineering**: ensures audit records are reliably generated and retained per organisational logging requirements.

## Acceptance criteria
- Override execution is possible **only** when incident status is **Active/Declared Emergency** and justification matches one of the two defined conditions.
- Override requires **both** ISSO and designated duty officer approvals (dual control) before execution.
- Only ISSO/duty officer (or explicitly configured delegates) can execute; other roles cannot execute.
- Each override execution produces an audit record containing incident reference, conditions, justification, approvers, executor, affected permissions/resources, and timestamps.
- Override grants are **scoped** and **time-bounded**, with automatic expiry.
- Audit records are visible in the central logging/SIEM and are retained and protected against tampering per organisational AU-2/AU-12-aligned controls.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define the incident/ticket statuses that qualify as “active incident” and “declared emergency” and map them to AC-3(10) eligibility checks
- [ ] Configure an IAM/privileged access break-glass workflow requiring independent approvals from the ISSO and the designated duty officer
- [ ] Implement policy-as-code eligibility rules that block override execution unless the justification matches the defined emergency conditions
- [ ] Restrict the execute step so only ISSO/duty officer (or explicitly configured delegates) can perform it, with separation of duties from requesters
- [ ] Configure override grants to be minimum-necessary and time-bounded with automatic expiry and optional re-approval
- [ ] Integrate override execution with central logging to generate tamper-evident audit records including incident reference, approvers, executor, affected permissions/resources, and timestamps
- [ ] Update the system security plan and access control procedures to document the override conditions, roles, workflow, and audit evidence fields

### ac-3.11 — Restrict Access to Specific Information Types (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This expectation is about keeping the most sensitive parts of your systems locked down so only the right people can reach them, specifically data repositories that hold cryptographic keys, authentication information, and selected system information. Without this, unauthorised staff or attackers could steal the keys that protect data, access accounts by obtaining login-related information, or view sensitive system details, leading to fraud, data theft, or wider system compromise. To meet this requirement, the organisation must define which information types are protected, publish an access rule that clearly states only authorised personnel may access those repositories, and create clear written steps for how access is granted, checked, and blocked. The system must be designed and configured to enforce these restrictions through the way permissions are set, with the approach recorded in the system design and security plan. Finally, the organisation must keep evidence in audit records showing access attempts are logged and that only authorised access is allowed.

#### Repository access rules for protected information types

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Restrict access to data repositories that contain the organisation-defined **information types** ({{ insert: param, ac-03.11_odp }}), specifically **cryptographic keys, authentication information, and selected system information**, so that only authorised personnel can access those repositories.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-3(11) enhancement requires restricting access to data repositories containing the specified **information types**. The intent is to prevent unauthorised access to the most sensitive data (e.g., keys and authentication material) by enforcing access at the repository/type level rather than allowing broad access to entire databases or storage.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define and map protected information types to repositories**: Create an authoritative mapping (data catalogue/asset register) that labels each repository (e.g., database schema, table/view, secret store, object storage bucket/prefix, configuration repository) that contains cryptographic keys, authentication information, and selected system information.
2. **Implement repository/type-level authorisation**: Configure the target platforms so permissions are granted only to specific authorised roles/groups for the protected repositories/types (e.g., dedicated database roles with grants limited to protected schemas/tables/views; secret-store policies scoped to key/secret paths; object storage bucket/prefix policies with explicit allow lists and deny-by-default).
3. **Enforce least privilege and separation of duties**: Ensure privileged access is limited to named roles (e.g., Key Custodian, IAM Admin, Security Auditor) and that standard users/service accounts cannot access protected repositories except where explicitly approved.
4. **Require controlled access workflows**: Establish an access request/approval process for changes to who can access protected repositories, including periodic access reviews and documented exception handling (including break-glass procedures).
5. **Enable auditability for allowed and denied attempts**: Turn on audit logging for repository access attempts (including denied events) and retain logs per organisational retention requirements; ensure logs can be correlated to user identity, repository/type, and action.
6. **Record evidence for assessment**: Maintain configuration evidence (exports/screenshots/policy-as-code outputs) showing the repository/type restrictions and retain audit extracts demonstrating enforcement.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Information Security Officer / Security Architect**: owns the protected information type mapping and control design.
- **Application Owner / Data Owner**: validates which repositories contain the protected information types.
- **Platform/Database/Secrets Administrators**: implement repository/type-level permissions and audit logging.
- **IAM Administrator**: manages authorised roles/groups and integrates with the enterprise identity provider.
- **Compliance/Assurance**: reviews evidence packages and access review outcomes.

## Acceptance criteria
- Every repository containing the protected information types is explicitly identified and labelled in the mapping.
- Technical controls enforce access at the repository/type level (no broad grants to entire databases/storage for protected types).
- Only authorised roles/groups can access the protected repositories/types; all other attempts are denied.
- Audit logs capture both successful and denied access attempts with sufficient detail for investigation.
- Evidence package exists showing (a) the configured restrictions and (b) audit log extracts demonstrating enforcement.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Create and maintain an authoritative mapping of repositories to protected information types (cryptographic keys, authentication information, selected system information)
- [ ] Configure repository/type-level permissions (database roles/grants, secret-store path policies, object storage bucket/prefix policies) with deny-by-default and explicit allow lists for authorised roles
- [ ] Integrate repository access with the enterprise identity provider and authorised role/group assignments for privileged access
- [ ] Implement an access request and approval workflow for changes to authorised access to protected repositories, including periodic access reviews and exception handling
- [ ] Enable and retain audit logging for repository access attempts, ensuring denied attempts are recorded with user identity and target repository/type
- [ ] Produce an evidence pack containing configuration/policy outputs and audit log extracts demonstrating enforcement for protected repositories

### ac-3.12 — Assert and Enforce Application Access (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This expectation is about making sure every app only gets the access it truly needs, and that it cannot quietly take more once it is installed. Without this, a harmless-looking app could be able to read or use sensitive things such as your contacts, location, camera, microphone, keyboard input, network details, phone functions, or other files, potentially leading to privacy breaches, spying, or misuse of company information. To meet this requirement, the organisation must require that, during installation, each application clearly states which access it needs across user contacts, global positioning systems, cameras, keyboards, microphones, networks, phones, and other files. The organisation must also have a way to enforce those stated permissions so unauthorised access is blocked immediately after installation. Finally, any later changes to an installed app’s access must be held back until they are reviewed and approved before the new access is allowed.

#### Enforce install-time app access declarations via managed policy gate

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement an application permission governance mechanism that (1) requires each application to declare which **system applications and functions** it needs (e.g., user contacts, GPS/location, cameras, keyboards, microphones, networks, phones, and other files) **at install/deployment time**, and (2) enforces those declared permissions immediately after installation, blocking any unauthorised access. Additionally, ensure any **post-install permission/access changes** are held back until they are reviewed and approved.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-3(12) enhancement is intended to address applications that need access to system applications and functions by ensuring access is **asserted and enforced** for the defined categories, and that access cannot be silently expanded after installation.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the authoritative permission categories** for the organisation’s managed app platform, aligned to the AC-3(12) set (user contacts, GPS/location, cameras, keyboards, microphones, networks, phones, and other files).
2. **Require install-time declaration** by only allowing application deployment through an enterprise-managed channel (e.g., MDM/enterprise app store/managed deployment pipeline) where the app’s required permission categories are captured as part of the deployment request (from app metadata and/or a controlled manifest provided by the app owner).
3. **Create an approved permission profile** per application/version in a central policy repository (policy-as-code), mapping each app to the exact allowed categories.
4. **Enforce at the endpoint/OS policy layer** using managed configuration so that the device grants only the categories in the approved profile and denies any other categories from the AC-3(12) set. Ensure enforcement is applied as part of the deployment workflow so there is no “first run” bypass.
5. **Implement an update gate**: when an app update introduces new or expanded permission declarations, automatically detect the permission diff and block rollout until the change is approved and the approved permission profile is updated.
6. **Implement post-install change control**: if the platform/app attempts to request additional categories after installation, route the request to the same approval workflow and keep the additional categories denied until approval.
7. **Generate audit evidence**: log (a) the install-time declared categories, (b) the approved profile applied, (c) the enforcement outcome (granted/denied per category), and (d) any update/post-install permission change approvals.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Owner / Business Owner**: validates business need for each permission category and approves the declared access scope.
