- **Security/Compliance Engineering**: maintains the permission category model, policy-as-code rules, and enforcement configuration.
- **Endpoint/MDM Administrator**: implements and operates the managed deployment pipeline and enforcement settings.
- **Change Management / CAB (or equivalent)**: ensures post-install permission changes follow the organisation’s approval process.

## Acceptance criteria
- For every deployed application, the system records an **install-time declared** set of AC-3(12) permission categories.
- Immediately after installation, the endpoint/OS grants **only** the categories present in the approved permission profile; any unauthorised category from the AC-3(12) set is denied.
- App updates that add new permission categories are **blocked** until an approval updates the approved permission profile.
- Post-install permission expansion requests are **not effective** until approved, and enforcement denials/grants are auditable in logs.
- Audit evidence exists for at least: declared permissions, approved profile, enforcement results, and approval records for any permission changes.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define the organisation’s approved permission categories aligned to AC-3(12) (contacts, GPS/location, camera, keyboard input, microphone, network, phone, other files).
- [ ] Configure the managed deployment pipeline so application installs are only permitted via a controlled channel that captures install-time permission declarations.
- [ ] Create and maintain an approved permission profile per application/version using policy-as-code mapping to the declared categories.
- [ ] Enforce the approved permission profile at the endpoint/OS/managed app layer so unauthorised categories are denied immediately after installation.
- [ ] Implement an update gate that blocks rollout when an app update introduces new or expanded AC-3(12) permission categories until approval completes.
- [ ] Route post-install permission expansion requests through the same approval workflow and keep additional categories denied until approved.
- [ ] Enable audit logging for declared permissions, approved profiles applied, enforcement outcomes, and approval records for permission changes.

### ac-3.13 — Attribute-based Access Control (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This expectation is about making sure people can only access the right systems and files when their role and permissions truly match what the information is allowed to be accessed for, and only from the right place and time. Without this, someone could view or change sensitive material they are not entitled to, or access it from an unauthorised location or outside approved working hours, increasing the risk of data leaks, fraud, or disruption. To meet this expectation, the organisation must clearly define which users and which resources are covered, create and keep a set of access rules that check job function, identity confidence level, clearance level (Secret or Confidential), system role, the resource’s classification, and the on-premises location and approved time window, and then configure systems so access is granted only when all these attributes match the rules and denied when they do not. The organisation must document how access enforcement works, record access decisions for later review, and assign responsibility to the right staff while keeping evidence that the settings are in place and operating as intended.

#### Implement ABAC policy-as-code with auditable allow/deny decisions

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT must be done
Implement an attribute-based access control (ABAC) policy that evaluates access requests using defined **subject attributes** (e.g., job function, identity assurance level, clearance), **object/resource attributes** (e.g., resource classification), and **environment attributes** (e.g., on-prem location and approved time window), and then **enforces allow/deny** decisions for each action.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-3(13)** enhancement requires: “Enforce attribute-based access control policy over defined subjects and objects and control access based upon {{ insert: param, ac-03.13_odp }}.” The guidance further expects ABAC rules based on organisational attributes, action attributes, and environmental/resource attributes, with enforcement that can be audited.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define ABAC scope (subjects/objects/actions):** Create an ABAC coverage matrix listing the subject types (e.g., employees, contractors, service accounts, privileged operators) and object types (e.g., application endpoints, APIs, datasets, document repositories) covered by the policy, including the action attributes (e.g., read/write/delete/admin).
2. **Establish authoritative attribute sources:**
   - Job function from HR system.
   - Identity assurance level from the authentication method used by the enterprise IdP (and step-up for higher assurance).
   - Clearance from the clearance workflow.
   - System role from the RBAC-to-role mapping used by the application.
   - Resource classification from the application’s metadata/asset registry (or CMDB/asset registry) and ensure it is retrievable at decision time.
   - Environment attributes from server-side signals (e.g., approved on-prem network context / egress ranges and server-evaluated time windows).
3. **Create ABAC rules as policy-as-code:** Implement ABAC logic in a version-controlled policy engine (e.g., OPA-like policy-as-code or the vendor’s policy DSL) that evaluates the required attributes and returns an explicit allow/deny decision per request/action. Ensure rules explicitly deny when required attributes are missing, stale, or do not match.
4. **Enforce at a central enforcement point:** Integrate the policy decision point with the application/API gateway or authorisation middleware so that every access attempt is evaluated against the ABAC policy before the action executes.
5. **Make decisions auditable:** Emit audit events for each decision including: subject identifier, subject attributes used (or references to them), object/resource identifier, action, environment attributes evaluated, ABAC rule identifier/version, decision (allow/deny), and reason codes suitable for audit review.
6. **Operationalise and evidence configuration:** Store policy-as-code in a controlled repository, deploy via CI/CD with change management, and retain configuration evidence (policy version, enforcement configuration, attribute mappings, and audit log retention settings) for audit.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Owner / IAM Architect:** Own ABAC scope definition and attribute mapping design.
- **Security Engineering (Authorisation/Policy):** Implement policy-as-code and enforcement integration.
- **Identity & Access Management (IdP/IAM) Team:** Ensure identity assurance and clearance attributes are correctly issued and refreshed.
- **Platform/DevOps Team:** Manage deployment, CI/CD, and evidence generation.
- **Security Operations / Compliance:** Validate audit log content and retention against organisational requirements.

## Acceptance criteria
- ABAC policy covers defined subjects, objects, and actions, and is enforced for every request/action at the chosen enforcement point.
- Access is **allowed only when** subject attributes (job function, identity assurance level, clearance, system role) match object/resource classification and environment conditions (on-prem location and approved time window).
- Requests with missing/invalid/stale attributes are denied by default.
- For each allow/deny decision, the system produces audit records containing the decision, rule identifier/version, and the evaluated attributes (or attribute references) sufficient for later review.
- Policy-as-code and enforcement configuration are version-controlled, deployed through controlled change management, and evidence is retained.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Create an ABAC coverage matrix defining subjects, objects/resources, and action attributes covered by the policy
- [ ] Map each required attribute (job function, identity assurance level, clearance, system role, resource classification, on-prem/time window) to an authoritative source and define refresh/staleness handling
- [ ] Implement ABAC rules as version-controlled policy-as-code that explicitly denies when attributes are missing or do not match
- [ ] Integrate the policy decision into the application/API gateway or authorisation middleware so every request/action is evaluated before execution
- [ ] Configure server-side evaluation of environment attributes (on-prem context and approved time windows) and prevent client-side bypass
- [ ] Enable audit logging for every ABAC decision with rule identifier/version, evaluated attributes (or references), and allow/deny reason codes
- [ ] Deploy ABAC policy and enforcement changes through CI/CD with change management and retain evidence for audit

### ac-3.14 — Individual Access (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making it possible for people to safely see what personal information an organisation holds about them, and why it is used. Without this, someone could be left unaware of inaccurate records, or their information could be exposed to the wrong person, or used and shared without proper transparency. To meet this expectation, the organisation must provide a secure web-based request service and a logged-in viewing area where individuals can access their own records held in the organisation’s official systems, including the specific items of information, the reasons the organisation processes it, and any history of sharing or transfers where this applies. The organisation must set clear rules and written procedures for how access decisions are checked and approved, involve the right staff responsible for enforcing access, and seek legal input where limits may apply. It must also keep the necessary documentation and records of access activity so requests and viewing can be traced to the correct person.

#### Identity-first SAR portal for individual PII access and viewing

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement a web-based subject access request (SAR) portal and an authenticated (logged-in) viewing interface that allows individuals to access their own personally identifiable information (PII) held in organisational system-of-record databases. The interface must present (1) the specific PII data fields held, (2) the processing purposes, and (3) disclosure/transfer history where applicable.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 AC-3(14) enhancement requires: “Provide {{ac-03.14_odp.01}} to enable individuals to have access to … {{ac-03.14_odp.02}}.” The organisation-defined parameters specify that the mechanisms are a “web-based subject access request portal and authenticated application interface to privacy records”, and the elements include “the individual’s PII … including data fields, processing purposes, and disclosure/transfer history”.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create the SAR portal experience**: Provide a web portal where an individual can submit a SAR and then access a secure, logged-in “My Privacy Records” view.
2. **Enforce strong authentication and subject binding**: Integrate the portal with the enterprise identity provider and require authenticated sessions. Bind every SAR/request reference to the authenticated identity (subject identifier) and enforce authorisation server-side on every data retrieval.
3. **Implement a privacy “view model”**: Define a canonical schema for the required elements: (a) PII data fields, (b) processing purposes in plain language, and (c) disclosure/transfer history where applicable. Populate this view model from privacy metadata sources (e.g., records of processing, data maps, and transfer/disclosure logs).
4. **Retrieve from system-of-record databases safely**: Use least-privilege, read-only service access (or privacy-safe extracts/replicas) to query system-of-record databases by subject identifier. Apply query-time filtering so only the individual’s own records are returned.
5. **Add auditability for request and viewing/export**: Log SAR submission, identity verification completion, authorisation decision outcomes, record retrieval, and any view/export actions with timestamps and the authenticated subject/request reference.
6. **Handle access limitations consistently**: Encode any access limitations/exemptions (e.g., where certain record types are not appropriate for disclosure) into the SAR workflow with documented decision rules and escalation to privacy/legal counsel.

## WHO
- **Product/Application Owner**: accountable for delivering the SAR portal and viewing interface.
- **Privacy Officer / Data Protection Lead**: defines the required “elements” presentation (data fields, purposes, transfer history) and approves limitation rules.
- **IAM Engineer**: implements authentication integration and session controls.
- **Security Engineer**: ensures authorisation enforcement, audit logging, and evidence generation.
- **Legal Counsel / Privacy Legal**: reviews exemptions/limitations and approves workflow decision rules.

## Acceptance criteria
- A user can authenticate to the portal and view only their own PII records.
- The “My Privacy Records” view includes: **data fields**, **processing purposes**, and **disclosure/transfer history** (where applicable) for the individual.
- All SAR request and viewing/export actions are recorded in audit logs tied to the authenticated subject and request reference.
- Authorisation is enforced server-side for every retrieval (no client-side-only filtering).
- Access limitations/exemptions are applied according to documented, approved rules with escalation where required.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Design the SAR portal and authenticated “My Privacy Records” UI to display required elements (data fields, processing purposes, and disclosure/transfer history where applicable)
- [ ] Integrate the portal with the enterprise identity provider and enforce strong authentication for viewing and any export actions
- [ ] Implement server-side authorisation that binds each SAR/request reference to the authenticated subject identifier on every data retrieval
- [ ] Create a privacy view model schema and map it to system-of-record sources for PII fields, processing purposes, and transfer/disclosure history
- [ ] Implement least-privilege, read-only data access (or privacy-safe extracts) with query-time filtering by subject identifier
- [ ] Enable audit logging for SAR submission, identity verification, authorisation decisions, record retrieval, and view/export events with request and subject identifiers
- [ ] Document and implement approved access limitation/exemption rules with escalation to privacy/legal counsel where required

### ac-3.15 — Discretionary and Mandatory Access Control (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure every person and every system resource can only be accessed in the right way, for the right reasons, every time. It helps prevent a serious breach where someone with lower clearance, or a user account that has been misused, could read or change more sensitive information than they are allowed to, or where one compromised account could be used to spread access across the whole system. To meet this expectation, the organisation must apply two layers of access checking across the specific in-scope users and resources it has identified: first, it must use fixed security levels (such as Confidential and Secret) so that a user can only access material at their clearance level or below; second, it must also apply permission rules based on job roles and file/service/network permissions, using the principle of least privilege. The organisation must ensure these rules are enforced consistently and that access decisions are recorded so they can be reviewed.

#### Enforce MAC+DAC via PDP/PEP with labels, clearance, RBAC and ACLs

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement both **mandatory access control (MAC)** and **discretionary access control (DAC)** for the in-scope application/data access paths, enforced together by a single authorisation decision flow.

- **MAC**: subjects have **clearance levels** and objects have **security classification labels**; access is permitted only when the subject clearance satisfies the object label.
- **DAC**: access is also constrained by **least-privilege discretionary permissions** using **RBAC (roles/groups)** and **ACLs** for the requested action (e.g., read/write/execute/service invocation).

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-3(15) enhancement guidance requires **simultaneous implementation** of MAC and DAC to reduce the risk that a single compromised user/process can enable unauthorised execution or access. The combined enforcement must ensure that **MAC and DAC cannot be bypassed** (i.e., MAC can deny even if DAC allows, and DAC can deny even if MAC allows).

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the MAC policy model**: create the authoritative sets of **clearance levels** and **object classification labels**, plus the explicit comparison rules (e.g., clearance >= label) for the covered subjects/objects.
2. **Define the DAC policy model**: create **RBAC roles/groups** and **least-privilege ACLs** for the covered objects and actions, including service-to-service permissions.
3. **Bind subject and object attributes**:
   - Integrate with the enterprise IdP to supply subject clearance (e.g., via group membership/custom claims) and RBAC role/group membership.
   - Ensure every covered object (documents, records, datasets, API resources, queues/topics, etc.) carries a stored classification label in metadata at creation/update time.
4. **Implement a central PDP/PEP enforcement pattern**:
   - Route all authorisation checks through a single **Policy Decision Point (PDP)** and **Policy Enforcement Point (PEP)** (e.g., API gateway/authorisation gateway or service mesh policy enforcement).
   - For each access request, the PDP evaluates **MAC first** (clearance vs object label) and then **DAC** (RBAC + ACL) and produces a single final **allow/deny** decision.
   - Ensure there is no alternate “admin bypass” or direct data-plane path that skips the PDP/PEP.
5. **Make decisions auditable**:
   - Emit structured audit events for every decision including: subject identifier, subject clearance, object label, requested action, RBAC role/group and ACL match (or reason for no match), and final allow/deny.
   - Forward events to the SIEM for retention and review.
6. **Operationalise with change control**:
   - Store MAC and DAC policies as **version-controlled policy-as-code** with peer review, approvals, and deployment pipelines.
   - Apply the same change control discipline to label/clearance mapping rules and to ACL/RBAC changes.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Security architect / IAM architect**: defines MAC/DAC models, label/clearance semantics, and enforcement architecture.
- **Application owner / platform engineer**: implements PDP/PEP integration and ensures all access paths are mediated.
- **IAM administrator**: maintains IdP claims/groups/roles and mapping to clearance.
- **Security operations / SIEM engineer**: validates audit event schema, retention, and alerting.

## Acceptance criteria
- For all in-scope access requests, the system enforces **both** MAC and DAC and returns a final decision derived from both policies.
- A request where **MAC denies** is rejected even if **DAC would allow**.
- A request where **DAC denies** is rejected even if **MAC would allow**.
- Every allow/deny decision generates an auditable record containing the MAC inputs (clearance, object label) and DAC inputs (RBAC/ACL basis) and is visible in SIEM.
- There is no supported access path that can reach the covered objects/actions without passing through the PDP/PEP enforcement flow.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define the MAC clearance levels and object classification labels, including explicit comparison rules for allow/deny
- [ ] Define RBAC roles/groups and least-privilege ACLs for each covered object type and action
- [ ] Configure the IdP to provide subject clearance and RBAC role/group membership as authoritative attributes
- [ ] Implement object labelling so every covered object stores its classification label in metadata at creation/update time
- [ ] Deploy a central PDP/PEP authorisation gateway/service-mesh policy that evaluates MAC then DAC and returns a single final allow/deny decision
- [ ] Remove or block any bypass paths so all access to covered objects/actions is mediated by the PDP/PEP
- [ ] Enable structured audit logging of MAC and DAC decision inputs and outcomes and forward to SIEM for retention and review
- [ ] Publish MAC/DAC policies as version-controlled policy-as-code with peer review and controlled deployment

### ac-4 — Information Flow Enforcement (Control)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure information can only travel where it is meant to go, both inside your systems and between connected systems, according to the organisation’s approved rules. Without this, sensitive or restricted information could be sent to the wrong place, disguised as coming from inside the organisation, or leaked to the public internet in an unprotected form, including export-controlled data; it could also break the rules of another business area or partner system, or allow unauthorised changes to how information is handled. To meet this expectation, the organisation must enforce its approved cross-domain information-flow rules, including checking that the sender is allowed to write, routing based on the information’s assigned handling labels, and applying special handling for export-controlled material. It must apply these rules to both normal data and system “control” traffic such as routing and name lookups, block traffic that pretends to be internal, restrict web requests to go through the organisation’s internal web proxy, limit transfers between organisations to agreed structures and content, and only allow such transfers when an agreement specifies enforcement. It must also use trusted enforcement mechanisms at boundary points, verify permissions before accepting incoming information, support one-way flows and any required label changes, and keep audit records showing what was allowed or blocked.

#### Label-aware cross-domain flow enforcement at boundaries

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement and operate an enforcement mechanism at all cross-domain boundary points (within the system and between connected systems) that permits, denies, or routes information flows only according to the organisation’s **approved information flow control policies** (ac-04_odp). Enforcement must cover both **data-plane** and **control-plane** traffic (e.g., DNS/routing-adjacent services), including **write-permission checks**, **label-based routing**, **export-controlled handling**, and any required **regrading/label reassignment** at designated policy enforcement points.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-4 requires the organisation to **enforce approved authorisations for controlling the flow of information** within the system and between connected systems based on the organisation-defined **information flow control policies** (ac-04_odp). Guidance explicitly calls out blocking spoofed internal traffic, restricting export-controlled information from being transmitted in the clear to the Internet, restricting web requests to the internal web proxy, limiting inter-organisation transfers, verifying write permissions before accepting information from another domain/system, and applying flow control to control-plane traffic.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create an authoritative policy model (policy-as-code)** that expresses ac-04_odp as enforceable rules: allowed source→destination pairs, permitted protocols/ports, required handling labels, required actions (permit/deny/route-to-proxy/route-to-guard), and explicit handling for export-controlled labels.
2. **Deploy boundary enforcement points** (e.g., firewall/segmentation gateways, API gateways, secure web gateways/proxies, and DNS enforcement) so that all cross-domain traffic is evaluated against the policy model before any session is established or data is accepted.
3. **Enforce write-permission checks on inbound flows** from other security/privacy domains/connected systems: only accept writes when the sending identity/service and the target resource are authorised for the relevant handling label(s).
4. **Implement label-based routing and (where required) trustworthy regrading** at designated enforcement points: route traffic based on labels; if labels must change across domains, perform regrading only via an approved mechanism with controlled trust boundaries and auditable outcomes.
5. **Prevent policy bypass** by blocking direct outbound web access except via the internal web proxy, and by denying traffic that claims to originate internally unless explicitly permitted by policy.
6. **Apply the same enforcement to control-plane traffic**: restrict DNS to approved resolvers, ensure routing/DNS-adjacent services are only reachable via approved paths, and ensure these decisions are logged.
7. **Add auditability and continuous verification**: log allow/deny decisions with policy rule identifiers, source/destination, labels, and enforcement point; periodically test with synthetic flows (including spoofed-internal attempts and export-controlled scenarios) to prove enforcement.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Information Security Architect / Security Engineering Lead**: owns the policy model and boundary enforcement design.
- **Network Security Engineer / Platform Security Engineer**: implements and configures enforcement points (firewalls/segmentation, proxies, gateways, DNS controls).
- **Application Owner / Integration Engineer**: ensures application/API interactions and label propagation integrate with the enforcement model.
- **GRC/Compliance**: validates that ac-04_odp rules are correctly represented and that evidence meets audit needs.

## Acceptance criteria
- For every defined cross-domain flow in ac-04_odp, the enforcement points **permit only** the approved flows and **deny** all others.
- Inbound transfers from other domains are accepted only when **write permissions** are validated against the policy model.
- Export-controlled labelled information cannot be transmitted to the Internet in the clear (default-deny cleartext egress; approved encrypted/approved destinations only).
- Web requests cannot bypass the internal web proxy (direct outbound HTTP(S) is blocked; proxy routing is enforced).
- Control-plane traffic (at minimum DNS and routing-adjacent services) is subject to the same boundary policy evaluation and is logged.
- Evidence exists: configuration/policy-as-code change records, and SIEM/DLP/proxy/firewall logs showing both permitted and blocked attempts with rule identifiers.


**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Translate ac-04_odp into a policy-as-code model defining allowed source/destination pairs, required labels, permitted protocols, and required actions (permit/deny/route-to-proxy/route-to-guard).
- [ ] Identify and inventory all cross-domain boundary points (network, API, web proxy, DNS/resolvers, and inter-organisation links) and map each to the corresponding policy enforcement rules.
- [ ] Configure boundary enforcement devices/services to evaluate every cross-domain session against the policy model before permitting traffic, including control-plane traffic.
- [ ] Implement inbound write-permission checks for flows originating from other security/privacy domains or connected systems, rejecting unauthorised writes by label and target resource.
- [ ] Enforce web proxy routing by blocking direct outbound HTTP(S) except to the internal web proxy and validating proxy routing rules against the same label model.
- [ ] Implement export-controlled handling at egress by denying cleartext Internet egress for export-controlled labels and allowing only approved encrypted destinations.
- [ ] Enable audit logging of allow/deny decisions with policy rule identifiers, labels, source/destination, and enforcement point; run periodic synthetic tests (including spoofed-internal and export-controlled scenarios) to verify enforcement.

### ac-4.1 — Object Security and Privacy Attributes (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure sensitive information can only be sent to the right places, and that personal data is shared only under the rules that apply. Without it, confidential material could be accidentally or deliberately passed to the wrong team, system, or country, and personal information could be combined or released in ways that breach privacy obligations, causing financial loss, legal exposure, and harm to people. To meet this expectation, the organisation must define the labels it will use for both sensitivity and privacy (including whether data is Secret or Top Secret, whether it is restricted to certain countries, and what kind of personal data it contains, how it is used, and how sharing is limited). It must ensure every piece of information, every sending device or service, and every receiving device or service is tagged with these labels, keep an up-to-date record of the labels and where they apply, and then enforce a simple rule: allow the transfer only when both the sensitivity and privacy sharing rules permit it; otherwise block it and raise an alert, while recording the decision in audit records and documenting the approach in the system security and privacy plans.

#### Enforce attribute-based allow/deny for security & privacy flows

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement information flow enforcement that uses **organisation-defined security attributes** and **privacy attributes** associated with **information objects**, **source objects**, and **destination objects** to make **allow/deny** decisions for each attempted flow.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-4(1) enhancement requires: **“Use {{ ac-4.1_prm_1 }} associated with {{ ac-4.1_prm_2 }} to enforce {{ ac-04.01_odp.09 }} as a basis for flow control decisions.”**
This means enforcement must compare security + privacy attributes and **deny and alert** when flows are not explicitly allowed by the information flow control policies.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the attribute model and decision rule** in a central, version-controlled policy set:
   - Security attributes (ac-04.01_odp.01 / ac-4.1_prm_1): `classification` (Secret|Top Secret|Unclassified), `releasability` (ITAR/EAR/US-only), `system` (owning enclave).
   - Privacy attributes (ac-04.01_odp.02 / ac-4.1_prm_1): `PII type` (none|direct|indirect), `processing` (HIPAA covered|GDPR personal data), `sharing` (restricted).
   - Object types to tag (ac-04.01_odp.03-08 / ac-4.1_prm_2): information objects (dataset/record/message/file), source objects (host/sensor/controller/application), destination objects (host/service/data store/OT historian).
   - Information flow control policy basis (ac-04.01_odp.09): **Allow only when** (source/destination security classification permits) **and** (privacy sharing rules permit); otherwise **deny and alert**.

2. **Create authoritative tagging/label propagation** so enforcement can reliably retrieve attributes:
   - Ensure information objects are tagged with security + privacy attributes at creation/ingestion (e.g., document/data platform metadata, dataset registry, message headers).
   - Ensure source and destination objects are tagged via asset/workload inventory (CMDB/workload identity) and service registry (e.g., endpoint/service catalogue).
   - Implement a deterministic mapping from runtime identifiers (hostnames, service IDs, dataset IDs, API routes, message topics) to the corresponding tagged objects.

3. **Implement an attribute-aware Policy Decision Point (PDP)** and enforcement integration:
   - PDP evaluates each attempted flow using the decision rule in ac-04.01_odp.09.
   - Enforcement points (e.g., API gateway, service mesh sidecar, next-gen firewall/segmentation gateway, ZTNA policy enforcement, OT gateway where applicable) must query the PDP (or consume signed policy decisions) and enforce **deny** when either security or privacy checks fail.

4. **Generate auditable deny decisions with attribute context**:
   - For denied flows, log: source object ID, destination object ID, information object identifier (or classification label), the security attributes involved, the privacy attributes involved, the specific policy rule/reason, and the final decision (deny).
   - Forward logs to SIEM for alerting and correlation (separate alert categories for “classification mismatch” vs “privacy sharing restriction”).

5. **Operationalise and test**:
   - Maintain a test matrix covering allowed and denied combinations (e.g., Secret→Secret allowed; Top Secret→Secret denied; restricted PII dataset denied to restricted destination).
   - Validate that enforcement behaves consistently across environments (prod/test/DR) and that label propagation failures default to deny (fail-closed) where technically feasible.

## WHO
- **Information Security Architect / Data Governance Lead**: defines attribute model and policy decision rule.
- **Platform/Network Security Engineer (IT/OT as applicable)**: implements PDP + enforcement integration.
- **Application Owner / Integration Engineer**: ensures information objects and runtime identifiers are correctly tagged and mapped.
- **SOC / SIEM Engineer**: configures alerting and audit evidence pipelines.

## Acceptance criteria
- For any attempted flow, the enforcement decision is based on **ac-4.1_prm_1** attributes associated with **ac-4.1_prm_2** objects.
- Flows that violate **security classification/releasability** or **privacy sharing rules** are **denied** and produce an **alert**.
- Denied-flow audit records include the relevant security and privacy attributes and the decision rationale.
- A documented test matrix demonstrates correct allow/deny outcomes for representative combinations of security and privacy attributes.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define and publish the security and privacy attribute schema and the allow/deny decision rule in a version-controlled policy repository
- [ ] Implement authoritative tagging for information objects, source objects, and destination objects using the organisation-defined object types
- [ ] Build or configure an attribute-aware PDP that evaluates security attributes and privacy attributes according to the decision rule
- [ ] Integrate enforcement points (e.g., API gateway/service mesh/segmentation/OT gateway) to query the PDP and enforce deny decisions
- [ ] Configure audit logging for denied flows to include source/destination identifiers, security attributes, privacy attributes, and the policy reason
- [ ] Create and execute a test matrix proving allowed and denied outcomes (e.g., Secret→Secret allowed; Top Secret→Secret denied; restricted PII sharing denied) and remediate any label propagation gaps

### ac-4.2 — Processing Domains (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about keeping information in the right “separate areas” inside your systems, and only letting it move where the rules say it may. Without this, sensitive data could be copied, leaked, or used in the wrong place, or one part of a system could quietly pass information to another part that should not receive it, leading to fraud, privacy breaches, or regulatory problems. To meet this expectation, the organisation must set up protected, controlled processing areas and define clear rules for which kinds of information are allowed to move between those areas and to or from specific data items, including which areas may communicate and which processes may switch from one area to another. The system must then make its allow-or-block decisions using those rules, with processes assigned to the correct areas and information clearly labelled by type. The organisation must document how this works, configure the system to enforce it, keep evidence through audit records, and assign responsibility to the relevant administrators and security staff.

#### Protected processing domains via domain+type enforcement

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement protected processing domains and enforce **information flow control policies** at runtime using **domain and type enforcement**. Flow decisions must be based on the **source processing domain**, the **destination processing domain/object**, and the **information type**, including whether **signalling** and **process transitions** between domains are permitted.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4(2)** enhancement requires: *“Use protected processing domains to enforce {{ insert: param, ac-04.02_odp }} as a basis for flow control decisions.”* The organisation-defined parameter **ac-04.02_odp** is: *“information flow control policies to be enforced by use of protected processing domains… Domain and type enforcement policies for controlled information flows between processing domains and to/from information objects.”*

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define protected processing domains** in the application/platform (e.g., `HR-Apps`, `Finance-Apps`, `Partner-Integration`, `Admin-Functions`, `Public-API`) and document the allowed interactions between them.
2. **Define information types** aligned to the organisation’s data classification (e.g., `PII`, `Confidential-Finance`, `Operational-Metrics`, `Public`). Ensure every information object that can cross domains is assigned a type at creation/ingest.
3. **Create domain+type enforcement policies** (policy-as-code preferred) that explicitly state:
   - allowed information accesses from **domain → domain/object** for each **information type**;
   - allowed **signalling** (events/notifications/callbacks/message publishes) between domains for each information type;
   - allowed **process transitions** (workflow state changes/job handoffs) between domains for each information type.
4. **Bind runtime processes to domains** so the system can reliably determine the processing domain context for each executing component (e.g., workload identity mapped to domain via namespace/project, service registry metadata, or signed domain claims).
5. **Enforce flow decisions centrally** at the enforcement points used by the application (e.g., API gateway/service mesh policy, message broker authorisation layer, data-access abstraction layer). Each decision must evaluate **(source domain, destination domain/object, information type, action)** and return allow/deny.
6. **Emit audit evidence for allow/deny decisions** including the rule/policy identifier, source/destination domains, information type, action (access/signalling/transition), and correlation identifiers.
7. **Test and validate** policy correctness with automated test cases covering allowed and denied flows, including negative tests for cross-domain signalling and unauthorised process transitions.

## WHO
- **Application Security Architect / Security Engineering Lead**: designs domain taxonomy, information types, and policy model.
- **Platform/Cloud Engineer or DevOps Lead**: implements enforcement points and domain binding (workload identity mapping).
- **Application Owner / Engineering Manager**: ensures all services and data objects participate in domain/type tagging and enforcement.
- **Security Operations / SIEM Analyst**: validates audit event ingestion and alerting for denied flows.

## Acceptance criteria
- The system enforces flow control decisions using **protected processing domains** and **domain+type policies** (not only ad-hoc checks).
- For each defined domain interaction, the policy explicitly covers **access**, **signalling**, and **process transitions** for each relevant information type.
- Runtime enforcement logs show **allow/deny** decisions with **source domain, destination domain/object, information type, action, and policy/rule reference**.
- Automated tests demonstrate correct behaviour for both permitted and prohibited cross-domain flows, including signalling and transitions.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define a protected processing domain taxonomy and map each application component/workload to a domain identity
- [ ] Define information types aligned to organisational data classification and enforce tagging at object creation/ingest
- [ ] Write domain+type enforcement policies covering allowed access, signalling, and process transitions between domains/objects
- [ ] Configure the application/platform enforcement point to evaluate (source domain, destination domain/object, information type, action) and return allow/deny
- [ ] Implement propagation of information type labels through transformations and inter-service messaging
- [ ] Enable structured audit logging for every allow/deny decision including policy/rule reference and correlation identifiers
- [ ] Create and run automated tests for permitted and denied cross-domain access, signalling, and process transitions

### ac-4.3 — Dynamic Information Flow Control (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about only letting information move in special, time-critical situations, and stopping it immediately when conditions change. Without this, staff could accidentally or automatically share sensitive information at the wrong time, or when the organisation is under heightened threat, increasing the chance of data leaks, fraud, or disruption to essential services. The organisation must set clear rules for when “dynamic” information sharing is permitted, based on two live checks: first, the specific mission-critical conditions must be met, and second, the latest threat and risk assessment must say the sharing is allowed. If either check fails, the system must block the dynamic sharing and immediately alert the Security Operations Centre so it can respond. The organisation must document the decision process, configure the system to enforce it, keep records of every allow or block decision with the reason, and include how this is implemented in system plans and design documentation.

#### Dynamic information flow gate with mission/risk checks and SOC alerts

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement a runtime “dynamic information flow gate” that permits dynamic information flows **only** when (1) **mission-critical conditions are met** and (2) the **latest threat/risk assessment permits** the flow; otherwise the system must **deny** the flow and **alert the SOC immediately** with auditable decision details.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4(3)** enhancement requires enforcing **{{ insert: param, ac-04.03_odp }}**. The organisation-defined parameter states: **“information flow control policies to be enforced are defined”** and the default rule: **“Allow dynamic flows only when mission-critical conditions are met and threat/risk assessments permit; otherwise deny and alert SOC.”**

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define the dynamic flow policy decision rule (allow/deny)
- Create an explicit policy decision model with two live inputs:
  - **Mission-critical condition status** (e.g., emergency mode/change window/incident response state) for the specific flow context (source/destination/app/service).
  - **Latest threat/risk assessment permit/deny** for the same flow context.
- Implement **deny-by-default** when either input is missing, stale, or indicates denial.
- Add **reason categories** for SOC triage (e.g., `MISSION_CRITICAL_NOT_MET`, `RISK_NOT_PERMITTED`, `ASSESSMENT_STALE_OR_MISSING`).

### 2) Enforce the decision at runtime (connection/session time)
- Deploy a policy decision point (PDP) and enforcement point (PEP) pattern:
  - **PDP** evaluates the policy using the two live inputs.
  - **PEP** blocks the dynamic flow at the network/segmentation enforcement layer (e.g., ZTNA gateway, micro-segmentation controller, next-gen firewall policy hook, or service-mesh policy enforcement where applicable).
- Ensure evaluation occurs:
  - **At flow initiation** (connection/session establishment), and
  - **Revalidated at runtime** (e.g., session re-check interval or event-driven revalidation) so that changes in mission/risk conditions mid-session can trigger denial.

### 3) Integrate “latest threat/risk assessment” as an authoritative, versioned input
- Connect the PDP to the organisation’s risk/threat posture outputs (risk engine, exposure management, or threat intel/risk scoring service).
- Require the PDP to record and use an **assessment identifier/version** and **timestamp**; deny if the assessment is older than the organisation-defined freshness window.

### 4) Detect potentially harmful/adverse events that change conditions
- Subscribe the PDP to security/operations signals that indicate conditions have changed (e.g., detection of adverse events, escalation to incident response, threat intel triggers, or risk posture downgrade).
- When such signals arrive, update the mission/risk inputs and trigger **immediate revalidation** for affected flows.

### 5) SOC alerting and auditable enforcement evidence
- When a dynamic flow is denied, generate a structured SOC alert containing:
  - flow identifiers (source/destination, application/service, user/service account if available),
  - the **decision outcome** (deny),
  - the **reason category**,
  - the **mission-critical status** used,
  - the **assessment ID/version and timestamp** used.
- Send alerts to SIEM/SOAR in near real time.
- Log every allow/deny decision with the above inputs; store logs in an append-only/immutable manner where feasible.

## WHO
- **Security Architect / IAM & Network Security Lead**: defines policy decision model, reason categories, and enforcement placement.
- **SOC Lead / Detection Engineering**: defines alert content, routing, and triage expectations.
- **Platform/Infrastructure Engineering**: implements PDP/PEP integration, session revalidation, and SIEM/SOAR event mapping.
- **Risk/Threat Management Owner**: provides authoritative “latest threat/risk assessment” outputs and freshness criteria.

## Acceptance criteria
- Dynamic flows are **allowed only** when both mission-critical conditions are met and the latest threat/risk assessment permits; otherwise they are **blocked**.
- If mission-critical status changes or threat/risk posture is downgraded during an active session, the system **revalidates** and denies affected flows.
- Denied flows generate **immediate SOC alerts** with actionable details and a reason category.
- Every decision (allow/deny) is logged with assessment version/timestamp and mission-critical status used; missing/stale inputs result in **deny-by-default**.
- Evidence is available for audit: policy-as-code/config, PDP/PEP configuration, SIEM alert samples, and decision logs demonstrating correct enforcement.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define the dynamic flow allow/deny decision rule using mission-critical status and latest threat/risk permit/deny outcomes, including deny-by-default for missing/stale inputs
- [ ] Implement a PDP/PEP “dynamic flow gate” that evaluates the rule at flow initiation and revalidates during active sessions
- [ ] Integrate the PDP with the authoritative risk/threat assessment source and enforce assessment freshness using an assessment ID/version and timestamp
- [ ] Configure enforcement at the appropriate mediation layer (e.g., ZTNA/micro-segmentation/firewall/service-mesh) to block denied dynamic flows
- [ ] Create structured SOC alert events for denied decisions with reason categories and the decision inputs used
- [ ] Enable immutable/append-only logging of every allow/deny decision and retain evidence for audit
- [ ] Test with scenarios where mission-critical conditions fail and where threat/risk posture changes mid-session, verifying denial and SOC alerting

### ac-4.4 — Flow Control of Encrypted Information (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about stopping secret-looking messages from slipping past your checks. If encrypted or encoded information could travel around your content checks, harmful or sensitive material could be sent without being identified, classified, or blocked according to your rules, creating real exposure for the organisation. To meet this expectation, the organisation must ensure that encrypted (and encoded) data is still treated as something that must be inspected and matched to the correct data type rules, not something that can bypass them. It must enforce this by using approved encrypted-traffic inspection gateways, configured so they only send traffic to explicitly approved destinations, and by blocking any encrypted traffic that would otherwise bypass the checks while ending any communication attempts that try to do so. The organisation must also alert the Security Operations Centre when blocks or session terminations occur, keep clear procedures and system design records, document the configuration settings, record events for later review, and include the approach in its security plan.

#### TLS inspection gateway to block encrypted bypass and terminate sessions

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Deploy and operate approved TLS inspection gateways so encrypted (and encoded) traffic cannot bypass the organisation’s information flow control mechanisms (content inspection security policy filters and data type identifiers). Configure the gateways to **block the flow** of non-compliant encrypted information and **terminate communications sessions** attempting to pass encrypted information, while generating SOC alerts and retaining auditable evidence.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 AC-4(4) enhancement requires: **“Prevent encrypted information from bypassing {{ insert: param, ac-04.04_odp.01 }} by {{ insert: param, ac-04.04_odp.02 }}.”**
- The organisation-defined information flow control mechanisms are: **content inspection security policy filters and data type identifiers** (ac-04.04_odp.01).
- The required enforcement is: **blocking the flow of the encrypted information and terminating communications sessions attempting to pass encrypted information** (ac-04.04_odp.02).
The guidance further clarifies that encryption includes **encoded data not recognised by filtering mechanisms**, so the enforcement must treat such traffic as inspectable and subject to policy.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Place inspection choke points**: Position approved TLS inspection gateways at network egress points where encrypted traffic could otherwise evade inspection (e.g., internet egress, inter-segment egress, and SaaS access paths where feasible).
2. **Define inspection policy scope**: Configure the gateway to decrypt/inspect TLS traffic for the relevant protocols/ports and apply the organisation’s **content inspection security policy filters** and **data type identifiers**. Include explicit handling for **encoded but unrecognised** payloads (e.g., treat unknown encodings as inspectable and apply conservative deny/terminate rules rather than allowing pass-through).
3. **Enforce allow-listed destinations**: Configure the gateway so that only **explicitly approved destinations** are permitted for inspected/allowed flows. Everything else must be blocked or only permitted if explicitly authorised by policy.
4. **Implement block + terminate actions**: For any session that would bypass filters/data-type rules, configure the gateway to (a) **block the flow** and (b) **terminate the TCP/TLS session** (not just log). Ensure the termination occurs at the gateway at the time of policy decision.
5. **Integrate SOC alerting and audit evidence**: Send structured events to the SIEM/SOC for each block/termination, including reason codes (policy/filter/data-type mismatch), gateway policy ID, source/destination, service identity, and session metadata. Retain logs to support investigation and audit.
6. **Operationalise with change control**: Manage gateway policies using version-controlled configuration with approvals and testing (policy-as-code where available). Maintain system design/security plan records showing how the gateway prevents encrypted bypass.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Network/Security Engineering**: implement gateway placement, TLS inspection configuration, allow-listing, and enforcement (block + terminate).
- **Security Operations (SOC) / SIEM Engineering**: implement alert routing, detections, and log retention.
- **Information Security Governance / Risk Owner**: approve inspection policy scope, destination allow-list governance, and encoded/unknown handling approach.

## Acceptance criteria
- Encrypted TLS sessions that would otherwise bypass content inspection are **blocked** and the **session is terminated** by the gateway.
- The gateway applies **content inspection security policy filters** and **data type identifiers** to inspected encrypted traffic.
- Encoded payloads that are **not recognised by filtering mechanisms** do not pass through; they are handled according to the configured conservative policy (deny/terminate).
- Only **allow-listed destinations** are permitted for allowed flows; non-allow-listed destinations are blocked/terminated per policy.
- SOC receives auditable alerts for each block/termination, and logs include sufficient metadata to identify the policy decision and affected session.
- Gateway policy configuration and design rationale are documented and change-controlled.


**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Select and deploy approved TLS inspection gateways at all relevant encrypted traffic choke points
- [ ] Configure gateway policies to apply content inspection security policy filters and data type identifiers to decrypted TLS traffic
- [ ] Define and implement conservative handling for encoded but unrecognised payloads (deny/terminate rather than allow pass-through)
- [ ] Configure destination allow-listing so only explicitly approved destinations are permitted for allowed flows
- [ ] Enable enforcement actions to block the flow and terminate communications sessions when bypass is detected
- [ ] Integrate gateway block/termination events with the SIEM/SOC using structured reason codes and session metadata
- [ ] Establish policy-as-code/version control, approvals, and documented design/security plan records for auditability

### ac-4.5 — Embedded Data Types (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about stopping files from hiding other files inside them too deeply, or in ways your organisation cannot properly check. If someone sends a document or message that contains nested content (for example, a file inside a file, or compressed material that contains further hidden items), it can be harder to spot what is actually inside, which increases the risk that harmful or unauthorised content slips through checks. To meet this expectation, the organisation must set and document clear rules that limit embedded content to a maximum of two nesting levels, and it must also refuse any embedded content that its inspection tools cannot analyse. Staff must follow procedures that enforce these limits, system settings must be configured to apply them when handling incoming or outgoing content, and the organisation must keep records showing enforcement decisions, including when items are blocked for being too deeply nested or unanalysable.

#### Enforce max-2 embedded data types with inspection-capability blocks

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement an enforcement mechanism in the application’s content handling pipeline that limits embedded data type nesting to **maximum 2 levels** and **blocks** any embedded content that the organisation’s inspection tools cannot unpack/analyse.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4(5)** (Enhancement) requires enforcing the organisation-defined limitations on embedding data types within other data types. The enhancement guidance notes that embedding can reduce flow control effectiveness (e.g., file-in-file, compressed/archived containers with multiple embedded items) and that limitations must consider **embedding levels** and **prohibit levels beyond inspection tool capability**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create/maintain an organisation “Embedding Limitations List”** that defines:
   - Allowed maximum embedding depth: **2 levels** (ac-04.05_odp “limitations” default: max 2 levels).
   - A list of embedded container/content types that are **inspectable** vs **uninspectable** by the organisation’s inspection tooling.
2. **Implement a content inspection gate** (service/library) that is invoked for all inbound and outbound content that could carry embedded data types (e.g., uploads, API document ingestion, email/file transfer, document storage release).
3. **Recursive unpacking with a hard stop at level 2**:
   - The gate must unpack/parse containers recursively and track nesting depth.
   - If **level 3** (or greater) embedded data type is detected, the gate must **block** the content.
4. **Inspection-capability enforcement**:
   - For each embedded object, the gate must determine whether the inspection tooling can unpack/analyse that embedded type.
   - If an embedded object is of an **uninspectable** type (per the limitations list), the gate must **block** the content (even if depth ≤ 2).
5. **Auditability and evidence**:
   - For every allow/block decision, write an audit log entry including: content identifier, detected embedding depth, embedded type(s), decision (allow/block), the rule version, and the specific reason ("depth exceeded" or "uninspectable embedded type").
6. **Operational integration**:
   - Integrate the gate into the application’s content workflow so that non-compliant content cannot be stored, forwarded, or released.
   - Ensure the gate is applied consistently across environments (prod/test/DR) using the same rule set and inspection capability configuration.

## WHO
- **Application Security Engineer / Platform Engineer**: implements the inspection gate and integrates it into content workflows.
- **Information Security Governance / Compliance Owner**: owns and approves the Embedding Limitations List and inspection capability matrix.
- **Security Operations (SIEM/Monitoring)**: validates alerting and incident workflows for repeated block events.

## Acceptance criteria
- The system blocks any content containing embedded data types beyond **2 nesting levels**.
- The system blocks any content containing embedded objects of types that are **not inspectable** by the organisation’s inspection tooling.
- For each blocked/allowed item, audit logs contain: embedding depth, embedded type(s), decision, reason, and rule version.
- The enforcement is applied to all relevant inbound/outbound content paths configured for the application (no bypass routes).
- The rule set (max depth and uninspectable types) is version-controlled and change-managed.


**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define and approve the Embedding Limitations List (max depth=2 and uninspectable embedded types) aligned to ac-04.05_odp
- [ ] Implement a recursive content inspection gate that tracks embedding depth and enforces a hard stop at level 2
- [ ] Integrate inspection-capability checks so uninspectable embedded types are blocked even when depth is within limits
- [ ] Wire the gate into all inbound/outbound content workflows (uploads, API ingestion, storage release, and file transfer paths) to prevent bypass
- [ ] Record audit events for every allow/block decision with depth, embedded type(s), decision reason, and rule version
- [ ] Add monitoring/alerting for repeated depth-exceeded and uninspectable-type block attempts and route to incident workflow

### ac-4.6 — Metadata (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This expectation is about making sure the labels and descriptions attached to each piece of information are trusted, travel with it, and are used to decide where that information is allowed to go. Without this, someone could tamper with those labels so sensitive information is wrongly shared, or information could be routed based on incorrect details, leading to unauthorised access, data leaks, or compliance failures. To meet this, the organisation must set clear rules for which information movements are allowed or blocked based on specific metadata tags, and document what kinds of tags are used. The system must securely bind those tags to the actual data item, so any unauthorised change is detected, and it must verify the tags’ integrity before allowing or blocking sharing. The tags must be kept in a metadata store that only authorised people and systems can access or update. Finally, the organisation must keep audit records showing who made decisions, which tags were used, what happened, and must safely handle any integrity failures.

#### Cryptographically bound metadata tags for flow control decisions

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement metadata-driven information flow control by cryptographically binding authoritative metadata tags to each data payload, and enforcing allow/block decisions only after verifying the metadata integrity.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 AC-4(6) (Enhancement) requires: **"Enforce information flow control based on {{ insert: param, ac-04.06_odp }}"**. The guidance further requires that metadata used for flow decisions is **trustworthy**, including:
- **Trustworthiness of metadata values** (correctness w.r.t. the data)
- **Data integrity** (protection against unauthorised changes to metadata tags)
- **Binding of metadata to the data payload** using **sufficiently strong binding techniques with appropriate assurance**

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define authoritative metadata tags and schema** for flow decisions (the organisation-defined parameter **ac-04.06_odp = metadata**). Include both the tag values and the metadata types used (structural and/or descriptive).
2. **Create a per-payload “security envelope”** that travels with the payload. The envelope must include:
   - metadata tag values (the ones used for flow decisions)
   - a payload hash (or equivalent digest)
   - envelope versioning and tag schema identifier
   - a cryptographic integrity check (e.g., signature or MAC) computed over **both** the payload digest and the metadata tag values to prevent tag/payload swapping.
3. **Use an access-controlled metadata store** to hold authoritative tag definitions and the allow/block policy mappings that interpret tag values. Restrict write access to a small set of authorised services/roles.
4. **Verify before enforcement at every enforcement point** (e.g., API gateway, service-to-service middleware, secure file transfer/release service, message broker consumer). Enforcement logic must:
   - verify the envelope integrity
   - reject/deny the flow if verification fails or if tag schema/version is invalid
   - use only the verified tag values to evaluate the allow/block policy.
5. **Handle integrity failures safely**: on verification failure, deny or quarantine the payload (no fallback to untrusted metadata), and emit a high-fidelity audit event indicating integrity verification failure.
6. **Generate audit evidence** for each decision: record actor/service identity, data identifier, destination, the verified metadata tag values used, the policy decision (allow/block), and the integrity verification result.
7. **Operationalise assurance**: implement automated tests that attempt metadata tampering (alter tag values, swap envelope/payload, replay old envelopes) and confirm enforcement denies the flow.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Application security architect / security engineering lead**: owns envelope design, enforcement integration points, and assurance testing.
- **Platform/integration engineers**: implement verification and policy evaluation in gateways/middleware and ensure envelope propagation.
- **IAM/PKI/KMS administrators**: manage signing/verification keys, rotation, and access controls.
- **Data governance/policy owner**: defines authoritative metadata tags and their correct semantics for flow decisions.

## Acceptance criteria
- For every outbound/inbound information flow subject to AC-4(6), the system verifies a cryptographic envelope before making an allow/block decision.
- If metadata tags are altered, swapped, or the envelope integrity check fails, the flow is denied/quarantined and an audit record is produced.
- Policy decisions are based only on **verified** metadata tag values bound to the payload.
- The metadata store and policy mappings used for interpretation are access-controlled with restricted write privileges.
- Audit logs include: actor/service identity, data identifier, verified tag values, destination, decision outcome, and integrity verification status.


**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define the authoritative metadata tag taxonomy and which tag types (structural/descriptive) drive flow decisions for ac-04.06_odp
- [ ] Design and implement a per-payload security envelope format that cryptographically protects both payload digest and metadata tag values
- [ ] Implement envelope signing/verification using enterprise-approved KMS/HSM-backed keys with rotation and restricted key access
- [ ] Implement an access-controlled metadata store for tag definitions and allow/block policy mappings with least-privilege write access
- [ ] Integrate envelope verification into every information-flow enforcement point before policy evaluation (deny on verification failure)
- [ ] Implement audit logging that records verified tag values, decision outcome, destination, and integrity verification status per flow
- [ ] Create automated tamper/replay test cases to prove that altered or swapped metadata cannot drive an allow decision

### ac-4.7 — One-way Flow Mechanisms (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about using a purpose-built piece of hardware that only allows information to move in one direction between two systems, so sensitive information can’t be pushed out where it shouldn’t go. Without this kind of one-way protection, someone could accidentally or deliberately export data from a more sensitive, restricted environment into a less restricted one, creating serious legal, financial, and reputational harm. To meet this expectation, the organisation must set out clear rules for which data is allowed to travel one way and which is not, and then design, document, and operate the system so that the one-way behaviour is enforced by hardware rather than relying on software settings alone. The organisation must keep records showing how the hardware is placed and configured, maintain procedures for keeping it working correctly, include the approach in its security planning, and retain audit records that demonstrate blocked attempts in the forbidden direction and successful transfers in the permitted direction.

#### Deploy hardware data diode for one-way cross-domain transfers

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Enforce one-way information flows between the higher-impact (e.g., confidential/classified) domain and the lower-impact (e.g., unclassified) domain using a purpose-built hardware one-way flow mechanism (data diode / unidirectional security gateway).

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 AC-4(7) enhancement requires that one-way information flows are enforced through **hardware-based flow control mechanisms**. This prevents prohibited export from the higher-impact domain and permits only the authorised direction.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define permitted direction(s) and scope**: Document which direction is allowed (e.g., lower → higher) and which data/protocols are in-scope for the one-way path, including explicit prohibition of the reverse direction.
2. **Architect placement as the only path**: Update network/security architecture so the diode/unidirectional gateway is the **only** connectivity path for the in-scope data exchange between the two domains (no shared VLANs, no alternate routing, no VPN/overlay paths that could bypass the mechanism).
3. **Procure and configure the hardware for true unidirectionality**: Configure the device in its supported unidirectional mode (e.g., TX-only/RX-only or diode mode) so reverse-direction interfaces are disabled/blocked by design (not by software policy).
4. **Harden adjacent connectivity to prevent bypass**: Apply routing and segmentation controls (e.g., ACLs, firewall rules, route filtering, micro-segmentation) so there is no alternate route that would allow higher → lower export outside the diode.
5. **Enable and centralise audit logging from the hardware**: Configure the diode/gateway to export logs/events (allowed transfers and blocked attempts) to the organisation SIEM/logging platform; ensure log retention aligns with the security plan.
6. **Run controlled verification tests and record evidence**: Perform test transfers that represent (a) permitted direction success and (b) prohibited direction failure; capture timestamps, device logs, and test results as audit evidence.
7. **Operationalise with change/configuration management**: Maintain configuration records (device model/serial, interface mapping, permitted protocol/port behaviour, firmware version) and define procedures for health checks, failure handling, and security sign-off for changes.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Network/OT-IT security architect**: defines permitted direction/scope and placement as the only path.
- **Infrastructure/network engineering team**: implements diode placement, configuration, and adjacent segmentation/routing controls.
- **Security operations / SOC**: validates logging to SIEM and monitors blocked/allowed events.
- **Change manager / security governance**: ensures change approvals and configuration management for the diode and its enforcement boundary.

## Acceptance criteria
- The hardware one-way mechanism is deployed between the higher-impact and lower-impact domains and is configured for unidirectional operation.
- For in-scope traffic, **higher → lower attempts fail** (blocked) and **lower → higher transfers succeed** (allowed), verified by controlled tests.
- Architecture and network controls ensure there is **no alternate path** that bypasses the diode for in-scope data exchange.
- Hardware configuration (including interface mapping, permitted protocol/port behaviour, firmware version) is documented and managed under configuration control.
- Device logs/events for allowed and blocked attempts are forwarded to the SIEM and retained per the security plan.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Document the permitted one-way direction(s) and in-scope data/protocols for the cross-domain exchange
- [ ] Design the network so the diode/unidirectional gateway is the only path between the two security domains for in-scope traffic
- [ ] Configure the hardware data diode/unidirectional gateway in true unidirectional mode and disable/blocked reverse-direction interfaces
- [ ] Implement segmentation and routing controls to prevent any alternate route that could bypass the one-way mechanism
- [ ] Enable hardware-generated logs/events and forward them to the SIEM with retention aligned to the security plan
- [ ] Execute controlled tests proving higher→lower is blocked and lower→higher is allowed, and store evidence (logs + timestamps)
- [ ] Record device configuration details (serial/interface mapping/firmware) and enforce change control with security sign-off

### ac-4.8 — Security and Privacy Policy Filters (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This expectation is about stopping sensitive or private information from quietly leaving the right places by checking it before it travels. It matters because, without these checks, staff could accidentally send the wrong file type or an oversized file, include hidden or prohibited content, or disclose personal details that should not be shared—especially when data moves from internal systems to external services or between different on-prem security areas. To meet this, the organisation must set up approved security and privacy checking rules (covering file type and size limits, field value limits for structured data, and detection of prohibited words and personal data patterns) and use them as the deciding step for allowing or blocking transfers. For the specific routes covered, transfers are allowed only when both security and privacy checks pass, including content, metadata, and file-type validation. If the checking process fails or the data fails the rules, the data must be quarantined by blocking the transfer or preventing release as appropriate, and the system owner and security operations must be notified within 1 hour; if the issue is privacy-related, the privacy officer must also be notified within 1 hour.

#### Inline security/privacy policy filters at egress and cross-domain gateways

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement organisation-defined **security policy filters** and **privacy policy filters** as an **inline decision point** for the specific information flows covered (egress to external/cloud-adjacent services and cross-domain transfers between Secret and Confidential zones). The system must **allow transfers only when both security and privacy filters pass**, and must **quarantine/block** when filter processing fails or when content fails the rules.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-4(8) enhancement requires that organisation-defined security/privacy policy filters be used to address **data structures and content** (eg file type/size limits, structured field validation, prohibited content patterns, and PII detection/minimisation) and that these filters are applied to the **defined information flows** to support information flow control decisions.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define and version filter sets (policy-as-code)** approved by the CISO (DoD mixed-environment security and privacy policy filters), including:
   - **Security filters**: file type allow/deny lists, maximum file length/size, structured field range checks, and prohibited keyword/pattern rules.
   - **Privacy filters**: PII/identifier detection (HIPAA/GDPR identifiers and sensitive personal data patterns), and minimisation/redaction rules.
2. **Bind filter sets to the covered flows**:
   - Network egress from internal enclaves to external endpoints/cloud-adjacent services.
   - Cross-domain transfers between **Secret and Confidential** on-prem zones.
3. **Enforce “both pass” logic** at the inline gateway/inspection point:
   - Compute **security filter result** and **privacy filter result**.
   - Permit transfer only if **security=pass AND privacy=pass**.
4. **Fail-closed behaviour for filter processing failures**:
   - If the filter engine cannot process (timeouts, schema mismatch, parser errors, inspection service unavailable), treat as **failure** and apply the organisation-defined actions.
   - For **security filter failures**: **quarantine the data, deny the transfer, and notify the system owner and security operations within 1 hour**.
   - For **privacy filter failures**: **quarantine the data, block release, and notify the privacy officer and security operations within 1 hour**.
5. **Generate audit evidence** for each attempted transfer:
   - Record flow identifier, filter set version, security result, privacy result, decision (allow/deny/quarantine), and failure reason (if any).
   - Forward logs to SIEM for correlation with transfer events.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **CISO / Security Governance**: approves filter definitions and quarantine actions.
- **Security Operations / SOC**: receives time-bound notifications and monitors quarantine queues.
- **Application/Platform Owner**: ensures the covered flows traverse the inspection point and that fail-closed behaviour is enabled.
- **Platform/Integration Engineers**: implement gateway/inspection configuration, policy-as-code, and logging.
- **Privacy Officer**: receives time-bound privacy failure notifications.

## Acceptance criteria
- For each covered flow, transfers are **allowed only when both** security and privacy filters return **pass**.
- For security filter processing failures, the system **denies transfer**, **quarantines data**, and triggers **system owner + security operations notification within 1 hour**.
- For privacy filter processing failures, the system **blocks release**, **quarantines data**, and triggers **privacy officer + security operations notification within 1 hour**.
- Audit logs show, per transfer attempt: flow ID, filter set versions, security/privacy outcomes, decision, and failure reason (when applicable).
- All covered egress/cross-domain paths are routed through the inspection point (no bypass paths for the defined flows).

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Implement inline inspection at the defined egress and Secret↔Confidential transfer gateways so all covered flows traverse the filter decision point
- [ ] Create CISO-approved security and privacy filter sets (file type/size limits, structured field range checks, prohibited keyword patterns, and PII detection/minimisation) as versioned policy-as-code
- [ ] Configure enforcement logic to allow transfers only when security filter result is pass AND privacy filter result is pass
- [ ] Configure fail-closed behaviour for filter processing failures to quarantine and deny/block according to the organisation-defined security vs privacy actions
- [ ] Integrate time-bound notifications (within 1 hour) to system owner + security operations for security failures, and privacy officer + security operations for privacy failures
- [ ] Enable audit logging of flow ID, filter set versions, security/privacy outcomes, decision, and failure reasons; forward logs to SIEM for review

### ac-4.9 — Human Reviews (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure important information is never passed on or accessed on autopilot when the system is unsure. It matters because, without a human check, confidential or highly sensitive data could be shared with the wrong place or people, or moved between different environments, simply because automated rules could not reliably judge whether the request is allowed, or because specific legal and contractual obligations (such as those covering export controls, certain compliance frameworks, or health and privacy laws) demand extra oversight. To meet this expectation, the organisation must clearly identify which data movements and access to confidential or secret datasets involve cross-domain transfers, external sharing, or such sensitive access, and document the exact situations that trigger a human review, including when automated decisions are unreliable and when regulations require it. It must route or pause those actions for review, record what was decided and why for each case, keep those records for later checks, assign named staff to perform the reviews, and ensure the system and its procedures, plans, and audit logs show that the review happens as required.

#### Human review gate for uncertain Confidential/Secret data flows

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
