Implement an enforcement mechanism that **pauses or routes** information-flow requests for **human review** whenever the request matches the organisation-defined **information flows** and **conditions**.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4(9)** (Enhancement) requires the organisation to **enforce human reviews** for the specified **information flows** (ac-04.09_odp.01) under the specified **conditions** (ac-04.09_odp.02). The intent is to prevent sensitive cross-domain/external sharing or Confidential/Secret access from proceeding when automated policy enforcement cannot reliably determine authorisation, or when regulations require additional oversight.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the in-scope flows and triggers in the enforcement layer**
   - Create a machine-readable policy set that maps:
     - **information flows**: cross-domain transfers, external sharing, and access to **Confidential/Secret** datasets (ac-04.09_odp.01)
     - **conditions**: (a) automated policy enforcement cannot reliably determine authorisation for Confidential/Secret data flows, or (b) required by **ITAR/EAR/CMMC-L2/L3** or **HIPAA/GDPR** (ac-04.09_odp.02).
   - Include an explicit “**automation confidence below threshold / unknown authorisation**” trigger (e.g., missing destination classification, destination not in allowlist, incomplete policy context).

2. **Enforce a hard approval gate at the point of decision**
   - Integrate the application’s authorisation decision path with an **information-flow control point** (e.g., API gateway / policy enforcement service / DLP/CASB enforcement integration) such that:
     - when triggers occur, the system **blocks or pauses** the transfer/access;
     - only a **human approval decision** can release the action.
   - Ensure the approval is not merely recorded—**it must be required for the action to proceed**.

3. **Implement a human review workflow that captures auditable decision data**
   - When a trigger fires, generate a review record containing at minimum:
     - flow identifier (source, destination, dataset label/classification)
     - trigger condition (which rule fired: automation uncertainty vs regulatory requirement)
     - requester identity and role
     - proposed action (allow/deny)
     - reviewer identity
     - reviewer decision and justification
     - timestamp and outcome.

4. **Restrict reviewer authorisation and require named accountability**
   - Configure the workflow so only **authorised, named reviewers** (from defined roles) can approve/deny.
   - Require strong authentication for reviewers (e.g., phishing-resistant MFA) and ensure approvals are tied to the reviewer identity.

5. **Log and retain evidence for auditability**
   - Export immutable audit logs to the enterprise SIEM/logging platform.
   - Ensure logs link the approval decision to the original flow request and the specific trigger condition.

6. **Operationalise and test the enforcement**
   - Add automated tests and runbooks to validate that:
     - uncertain authorisation for Confidential/Secret flows always results in a human review gate;
     - regulatory-triggered cases (ITAR/EAR/CMMC-L2/L3, HIPAA/GDPR) always require review even if automation could otherwise decide.

## WHO
- **Application owner / security architect**: defines the policy rules for ac-04.09_odp.01 and ac-04.09_odp.02 and approves the enforcement design.
- **Security engineering / IAM team**: implements the policy enforcement integration, reviewer access restrictions, and audit logging.
- **Compliance/legal (ITAR/EAR/CMMC-L2/L3, HIPAA/GDPR)**: validates the regulatory trigger mappings.
- **Designated human reviewers** (security/privacy/export-control/regulated-data stewards): perform approvals/denials and provide justification.

## Acceptance criteria
- For any request matching **ac-04.09_odp.01** (cross-domain/external sharing or Confidential/Secret dataset access) where **ac-04.09_odp.02** conditions apply, the system **does not complete the transfer/access** until a **human reviewer** approves.
- Every gated event produces an auditable record containing: flow details, trigger condition, requester identity, reviewer identity, decision, justification, and timestamps.
- Reviewer approvals are enforced by the system (no bypass path) and are restricted to authorised named reviewers.
- Audit logs are exported to SIEM and can be used to reconstruct the decision chain for sampled cases.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Create a data-flow catalogue identifying cross-domain transfers, external sharing, and Confidential/Secret dataset access paths
- [ ] Encode ac-04.09_odp.01 flows and ac-04.09_odp.02 triggers (automation uncertainty threshold and regulatory-required cases) into policy-as-code
- [ ] Integrate the application/API/data-transfer decision point with an enforcement gate that blocks/pauses on trigger and requires human approval to proceed
- [ ] Implement a human review workflow that records flow ID, trigger condition, requester identity, reviewer identity, decision, justification, and timestamps
- [ ] Restrict reviewer approvals to authorised named roles and require strong authentication for reviewers
- [ ] Configure immutable audit logging and SIEM export linking each approval decision to the original flow request and trigger condition
- [ ] Run test cases proving that uncertain authorisation and regulatory-triggered Confidential/Secret flows always invoke the human review gate

### ac-4.10 — Enable and Disable Security or Privacy Policy Filters (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure only trusted, highly authorised people can switch key privacy and security checks on or off for particular transfers of sensitive information. Without this, someone could accidentally or deliberately turn off protections such as blocking harmful software or suspicious indicators, limiting what websites can be accessed, or applying rules that prevent sensitive data from leaving approved boundaries, and personal data could be sent without the right consent or redaction safeguards. To meet this expectation, the organisation must define exactly which filters can be toggled, restrict the ability to do so to privileged administrators, and only allow changes when the data transfer is approved for Confidential or Secret information within the authorised security areas and the change is within the system’s permitted scope. For privacy consent or redaction, the destination must require privacy controls and the administrator must have an approved privacy impact assessment. The organisation must also keep auditable records of what was changed, for which data flow, and maintain clear procedures and plans describing how these decisions are governed.

#### Privileged, policy-gated enable/disable of security & privacy filters

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement a capability for privileged administrators to enable and disable **authorised security and privacy policy filters** for **specific data flows**, but only when the defined **conditions** are met.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4(10)** enhancement requires that the system provides the capability to enable/disable **{{ac-4.10_prm_1}}** only under **{{ac-4.10_prm_2}}**. The guidance further expects administrators can select filters executed on a specific data flow based on **data type**, **source/destination security domains**, and other security/privacy relevant features.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define an authoritative toggle catalogue and mapping
- Create a configuration/policy catalogue of toggleable filters matching **{{ac-4.10_prm_1}}** (e.g., **DLP**, **web content filtering**, **malware/IOC blocking**, **privacy consent/redaction**).
- For each filter, define which **data types** and **data-flow attributes** it may apply to.

### 2) Enforce enable/disable only for approved data flows and authorised domains
- Implement a **security-domain matrix** that enumerates allowed **source/destination security domains** and permitted **data classifications**.
- Gate all enable/disable actions so they are permitted only when:
  - the data flow is approved in the security domain matrix, and
  - the change is within the system authorisation scope, and
  - the data flow involves **Confidential or Secret** data (per **{{ac-4.10_prm_2}}**).

### 3) Add privacy-specific conditions (personal data)
- For **privacy filters** (privacy consent/redaction), allow enable/disable only when:
  - the destination requires privacy controls for personal data, and
  - the administrator provides/attests an **approved privacy impact assessment** reference.

### 4) Restrict the capability to privileged administrators
- Integrate with enterprise IAM so only roles explicitly designated as **privileged administrators** can submit enable/disable requests.
- Ensure the enforcement points (DLP/CASB/web gateway/email/API gateway, etc.) accept changes only from tightly scoped service identities.

### 5) Apply changes per data flow with auditable, tamper-evident records
- Implement the enable/disable operation as a controlled workflow that:
  - requires the administrator to specify the **data flow identifier** (or equivalent: source/destination, data type, and domains),
  - validates the request against the security-domain matrix and system authorisation scope,
  - validates privacy-impact assessment presence for privacy filters,
  - applies the filter selection to the relevant enforcement point(s), and
  - records an audit event containing: admin identity, data-flow identifier, requested filters, enabled/disabled state (before/after), approval references (system authorisation and privacy impact assessment where applicable), timestamp, and target domains.

### 6) Provide operational guardrails
- Maintain separate configuration for production vs test/dev.
- Include a documented procedure for how administrators request and approve filter toggles, including how the system behaves when validation fails (deny-by-default).

## WHO
- **Security Architect / Compliance Lead**: defines toggle catalogue, security-domain matrix, and conditions.
- **IAM Engineer**: implements privileged-admin role mapping and least-privilege authorisation.
- **Platform/Integration Engineer**: implements the policy-gated workflow and enforcement-point integrations.
- **Security Operations / Privacy Officer**: maintains privacy impact assessment approval references and reviews audit outputs.

## Acceptance criteria
- Only privileged administrators can initiate enable/disable requests (all other attempts are denied and logged).
- Any enable/disable action for security filters is rejected unless the data flow is approved in the security-domain matrix, the data classification is **Confidential/Secret**, and the change is within system authorisation scope.
- Any enable/disable action for privacy filters is rejected unless the destination requires privacy controls for personal data and an **approved privacy impact assessment** reference is provided/validated.
- The system applies the selected filters to the specified data flow (not globally) and produces tamper-evident audit records with before/after state and approval references.
- Validation is deny-by-default and demonstrably prevents toggling outside the defined conditions.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define the toggle catalogue for security and privacy filters matching ac-4.10_prm_1 and map each filter to allowable data-flow attributes
- [ ] Implement a security-domain matrix and enforce that filter toggles are permitted only for approved flows involving Confidential/Secret data and within system authorisation scope (ac-4.10_prm_2)
- [ ] Integrate IAM so only privileged administrators can submit enable/disable requests and only least-privilege service identities can apply changes to enforcement points
- [ ] Add privacy-filter gating that requires validation of an approved privacy impact assessment when the destination requires privacy controls for personal data (ac-04.10_odp.04)
- [ ] Implement per-data-flow application of selected filters based on source/destination domains and data type, with deny-by-default validation
- [ ] Configure tamper-evident audit logging for every enable/disable event including admin identity, data-flow identifier, filters toggled, before/after state, and approval references

### ac-4.11 — Configuration of Security or Privacy Policy Filters (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about letting only trusted, senior administrators set up and fine-tune the rules that scan information for harmful or sensitive content, separately for each system and type of data. Without this, the organisation could miss dangerous material such as malware, exploit attempts, or other prohibited content, or it could accidentally expose personal or health information by failing to block the right terms while still allowing approved exceptions. To meet this expectation, the organisation must provide a way for privileged administrators to configure security and privacy filter sets using word lists, pattern-matching rules, and allow/deny lists, with security filters covering malware, exploit, and prohibited-content terms aligned to the organisation’s DoD and CMMC expectations, and privacy filters blocking PII and PHI terms aligned to GDPR and HIPAA, including clearly defined exceptions. The organisation must keep clear configuration documentation, maintain an up-to-date list of the configured filters, restrict editing to privileged roles, and record who changed what and when in audit records.

#### Privileged configuration of versioned security/privacy filter sets

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement a capability for privileged administrators to configure **security or privacy policy filters** as **configurable filter sets** (wordlists, regex rules, and allow/deny lists) that can be selected and applied **per system and per data classification**.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4(11)** (enhancement) requires: “Provide the capability for privileged administrators to configure {{ insert: param, ac-4.11_prm_1 }} to support different security or privacy policies.” The guidance further expects administrators can configure filters (e.g., inappropriate words) and that the organisation maintains documentation and configuration detail.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create a filter-set catalogue** (separate namespaces for **security policy filters** and **privacy policy filters**) where each filter set is keyed by **system identifier** and **data classification**.
2. **Define a structured filter-set schema** for `ac-4.11_prm_1` that supports:
   - wordlists
   - regex rules
   - allow/deny lists
   - exception/allow conditions (for privacy filters)
   - rule metadata (owner, intended policy, effective dates, version)
3. **Implement a privileged admin configuration workflow**:
   - Only privileged roles can create/edit filter sets.
   - Changes must go through a controlled workflow (e.g., approval + peer review) and produce a versioned artefact.
4. **Add automated validation and safety checks** before deployment:
   - regex linting and catastrophic backtracking checks
   - size/complexity limits
   - validation against a curated set of test vectors (expected matches/non-matches) for:
     - security filters (malware/exploit/prohibited-content keyword/regex)
     - privacy filters (PII/PHI blocklists with GDPR/HIPAA-specific terms and exceptions)
5. **Deploy filter sets to enforcement points** using a standard mechanism (e.g., signed configuration bundle, API-driven config, or configuration package) and record the target **system + data classification + filter-set version**.
6. **Maintain documentation and an inventory**:
   - publish administrator-facing documentation describing how to configure filters, including examples aligned to the organisation’s definitions for security and privacy filters
   - maintain an inventory/list of configured security and privacy filter sets with version identifiers and scope (system/classification).
7. **Ensure auditability**:
   - log who changed which filter set, what changed (diff/version), when, and the deployment outcome (success/failure) to support audit evidence.

## WHO
- **Security/Privacy Policy Administrator (privileged)**: authors and approves filter-set changes.
- **Application/Platform Configuration Owner**: ensures filter-set schema and deployment integration are correctly wired to enforcement points.
- **Security Engineering/Compliance**: maintains validation test vectors and reviews exceptions/allow conditions.
- **SIEM/Logging Administrator**: ensures audit logs are retained and searchable.

## Acceptance criteria
- Privileged administrators can configure **security policy filters** and **privacy policy filters** as structured filter sets (wordlists/regex/allow-deny) and select them **per system and data classification**.
- Security filter sets include configurable rules for **malware, exploit, and prohibited content** aligned to the organisation’s DoD/CMMC expectations.
- Privacy filter sets include configurable **PII/PHI** blocklists aligned to **GDPR/HIPAA-specific terms**, with clearly represented **exceptions/allow conditions**.
- Every filter-set change is versioned, validated (including regex safety checks), and produces auditable evidence: **who/what/when/where/version/outcome**.
- Administrator documentation and an inventory/list of configured filter sets exist and match the deployed versions.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define a versioned filter-set schema supporting wordlists, regex rules, and allow/deny lists keyed by system and data classification
- [ ] Implement a privileged admin workflow that restricts edit access to authorised roles and requires approval for filter-set changes
- [ ] Create automated validation for regex safety and curated test vectors for malware/exploit/prohibited-content and PII/PHI with exceptions
- [ ] Build a deployment mechanism that applies the selected filter-set version to the correct enforcement points per system/classification
- [ ] Enable audit logging capturing admin identity, filter-set version, scope (system/classification), change details, and deployment outcome
- [ ] Publish administrator documentation and maintain an inventory of configured security and privacy filter sets with version identifiers

### ac-4.12 — Data Type Identifiers (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure that when information moves between different parts of the organisation, the receiving system can tell what kind of data it really is by checking the data’s actual identifying markers and then confirming the content matches an approved format. Without this, a file could be disguised to look legitimate (for example by using the right name or file type) while containing the wrong structure or meaning, leading to incorrect decisions, fraud, or sensitive information being passed on when it should not be. To meet this expectation, the organisation must keep a list of approved data formats and the matching allowed identifiers (such as file signatures and any required tokens), and ensure systems use those identifiers rather than filenames or file counts alone. The system must then check both the structure and the meaning of the data against the approved specification, allow only compliant transfers, block non-compliant ones, and record the outcome in audit records so it’s clear what was checked and why a transfer was accepted or rejected.

#### Data type identifier validation at cross-domain transfer boundaries

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement a cross-domain “data type validation gate” that identifies the data type using **approved data type identifiers** (e.g., **file signatures/magic bytes** and/or **token-based identifiers**) and then permits the transfer only after **syntactic and semantic validation** against the organisation’s **approved data format specifications**.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4(12)** enhancement requires that when transferring information between different security domains, the system uses the organisation-defined parameter **ac-04.12_odp (data type identifiers)** to validate data essential for information flow decisions. The guidance further requires that identification/validation is based on **defined specifications associated with each allowed data format**, and that **filenames and number alone are not used**; content must be validated **syntactically and semantically**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define approved data format specifications** for each allowed cross-domain data type (e.g., schema/structure rules for syntactic validation, plus semantic/business rules for semantic validation). Version each specification.
2. **Create and maintain an identifier-to-spec registry** mapping each approved data type to the allowed **data type identifiers** (file signature patterns and/or token identifiers). Support **multiple internal signatures/tokens** per data type where required by the specification.
3. **Implement a validation gate at each transfer boundary** (e.g., API gateway, message broker interceptor, file transfer service, or service-to-service middleware). On each transfer attempt:
   - Extract/compute **file signature(s)** from the payload (not extension/filename) and/or verify **token-based identifiers**.
   - Map the observed identifiers to the correct approved data format specification.
   - Run **syntactic validation** (schema/required fields/format constraints) and **semantic validation** (meaning/content consistency and cross-field rules) against that specification.
   - **Fail closed**: reject the transfer if identifier mapping fails or either validation fails.
4. **Produce audit evidence** for every attempt, including: source/destination domain, data type identifier(s) observed, mapped specification version, syntactic validation result, semantic validation result, and a reason code for accept/reject.
5. **Operationalise governance**: manage the registry and validation rules via controlled change (review, versioning, testing in non-prod, and promotion to prod) so that only approved identifiers/specifications are used.

## WHO
- **Application/Platform Engineering Lead**: implements the validation gate and integrates it into transfer paths.
- **Data/Domain Owner (with Security)**: defines and approves data format specifications and semantic rules.
- **Security Engineering/Compliance**: ensures audit logging, evidence retention, and controlled change processes.

## Acceptance criteria
- For every cross-domain transfer, the system determines data type using **file signature(s) and/or token-based identifiers** (not filename/extension alone).
- Transfers are **rejected** when identifier mapping fails or when syntactic or semantic validation fails.
- The gate supports **multiple internal signatures/tokens** for a given allowed data type where specified.
- Audit logs show pass/fail outcomes tied to the **mapped specification** and include reasons suitable for audit review.
- Registry/specification changes are versioned and deployed through the organisation’s change management process.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define and approve versioned data format specifications for each allowed cross-domain data type, including syntactic and semantic rules
- [ ] Build an identifier-to-spec registry mapping approved file signature patterns and/or token identifiers to the correct specification version
- [ ] Implement a fail-closed validation gate in each cross-domain transfer path to extract/verify identifiers and run syntactic and semantic validation
- [ ] Configure the gate to reject transfers when identifier mapping fails or when syntactic/semantic checks do not pass
- [ ] Enable audit logging for every transfer attempt with identifier observations, mapped specification version, validation results, and reject/accept reasons
- [ ] Establish controlled change management for the registry and validation rules (review, test, and promotion across environments)

### ac-4.13 — Decomposition into Policy-relevant Subcomponents (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This expectation is about making sure that, before any information is moved from one protected area of your organisation to another, it is first broken down into clear, decision-ready details about where it is coming from, where it is going, how sensitive it is, what kind of information it is, what special handling it needs, what attachments it includes, and what security label or certificate it carries. Without this, the wrong rules may be applied, allowing sensitive material to be sent to the wrong place, mishandled, or released without the right checks, which can lead to data leaks, regulatory breaches, and reputational damage. To meet this expectation, your organisation must determine and record the source and destination areas, the classification (Secret or Confidential), the data type, handling requirements, attachments metadata, and certificate or label information, package these as a single set of transfer details, and submit them to the organisation’s information-movement decision process before the transfer is allowed to complete. You must also document the approach, configure the decision process to use these details for filtering, checking, and possible sanitisation, assign named responsibilities, and keep records showing the decisions made for each transfer.

#### Cross-domain transfer gateway with policy-relevant transfer context

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement a cross-domain information transfer mechanism that requires a structured **transfer context** (the policy-relevant subcomponents) to be submitted **before** the payload is released to a different security domain.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-4(13) (enhancement) requires that, when transferring information between different security domains, the organisation **decomposes information into policy-relevant subcomponents** ({{ insert: param, ac-04.13_odp }}) for **submission to policy enforcement mechanisms**. The policy enforcement mechanisms then use those subcomponents for **filtering, inspection, and/or sanitisation** to enable flow enforcement prior to transfer.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define enforceable policy-relevant subcomponents** for the application’s transfer context, aligned to `ac-04.13_odp` (source domain, destination domain, classification Secret/Confidential, data type, handling requirements, attachments metadata, certificate/label information).
2. **Create a transfer context schema** (e.g., JSON) that the transfer gateway/API requires alongside the payload. Include fields for:
   - `sourceDomain`, `destinationDomain`
   - `classification` (Secret/Confidential)
   - `dataType`
   - `handlingRequirements`
   - `attachmentsMetadata` (filename/extension/MIME/type/count/size where available)
   - `certificateLabelInfo` (certificate reference or label ID from the client/session)
3. **Integrate a policy decision point (PDP)** (policy engine) that accepts the transfer context and returns an enforcement action (e.g., allow, block, or sanitise/inspect-then-allow). The PDP must be configured to use the subcomponents for filtering/inspection/sanitisation decisions.
4. **Enforce pre-transfer gating** in the gateway: the gateway must pause/hold the transfer until the PDP decision is received, and must record the decision and the submitted transfer context for auditability.
5. **Prevent bypass paths** for the application’s supported transfer channels (e.g., ensure the application’s email/file/portal download and export functions route through the gateway; disable or restrict direct cross-domain copy paths where feasible).
6. **Operationalise evidence**: log (at minimum) the transfer context fields, PDP decision, enforcement outcome (including sanitisation actions if taken), and correlation identifiers (request ID, user, source/destination).

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Application owner / security architect**: defines the transfer context model and enforcement outcomes.
- **Platform/integration engineer**: implements the gateway/API and PDP integration.
- **Security operations / compliance**: validates policy rules and reviews audit evidence.

## Acceptance criteria
- For every cross-domain transfer initiated through the application’s supported channels, the gateway submits a complete transfer context containing all `ac-04.13_odp` subcomponents to the policy enforcement mechanism **before** payload release.
- The policy enforcement mechanism demonstrably uses those subcomponents to make decisions (allow/block/sanitise) and the gateway enforces the decision.
- Audit logs contain the submitted transfer context, PDP decision, and enforcement outcome for sampled transfers across Secret and Confidential data types.
- Attempts to transfer without a valid/complete transfer context are blocked (or routed to a remediation flow) and do not result in payload release to the destination domain.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define the policy-relevant transfer context fields exactly as per ac-04.13_odp (source domain, destination domain, Secret/Confidential classification, data type, handling requirements, attachments metadata, certificate/label information).
- [ ] Implement a transfer gateway/API endpoint that requires the transfer context schema alongside each payload submission.
- [ ] Integrate the gateway with a policy decision point that evaluates the transfer context and returns allow/block/sanitise-then-allow decisions.
- [ ] Enforce pre-transfer gating so the payload is released only after the policy decision is received and recorded.
- [ ] Configure audit logging to capture the submitted transfer context, PDP decision, enforcement outcome, and correlation identifiers for each transfer.
- [ ] Route all application-supported cross-domain export/email/download functions through the gateway and restrict or disable direct bypass transfer paths where feasible.

### ac-4.14 — Security or Privacy Policy Filter Constraints (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This expectation is about making sure any information passed between different parts of your organisation follows a strict, pre-approved message format, rather than a free-for-all. If you allow flexible or loosely structured messages, attackers or careless systems could slip in unexpected data, hidden instructions, or malformed content that bypasses checks, leading to unauthorised actions, privacy breaches, or corrupted records. To meet this requirement, the organisation must define and document the approved cross-domain message rules and ensure the receiving systems only accept messages that exactly match the fixed structure (no extra or unknown fields), are written using UTF-8, are no larger than 10KB per message, and contain only letters and numbers with no special characters. Any message that fails any of these checks must be rejected, and the organisation must keep a documented list of the filters used, record acceptance or rejection in audit logs, and include these constraints in both the system security plan and the privacy plan.

#### Enforce enumerated UTF-8/10KB/alpha-numeric policy filters at boundaries

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement security and privacy policy filters that enforce **fully enumerated, fixed-format cross-domain transfer payloads**. The filters must restrict **data structure and content** by enforcing: **fixed schema/field set (no free-form fields)**, **UTF-8 encoding**, **maximum 10KB payload size**, and **alpha-numeric only (no special characters)**. Any transfer that fails validation must be rejected.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4(14)** enhancement requires that, when transferring information between different security domains, the organisation implements **{{ insert: param, ac-4.14_prm_1 }}** requiring **fully enumerated formats** that restrict **data structure and content**. The guidance explicitly calls out restricting **file sizes/field lengths**, **character encoding**, **alpha-numeric-only content**, **prohibiting special characters**, and **validating schema structures**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Centralise enforcement at every cross-domain boundary** (e.g., API gateway and/or message broker ingress/egress) so all security-domain transfers pass through a single validation point.
2. **Create two version-controlled schema/policy sets** in a policy repository:
   - **Security policy filters**: XML/JSON schema with **fixed tags** and **no free-form fields**.
   - **Privacy policy filters**: JSON schema with a **fixed field set** and **no free-form fields**.
3. Implement deterministic validation logic that, for each message:
   - Validates **UTF-8** decoding (reject invalid encodings).
   - Enforces **max 10KB** on the canonical payload bytes (reject larger payloads).
   - Validates **schema structure** (reject unknown/missing fields; reject any deviation from the fixed schema).
   - Enforces **alpha-numeric only** across all character fields and **prohibits special characters** (define and implement the exact character allow-list; reject anything outside it).
4. **Hard-reject** non-conforming transfers at the boundary with consistent error codes (do not forward to downstream systems).
5. **Audit every decision** (accept/reject) with structured logs that include: rule set ID/schema version, boundary component identifier, payload size, and the specific failing constraint (without echoing sensitive payload content).
6. Update the **system security plan** and **privacy plan** to explicitly reference the boundary enforcement component, the schema/policy artefacts, and the enforcement behaviour.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Solution/Platform Engineer**: implement gateway/broker validation and schema/policy enforcement.
- **Security Architect / Compliance Lead**: define the enumerated filter rules, confirm “special characters” definition, and ensure documentation updates.
- **App/API Owner**: ensure all cross-domain integrations route through the boundary enforcement point.

## Acceptance criteria
- For both security and privacy transfers, any payload that violates **schema structure**, **UTF-8**, **>10KB**, or **alpha-numeric-only/no special characters** is **rejected** at the boundary and not delivered downstream.
- Validation is based on **fully enumerated fixed schemas** (no free-form fields; unknown fields rejected).
- Boundary logs show **accept/reject** outcomes with rule set ID/schema version and failing constraint reason.
- System security plan and privacy plan reference the enforcement mechanism and the specific policy artefacts (schema/policy versions).

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Identify and route all cross-domain transfer paths through a single boundary enforcement component (API gateway and/or message broker).
- [ ] Define and version-control the security XML/JSON schema with fixed tags and no free-form fields, including the alpha-numeric/no-special-characters character allow-list.
- [ ] Define and version-control the privacy JSON schema with a fixed field set and no free-form fields, including the same encoding and character constraints.
- [ ] Implement boundary validation to enforce UTF-8 decoding, reject payloads >10KB, validate schema structure, and enforce alpha-numeric-only content with special characters prohibited.
- [ ] Configure hard-deny behaviour so non-conforming messages are rejected and never forwarded downstream.
- [ ] Enable structured audit logging for every accept/reject decision with schema version/rule set ID and failing constraint reason (without sensitive payload content).
- [ ] Update the system security plan and privacy plan to reference the boundary enforcement component and the specific schema/policy versions used.

### ac-4.15 — Detection of Unsanctioned Information (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This expectation is about stopping dangerous or private information from being passed from one part of the organisation to another. When information moves between different security areas, the organisation must examine what is being sent for three things: malicious code, data that is not approved to leave the source area, and executable content that could disrupt or harm the systems at the destination. If any of these organisation-defined unsanctioned items are detected, the transfer must be blocked so the receiving area never gets the item. This matters because, without such checks, harmful software could be introduced, confidential or restricted information could be exposed to the wrong place, or the destination systems could be damaged or disrupted by run-capable files. To meet this expectation, the organisation must follow its Cross-Domain Security Transfer Policy and Data Transfer Privacy Policy, using its defined rules and procedures to detect these items during cross-area transfers and prevent the transfer when they are found.

#### Policy-driven inspection to block unsanctioned cross-domain transfers

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement a cross-domain transfer inspection capability that examines transferred information for organisation-defined **unsanctioned information** (malicious code, unauthorised release data, and harmful executable content) and **prohibits** the transfer when such content is detected, in accordance with the organisation-defined cross-domain security transfer and data transfer privacy policies.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4(15)** requires that, when transferring information between different security domains, the organisation **examines the information for the presence of the defined unsanctioned information** and **prohibits the transfer** in accordance with the organisation-defined security/privacy policy.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Identify all cross-domain transfer paths** for the application (e.g., web uploads/downloads, API payloads, file transfer, messaging/SaaS connectors, batch exports) and ensure each path is routed through a single inspection enforcement point (gateway/proxy/connector).
2. **Define machine-actionable detection rules** for the organisation-defined unsanctioned categories:
   - **Malicious code**: malware signatures plus archive/embedded content scanning and (where feasible) sandbox/detonation for high-risk artefacts.
   - **Unauthorised release data**: enforce data classification/entitlement checks (e.g., document labels, data tags, DLP-style detectors) against the source→destination release rules.
   - **Harmful executable content**: block run-capable formats and embedded scripts/macros (e.g., script-bearing documents, executables, and other content indicators that could disrupt destination services).
3. **Enforce “deny/prohibit” before delivery**: when any rule matches, the inspection point must reject/quarantine the transfer so the destination domain never receives the payload.
4. **Bind enforcement to the organisation-defined policy** (policy-as-code): map each detection category to the relevant policy rationale (cross-domain security transfer policy and/or data transfer privacy policy) and record the specific rule/policy reference used.
5. **Generate audit evidence** for each blocked transfer: include transfer/session identifier, source and destination domains, payload hash/fingerprint, detected unsanctioned category, triggered rule name/version, and the enforcement decision (blocked/quarantined) with timestamp.
6. **Fail-safe behaviour**: ensure the inspection enforcement point is configured to **fail closed** (or otherwise prevent delivery) when inspection cannot be performed for reasons such as policy retrieval failure, scanner unavailability, or connector errors.

## WHO
- **Application owner / security architect**: owns the cross-domain transfer inventory, policy mapping, and acceptance criteria.
- **Platform/network security team** (or cloud security engineering): implements the inspection enforcement point and routing.
- **Security engineering / SOC**: validates detection quality, tuning, and audit evidence.
- **Change management**: governs policy-as-code changes and releases.

## Acceptance criteria
- All identified cross-domain transfer mechanisms for the application are routed through the inspection enforcement point.
- For test payloads containing (a) malicious code, (b) unauthorised release data, or (c) harmful executable content, the transfer is **prohibited** and the destination domain does not receive the payload.
- Each blocked event produces audit evidence showing the triggered unsanctioned category and the specific organisation-defined policy/rule reference.
- The enforcement point is configured to prevent delivery when inspection/policy enforcement is unavailable (fail-safe/fail-closed).

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Inventory and document every cross-domain information transfer path for the application (source domain → destination domain).
- [ ] Configure routing so all cross-domain transfers traverse a single inspection enforcement point (gateway/proxy/connector).
- [ ] Translate ac-04.15_odp.01 unsanctioned information into machine-actionable detection rules (malware, unauthorised release data, harmful executable content).
- [ ] Implement policy-as-code enforcement that blocks/quarantines transfers when any unsanctioned category is detected, per ac-4.15_prm_2.
- [ ] Enable audit logging for each transfer decision with payload fingerprint, triggered rule/policy reference, and source/destination domains.
- [ ] Test fail-safe behaviour by simulating scanner/policy unavailability and verify the system prevents delivery (fails closed or equivalent).

### ac-4.17 — Domain Authentication (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure that, for every piece of information your systems send and receive, the organisation can clearly and reliably say who sent it and who received it. Without this, it becomes easy for wrongdoing, mistakes, or policy breaches to be hidden behind missing or unclear identities, making investigations harder and leaving personal information journeys impossible to verify or correct. To meet this expectation, your organisation must set up a clear way to label and validate the sending and receiving points for information transfers, using distinct identifiers for the organisation, a system, an application, a service, and an individual, and ensure these labels are different and unambiguous. The system must apply this validation as part of the transfer, record both the authenticated sender and receiver details in audit records for later tracing, and keep the necessary procedures and documentation up to date, including how staff should respond if identification cannot be confirmed.

#### Domain-authenticate source/destination with authenticated labels

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement domain authentication for every information transfer by uniquely identifying and cryptographically authenticating both the **source (sender)** and **destination (receiver)** domain points, using organisation-defined domain types: **organisation, system, application, service, individual**.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4(17)** requires that source and destination points for information transfer are **uniquely identified and authenticated** using the organisation-defined parameter **{{ insert: param, ac-04.17_odp }}** (i.e., `ac-04.17_odp`). The guidance emphasises that attribution depends on system labels distinguishing among systems, organisations, and individuals, enabling forensic reconstruction, policy compliance attribution, and personal data lineage.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define a domain label taxonomy and registry** aligned to the required domain types (organisation/system/application/service/individual). Ensure each label is unique, unambiguous, and has an owner and lifecycle (creation, change, deprecation).
2. **Bind labels to authenticated identities at runtime**:
   - For **individual**: derive the `ind:` domain label from the enterprise IdP authenticated principal (session token claims), ensuring the individual is authenticated (not just presented).
   - For **system/application/service**: derive `sys:/app:/svc:` labels from the authenticated caller/receiver identity (e.g., workload identity, signed service assertions, or mTLS identity), ensuring the endpoint identity is cryptographically verifiable.
   - For **destination**: derive destination domain labels from the target endpoint/service identity (not from client-supplied free text).
3. **Enforce domain authentication at the transfer boundary** (the API/messaging boundary where transfers occur):
   - Reject transfers when either source or destination domain labels are missing, invalid, or not cryptographically bound to the authenticated session/caller.
   - Ensure the application does not accept unauthenticated or mismatched domain labels.
4. **Propagate and record authenticated endpoints in audit logs**:
   - For every transfer event, write audit fields for both authenticated **source** and **destination** domain labels (organisation/system/application/service/individual where applicable).
   - Ensure logs are tamper-evident/immutable per existing enterprise logging standards and are forwarded to the SIEM.
5. **Document failure handling and operational procedures**:
   - Define what happens when domain authentication fails (e.g., deny-by-default, error codes, incident logging, and escalation).
   - Maintain runbooks for label registry changes and identity binding changes.

## WHO (roles responsible)
- **Application Security Engineer / Architect**: implement label taxonomy, boundary enforcement, and audit schema.
- **Identity & Access Management (IAM) Lead**: confirm IdP/workload identity claims and authentication assurance.
- **Platform/Integration Engineer**: implement service-to-service identity (mTLS/workload identity) and token validation.
- **SOC/Monitoring Lead**: validate audit fields, SIEM ingestion, and forensic reconstruction queries.
- **Data Protection/Privacy Officer (as applicable)**: confirm personal data lineage and consent/request traceability implications.

## Acceptance criteria
- For 100% of production transfer events, audit records contain **authenticated** source and destination domain labels covering the required domain types (as applicable to the transfer).
- Transfers with missing/invalid/unbound domain labels are **rejected** (deny-by-default) and generate an audit/alert record.
- Domain labels are derived from cryptographically verifiable authentication context (IdP claims and/or workload/service identity), not from client-supplied values.
- SIEM queries can reconstruct end-to-end source→destination attribution for a sample of transfer events.
- Operational runbook exists for domain label registry management and domain authentication failure handling.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Create a domain label taxonomy and controlled registry for organisation/system/application/service/individual labels
- [ ] Configure the application to derive source and destination domain labels from cryptographically verifiable authenticated context (IdP claims and workload/service identity)
- [ ] Implement deny-by-default boundary enforcement to reject transfers with missing/invalid/unbound domain labels
- [ ] Extend audit logging to record authenticated source and destination domain labels for every transfer event and forward to SIEM
- [ ] Implement automated validation tests to confirm labels are bound to authentication context and not client-supplied
- [ ] Publish runbooks for label registry lifecycle management and domain authentication failure handling (including escalation)

### ac-4.19 — Validation of Metadata (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This expectation is about making sure that when information moves between different parts of the organisation that have different rules, the extra details that travel with it are checked and cleaned up before delivery. Without this, sensitive information could be labelled incorrectly, released to the wrong place, or include personal details that should have been limited or removed, creating legal and reputational risk. To meet this expectation, the organisation must define and apply metadata checks during every cross-domain transfer so that the metadata follows the required Department of Defense handling rules, including the correct classification, what can be shared, the system of record, and the required security markings, and so that the metadata is consistent with the classification of the main information it describes. It must also apply privacy protections to the metadata by limiting personal data to what is strictly necessary, ensuring the required lawful basis information is present, and redacting personal-data fields. The organisation must document how this works, configure the system to enforce it, and keep audit records showing what was validated, filtered, corrected, or blocked for each transfer.

#### Metadata validation and filtering on cross-domain transfers

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement metadata validation filters that run automatically whenever information is transferred between different security domains, ensuring metadata is checked, corrected, or blocked according to the organisation-defined security/privacy policy filters (including DoD domain-transfer rules) and that metadata handling is correctly bound to the payload’s classification.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 AC-4(19) (Enhancement) requires: “When transferring information between different security domains, implement {{ insert: param, ac-4.19_prm_1 }} on metadata.” The guidance clarifies that metadata and the data to which it applies may be treated as part of the payload or separately; therefore the implementation must enforce consistent filtering/inspection for both metadata and its bound payload classification.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the metadata validation filter set** per `ac-4.19_prm_1`, including the required DoD metadata domain-transfer rules: classification, releasability, system of record, and required security markings (use `ac-04.19_odp.01`). Include privacy filters such as HIPAA minimum necessary, GDPR lawful-basis tags, and redaction of personal data fields in metadata (use `ac-04.19_odp.02`).
2. **Create an authoritative metadata schema and mapping** for the application’s transfer formats (e.g., message headers, JSON fields, document metadata, database record attributes). Mark which fields are personal data and which fields carry security markings/classification.
3. **Implement a cross-domain transfer enforcement point** (e.g., API gateway, managed file transfer service, message-broker egress component, or application-level transfer service) that intercepts every approved cross-domain transfer path and performs metadata validation before release.
4. **Bind metadata validation to payload classification**: derive the effective payload classification from the payload label/trusted classification header and validate that metadata claims (classification/releasability/system of record/security markings) match the payload’s effective classification. If mismatched, apply “correct-and-forward” only when deterministic and policy-approved; otherwise block.
5. **Apply privacy filtering to metadata** during transfer: enforce HIPAA minimum-necessary reduction, require/validate GDPR lawful-basis tags for the processing purpose, and redact/mask personal-data fields in metadata according to the organisation’s redaction rules.
6. **Enforce outcomes and auditability**: for each transfer, record whether metadata was validated, corrected, redacted, or blocked, and include which rules were evaluated and the resulting decision (allowed/corrected/blocked). Ensure the enforcement point emits audit events to the enterprise SIEM/logging platform.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Owner / Data Protection Officer (DPO)**: confirm the metadata schema, DoD handling rules, and privacy filter requirements.
- **Security Architect**: design the enforcement point and rule logic for domain-transfer validation and classification binding.
- **Platform/Integration Engineer**: implement the gateway/transfer-service logic and integrate with logging/SIEM.
- **Compliance/Assurance**: verify evidence (audit logs, test results) and approve correction vs block behaviour.

## Acceptance criteria
- For every cross-domain transfer, the enforcement point performs metadata validation against the filter set defined in `ac-4.19_prm_1`.
- Metadata that violates DoD domain-transfer rules (classification/releasability/system of record/security markings) is either corrected deterministically or the transfer is blocked.
- Metadata is privacy-filtered: HIPAA minimum-necessary is enforced, GDPR lawful-basis tags are validated, and personal-data fields in metadata are redacted/masked.
- Metadata handling is consistent with the payload’s effective classification (mismatches are detected and handled per policy).
- Audit evidence exists for each transfer decision (validated/corrected/redacted/blocked) including rule checks performed and outcome.
- The implementation covers all cross-domain transfer paths used by the application (no bypass routes).

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define the metadata validation filter ruleset per ac-4.19_prm_1, including DoD domain-transfer rules (classification, releasability, system of record, required security markings) and privacy filters (HIPAA minimum necessary, GDPR lawful-basis tags, metadata personal-data redaction).
- [ ] Create an authoritative metadata schema for all transfer formats and identify which metadata fields are personal data and which carry security markings/classification.
- [ ] Implement a single enforcement point for all cross-domain transfer paths (API gateway/transfer service/message-broker egress) that validates metadata before release.
- [ ] Add classification binding logic that derives effective payload classification and validates/corrects metadata claims to match; block when mismatches cannot be deterministically corrected.
- [ ] Implement privacy filtering on metadata during transfer (minimum-necessary reduction, lawful-basis validation, and redaction/masking of personal-data fields).
- [ ] Configure audit logging for each transfer decision (validated/corrected/redacted/blocked) including rule checks evaluated and the final outcome, and forward events to the SIEM.

### ac-4.20 — Approved Solutions (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This expectation is about making sure that highly sensitive information is only allowed to move between different security areas in approved ways. Without this, confidential or secret material could be passed through the wrong systems, blocked incorrectly, or routed using unapproved tools or settings, increasing the risk of unauthorised access, data leaks, or improper handling. To meet this requirement, the organisation must use only NSA-approved cross-domain solutions and configurations to manage how Confidential and Secret data flows between security domains, and it must clearly document this in its cross-domain information flow rules, the step-by-step procedures staff follow, the system design, and the actual configuration settings. It must also maintain an up-to-date list and baseline of the approved configurations, keep audit records showing the approved enforcement is being applied, and include the approach and named responsibilities in the system security plan so it can be independently verified.

#### Enforce NSA-approved cross-domain solutions for Confidential/Secret flows

- **Category:** Manual
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT must be done
Implement and operate cross-domain information-flow enforcement using **only NSA-approved cross-domain solutions and configurations** to control the flow of **Confidential and Secret data** across security domains.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4(20)** enhancement requires the organisation to **employ the approved solutions/configurations** to control the flow of **defined information types** across security domains. This must be supported by cross-domain policy/guidance, system design alignment, configuration baselines, and audit evidence.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create/maintain an approved cross-domain solution inventory** covering every enforcement point (e.g., gateway/relay, proxy, broker, data diode/transfer mechanism where applicable), including directionality (Domain A→B and B→A), the **information types** authorised (Confidential/Secret), and the **exact NSA-approved solution name/version** and **approved configuration profile ID(s)**.
2. **Update cross-domain information flow enforcement policy** to explicitly list:
   - the **approved solutions/configurations** used for Confidential and Secret flows (AC-04.20_odp.01), and
   - the **information types** controlled (AC-04.20_odp.02),
   - the allowed/denied flow rules per direction and protocol/application, referencing the approved configuration profiles.
3. **Implement configuration baselines as the single source of truth** for each enforcement point:
   - store the approved configuration profiles/templates in version control,
   - generate device/service configurations from templates (policy-as-code where feasible),
   - ensure change control only permits updates that remain within the approved profiles.
4. **Align system design and deployment configuration** so the running enforcement matches the approved baseline:
   - document the enforcement architecture (where enforcement occurs in the multi-tier design) and mapping to the approved profiles,
   - verify that production/test/DR environments use the same approved profiles for Confidential/Secret cross-domain flows.
5. **Enable and retain audit evidence** that enforcement decisions are attributable to the approved solutions/configurations:
   - centralise logs to SIEM (or equivalent) and ensure log fields include component identifier, policy/profile name, decision outcome (allow/deny), and timestamps,
   - retain logs per organisational retention requirements and ensure they can be correlated to the approved configuration profile.
6. **Record the enforcement approach and named responsibilities in the System Security Plan (SSP)** so independent verification is possible (e.g., who owns the inventory, who approves profile changes, who verifies drift and audit logging).

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Cross-domain Security Architect / Security Engineering Lead**: owns the approved solution inventory, policy updates, and design-to-profile mapping.
- **Infrastructure/Network/Platform Engineering (or Cloud Security Engineering)**: implements configuration baselines and automated configuration generation.
- **Configuration Management & Change Control Owner**: ensures only approved profile-aligned changes are deployed.
- **SOC/SIEM Operations**: ensures audit logging is enabled, centralised, and retained.
- **System Owner / Authorising Official (or delegated governance)**: ensures SSP updates and accountability are in place.

## Acceptance criteria
- The organisation can demonstrate that **every Confidential/Secret cross-domain flow** is mediated by an enforcement point using an **NSA-approved solution/configuration profile** listed in the inventory.
- Cross-domain policy explicitly references the **approved solutions/configurations (AC-04.20_odp.01)** and the **controlled information types (AC-04.20_odp.02)**, with rules mapped to those profiles.
- For each enforcement point, the **running configuration** matches the approved baseline (verified via automated compliance checks or documented configuration review).
- Audit logs show **allow/deny enforcement decisions** and are attributable to the **approved component/profile**, and are retained for verification.
- The **SSP** documents the enforcement approach and **named responsibilities** for inventory, configuration baseline control, drift verification, and audit evidence handling.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Obtain and record the current NSA-approved cross-domain solutions and configuration profiles applicable to Confidential and Secret flows
- [ ] Build and maintain an approved cross-domain solution inventory mapping each enforcement point to NSA-approved solution/version and profile ID(s)
- [ ] Update cross-domain information flow enforcement policy to explicitly reference approved solutions/configurations and define allowed/denied rules for Confidential/Secret
- [ ] Create configuration baselines (templates/templates-as-code) for each enforcement point strictly aligned to the approved profiles and lock them under change control
- [ ] Verify and document that deployed configurations in production/test/DR match the approved baselines for every Confidential/Secret cross-domain path
- [ ] Enable centralised audit logging for enforcement decisions and retain evidence showing allow/deny outcomes tied to the approved component/profile
- [ ] Update the System Security Plan with the enforcement approach and named responsibilities for inventory control, baseline verification, and audit evidence

### ac-4.21 — Physical or Logical Separation of Information Flows (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This expectation is about keeping different kinds of information traffic on separate paths through your network, so they cannot get mixed up while travelling. Without this, a mistake or misuse could let sensitive data travel into the wrong environment, allow unauthorised access between systems, or cause operational technology (the systems that run equipment) to be reached from the wrong place, increasing the chance of disruption or data exposure. To meet this, the organisation must first decide and document which traffic must be separated, including incoming versus outgoing communications, requests versus replies, Secret versus Confidential information, and IT versus OT control traffic. It must then set up and document the logical and physical ways to enforce those separations, such as dedicated network segments and firewall areas, separate encrypted connection paths for each traffic type and sensitivity level, and separate routing paths for each category, plus dedicated physical network and switching routes for Secret and OT control. The organisation must keep the rules, designs, configuration details, and evidence of enforcement up to date, assign named responsibilities, and retain records so separation can be checked and verified.

#### Implement VRF/VLAN + firewall zones + per-class VPN tunnels

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement logical separation of network information flows for the application by mapping each required traffic type (inbound vs outbound, service requests vs responses, Secret vs Confidential, and IT vs OT control traffic) to dedicated logical constructs (VLANs/VRFs, firewall security zones, and separate VPN tunnels per traffic type/classification).

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 AC-4(21) enhancement requires that information flows are separated logically or physically using the organisation-defined mechanisms (e.g., VLANs/VRFs, dedicated firewall zones, separate VPN tunnels, distinct routing domains) to accomplish the organisation-defined required separations (e.g., inbound vs outbound, requests vs responses, Secret vs Confidential, IT vs OT control traffic). This prevents commingling in transit and enables flow control by the intended transmission paths.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define and document the separation mapping**: Create a traffic taxonomy and a mapping table that assigns each traffic type to a specific logical construct (e.g., *Inbound* → “inbound entry zone”, *Outbound* → “outbound egress zone”, *Requests* → “service request zone”, *Responses* → “service response zone”, *Secret* → “Secret VRF/routing domain”, *Confidential* → “Confidential VRF/routing domain”, *IT vs OT control* → separate VRFs/zones).
2. **Implement logical separation using VLANs/VRFs and routing domains**: Configure VLANs/VRFs (or equivalent routing instances) so that Secret and Confidential traffic traverse distinct routing domains. Ensure routing between domains is either not permitted or is explicitly controlled via documented route leaking/redistribution rules.
3. **Enforce separation at the security boundary with stateful firewall zones**: Create dedicated firewall security zones aligned to the mapping table and enforce **default-deny** between zones. Allow only explicitly approved flows (directionally correct) and ensure stateful inspection is enabled so request/response paths are controlled by the intended transmission paths.
4. **Separate VPN tunnels per traffic type and classification**: For remote access and any inter-site connectivity, create distinct VPN tunnels per traffic type/classification (e.g., one tunnel for Secret, one for Confidential; separate tunnels for IT vs OT control traffic where applicable). Bind each tunnel to dedicated address pools and restrict permitted internal destinations to the corresponding VRF/zone.
5. **Operationalise and evidence enforcement**: Maintain configuration-as-code or controlled change records for VRFs/VLANs, firewall zone rules, and VPN tunnel bindings. Ensure firewall/VPN logs include zone/VRF/tunnel identifiers so separation can be verified during reviews and investigations.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Network Security Architect / Network Engineering Lead**: designs the segmentation model (VRFs/VLANs, firewall zones, VPN tunnel bindings) and produces the mapping documentation.
- **Security Operations / SOC**: validates logging fields and monitors for cross-zone/incorrect-path attempts.
- **Change Manager / Infrastructure Change Control**: ensures controlled deployment and evidence retention.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
- A documented mapping exists showing each required separation (inbound vs outbound, requests vs responses, Secret vs Confidential, IT vs OT control) mapped to specific VLAN/VRF, firewall zones, routing domains, and VPN tunnels.
- Secret and Confidential traffic are routed via distinct VRFs/routing domains with no unintended commingling paths.
- Firewall policy enforces default-deny between the defined zones, with only explicitly approved, directionally correct flows permitted.
- VPN connectivity is implemented using separate tunnels per traffic type/classification, each restricted to the correct VRF/zone address space.
- Evidence is available (diagrams/config snapshots/change records and representative logs) demonstrating that traffic is constrained to the intended separated paths.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Create a documented traffic taxonomy and mapping table from required separations to VLAN/VRF, firewall zones, routing domains, and VPN tunnels
- [ ] Configure VLANs/VRFs and distinct routing domains for Secret vs Confidential and for IT vs OT control traffic
- [ ] Implement stateful firewall security zones aligned to inbound/outbound and request/response paths with default-deny between zones
- [ ] Create separate VPN tunnels per traffic type and classification and bind each tunnel to dedicated address pools restricted to the correct VRF/zone
- [ ] Deploy and validate firewall/VPN rules to ensure request/response and inbound/outbound traffic cannot traverse incorrect zones
- [ ] Collect and retain evidence (configuration snapshots/diagrams and representative logs showing zone/VRF/tunnel enforcement) for audit and ongoing verification

### ac-4.22 — Access Only (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This expectation is about letting people use one single computer to look at information from different security areas, while making sure that nothing from one area can leak into another. Without this, a user could accidentally or deliberately copy sensitive material from a higher-protection area into a lower-protection one, creating unauthorised disclosure, fraud, or regulatory breaches. To meet this, the organisation must provide access-only behaviour from the single device: users can open and view content in each area, but the system must actively block any ways of transferring information between areas, such as copying and pasting, saving or exporting, printing, using shared storage, or moving data via removable media. The organisation must document the rules for how separation is enforced, the procedures for running and maintaining it, and the system design and configuration settings that make it work. It must also keep audit records showing that access happened and that blocked transfer attempts were prevented, and clearly assign named responsibilities to the right staff so administrators and security personnel oversee the separation properly.

#### Access-only cross-domain terminal with enforced no-transfer controls

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement a dedicated “access-only” access device (hardened workstation/terminal or VDI session) that allows users to view information from multiple security domains on a single device while **preventing any information transfer between those domains**.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-4(22) enhancement requires: “Provide access from a single device to computing platforms, applications, or data residing in multiple different security domains, while preventing information flow between the different security domains.” The guidance further expects an access-only solution where users can access different classifications **without mechanisms to transfer data/information between domains**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create a standard access-only terminal build** (gold image) with minimal software, hardened OS baseline, and centrally managed configuration (e.g., endpoint management/GPO/MDM).
2. **Enforce domain separation at the session level**: run each security domain in a separate isolated execution context (e.g., VDI session per domain, or browser/container per domain) with **no cross-context clipboard, no shared filesystem, and no shared browser storage**.
3. **Disable or strictly block transfer primitives** across domains within the access-only environment:
   - block cross-domain **copy/paste** (clipboard broker disabled/controlled),
   - block **downloads/export/save-as** to local or shared storage,
   - block **printing** to general printers (optionally allow only domain-specific, controlled print queues if the organisation explicitly approves an exception),
   - disable **drag-and-drop** and any **removable media** access,
   - prevent access to shared drives/network shares that could enable cross-domain movement.
4. **Centralise and harden logging**: forward terminal/session logs to the SIEM showing (a) which domain(s) were accessed and (b) any denied attempts to copy/export/print/download/transfer.
5. **Operationalise with documented procedures**: define how the access-only terminal is provisioned, how domain mappings are configured, how exceptions (if any) are requested/approved, and how the gold image is updated.
6. **Assign named responsibilities**: designate an owner for the access-only terminal build/configuration and a security reviewer for separation enforcement and exception handling.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Security Engineering / IAM & Endpoint Security team**: design and implement the hardened access-only terminal build and isolation/transfer-blocking configuration.
- **Platform/Infrastructure team**: operate VDI/terminal infrastructure and ensure central logging/SIEM integration.
- **Information Security (or CISO office)**: approve domain mapping, review exception requests, and verify audit evidence.
- **Service Desk / Operations**: follow provisioning and change procedures for the access-only device.

## Acceptance criteria
- Users can access multiple security domains from the same terminal, but **cannot transfer information between domains** using any supported transfer mechanism (clipboard, copy/paste, save/export/download, print, drag-and-drop, removable media, shared storage).
- Attempted transfer actions generate **audit events** in SIEM (denied copy/export/print/download/transfer) and access events show domain context.
- The access-only terminal is deployed from a **controlled gold image** with repeatable configuration management, and separation controls remain enabled after updates.
- Documented procedures and named responsibilities exist for provisioning, exception handling, and maintenance, and are evidenced during audit.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define domain-to-application/data mappings and which user roles may view each security domain on the access-only terminal
- [ ] Build and deploy a hardened access-only terminal gold image with centrally managed configuration and minimal installed software
- [ ] Implement per-domain session isolation (e.g., separate VDI sessions or browser/container instances) with clipboard, storage, and session data sharing disabled
- [ ] Disable or block transfer primitives (copy/paste, drag-and-drop, save/export/download, general printing, removable media, shared drives) and verify enforcement in each domain pairing
- [ ] Integrate terminal/session logging with the SIEM and create audit queries/dashboards for domain access and denied transfer attempts
- [ ] Document operating procedures for provisioning, exception handling (if any), and gold-image maintenance, and assign named owners for build/configuration and security review

### ac-4.23 — Modify Non-releasable Information (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure that when information moves between different parts of the business that are not equally trusted, anything that is not approved to be shared is deliberately hidden or altered before it leaves. Without this, sensitive details such as secret identifiers, confidential customer information, or internal-only data could be accidentally exposed during transfers, previews, or records, leading to data spills, misuse, or targeted attacks. To meet this expectation, the organisation must clearly define which cross-area transfers require this treatment, document that non-approved information will be handled using redaction and masking, and build those rules into its official guidance, day-to-day procedures, system design, and security plans. It must also configure the systems so the masking happens automatically for the relevant transfer routes, ensure the right named staff are responsible (including system and network administrators and information security staff), and keep audit records showing that masking was applied.

#### Enforce redaction/masking on cross-domain transfers via secure gateway

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement an enforcement mechanism for cross-domain information transfers that **modifies non-releasable information** using the organisation-defined **modification action** (parameter **ac-04.23_odp**), specifically **redaction and masking**, before the information enters the receiving security domain.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4(23)** requires that when transferring information between different security domains, the organisation **modifies non-releasable information** by implementing the defined **modification action** (**ac-04.23_odp**). This prevents data spill and reduces the risk of an attacker leveraging sensitive content during or after domain transfer.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the cross-domain transfer routes** for the application (e.g., web downloads, email attachments, API responses, file exports, message bus events, reporting outputs) and map each route to a **sending domain** and **receiving domain**.
2. **Create a non-releasable data rule set** aligned to the organisation’s release/authorisation decisions (e.g., based on data classification tags, field-level sensitivity, and user/service authorisation context). For each rule, specify what must be **redacted and/or masked** (e.g., identifiers, secrets, customer data fields, internal-only metadata) and the conditions under which the rule applies.
3. **Implement transformation at the transfer enforcement point** (secure transfer gateway / secure exchange / content-aware proxy) so that the gateway performs **masking/redaction** on content that is not approved for release **before** it is delivered to the receiving domain.
4. **Ensure non-recoverability for modified content** by configuring the transformation so that the receiving domain receives only the modified output (e.g., `[REDACTED]`, `****1234`, removed fields), and that downstream previews/secondary views (thumbnails, previews, cached copies, “view source”, report drill-down) use the same transformed payload.
5. **Generate audit evidence**: log transformation events including (a) transfer route identifier, (b) rule identifier(s) matched, (c) transformation type (redaction/masking), (d) timestamp, and (e) a non-sensitive indicator of affected fields (e.g., counts, hashes of patterns) — without logging original sensitive values.
6. **Operationalise ownership and change control**: assign named responsibilities for maintaining the rule set and gateway configuration (application owner, security engineering, and platform/infrastructure administrators), and manage updates via the organisation’s change/configuration management.
7. **Test and validate** across all defined transfer routes and representative data samples: confirm that non-releasable fields are always redacted/masked and that authorised/releasable data is not incorrectly modified.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Owner / Product Owner**: approves the cross-domain transfer mapping and release rules.
- **Security Engineering / Data Protection Lead**: defines redaction/masking rules and ensures they align to ac-04.23_odp.
- **Platform/Infrastructure Administrator (or Gateway Owner)**: implements and maintains the secure transfer gateway configuration.
- **Security Operations / SIEM Engineer**: ensures audit logs are correctly forwarded, retained, and searchable.

## Acceptance criteria
- For every defined cross-domain transfer route, when data is classified as **non-releasable**, the receiving domain receives **redacted/masked** content consistent with **ac-04.23_odp**.
- Modified content is not recoverable in the receiving domain (including previews/secondary views/caches) and original sensitive values are not present in outputs.
- Audit logs show transformation occurred (rule IDs and transformation actions) **without** exposing original sensitive values.
- Tests demonstrate correct behaviour for both non-releasable and releasable cases across at least the top critical transfer routes (web/API/file/email/reporting).

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Map and document all cross-domain transfer routes for the application (send/receive domains and transfer mechanisms).
- [ ] Define non-releasable data rules and specify redaction/masking formats and conditions using ac-04.23_odp.
- [ ] Configure a secure transfer enforcement point (gateway/proxy/DLP-enabled exchange) to apply redaction/masking before delivery to the receiving domain.
