- [ ] Ensure previews, secondary views, caches, and drill-down outputs use the transformed payload (no bypass paths).
- [ ] Implement audit logging for each transformation event using non-sensitive indicators (rule IDs, transformation type, affected-field indicators).
- [ ] Assign named owners for rule-set and gateway configuration changes and manage updates via change/configuration control.
- [ ] Run end-to-end tests for each transfer route to verify non-releasable fields are always redacted/masked and releasable data is not incorrectly modified.

### ac-4.24 — Internal Normalized Format (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure information is understood correctly when it moves between different parts of the organisation, by first translating it into a standard internal form and then recreating it in the exact format the receiving area expects. Without this, attackers or careless systems could slip in data with unexpected formatting that gets misread, causing incorrect decisions, corrupted records, or even helping harmful data leave the organisation in the wrong shape. To meet this expectation, the organisation must clearly identify which information transfers cross between different security areas, ensure the system parses incoming data into a consistent internal representation, and then regenerates it to match the destination’s rules for fields, types, structure, and formats. Invalid or unparseable data must be rejected or handled safely, with clear records kept to show what happened, who is responsible, and how the approach is documented in design, procedures, configuration, and the organisation’s security planning.

#### Canonical parse-and-regenerate at cross-domain transfer boundaries

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement a defensive “parse → normalise (canonical internal format) → regenerate” processing step at every information transfer boundary that crosses security domains, so that incoming data is interpreted safely and then re-emitted in the destination’s exact specification-compliant format.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-4(24) requires that when transferring information between different security domains, the system **parses incoming data into an internal normalized format** and **regenerates the data to be consistent with its intended specification**. This reduces the risk of malicious or malformed payloads being misinterpreted, and supports correct, consistent handling across domains.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Identify cross-domain transfer points** (e.g., API gateway routes, message-broker consumers/producers, ETL jobs, integration services) and maintain an inventory mapping each transfer to the destination domain and its expected data specification.
2. **Define canonical internal models (“data contracts”)** per data type (e.g., Customer, Invoice, Alarm event) with strict typing and canonicalisation rules (e.g., ISO-8601 timestamps, canonical decimal representation, UTF-8 normalisation, controlled enumerations, explicit handling of optional/required fields).
3. **Enforce parse-and-normalise on ingress** at each boundary using strict schema validation and safe parsing (reject unknown fields, enforce type/encoding constraints, normalise whitespace/character encoding, convert time zones/units to canonical forms). Ensure the canonical model is the only representation used for subsequent processing in the destination domain.
4. **Regenerate on egress** from the canonical model into the destination’s required format (schema, field rules, structure, ordering, and encoding). Do not pass through original payload formatting.
5. **Fail safely on invalid input**: if parsing or regeneration fails, do not forward to the destination domain. Quarantine the payload (restricted access) or reject the transaction, and emit an event for triage.
6. **Produce audit evidence** for each transaction: correlation/transaction ID, canonical model version, destination contract version, parse/normalise outcome, regeneration validation outcome, and reason codes for failures.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Integration/Platform Engineer**: implements the boundary processing (gateway/consumer/ETL) and canonical model transformations.
- **Data/Domain Architect**: defines canonical internal models and destination specifications.
- **Security Engineering / SecOps**: reviews validation/quarantine behaviour and ensures audit events are routed to SIEM.

## Acceptance criteria
- For every identified cross-domain transfer, the boundary performs **parse → canonicalise → regenerate**; the destination receives only regenerated, specification-compliant payloads.
- Malformed/unsafe inputs (e.g., wrong types, invalid encodings, schema violations, unexpected fields) are **rejected or quarantined** and never processed as valid business data.
- Audit logs/events exist for each transaction with correlation ID, canonical model version, destination contract version, and parse/regenerate outcomes.
- Integration tests demonstrate correct canonicalisation and regeneration for representative payloads, including negative tests for invalid inputs.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Create and maintain an inventory of cross-domain information transfers and map each to the destination specification
- [ ] Define canonical internal data contracts (strict schemas, canonical types, and normalisation rules) for each transferred data type
- [ ] Implement strict parse-and-normalise at each integration boundary (API gateway, message consumer, or ETL ingress) and ensure downstream logic uses only the canonical model
- [ ] Implement regeneration from the canonical model to the destination’s required schema/encoding/structure and block pass-through of original formatting
- [ ] Configure safe failure handling (reject or quarantine on parse/regenerate errors) with restricted access to quarantined payloads
- [ ] Add audit logging with correlation IDs, canonical model version, destination contract version, and parse/regenerate outcome codes, and forward events to the SIEM

### ac-4.25 — Data Sanitization (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This expectation is about making sure that when information moves between different security areas, any data that should not travel safely is permanently wiped or destroyed so it cannot be recovered. Without this, sensitive details could leak into the wrong place, where they might be read, misused, or used to support harmful activity such as malicious content delivery, taking control of malicious code, adding malicious capabilities, or hiding information in plain sight. To meet this requirement, the organisation must first identify every time information is transferred between security areas, then apply its own data sanitisation policy for each such transfer. The organisation must have clear written procedures, ensure the right staff can access and follow them, and assign named responsibilities to accountable roles, including system and network administrators. It must also build sanitisation into system design and settings, keep records showing sanitisation happened, retain those records for verification, include the approach in the system security plan, and maintain any supporting documentation needed to prove compliance.

#### Policy-driven sanitise-on-transfer for cross-domain data

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement an automated, policy-driven “sanitise-on-transfer” mechanism that detects when information moves between security domains and irreversibly sanitises the data in accordance with the organisation’s data sanitisation policy.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-4(25) (Enhancement) requires that when transferring information between different security domains, the organisation sanitises data to minimise **spillage of sensitive information** (ac-04.25_odp.01) in accordance with the organisation’s **data sanitization policy** (ac-04.25_odp.02).

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create a cross-domain transfer map** covering all pathways used by the application and its integrations (e.g., API calls, message bus topics/queues, file transfers, email/SaaS connectors, replication/backup/restore flows, removable media workflows, and any DMZ/relay components).
2. **Define sanitisation policy mappings** that translate the organisation’s sanitisation policy into enforceable rules per transfer type and target medium (e.g., cryptographic erasure for encrypted storage/key material; secure erase for HDD/SSD; destruction for media types where required). Ensure each rule explicitly targets minimising spillage (ac-04.25_odp.01).
3. **Enforce sanitisation at the transfer boundary** by integrating with the transfer mechanisms used by the application stack:
   - For application/API transfers: require a sanitisation step (or policy-based proof that no sensitive data is persisted) before the payload is released to the destination domain.
   - For storage/replication/backup: ensure cryptographic erasure or secure wipe is executed for the source copy and any intermediate staging locations as defined by policy.
   - For managed endpoints/devices: trigger secure wipe/cryptographic erase on re-imaging, decommissioning, or domain movement.
4. **Generate audit evidence for every sanitisation event** (who/what/when/where): record the transfer identifier, source/destination domain, data classification, sanitisation method selected, target identifiers, workflow identity/operator identity, and verification outcome.
5. **Retain evidence and document the approach** in the system security plan and supporting runbooks so that the sanitisation procedures are available to the responsible teams.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Security Architect / Security Engineering Lead**: owns the transfer map, policy mappings, and boundary enforcement design.
- **Platform/Infrastructure Engineer**: implements storage/backup/endpoint sanitisation integrations.
- **IAM/Operations Lead**: ensures workflow identities, approvals, and audit logging are correctly configured.
- **System Owner / Authorising Official (or delegate)**: ensures the approach is documented in the system security plan and that procedures are operationally adopted.

## Acceptance criteria
- For every identified cross-domain transfer pathway, the system either (a) performs irreversible sanitisation before/as part of transfer in line with the organisation’s sanitisation policy, or (b) provides auditable policy-based justification that sensitive data is not retained/persisted in a way that could spill.
- Sanitisation events produce complete, tamper-evident audit records including method and verification results.
- A test transfer between two security domains results in sanitisation of the source/intermediate data and prevents recoverable sensitive data from being accessible in the destination domain.
- The sanitisation approach and procedures are documented and available in the system security plan and operational runbooks.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Inventory and document all cross-domain information transfer pathways for the application and its integrations
- [ ] Translate the organisation’s data sanitisation policy into enforceable sanitisation rules by transfer type and target medium
- [ ] Implement boundary enforcement so transfers are blocked or sanitised until the required sanitisation step completes
- [ ] Integrate device and storage sanitisation triggers for decommissioning, re-imaging, and domain movement workflows
- [ ] Configure audit logging to capture sanitisation method, source/destination domains, target identifiers, and verification results
- [ ] Update the system security plan and publish operational runbooks for the sanitise-on-transfer procedures and responsibilities

### ac-4.26 — Audit Filtering Actions (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about keeping a clear record of what happened to each message when it passes between different parts of the organisation’s environment that have different rules. It matters because, without detailed records, you cannot prove that the right filtering rules were applied, and you may not be able to explain why something was changed, blocked, or quarantined—leaving you exposed to inappropriate information crossing boundaries and making mistakes hard to correct. To meet this expectation, the organisation must define which cross-boundary transfers count, ensure every individual message that is inspected has its specific filtering action recorded and linked to that message, and also record the final outcome of the filtering decision. These records must be kept for the required period, be searchable for later review, and include enough detail to support troubleshooting, such as the reason a message was modified or blocked.

#### Per-message content filtering audit at cross-domain boundaries

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement cross-domain content filtering so that, for every individual message inspected at a security-domain boundary, the system records **(1) the filtering action** taken and **(2) the filtering result/outcome** for that specific message.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-4(26) requires that when information is transferred between different security domains, the organisation **records and audits content filtering actions and results** for the information being filtered. The guidance further states that content filter reports support troubleshooting (e.g., why content was modified or why it failed filtering) and that audit events are defined in **AU-2** and audit records are generated in **AU-12**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Identify and scope cross-domain transfers** where content filtering occurs (e.g., email gateway, secure web gateway, secure file transfer gateway, API gateway/WAF if it performs content inspection).
2. **Configure the boundary enforcement point** to apply a single authoritative content filtering policy per boundary path (rule set), including actions such as allow, modify/sanitise, block/quarantine, or replace.
3. **Emit per-message audit fields** for every inspected message, ensuring the audit record includes:
   - **Message correlation identifier** (e.g., Message-ID, transaction/request ID, session ID, file reference) and timestamps.
   - **Filtering action** (what the filter did).
   - **Filtering outcome/result** (final disposition such as passed/failed policy; delivered/quarantined/rejected).
   - **Troubleshooting metadata** sufficient to explain modifications/failures (e.g., rule name, policy version, reason code/category).
4. **Normalise and forward logs to the SIEM/log platform** with consistent field names and ensure the correlation identifier is preserved end-to-end (including quarantine portal/ticketing references where applicable).
5. **Validate coverage and correctness** using automated test messages that exercise each expected action/outcome (allowed, modified, blocked/quarantined) and confirm that each message produces an audit event containing both action and outcome with the correct correlation identifier.
6. **Enforce retention and audit integrity** for these audit events in accordance with organisational audit retention requirements, and ensure access to audit logs is restricted and monitored.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Security Architecture Lead / Compliance Engineer**: defines boundary scope and required audit fields.
- **Platform/Integration Engineer (IT/OT boundary gateway owners)**: implements gateway/WAF/SFTP/email security configuration and log forwarding.
- **SIEM/Logging Engineer**: normalises fields, ensures correlation, and configures retention/integrity.
- **Security Operations (SOC) / QA Security Testing**: runs validation tests and confirms troubleshooting metadata usefulness.

## Acceptance criteria
- For each configured cross-domain boundary path, sending a test message results in **an audit record per message** that includes **both** filtering action and filtering outcome/result.
- Audit records include an **unambiguous correlation identifier** that can be used to trace the message through delivery/quarantine.
- Audit records include **policy version/rule name and reason codes** sufficient to explain why content was modified or blocked.
- SIEM/log store shows **no gaps** for test traffic (ingestion failures are detected/alerted) and retention is configured per organisational requirements.
- Automated validation confirms expected action/outcome mappings for all test cases.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define the set of cross-domain boundary paths where content filtering is applied and map each to its enforcement point
- [ ] Configure the boundary gateway/WAF/SFTP/email security policy to produce per-message action and outcome dispositions
- [ ] Implement audit event fields for each inspected message: correlation identifier, filtering action, filtering outcome, policy version, rule name, and reason code
- [ ] Normalise and forward these audit events to the SIEM/log platform while preserving the correlation identifier end-to-end
- [ ] Create automated test cases that send messages exercising allow/modify/block outcomes and verify an audit record exists for each message with correct action+outcome
- [ ] Set audit log retention and integrity controls for the filtering audit events and alert on ingestion gaps or failures

### ac-4.27 — Redundant/Independent Filtering Mechanisms (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure that when information moves between different protected areas of the business, it is checked against the agreed rules more than once, in ways that do not rely on a single system. Without this, a failure, outage, or bug in one checking tool could let unsafe or unwanted content slip through, or stop enforcement entirely, creating real exposure such as malware, sensitive data leaks, or policy breaches. To meet the expectation, the organisation must define what kinds of information (each data type) must be inspected during cross-area transfers, set out clear procedures for how the checks are carried out and how decisions are applied, and document how the filtering is built, configured, and monitored. It must also keep records showing the checks happened and what decision was taken, include the approach in the security plan, and assign clear responsibility to relevant staff. For each data type, the system must use redundant and independent filtering mechanisms—such as different implementations and separate running components—so that if one fails, the other still enforces the rules.

#### Active-active dual content filtering per data type at domain boundaries

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement cross-domain content filtering using **redundant and independent filtering mechanisms for each defined data type**. In practice, deploy a cross-domain inspection point (e.g., secure email/web/file-transfer gateway or API inspection service) where **two independent filter engines** inspect the same content and enforce policy decisions.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-4(27) enhancement requires that when transferring information between different security domains, the organisation implements **content filtering solutions** that provide **redundant and independent filtering mechanisms for each data type**. Independence must mean different code bases/libraries (e.g., different vendor stacks) and multiple independent system processes, so that there is **no single point of failure** in filtering.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define data types and enforcement actions** for cross-domain transfers (e.g., email attachments, web uploads/downloads, file-transfer payloads, document/image types, API payloads) and map each data type to an inspection policy (e.g., block/quarantine/allow, and what constitutes a match).
2. **Select a cross-domain enforcement point** that sits on the information path between domains (gateway/service) and ensures all relevant traffic for each data type passes through it.
3. **Implement two independent filtering engines per data type**:
   - Engine A and Engine B must use **different code bases and supporting libraries** (e.g., different vendor products or separately developed components) and run as **separate processes/services** with distinct operational boundaries.
   - Configure both engines to evaluate the same content stream for the same data type.
4. **Enforce redundant decisioning with fail-closed behaviour**:
   - Use a defined decision rule such as **"block/quarantine if either engine flags"** (or an equivalent consensus rule that prevents bypass).
   - Ensure that if one engine is unavailable/crashes/updates fail, the other engine continues to enforce the policy for the affected data type (avoid “soft-fail/allow on error”).
5. **Make the configuration auditable and reproducible**:
   - Store gateway/filter configuration and policy-as-code in a version-controlled repository with change control.
   - Record engine versions, rule set identifiers, and configuration hashes used for each inspection.
6. **Generate audit evidence per inspection**:
   - Log inspection events including: data type, source/destination domains, content identifiers (non-sensitive where possible), engine A/B outcomes, final enforcement action, and timestamps.
   - Forward logs to the enterprise SIEM for retention and correlation.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Security Architect / Solution Engineer**: designs the dual-engine architecture and independence boundaries.
- **Platform/Network Engineering**: implements the gateway/service deployment, process separation, and fail-closed behaviour.
- **Security Operations / SOC**: validates alerting, monitoring, and audit log integrity; performs operational acceptance testing.
- **Change Manager / Configuration Manager**: ensures policy-as-code and configuration changes follow approved release processes.

## Acceptance criteria
- For each defined cross-domain **data type**, both Engine A and Engine B inspect the content during normal operation.
- Engines are demonstrably **independent** (different vendor stacks/code bases/libraries and separate processes/services).
- If Engine A fails or is unreachable, Engine B still enforces the policy for the affected data type (**no allow-by-default**).
- If Engine B fails or is unreachable, Engine A still enforces the policy for the affected data type (**no allow-by-default**).
- Audit logs show per inspection: data type, domains, engine A/B outcomes, and final enforcement action, and are retained and queryable in SIEM.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define cross-domain data types and the required enforcement action per data type (block/quarantine/allow) in the gateway policy
- [ ] Deploy a cross-domain inspection point on the information path for all relevant transfers (email/web/file/API) and ensure traffic cannot bypass it
- [ ] Implement Engine A and Engine B for each data type using different vendor stacks or separately developed components and run them as separate processes/services
- [ ] Configure redundant decisioning with fail-closed enforcement (e.g., block/quarantine if either engine flags; no allow-on-error) and test engine outage scenarios
- [ ] Enable audit logging for each inspection including data type, source/destination domains, engine A/B outcomes, and final action; forward to SIEM
- [ ] Store gateway/filter configuration and rule sets as version-controlled policy-as-code with controlled releases and record engine/rule versions used per inspection

### ac-4.28 — Linear Filter Pipelines (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure that whenever information moves between different parts of the organisation that have different protection levels, it is checked in one straight, step-by-step way every time, with no way to skip the check. Without this, sensitive or inappropriate information could slip through by taking an alternative route, using a misconfiguration, or being processed in a way that allows the inspection to be avoided, leading to unauthorised access, policy breaches, or data leaks. To meet this expectation, the organisation must clearly define which transfers count, publish a rule that all such transfers go through a single sequential filtering pipeline, and design the system so that pipeline cannot be bypassed and is always invoked. It must also enforce the filtering outcome using both permission rules (who is allowed to send or receive) and policy rules (what content is allowed), and keep clear documentation and audit records showing the pipeline ran, the decision made, and the final allow or block result.

#### Gateway linear content filtering for cross-domain transfers

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement a **single, sequential (linear) content filter pipeline** at the **only permitted cross-domain transfer choke point** (e.g., secure web/email/file/API gateway) so that every cross-domain information transfer is inspected **in one non-bypassable order** and the outcome is enforced using **discretionary access controls (who may transfer)** and **mandatory access controls (policy-based content allow/deny/quarantine)**.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-4(28) enhancement requires that when transferring information between different security domains, the organisation implements a **linear content filter pipeline** that is **enforced with discretionary and mandatory access controls**. The guidance emphasises that **linear pipelines** help avoid **bypass/non-invocation issues** seen in **parallel filtering architectures**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define and document scope**: Create an information-flow definition of what constitutes a “cross-domain information transfer” for this application (protocols, interfaces, connectors, message types, and data classes), and map each to a single gateway path.
2. **Enforce a single choke point**: Configure network/routing/firewall/egress controls so that Domain A → Domain B traffic for the defined transfer types can reach the destination **only via the inspection gateway** (no alternate routes, no direct bypass paths). Use active/standby for resilience, but ensure only one inspection path is active at a time.
3. **Implement a strictly linear pipeline**: Configure the gateway’s inspection chain as a **single ordered sequence** (e.g., normalise → decode → extract → classify → scan → policy decision → action). Ensure the pipeline is **fail-closed** (if inspection fails, the transfer is denied or quarantined).
4. **Bind discretionary + mandatory decisions**:
   - Discretionary: enforce **authorisation** based on identity/role/entitlements (e.g., user/service account permissions to send/receive to/from the target domain).
   - Mandatory: enforce **content policy** based on classification/labels and rules (allow/deny/quarantine/strip) derived from the organisation’s data handling policy.
   - Ensure the mandatory outcome cannot be overridden by discretionary allow (i.e., a mandatory deny/quarantine always results in enforcement).
5. **Prevent parallel bypass**: Validate that the gateway configuration does not create parallel inspection branches for the same content type that could be skipped; where multiple content types exist, ensure each transfer still traverses the same linear chain for its applicable path.
6. **Produce audit-ready evidence**: Enable logging that records, for every transfer attempt, the pipeline invocation, the ordered stages outcome (or a summarised pipeline result), the discretionary authorisation result, the mandatory content policy decision, and the final enforcement action (allow/quarantine/deny).

## WHO
- **Application Owner / Security Architect**: define scope and required enforcement semantics.
- **Network/Platform Engineer**: implement routing/firewall rules to guarantee the single choke point.
- **Security Engineer (IAM/Policy)**: implement discretionary authorisation integration and mandatory content policy rules.
- **SOC/Monitoring Lead**: ensure logs are forwarded to SIEM and retained per policy.

## Acceptance criteria
- For all defined cross-domain transfer types, traffic from the source domain to the destination domain is only possible via the inspection gateway (verified by routing/firewall configuration review and test).
- Every transfer attempt results in a **linear pipeline invocation** (verified by gateway logs showing the ordered chain and fail-closed behaviour).
- Discretionary authorisation and mandatory content policy decisions are both recorded, and mandatory deny/quarantine always results in enforcement regardless of discretionary allow.
- Audit logs demonstrate pipeline invocation and final enforcement action for 100% of tested transfer attempts (including malformed/inspection-failure cases).

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define and publish the scope of “cross-domain information transfer” (protocols/interfaces/data types) and map each to a single gateway path
- [ ] Configure network routing/firewall/egress rules so cross-domain transfers are only reachable via the inspection gateway (no alternate routes)
- [ ] Implement the gateway inspection chain as a single ordered linear pipeline and configure fail-closed behaviour
- [ ] Integrate the gateway with enterprise IAM to enforce discretionary send/receive authorisation for the source and destination domains
- [ ] Implement mandatory content policy rules (allow/deny/quarantine/strip) and ensure mandatory outcomes cannot be overridden by discretionary allow
- [ ] Enable and forward audit logs capturing pipeline invocation, discretionary decision, mandatory decision, and final enforcement action for every transfer attempt
- [ ] Test and verify non-bypassability by attempting alternate routes and by inducing inspection failures to confirm deny/quarantine

### ac-4.29 — Filter Orchestration Engines (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure that when information moves between different parts of the organisation that have different protection rules, it is checked in a coordinated, step-by-step way, and that the checking process finishes properly. Without this, checks could silently fail, stop halfway, or run in the wrong sequence, meaning unsafe or incorrect information could slip through or the organisation could be unable to prove what happened. To meet this expectation, the organisation must use a coordinating “orchestration” system for these cross-domain transfers so that the content checks run to completion without errors (including unexpected stopping or unusual behaviour), each check happens in the approved order, and the actions follow the organisation’s approved cross-domain data handling policy. The system must also produce clear completion reports showing success in the required sequence, so that anyone reviewing the records can confirm the process ran as intended.

#### Policy-driven orchestration for cross-domain content filtering

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement a content filter orchestration engine for cross-domain information transfers so that filtering activities are sequenced in the approved order, complete successfully, and produce successful completion reports. Treat anomalous actions and unexpected termination as orchestration errors.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-4(29) enhancement requires that, when transferring information between different security domains, the organisation employs content filter orchestration engines to ensure filtering is coordinated, runs to completion without errors (including anomalous actions or unexpected termination), follows the approved action sequence, and emits successful completion evidence in the required sequence. The organisation-defined parameter requires that content-filter orchestration actions follow the approved cross-domain data handling policy and produce successful completion reports in the required sequence.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create policy-to-workflow mappings**: Define, for each cross-domain transfer type, the approved content-filtering action sequence (e.g., inspect/classify → apply policy decision → redact/quarantine if required → deliver/forward → finalise). Encode the sequence as a workflow definition that is version-controlled.
2. **Enforce orchestration-only execution**: Route all cross-domain transfers through a single orchestration entrypoint (API/service) so that filtering steps cannot be executed ad-hoc outside the workflow.
3. **Implement deterministic sequencing and guards**: Use explicit step dependencies and state transitions so steps cannot run out of order. Add runtime checks that block progression if prior steps have not completed successfully.
4. **Define orchestration error semantics**: Distinguish “policy non-compliance” (a policy decision outcome) from orchestration errors (anomalous actions, timeouts, unexpected termination, or missing expected events). On orchestration errors, stop further processing and mark the run as failed.
5. **Generate audit-ready completion evidence**: For every transfer, emit (a) stage reports and (b) a final completion report that includes transfer ID, workflow version, step-by-step status, timestamps, and the required sequence of successful stages. Ensure the final report is only produced when the workflow reaches the successful terminal state.
6. **Make evidence tamper-evident and SIEM-integrated**: Store orchestration logs and completion reports in an immutable or cryptographically signed store and forward key events to the SIEM for audit correlation.
7. **Operationalise via change control and testing**: Require change management for workflow/policy updates, and run integration tests that validate correct sequencing and that unexpected termination/anomalous actions result in failed orchestration (no “success” report).

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Solution/Platform Architect**: designs workflow and sequencing model.
- **Application Integration Engineer**: implements orchestration entrypoint and workflow execution.
- **Security Engineering / GRC**: validates policy-to-workflow mapping and evidence requirements.
- **Operations / SOC**: monitors orchestration failures and ensures evidence retention.

## Acceptance criteria
- For each supported cross-domain transfer type, the orchestration engine executes filtering steps in the approved order and cannot progress out of sequence.
- For induced orchestration faults (e.g., forced termination, anomalous step invocation, missing expected events), the workflow is marked failed and does **not** emit a successful completion report.
- For successful transfers, the final completion report is produced only after all required stages complete successfully, and stage ordering in the report matches the approved sequence.
- Completion reports and orchestration logs are retained and are tamper-evident/traceable to a unique transfer ID and workflow version.
- All cross-domain transfers are routed through the orchestration entrypoint (no bypass paths).

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define the approved cross-domain content-filtering action sequence per transfer type and map it to workflow stages
- [ ] Implement a single orchestration entrypoint so all cross-domain transfers route through the orchestration engine
- [ ] Configure deterministic step dependencies and runtime guards to prevent out-of-sequence execution
- [ ] Implement orchestration error handling that treats anomalous actions and unexpected termination as orchestration failures
- [ ] Generate stage reports and a final successful completion report only upon reaching the successful terminal state
- [ ] Store orchestration logs and completion reports in an immutable or cryptographically signed evidence store and forward key events to SIEM
- [ ] Run integration tests that validate correct sequencing and verify that orchestration faults do not produce success evidence

### ac-4.30 — Filter Mechanisms Using Multiple Processes (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure that when information moves between separate security areas, it is checked for unsafe or unwanted content by more than one independent checking step, so one failure cannot let bad material slip through. Without this, a single broken or bypassed check could create a weak point where harmful files, messages, or data could reach the wrong area, potentially leading to fraud, disruption, or exposure of sensitive information. To meet this expectation, the organisation must clearly state in its information-sharing rules that cross-area transfers use multiple independent content checks, and it must write and follow step-by-step procedures for how those checks are applied and what happens if one check fails. It must also design and configure the systems so the multiple checks are actually in place, keep records showing the checks ran during transfers, and include this approach and the responsible roles in the system’s security plan.

#### Dual-engine content filtering for cross-domain transfers

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement content filtering for cross-domain information transfers using **multiple independent processes** (e.g., two separate filtering engines/stages) so that a single process failure cannot result in an allow decision.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-4(30) enhancement requires that when transferring information between different security domains, content filtering mechanisms are implemented using multiple processes. The guidance states this reduces the likelihood of a **single point of failure**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Identify cross-domain transfer paths** for the application (e.g., API gateway calls, message bus topics, file transfer, email/web relay, SaaS connectors) and define the source/destination security domains.
2. **Place a cross-domain enforcement point** (secure gateway/proxy or equivalent transfer mediation layer) on each path where domain crossing occurs.
3. Configure the enforcement point to run **at least two independent content filtering processes** per transfer attempt (e.g., Engine A = malware/content classifier; Engine B = DLP/policy classifier, or sandbox + static classifier). Independence must be enforced by separate services/processes, separate configuration objects/rulesets, and distinct logging identifiers.
4. Implement **decision logic that prevents bypass**: the transfer is only allowed when the policy-defined condition is met (e.g., **AND/quorum**). If one engine fails, times out, or is unavailable, the system must **block or quarantine** by default (no silent fallback to a single engine).
5. **Generate audit evidence per transfer**: log a single correlation identifier (message ID / transaction ID / file hash) and record the verdict from each filtering process (including failures/timeouts) plus the final allow/block/quarantine outcome.
6. Document the design and operating procedure in the **system security plan** and assign named responsibilities for operation, monitoring, and review of the multi-process filtering behaviour.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Application/Security Architect**: defines transfer paths, independence model, and decision logic.
- **Platform/Network Engineering or Gateway Administrators**: implement gateway/proxy configuration and engine integration.
- **SOC/Security Operations**: monitors audit events, handles quarantine/incident workflows.
- **Information Security Governance**: ensures the approach is recorded in the system security plan and reviewed during change management.

## Acceptance criteria
- For each defined cross-domain transfer path, the enforcement point executes **two independent filtering processes** and records per-process verdicts.
- If either filtering process fails, times out, or is unavailable, the transfer is **not allowed** and is instead **blocked or quarantined** according to policy.
- Audit logs show, for a sample of transfer attempts, the correlation ID, per-engine verdict/failure reason, and the final decision.
- The system security plan includes the multi-process filtering design, failure handling, and roles/responsibilities.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Map and document all cross-domain transfer paths for the application (source/destination security domains and mediation points).
- [ ] Select and integrate two independent content filtering engines/stages that can produce separate verdicts and failure outcomes.
- [ ] Configure the enforcement point to execute both processes per transfer and enforce AND/quorum decision logic.
- [ ] Set default failure behaviour so any engine failure/timeout results in block or quarantine (no single-engine fallback).
- [ ] Implement correlation-based audit logging capturing per-engine verdicts, failure reasons, and final allow/block/quarantine outcomes.
- [ ] Update the system security plan with the multi-process filtering architecture, operating procedures, and named roles for monitoring and review.

### ac-4.31 — Failed Content Transfer Prevention (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure that when information is passed between separate parts of your organisation, anything that fails the required checks is stopped at the boundary and never reaches the other side. Without this, a corrupted or unsafe item could slip through and then be stored, processed, or acted upon in the receiving area, potentially causing damage, errors, or further security problems. To meet this expectation, your organisation must clearly identify which information transfers cross between separate security areas, ensure the system performs the required checks before any transfer completes, and block any item that does not pass so it is not delivered in any form. You also need to document the rule and the way it is enforced, describe how the system is designed to stop failed items, configure the relevant settings, keep evidence of those settings, and retain records showing failed transfers were blocked. Named staff must be responsible for overseeing and maintaining this enforcement.

#### Reject-and-quarantine failed content at cross-domain transfer gateways

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement cross-domain information transfer enforcement points (gateways/relays/proxies) that perform required filtering/validation checks before any content is delivered to the receiving security domain. If content fails, the transfer is rejected and the failed content is quarantined in an isolated location that is not reachable from the receiving domain.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-4(31) enhancement requires that when transferring information between different security domains, failed content is prevented from being transferred to the receiving domain. This prevents failed/unsafe artefacts from corrupting the receiving environment.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Identify in-scope cross-domain transfers** for the application (e.g., API calls, file uploads/downloads, message delivery, event payloads, batch exports) and map each transfer to a single enforcement point (the “choke point”) that sits on the boundary between domains.
2. **Enforce pre-delivery validation** at the enforcement point so that acceptance into the receiving domain occurs only after checks pass (avoid patterns where the receiver accepts first and scans later).
3. **Implement reject-and-quarantine**: on validation failure, return a hard rejection to the sender workflow and ensure the payload is not written to any receiving-side inbox/share/queue/storage/processing pipeline. Store failed artefacts only in a quarantined repository with strict access controls and no network path from the receiving domain.
4. **Record auditable evidence** for every attempted transfer: transfer ID, sender identity, source/destination domains, validation rule(s) triggered, decision outcome (blocked/rejected), and quarantine reference. Ensure logs are forwarded to the organisation SIEM.
5. **Prevent bypass** by routing traffic through the enforcement point (network controls, service routing, API gateway policies) and detecting direct-to-destination attempts.
6. **Operationalise governance**: manage enforcement rules via version-controlled configuration (policy-as-code where feasible) with change approvals; define named owners for gateway enforcement and quarantine handling.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Application/Integration Owner**: ensures all cross-domain transfer paths are identified and routed through the enforcement point.
- **Security Engineering / IAM & Security Gateway Team**: implements validation logic, reject-and-quarantine behaviour, and audit logging.
- **SOC/Security Operations**: monitors blocked-transfer events and manages quarantine access/retention.

## Acceptance criteria
- For every tested cross-domain transfer that fails validation, the receiving domain shows **no delivery** (no message/file/payload stored, queued, processed, or acted upon).
- Failed artefacts are only present in the quarantined repository, which is **not reachable** from the receiving domain.
- Gateway logs provide audit evidence for each attempted transfer (attempted → blocked/rejected → not delivered) with consistent identifiers and rule outcomes.
- Bypass attempts are either prevented by routing controls or detected and blocked, with corresponding audit records.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Inventory and map all cross-domain transfer paths for the application to a single boundary enforcement point per path
- [ ] Configure the boundary enforcement point to perform required filtering/validation before any receiving-side acceptance occurs
- [ ] Implement reject-and-quarantine so failed content is rejected and never written to receiving-side inboxes/shares/queues/storage
- [ ] Create an isolated quarantine store with access controls and no network path from the receiving domain
- [ ] Enable audit logging for each attempted transfer including transfer ID, sender identity, source/destination domains, rule outcome, and final decision
- [ ] Enforce routing so transfers cannot bypass the enforcement point and add detections for direct-to-destination attempts
- [ ] Put gateway validation rules under version control with approved change management and assign named owners for enforcement and quarantine handling

### ac-4.32 — Process Requirements for Information Transfer (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure information is passed between different parts of your organisation only after it has been checked properly and is sent on unchanged. Without this, sensitive or restricted information could slip through to the wrong place, or be incorrectly handled because the instructions for how it should be treated were wrong or not completed. To meet this expectation, the organisation must use a transfer process that never alters or filters the message content during the handover, but does check that the “filtering instructions” or labels that describe what should happen are valid. It must only forward the content once the associated filtering step has finished successfully, and then send the approved content to the destination stage. The transfer mechanism should be kept as simple and limited as possible, with enough built-in assurance to show it is working correctly, supported by clear procedures, design and configuration records, and audit logs.

#### Immutable, metadata-validated transfer with success-gated forwarding

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement a transfer mechanism between filter pipelines that treats the information payload as immutable (no content transformation during handover), validates the filtering metadata (labels/instructions) before use, and forwards the payload to the destination only after the associated filtering stage reports successful completion.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-4(32) enhancement requires that, when transferring information between different security domains, the process that transfers information between filter pipelines has **minimum complexity and functionality to provide assurance that the processes operate correctly**. The guidance further requires that the transferring processes have **minimum complexity and functionality** to provide assurance they operate correctly.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define an information-flow contract** for the transfer interface:
   - Payload is an immutable byte sequence (or application-level object) that is passed through without normalisation, redaction, re-encoding, or schema re-mapping.
   - Filtering metadata is carried separately (e.g., an envelope) and includes: correlation identifier, filter policy identifier/version, and destination pipeline identifier.
2. **Implement a small transfer component/service** (or workflow step) that performs only:
   - **Metadata validation**: strict schema validation (types, lengths, allowed values), and integrity/trust validation (e.g., metadata signed by an authorised component or referenced from a trusted policy registry).
   - **Correlation enforcement**: metadata correlation ID must match the payload’s processing context; reject on mismatch.
   - **Success gating**: do not forward the payload until the filtering stage emits an explicit success outcome tied to the same correlation identifier (e.g., event/message `filtering_succeeded` with correlation ID). If the filtering stage fails or times out, do not forward.
   - **Byte-for-byte pass-through**: ensure the transfer component does not alter payload content; if transport framing is required, keep framing outside the payload.
3. **Add audit evidence** for each transfer decision:
   - Record metadata validation result, correlation match result, filtering success/failure outcome, and destination hand-off timestamp.
4. **Keep the transfer logic minimal**:
   - Avoid embedding business logic in the transfer component; enforce allowed metadata values and destination mappings via declarative configuration (policy-as-code) with automated tests.
5. **Operational controls and monitoring**:
   - Alert on and investigate any attempt to forward without a corresponding filtering success signal, metadata validation failures, or correlation mismatches.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Application/Integration Engineer**: implement the transfer component/service and interface contract.
- **Security Engineer**: define metadata trust/integrity approach and validation rules; review policy-as-code.
- **Platform/DevOps Engineer**: integrate logging/audit outputs with SIEM/observability and implement alerting.

## Acceptance criteria
- For every payload transferred between filter pipelines, the system produces audit evidence showing: (a) metadata validation passed, (b) correlation matched, (c) filtering stage reported success for the same correlation ID, and (d) the payload was handed off to the destination.
- The transfer component performs **no payload content transformation** (verified via byte-for-byte comparison in tests and/or instrumentation).
- If metadata validation fails, correlation mismatches, or filtering does not succeed (failure/timeout), the payload is **not** forwarded to the destination pipeline.
- The transfer component code/config is demonstrably minimal (no business logic beyond validation, correlation enforcement, success gating, and pass-through).

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define an immutable payload + separate metadata envelope contract with correlation ID, filter policy version, and destination pipeline identifier
- [ ] Implement a transfer component that performs strict metadata schema validation and trusted integrity checks before any forwarding decision
- [ ] Enforce correlation matching between payload context and metadata correlation ID; reject on mismatch
- [ ] Integrate explicit success gating by requiring a filtering success signal tied to the same correlation ID before forwarding
- [ ] Configure the transfer component to perform byte-for-byte pass-through of the payload (no normalisation/redaction/re-encoding)
- [ ] Add audit logging for metadata validation outcome, correlation match, filtering success/failure, and destination hand-off timestamp; forward logs to SIEM
- [ ] Create automated tests and monitoring alerts for failure modes (forward-without-success, metadata validation failure, correlation mismatch, filtering timeout)

### ac-5 — Separation of Duties (Control)

> **Overview**
>
> This expectation is about making sure the same people do not both hold the keys to powerful systems and also approve or check the evidence that those keys were used properly. Without this separation, one person could quietly change access rights, adjust the rules that govern access, and then review the records in a way that hides wrongdoing, whether accidental or deliberate, across the whole set of systems covered by the organisation’s policy. To meet this requirement, the organisation must clearly identify and document who is responsible for three distinct duties: administering privileged access, making security policy or role changes, and reviewing and approving audit log information. It must then set up system access authorisations so these duties are separated in practice, including ensuring that no single person is authorised to both administer privileged access and handle audit log review or approval, and that the same separation rules also apply to security policy or role changes where required, across all relevant systems and components.

#### Enforce separation of duties for privileged admin, policy change, and audit approval

- **Category:** Manual
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT must be done
Define and enforce separation of duties so that the same individuals (or roles) cannot simultaneously: (1) administer privileged access, (2) make security policy/role changes, and (3) review and approve audit log information.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-5 requires separation of duties to reduce the risk of abuse of authorised privileges and malevolent activity without collusion. It specifically calls out separating: privileged access administration, security policy/role changes, and audit log review/approval, including ensuring security personnel who administer access control functions do not also administer audit functions. The guidance notes that enforcement is supported by AC-2, AC-3, and identity management activities in IA-2, IA-4, and IA-12.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create an organisation-wide Separation of Duties (SoD) matrix** for the in-scope application and all its supporting components (identity, PAM, logging/SIEM, admin consoles, CI/CD tooling, infrastructure management). Use the organisation-defined parameter **ac-05_odp (duties of individuals requiring separation)** to explicitly list the three duty types and the required “no-overlap” combinations.
2. **Assign named roles/teams to each duty type** (e.g., “Privileged Access Administrators”, “Security Policy/Role Change Administrators/Implementers”, “Audit Log Review/Approvers”). Ensure each duty type has distinct authorisation owners.
3. **Implement technical enforcement via identity/PAM and admin tooling**:
   - Configure the identity provider and PAM so that only the “Privileged Access Administrators” group can perform privileged access administration actions (e.g., managing privileged accounts, approving/issuing privileged access, configuring privileged access settings).
   - Configure the audit/SIEM workflow so that only “Audit Log Review/Approvers” can perform audit log review/approval actions (e.g., approving investigation outcomes, sign-off of administrative activity reviews).
   - Configure security policy/role change pathways (e.g., IAM role/group changes, application authorisation changes, security configuration changes) so they are performed by the designated “Policy/Role Change” duty group and are not performed by the audit approvers.
4. **Prevent same-person overlap at the authorisation layer** by using group/role membership constraints and, where supported, automated checks (e.g., deny conflicting role assignments; require exception tickets for any overlap).
5. **Define and govern exceptions**: if overlap is unavoidable, require a documented, time-bound exception approved by an independent authority, with compensating controls (e.g., enhanced monitoring, additional independent review, restricted scope, and shorter review cycles).
6. **Generate audit-ready evidence**: store the SoD matrix, role-to-duty mapping, configuration screenshots/exports of identity/PAM/SIEM authorisation settings, and workflow evidence showing that approvals are performed by the correct duty group.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Information Security Manager / IAM Lead**: owns the SoD matrix and exception governance.
- **Privileged Access Management (PAM) Administrator**: implements privileged access administration role restrictions.
- **Security Operations (SOC) / Audit Review Lead**: configures audit log review/approval workflows and ensures approvers are distinct.
- **Application Owner / Security Architect**: ensures the application’s admin functions and security policy/role change mechanisms align with the SoD matrix.

## Acceptance criteria
- The organisation has a documented SoD matrix covering the three duty types from **ac-05_odp** and the in-scope systems/components.
- No user account is authorised to perform both privileged access administration and audit log review/approval (and, where applicable per the matrix, policy/role changes) without an approved, time-bound exception.
- Identity/PAM and audit/SIEM workflows enforce the separation (conflicting role assignments are blocked or require exception handling).
- Evidence exists showing correct duty-group membership and that audit log approvals are performed by the designated audit approver role, not by privileged access administrators or policy/role change implementers.


**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Document a separation-of-duties matrix defining the three duty types (privileged access administration, security policy/role changes, audit log review/approval) using ac-05_odp
- [ ] Assign named roles/teams to each duty type for the in-scope application and supporting components (identity, PAM, admin consoles, SIEM/log review workflows)
- [ ] Define explicit no-overlap rules and exception handling (time-bound, risk-accepted, compensating controls) for any conflicting duty combinations
- [ ] Implement and verify identity/PAM and audit/SIEM authorisation mappings so conflicting duty memberships are blocked or routed through the exception process
- [ ] Configure audit log review/approval workflows so approvers are distinct from privileged access administrators and policy/role change implementers
- [ ] Collect and retain audit-ready evidence (SoD matrix, role mappings, configuration exports, and workflow approval records) demonstrating enforcement

### ac-6 — Least Privilege (Control)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about giving people and automated tasks only the access they truly need to do their jobs, and nothing more. Without this, a mistake or misuse could let someone view, change, or damage information or systems they were never meant to touch, and automated tasks could cause wider harm if they were compromised. To meet this expectation, the organisation must set out and keep up to date a clear written rule for least-privilege, along with step-by-step procedures for requesting, approving, changing, and removing access. It must maintain a current, documented list of what each person is allowed to do, and ensure systems are configured to match that list. The organisation also needs to document and keep current the system settings, keep relevant records of access activity for review, and maintain a system plan showing how least-privilege is applied, including ensuring background processes run with no higher privileges than required.

#### Identity-driven least privilege with enforced role-to-permission mapping

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement least privilege by engineering an authoritative role/permission model in the enterprise identity layer, mapping each permission to a documented business task, and enforcing that mapping end-to-end (users and processes acting on behalf of users). Ensure technical authorisation and system configuration reflect the current assigned access authorisations list, with auditable evidence.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-6 requires that only authorised accesses necessary to accomplish assigned organisational tasks are allowed, and that least privilege is applied to system processes (including those acting on behalf of users) at privilege levels no higher than necessary. The control guidance further requires applying least privilege to development, implementation, and operation, and creating additional roles/accounts as necessary.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create/maintain a least-privilege policy and role catalogue**: define the rule for users and processes, standard role types (e.g., standard, privileged, service), and require justification per permission.
2. **Build and govern an assigned access authorisations list**: maintain an access matrix mapping job functions (and service identities) to application roles/permissions, including justification and environment scope (prod/test/DR). Treat this as the source of truth for authorisation.
3. **Enforce authorisation via identity and application integration**: implement RBAC/ABAC in the enterprise IdP and ensure the application consumes identity claims/groups/roles so that permissions are granted only through the governed role catalogue (no ad-hoc direct grants).
4. **Implement least-privilege for processes acting on behalf of users**: for API calls, background jobs, and workflow engines, use dedicated service identities and scoped tokens/claims; ensure the application uses the caller’s authorisation context where required, and that service accounts have only the minimum database/API permissions needed.
5. **Establish joiner/mover/leaver and privileged access procedures**: automate provisioning/deprovisioning from HR/job profile changes; require approval and time-bound elevation for privileged roles; remove access promptly on role change/termination.
6. **Generate audit evidence and keep it current**: centralise logs for authentication, role/group changes, and privileged actions; restrict access to audit logs; retain evidence sufficient to demonstrate that current configuration matches the assigned access authorisations list.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Information Security / IAM Governance Lead**: owns AC-6 policy, role catalogue governance, and access review cadence.
- **Identity & Access Management (IAM) Engineer**: implements IdP role/claim mappings, provisioning workflows, and audit logging.
- **Application Owner / Engineering Lead**: ensures application authorisation consumes identity roles and that service/process permissions are scoped.
- **Operations Lead**: ensures operational processes (reviews, deprovisioning, evidence retention) are executed.

## Acceptance criteria
- A documented least-privilege policy and role catalogue exists and is approved by security governance.
- An assigned access authorisations list (access matrix) exists, is current, and is traceable to job functions/service identities with justification.
- The application authorisation model is enforced via identity roles/claims (no unmanaged direct grants for standard access).
- Service identities and background processes use scoped permissions (no standing excessive privileges) and are demonstrably limited to required capabilities.
- Joiner/mover/leaver and privileged elevation workflows are implemented and auditable (role changes and approvals are logged).
- Audit evidence shows that current access assignments and application configuration match the assigned access authorisations list for production and other in-scope environments.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define and approve a least-privilege policy and role catalogue with permission-level justification
- [ ] Create and maintain an assigned access authorisations list (access matrix) mapping job functions/service identities to application permissions by environment
- [ ] Configure the enterprise IdP to enforce RBAC/ABAC so application access is granted only via governed roles/claims
- [ ] Update the application to consume identity roles/claims for authorisation and remove any unmanaged direct permission grants
- [ ] Provision dedicated service identities for background jobs and API/workflow actions with scoped database/API permissions
- [ ] Implement joiner/mover/leaver and time-bound privileged elevation workflows with approval and automated deprovisioning
- [ ] Centralise and retain audit logs for authentication, role/group changes, and privileged actions; restrict log access to authorised reviewers

### ac-6.1 — Authorize Access to Security Functions (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure only a small, named set of trusted roles can access and change the tools that protect your systems and decide who gets in. Without this, the wrong people could quietly weaken key defences, alter the rules that block unwanted traffic, change how suspicious activity is detected, switch off or tamper with what gets recorded for investigations, or modify the information used to manage encryption keys—leaving the organisation exposed to fraud, data theft, and undetected attacks. To meet this expectation, the organisation must explicitly authorise only System Security Officer, Security Administrator, System Administrator, System Programmer, and Privileged Access Manager to manage the security functions used for access decisions, including hardware and software protections and the related firmware settings, as well as the security-relevant configuration data such as firewall and router access rules, detection settings, audit recording settings, and cryptographic key management metadata. The organisation must document who is allowed, keep clear procedures based on giving the minimum access needed, set up system accounts and permissions accordingly, and maintain records showing the authorisations and the system’s audit trail for changes.

#### PAM-enforced authorisation for security functions and security-relevant data

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement an authorisation mechanism so that only the explicitly authorised roles—**System Security Officer, Security Administrator, System Administrator, System Programmer, and Privileged Access Manager**—can access and change **security functions** and **security-relevant information** used for access decisions.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST **AC-6(1)** enhancement requires authorising access for the organisation-defined set of individuals/roles (param **ac-06.01_odp.01**) to the organisation-defined **security functions** (hardware/software/firmware) and **security-relevant information** (e.g., firewall/router ACLs, IDS/IPS parameters, SIEM audit event settings, cryptographic key management metadata). This ensures only authorised personnel can modify protections, audit settings, detection parameters, and key-management metadata.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create an explicit authorisation matrix** mapping each of the five roles to allowed actions (e.g., view vs change) for each security function category: **HSMs, firewalls/routers, IDS/IPS, SIEM, endpoint security agents, key management services** (param **ac-6.1_prm_2**; ac-06.01_odp.02–04).
2. **Centralise privileged access via PAM** so administrative sessions to the management planes/APIs of those security functions are brokered and enforced by role-based policy derived from the matrix.
3. **Enforce least privilege at the target systems** by configuring RBAC/permissions so that only PAM-brokered identities (and only for the permitted actions) can change:
   - **Firewall/router filtering rules and ACLs** (param **ac-06.01_odp.05**)
   - **IDS/IPS detection parameters** (param **ac-06.01_odp.05**)
   - **SIEM audit event settings** (param **ac-06.01_odp.05**)
   - **Cryptographic key management metadata** (param **ac-06.01_odp.05**)
4. **Require strong authentication and controlled elevation** for the five roles (e.g., phishing-resistant MFA and just-in-time elevation) so access is limited to authorised personnel and time-bound where supported.
5. **Make changes auditable and protect the audit configuration** by enabling session recording/command logging for PAM sessions and ensuring that SIEM audit-event settings themselves are changeable only by authorised roles through the same PAM-enforced path.
6. **Document and evidence authorisations**: store the authorisation matrix, PAM policy configuration, and role-to-capability mappings as auditable artefacts tied to the system security plan and change records.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Privileged Access Manager / Security Administrator**: owns PAM policy, role mappings, and enforcement.
- **System Security Officer**: validates that the authorisation matrix covers all required security functions and security-relevant information.
- **System Administrator / System Programmer**: implements target-side RBAC and integrates management endpoints with PAM.
- **Configuration/Change Manager**: ensures changes to security-function authorisations and policies follow approved change control.

## Acceptance criteria
- A documented, approved authorisation matrix exists for **ac-06.01_odp.01** roles covering all security functions in **ac-6.1_prm_2**.
- For each security function (HSM/firewall-router/IDS-SIEM/endpoint/key management), only the authorised roles can perform **change** actions on the security-relevant information in **ac-06.01_odp.05**.
- All administrative sessions that can change security functions are brokered through PAM with session/command logging enabled, and evidence is retained for audit.
- Attempts by non-authorised roles to access or change security functions are denied and logged.
- SIEM audit-event settings and cryptographic key metadata changes are demonstrably restricted to authorised roles via PAM-enforced controls.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Create and approve an authorisation matrix mapping the five roles to allowed actions for each security function category (HSM, firewall/router, IDS/IPS, SIEM, endpoint security agents, key management services).
- [ ] Configure PAM to broker all administrative access to the management planes/APIs of those security functions and enforce role-based policies derived from the matrix.
- [ ] Implement target-side RBAC so only PAM-brokered identities can change firewall/router ACLs, IDS/IPS detection parameters, SIEM audit-event settings, and cryptographic key management metadata.
- [ ] Require phishing-resistant MFA and just-in-time elevation for the five authorised roles when accessing security-function management interfaces.
- [ ] Enable session recording/command logging for privileged actions and forward logs to the SIEM; restrict SIEM audit-event setting changes to authorised roles via PAM.
- [ ] Record evidence of authorisations (matrix, PAM policy versions, and change tickets) and retain it for audit readiness.

### ac-6.2 — Non-privileged Access for Nonsecurity Functions (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This expectation is about making sure that people who can view sensitive security material or use security tools do not use their most powerful access when they are doing ordinary, non-security tasks. The real-world risk is that if someone works inside a highly privileged account and then uses it for everyday activities, a mistake, misuse, or compromise could expose security records or settings, making it easier for an attacker to hide activity or change protections. To meet this expectation, the organisation must clearly document which security-related information and security tools are covered (including audit logs, security monitoring screens, and security configuration settings) and which user accounts or roles have access to them. It must define what counts as non-privileged access and what counts as non-security tasks, then ensure the system only allows access to non-security functions when the person is using a non-privileged account or, where roles are used, an equivalent non-privileged role. The organisation must document the approach, assign responsibilities to the right staff, configure the system to enforce it, and keep records showing that the rule is being followed.

#### Enforce non-privileged role for nonsecurity functions

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement technical enforcement so that users (or roles) granted access to **security functions or security-relevant information** (e.g., audit logs, security monitoring consoles, security configuration management) can access **nonsecurity functions** only while operating under a **non-privileged account or equivalent non-privileged role**.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-6(2)** (Enhancement) requires that users of system accounts/roles with access to **{{ insert: param, ac-06.02_odp }}** use **non-privileged accounts or roles** when accessing **nonsecurity functions**. This limits exposure when operating from within privileged accounts/roles.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the covered scope**: Create an authoritative mapping of application capabilities and UI/API endpoints to categories: (a) **security functions or security-relevant information** per **ac-06.02_odp** (at minimum: audit logs, security monitoring consoles, security configuration management), and (b) **nonsecurity functions**.
2. **Create a role taxonomy with equivalent assurance**: In the enterprise IAM/IdP, define at least two role sets:
   - a **privileged security role** (e.g., Security-Console Operator / Security-Config Manager)
   - a **non-privileged role** for day-to-day access (e.g., Non-Security User)
   Ensure that **role switching** to the non-privileged role provides the **same assurance** for identity/session controls (e.g., same MFA strength, same device/session posture requirements, same session management), while only reducing authorisation scope.
3. **Enforce via authorisation policy**: Configure the application and/or gateway (e.g., API gateway, web app authorisation layer, RBAC middleware) so that:
   - Requests to **nonsecurity functions** are denied when the caller is in a **covered privileged security role**.
   - Access to **nonsecurity functions** is permitted only when the caller is in the **non-privileged role** (or equivalent non-privileged account).
   - For RBAC environments, require an explicit role switch (or separate entitlement) rather than relying on implicit privilege reduction.
4. **Prevent privileged session reuse**: Ensure the enforcement is based on the **active role/entitlement in the current session**, not just the user identity. Use separate application entitlements and/or session policies so that a privileged session cannot be used to reach nonsecurity endpoints.
5. **Generate audit evidence**: Enable and centralise logs capturing:
   - the **active role/account** used for each request to nonsecurity functions
   - the **authorisation decision** (allow/deny) for covered privileged roles attempting nonsecurity access
   - the **role-switch event** (who switched, when, from/to)
   Send to SIEM for audit-ready evidence.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **IAM/Identity Engineer**: role taxonomy, IdP policies, role-switch assurance alignment.
- **Application Security/Backend Engineer**: endpoint-to-function mapping, authorisation enforcement, session/entitlement checks.
- **Security Operations / SOC**: SIEM log configuration, detection of denied attempts, evidence retention.
- **System Owner**: approves the covered scope mapping and nonsecurity function classification.

## Acceptance criteria
- For every endpoint/API classified as **nonsecurity**, access is **denied** when the user is in a role/account that has access to **ac-06.02_odp** security functions/information.
- Users can access nonsecurity functions only after switching to the defined **non-privileged role/account**, and the role switch uses the **same assurance** controls (e.g., MFA/session posture).
- Audit logs show, for nonsecurity access, the **active role/account** and the **authorisation outcome**, and show denied attempts when privileged roles attempt nonsecurity access.
- Evidence is retained per organisational logging/retention requirements and is reviewable by audit.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Create and approve an endpoint/API capability map separating ac-06.02_odp security functions/information from nonsecurity functions
- [ ] Define privileged security roles and equivalent non-privileged roles in the IdP with matching assurance controls (MFA/session posture)
- [ ] Implement authorisation rules so nonsecurity endpoints deny access when the active role is a covered privileged security role
- [ ] Enforce role/entitlement checks at the application or gateway layer based on the active session role (no privileged session reuse)
- [ ] Enable audit logging for active role/account, role-switch events, and allow/deny decisions for nonsecurity access attempts
- [ ] Validate with test cases that privileged-role attempts to nonsecurity functions are denied and nonsecurity access works only via non-privileged roles

### ac-6.3 — Network Access to Privileged Commands (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about allowing trusted staff to carry out important system changes from a distance, but only when there is a genuine, urgent need. Without tight limits, someone could use remote access to run powerful commands when they should not, potentially causing outages, data loss, or unauthorised changes that are hard to trace. The organisation must approve which remote methods are allowed for running system administration commands (such as approved tools like sudo, PowerShell remoting, or vendor administration command tools), and must only grant network-based access to those commands for 24x7 on-call support for time-critical incident response and maintenance when getting to the device locally is impractical. It must also record the reason for granting this remote access in the system’s security plan, including the specific operational need that justifies it.

#### Policy-gated remote execution of approved privileged commands

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement a controlled, auditable mechanism for executing **privileged commands over the network** only when the requester has a **compelling operational need** (e.g., **24x7 on-call incident response/maintenance**) and only via **authorised privileged command tooling**.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-6(3)** (enhancement) requires that network access to **{{ param, ac-06.03_odp.01 }} (privileged commands)** is authorised **only for {{ param, ac-06.03_odp.02 }} (compelling operational needs)**, and that the **rationale** for such access is **documented in the system security plan**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define the exact privileged command set and approved remote execution paths
- Create an inventory per system of:
