  - the **privileged commands** (e.g., sudo-allowed commands, PowerShell remoting endpoints, vendor admin CLI operations)
  - the **approved privileged access tooling** that must be used to run them
  - the **target hosts/services** where those commands may run
- Ensure remote execution is **brokered** so users cannot open ad-hoc admin shells or bypass the approved tooling.

### 2) Enforce authorisation conditions at session start (policy gate)
- Configure the privileged access workflow so a network session can start only when all conditions are met:
  - requester is in an **authorised on-call/incident-response role** (24x7 coverage)
  - requester provides/attaches an **incident/maintenance reference**
  - requester selects/attests **local access impracticality** (the trigger for network access)
  - the session is constrained to the **approved command set** and **approved tooling** only

### 3) Implement least-privilege and strong authentication for privileged execution
- Use role-based access so only the minimum identities can initiate the workflow.
- Require strong authentication (integrated with enterprise identity) and step-up authentication for privileged actions.

### 4) Capture audit evidence that supports the security-plan rationale
- Record, at minimum:
  - who initiated the session, when, from where
  - which approved privileged command(s) were executed
  - the attached incident/maintenance reference
  - the stated **local access impracticality** rationale
  - session metadata and command-level logs

### 5) Update the system security plan with the documented rationale
- For each system, update the security plan to document:
  - the operational need category (**24x7 on-call incident response/maintenance**)
  - why **local access is impractical** for those scenarios
  - the approved privileged command tooling and command scope
  - how the policy gate and logging provide auditable justification

## WHO (role responsible)
- **System Owner / Security Architect**: approves the privileged command scope and security-plan rationale.
- **Privileged Access/PAM Administrator**: implements the broker/workflow, command allow-lists, and session gating.
- **On-call Manager / Incident Response Lead**: maintains the on-call role membership and validates operational need definitions.
- **Security/Compliance**: reviews evidence and verifies security-plan updates.

## Acceptance criteria
- Network-based execution of privileged commands is possible **only** through the approved tooling and **only** for users/roles meeting the defined **compelling operational need**.
- Every privileged network session includes an auditable record of:
  - incident/maintenance reference
  - local access impracticality rationale
  - executed commands within the approved set
- The system security plan contains the documented rationale required by AC-6(3), referencing the implemented workflow and logging.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Inventory and document per system the approved privileged commands and the approved remote execution tooling that must be used
- [ ] Configure the privileged access broker/workflow to allow network sessions only for the defined on-call/incident-response role
- [ ] Implement a session-start policy gate requiring an incident/maintenance reference and an explicit local-access-impracticality rationale
- [ ] Restrict execution to an allow-listed command set so users cannot run ad-hoc shells or unauthorised privileged commands
- [ ] Enable and retain command-level audit logging and session metadata sufficient to evidence the AC-6(3) rationale
- [ ] Update each affected system security plan to document the operational need (24x7 on-call) and why local access is impractical, referencing the implemented controls

### ac-6.4 — Separate Processing Domains (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about keeping different parts of a system clearly walled off from each other so people only get the permissions they truly need in the specific area they are working on. Without this separation, a user who has extra rights in one area could potentially use them to reach other areas, or even the underlying system that runs everything, increasing the chance of unauthorised changes, data exposure, or wider damage if something goes wrong. To meet this expectation, the organisation must design and set up the system using separate, isolated processing areas (such as separate virtual machines, separate physical environments, or other built-in isolation methods) and ensure privileges do not carry over between these areas or to the underlying platform. It must also document the rules for access and least privilege, keep clear system design and configuration records showing the separation is in place, maintain audit records of domain access and privilege changes, and include the approach and responsibilities in the system security plan, with named people responsible for defining least-privilege access, supported by information security and system developers.

#### Isolated VM domains for fine-grained privilege separation

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement separate processing domains using virtualisation (e.g., multiple hardened VMs or VM clusters) so that user privileges can be granted within a specific domain while being restricted from other domains and from the underlying physical/management host.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-6(4) enhancement requires providing separate processing domains to enable finer-grained allocation of user privileges. The guidance explicitly includes using virtualization techniques to permit additional privileges within a virtual machine while restricting privileges to other virtual machines or to the underlying physical machine.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define domain boundaries** by mapping business functions and administrative functions to separate VMs (e.g., Domain A: application runtime, Domain B: admin tooling, Domain C: data services; optionally separate Dev/Test/DR).
2. **Harden and restrict the management plane** so only a dedicated privileged admin identity group can access the hypervisor management interfaces (console/API/SSH/RDP), with strong MFA and conditional access; ensure domain users cannot reach hypervisor credentials or management sockets.
3. **Enforce cross-domain separation** using virtual network segmentation (security groups/NSGs/VLAN/VXLAN/SDN) with default-deny rules and explicit allow-lists for only required traffic; block lateral administrative protocols between domains.
4. **Implement least-privilege inside each domain** by granting local OS/app admin rights only to the relevant domain-specific admin roles; ensure “Domain A admin” cannot administer “Domain B” (no shared admin accounts, no shared local admin groups across domains).
5. **Use configuration-as-code and drift controls** to keep domain separation settings consistent over time (VM hardening baselines, network rules, RBAC role assignments).
6. **Generate audit evidence** by enabling and centralising logs for (a) domain access attempts, (b) privilege/role changes within each domain, and (c) management-plane actions; retain logs per enterprise policy and forward to SIEM.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Infrastructure/Cloud Engineer**: implement VM domain layout, hypervisor management-plane restrictions, and network segmentation.
- **Application Owner / Security Engineer**: define domain-to-function mapping and domain-specific admin roles.
- **IAM Administrator**: configure identity groups, MFA/conditional access, and role assignments.
- **SOC/SIEM Analyst**: validate audit log completeness and alerting for cross-domain boundary violations.

## Acceptance criteria
- Distinct processing domains exist as separate VMs (or VM clusters) with documented boundary rationale.
- Hypervisor management-plane access is restricted to authorised admin identities only; standard domain users cannot access hypervisor management interfaces.
- Network policy is default-deny between domains with explicit, minimal allow rules; cross-domain administrative traffic is blocked.
- Privileged roles are scoped per domain; there are no shared admin accounts/groups that grant privileges across domains.
- Centralised audit logs show privilege changes and domain access attempts, including management-plane actions, and can be used to demonstrate enforcement of domain boundaries.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Map application and administrative functions to separate VM-based processing domains and document the boundary rationale
- [ ] Restrict hypervisor management-plane access to a dedicated admin identity group using MFA and conditional access
- [ ] Implement default-deny virtual network segmentation between domains and add only required, minimal allow rules
- [ ] Create domain-scoped OS/app admin roles and remove any shared admin accounts or cross-domain admin group memberships
- [ ] Deploy VM hardening and RBAC/network settings via configuration-as-code and enable drift detection/continuous validation
- [ ] Enable detailed audit logging for domain access, privilege/role changes, and management-plane actions; forward to SIEM and verify retention

### ac-6.5 — Privileged Accounts (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This expectation is about keeping the keys to the system tightly held by only the right people. If ordinary staff could use powerful administrator or super-user accounts, they could accidentally or deliberately change security settings, access sensitive information, or leave the system in a vulnerable state. To prevent this, the organisation must ensure that every privileged account on the system, including any built-in super-user accounts, is usable only by the authorised system administrator role and the authorised information system security officer (ISSO) role. The organisation must also be able to prove this in practice by maintaining clear written rules for who can have privileged access, step-by-step procedures for granting and removing the minimum access needed, an up-to-date list of all system-generated privileged accounts, a current list of the authorised administrators, documentation of the system settings that enforce these limits, and retained records showing privileged activity. Finally, it must include this approach in the system’s security plan and keep related supporting records so the organisation can demonstrate ongoing, limited use.

#### Restrict privileged accounts to System Admin and ISSO roles

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Restrict every privileged account on the system (including any built-in/super-user accounts and any accounts with administrator-equivalent rights) so they are usable only by the authorised **System Administrator** role and the authorised **Information System Security Officer (ISSO)** role (as defined in `ac-06.05_odp`).

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-6(5)** (enhancement) requires that privileged accounts on the system are restricted to the organisation-defined personnel or roles (`ac-06.05_odp`). This prevents day-to-day users from accessing privileged information or privileged functions and ensures the restriction is enforceable and demonstrable.

## HOW (specific approach)
Implement an identity- and configuration-enforced privileged access model that:
1) **Defines the authorised privileged roles** in the enterprise identity provider (IdP) and maps all privileged access to only those roles.
2) **Eliminates or tightly constrains standing privileged rights** by ensuring privileged capabilities are granted only through controlled role membership (and, where supported, time-bound elevation).
3) **Disables or removes non-authorised privileged paths** (e.g., local admin group membership for non-privileged users; any built-in super-user accounts that can be used outside the authorised role model).
4) **Enforces the restriction technically** on the system by configuring administrative interfaces and OS/application admin functions to accept only authenticated identities that are members of the authorised roles.
5) **Maintains auditable inventories and evidence**:
   - Current list of system-generated privileged accounts (including built-in accounts) and their enforced status.
   - Current list of privileged account administrators/personnel aligned to the authorised roles.
   - Retained audit logs showing privileged session establishment and privileged actions.
6) **Keeps the control current** via periodic review and change-management integration (so role mappings and privileged account states cannot drift).

### Concrete implementation steps (typical enterprise application / managed infrastructure)
- **IdP role model**: Create/confirm two privileged roles/groups in the IdP: **System Administrator** and **ISSO** (the values for `ac-06.05_odp`). Ensure all privileged access grants map exclusively to these roles.
- **Privileged account inventory**: Produce an inventory of all privileged accounts on the system, including:
  - Built-in/super-user accounts (e.g., OS/application admin accounts, default admin accounts, service accounts with admin rights).
  - Any local/domain groups that confer administrator-equivalent rights.
- **Technical enforcement**:
  - Configure the system’s administrative entry points (admin consoles, management APIs, SSH/RDP/remote management endpoints, database admin access paths, and any application “admin” functions) to authorise only identities that are members of the IdP roles.
  - Remove standing admin rights from non-privileged identities; require elevation through the controlled role model (and, where available, JIT/JEA or privileged session brokering).
  - Disable or lock built-in privileged accounts where feasible; if they must exist for operational reasons, ensure they cannot be used except via the authorised role-controlled path (e.g., break-glass accounts are restricted and separately governed).
- **Evidence and auditability**:
  - Forward privileged session logs and privileged action logs to the SIEM and retain per enterprise logging retention policy.
  - Maintain a current export of role membership for the two authorised roles and correlate it to privileged account usage.
  - Document the enforcement configuration (admin endpoint authorisation settings, group/role mappings, and any account disablement/lock status).
- **Governance**:
  - Integrate privileged role assignment and removal into the organisation’s access request/approval workflow with periodic recertification.
  - Include this restriction in the system security plan and ensure it is reviewed during scheduled security reviews.

## WHO
- **System Owner / Application Owner**: accountable for ensuring admin functions are configured to enforce the restriction.
- **Identity & Access Management (IAM) Lead**: implements and maintains the IdP role model and role-to-permission mappings.
- **ISSO**: owns the privileged access governance, approves privileged role membership, and validates evidence.
- **Infrastructure/Platform Administrators**: implement technical changes (disable/lock built-in accounts, adjust admin endpoint authorisation, ensure logging).
- **Security Operations / SOC**: ensures privileged activity logging is collected, monitored, and retained.

## Acceptance criteria
- All privileged accounts (including built-in/super-user accounts) are either disabled/locked or enforceably usable only by identities in the **System Administrator** and **ISSO** roles defined in `ac-06.05_odp`.
- Non-authorised identities cannot obtain privileged access via any administrative interface, management API, or remote management path.
- A current inventory exists showing all privileged accounts and their enforced status.
- A current list of privileged administrators/personnel exists and maps to the authorised roles.
- Privileged session and privileged action logs are generated, forwarded to SIEM, and retained; sample evidence demonstrates that privileged actions are attributable to authorised role members.
- The system security plan references this restriction and the enforcement mechanism, and periodic review is scheduled/operational.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define and publish the authorised privileged roles in the enterprise IdP as the System Administrator role and the ISSO role (per `ac-06.05_odp`).
- [ ] Inventory all privileged accounts on the system, including built-in/super-user accounts and any accounts/groups with administrator-equivalent rights.
- [ ] Disable, lock, or reconfigure privileged accounts so they are usable only through identities mapped to the authorised roles.
- [ ] Configure all administrative entry points (admin consoles, management APIs, remote management, database admin paths, and application admin functions) to authorise only the authorised IdP roles.
- [ ] Remove standing privileged rights from non-authorised users and enforce controlled elevation/role membership for privileged actions.
- [ ] Implement and verify privileged session and privileged action logging, forwarding to SIEM, with retention aligned to enterprise requirements.
- [ ] Create and maintain audit-ready evidence: current privileged account inventory, current authorised privileged role membership export, and a documented mapping to enforcement configuration.

### ac-6.6 — Privileged Access by Non-organizational Users (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This expectation is about making sure only trusted people can do high-level, sensitive system tasks, and that no one outside the organisation is ever given those powers. Without this, a contractor, guest, or other non-employee-equivalent person could accidentally or deliberately change security settings, access confidential information, or take control of the system, creating serious financial, legal, and reputational harm. To meet this requirement, the organisation must clearly define what counts as privileged access and what a non-organisational user is, then write and follow rules that explicitly block non-organisational users from being granted any privileged role or account. The organisation must keep up-to-date lists of non-organisational users and any system-created privileged accounts, configure the system so these privileges cannot be assigned to non-organisational users, include the restriction in its system plans, and keep audit records that show attempts are blocked. It should also regularly check that privileged access assignments match the lists and review the audit records to confirm no successful privileged access occurred.

#### Block non-organisational users from privileged roles via IdP deny

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement an identity-enforced prohibition so that **non-organisational users** (i.e., not employees or employee-equivalent individuals such as contractors/guests without equivalent status) **cannot be granted or obtain privileged access** to the application and its administrative functions.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-6(6)** enhancement requires: **“Prohibit privileged access to the system by non-organisational users.”** The guidance clarifies that organisational users include contractors/guests only when the organisation has granted them **equivalent employee status** via defined criteria (e.g., need-to-know, citizenship, relationship to the organisation). Therefore, privileged access must be technically prevented for everyone who lacks that status.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define privileged access in the application’s access model**: treat as privileged any role/account that can perform administrative/security-impacting actions (e.g., admin console access, role/permission management, configuration changes, audit-log configuration, secret/key management, production data access with elevated scope).
2. **Model “employee-equivalent status” as an authoritative identity attribute** in the enterprise IdP (e.g., `isEmployeeEquivalent=true`, `equivalentStatusApproved=true`, `sponsorOrg`, `needToKnowApproved`). Source values from the organisation’s HR/workforce and access-approval workflow, not from user self-service.
3. **Create an IdP-enforced deny-by-default mapping**:
   - Map privileged application roles only to an **organisational-only** group (e.g., `App-Privileged-OrgOnly`).
   - Add an explicit conditional access/authorisation rule that **denies** privileged role claims/tokens when `isEmployeeEquivalent != true` (or when the user is not a member of the organisational-only group).
4. **Enforce at the application authorisation layer** (defence in depth):
   - Validate incoming identity claims/roles and reject any request that attempts to use privileged roles without the required employee-equivalent attribute/group membership.
   - Ensure privileged endpoints (admin UI, privileged APIs) require privileged authorisation checks that cannot be bypassed by client-side role data.
5. **Integrate privileged elevation via PAM (where applicable)**: if the application uses a privileged session model or privileged admin accounts, require step-up approval through PAM and ensure PAM only allows elevation for identities that satisfy the employee-equivalent attribute/group.
6. **Produce audit evidence of enforcement**:
   - Log and retain events for: privileged role assignment attempts, denied access due to non-organisational status, and successful privileged access.
   - Forward logs to the SIEM for review and correlation.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Identity & Access Management (IAM) lead**: IdP attribute design, group/role mappings, conditional access rules.
- **Application security/engineering lead**: application-side privileged authorisation enforcement and privileged endpoint protection.
- **Security operations (SOC/SIEM) lead**: log configuration, retention, and alerting for denied privileged access attempts.
- **Governance/HR-access approver**: maintains the process that grants employee-equivalent status.

## Acceptance criteria
- A non-organisational user (lacking employee-equivalent status) cannot obtain privileged access to the application admin functions or privileged APIs, even if authenticated.
- IdP logs show privileged role/claim issuance is denied when employee-equivalent status is absent.
- Application logs show authorisation denials for non-organisational users attempting privileged actions.
- Successful privileged access is only observed for users with employee-equivalent status (as evidenced by claims/groups in logs).

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define privileged access roles/endpoints for the application and document them in the access control policy
- [ ] Create an authoritative IdP attribute (e.g., isEmployeeEquivalent) and ensure it is set only via the approved employee-equivalent status workflow
- [ ] Configure IdP conditional access/authorisation rules to deny issuance of privileged role claims/tokens when employee-equivalent status is absent
- [ ] Implement application-side authorisation checks that reject privileged actions unless required privileged claims/groups and employee-equivalent status are present
- [ ] Integrate PAM (if used for admin sessions) so elevation is only possible for employee-equivalent identities
- [ ] Enable and retain audit logs for privileged access denials and successful privileged actions, and forward them to the SIEM

### ac-6.7 — Review of User Privileges (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure only the right people and automated services keep the highest levels of access, and that this is still justified. Over time, roles change, systems are updated or retired, and business needs shift; if elevated access is left in place without regular checks, it increases the risk that someone can do more than they should, either by mistake or misuse, potentially leading to unauthorised changes or damage. To meet this expectation, the organisation must review, at least every 90 days, the access privileges held by privileged users, system administrators, and service accounts with elevated access, confirm for each one whether it is still needed for the organisation’s mission and business needs, and promptly reassign or remove privileges that are no longer required. The organisation must also keep clear records of what was reviewed, the reasons for decisions, and any privilege removals or reassignments, supported by its documented access rules and least-privilege approach.

#### 90-day privileged access recertification with automated entitlement changes

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement a recurring (at least every 90 days) review of assigned privileges for **privileged users, system administrators, and elevated service accounts**, including any **system-generated roles/classes**. Each review must **revalidate the ongoing need** for each privilege and, where the need cannot be revalidated, **promptly remove or reassign** privileges.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-6(7) enhancement guidance requires that the organisation periodically reviews assigned user privileges to determine whether the rationale for assignment remains valid, and takes appropriate corrective actions when the need cannot be revalidated. The organisation-defined parameters specify a **review frequency of every 90 days** and define the **roles/classes in scope** (privileged users, system administrators, and service accounts with elevated access).

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define in-scope privilege sources and mappings**: Use the enterprise identity/access management system as the authoritative source of privileged entitlements. Map privileged users, system administrators, and elevated service accounts (including non-human/service identities) and ensure system-generated roles/classes are included via entitlement-to-role/class mappings.
2. **Create a 90-day recertification campaign**: Configure the governance workflow to run on a 90-day cadence and to generate review tasks for each in-scope entitlement/role/class assignment.
3. **Require evidence-based revalidation**: For each entitlement, require the reviewer to select an outcome (e.g., “still required” vs “remove/reduce”) and provide a **mandatory rationale** tied to current mission/business need (not just confirmation that access exists). Reject/flag records with missing or non-meaningful rationale.
4. **Automate corrective actions with guardrails**: When “remove/reduce” is selected (or when revalidation cannot be completed), automatically trigger entitlement removal/reduction where technically feasible; otherwise, open an access change request that is approved and executed within the organisation’s defined corrective-action SLA.
5. **Record audit evidence per cycle**: Store an audit-ready evidence pack for each campaign including: entitlement/role/class reviewed, reviewer identity, review timestamp, rationale, outcome, and the resulting entitlement delta (removed/reduced/revalidated). Retain evidence according to organisational retention requirements.
6. **Operationalise ownership and escalation**: Assign named responsibilities (e.g., application/system owner or delegated business owner as reviewer; security/identity governance team as oversight) and implement escalation for overdue reviews and for unresolved “remove/reduce” actions.
7. **Integrate with provisioning and monitoring**: Ensure the entitlement changes are applied through the standard provisioning mechanism and that administrative actions are logged and forwarded to central monitoring/SIEM for traceability.

## WHO
- **Identity governance lead / IAM engineer**: configures the recertification workflow, mappings, automation, and evidence capture.
- **System/application owners and delegated business owners**: perform the privilege revalidation and provide rationale.
- **Information security / security governance**: oversees policy alignment, monitors completion, and reviews exceptions.
- **Change management / access provisioning team**: ensures corrective actions are executed promptly when automation is not possible.

## Acceptance criteria
- A recertification campaign runs **at least every 90 days** for all in-scope categories (privileged users, system administrators, elevated service accounts, and system-generated roles/classes).
- For each reviewed entitlement, reviewers provide a **non-empty, evidence-based rationale** and an explicit outcome.
- For any entitlement where the need cannot be revalidated, the system either **removes/reduces privileges promptly** (automation) or generates an approved change that is executed within the defined corrective-action SLA.
- For each cycle, an **audit-ready evidence pack** exists showing reviewed entitlements, reviewer, timestamps, rationale, outcomes, and entitlement deltas.
- Demonstrated coverage is verifiable via an export/report for the most recent cycle.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Identify and document all in-scope privileged users, system administrators, elevated service accounts, and system-generated roles/classes from authoritative IAM sources
- [ ] Configure an identity governance recertification workflow to run on a 90-day cadence for each in-scope entitlement/role/class assignment
- [ ] Implement mandatory reviewer fields for evidence-based rationale and explicit outcomes (still required vs remove/reduce) with validation to prevent blank/invalid submissions
- [ ] Integrate the workflow with the standard provisioning mechanism to automatically remove/reduce entitlements when “remove/reduce” is selected, or to open an approved change when automation is not possible
- [ ] Generate and retain an audit-ready evidence pack per cycle capturing reviewer, timestamps, rationale, outcomes, and entitlement deltas
- [ ] Define escalation paths and an SLA for corrective actions and overdue reviews, and monitor campaign completion through dashboards/reports

### ac-6.8 — Privilege Levels for Code Execution (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This expectation is about stopping risky or unknown code from quietly getting more power than the person who started it. If an unsigned or unapproved script, program, or installer (including PowerShell, batch files, and MSI installers) is launched using an elevated prompt, it must not end up running with higher privileges than the user who clicked to start it; otherwise, a normal user could indirectly gain access they are not authorised to have, potentially leading to unauthorised changes, data theft, or system takeover. To meet this, the organisation must keep a documented list of software that is not allowed to run with higher privileges, including anything unsigned or not approved, and set clear rules that enforce this “no privilege gain” behaviour even when elevated prompts are used. Named roles must be responsible for keeping the list and rules accurate, information security must review and approve them, system designs and settings must be documented and configured to enforce the restriction, and audit records must be kept to show attempts are blocked and outcomes are recorded.

#### Block unsigned/unapproved elevated execution (no privilege gain)

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Prevent execution of the organisation-defined **prohibited software** (all unsigned or unapproved scripts, binaries, and installers, including **PowerShell, batch, and MSI**) from resulting in **higher privilege levels** than the user who launched it, even when the user starts the software via an elevated prompt.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-6(8)** (enhancement) requires: *“Prevent the following software from executing at higher privilege levels than users executing the software: {{ insert: param, ac-06.08_odp }}.”* The organisation-defined parameter **ac-06.08_odp** specifies the prohibited software categories.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
Implement a technical enforcement pattern that is **deny-by-default** for the prohibited categories and ensures **no privilege gain** from elevation:
1. **Create/maintain an authoritative prohibited list** for **ac-06.08_odp** covering unsigned/unapproved PowerShell scripts, batch files, binaries, and MSI installers. Each entry must include the identification method used for enforcement (e.g., publisher/signing certificate, hash, or approval identifier) and the environments it applies to.
2. **Configure endpoint application control** (or equivalent enterprise execution control) to **block** execution of prohibited items regardless of token elevation. Enforce rules so that:
   - Unsigned/unapproved PowerShell/batch/MSI/binaries are **denied**.
   - Elevation (e.g., “Run as administrator”) does not create an exception path that allows prohibited content to run.
3. **Eliminate common privilege-escalation execution paths** by policy/configuration hardening:
   - Block or restrict execution mechanisms that commonly bypass allowlisting (e.g., PowerShell execution policy bypasses, script host launch patterns, MSI execution from user-writable locations) consistent with the chosen application control product’s capabilities.
   - Ensure “run as SYSTEM/admin” patterns are not used for prohibited content; if elevation is required for legitimate admin tasks, route through controlled admin workflows (approved tooling) rather than arbitrary scripts/installers.
4. **Centralise and automate rule deployment** using configuration management so the prohibited list and enforcement rules are consistently applied across production/test/DR endpoints.
5. **Log and retain audit evidence** for every blocked attempt and relevant context (user, process lineage, command line, file identity/signature status, elevation context). Forward events to the SIEM for reporting and alerting on repeated attempts.
6. **Validate with repeatable tests** in each environment: attempt to run prohibited unsigned/unapproved PowerShell scripts, batch files, binaries, and MSI installers from elevated prompts and confirm they are blocked and do not perform privileged actions.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Information Security (lead):** owns the prohibited list definition, approval criteria, and review/oversight.
- **Endpoint/Platform Engineering (implementer):** configures and maintains application control policies and deployment automation.
- **Change Management (governance):** ensures updates to approved/prohibited software and enforcement rules follow the organisation’s change process.
- **SOC/SIEM team (operations):** ensures logs are collected, retained, and monitored for blocked elevated execution attempts.

## Acceptance criteria
- For each prohibited category in **ac-06.08_odp** (unsigned/unapproved PowerShell, batch, binaries, MSI), execution from an elevated prompt is **blocked** (or fails) and does **not** result in higher privilege than the launching user.
- Application control policy is **deny-by-default** for the prohibited categories and is consistently deployed to all relevant endpoints/environments.
- Audit logs show blocked attempts with sufficient context to demonstrate enforcement (user identity, elevation context, file identity/signature status, and process lineage).
- Evidence exists that the prohibited list and enforcement rules are reviewed/approved by Information Security and are updated via controlled change management.
- Test results confirm that prohibited items cannot execute privileged actions when launched from elevated prompts.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define and approve the prohibited elevated-execution software list for ac-06.08_odp (unsigned/unapproved PowerShell, batch, binaries, MSI) with the enforcement identifiers used (hash/signature/approval ID).
- [ ] Configure enterprise endpoint application control in deny-by-default mode to block prohibited items regardless of elevated prompt context.
- [ ] Harden execution pathways to prevent bypasses (e.g., restrict script/batch/installer execution patterns that circumvent allowlisting) consistent with the application control capability.
- [ ] Deploy application control policies centrally via configuration management to all relevant production/test/DR endpoints and validate policy consistency.
- [ ] Enable and forward audit logging for blocked elevated execution attempts (user, elevation context, process lineage, file signature/hash status) to the SIEM and retain per policy.
- [ ] Run repeatable validation tests: launch prohibited PowerShell/batch/binary/MSI from elevated prompts and confirm execution is blocked and no privileged actions occur.

### ac-6.9 — Log Use of Privileged Functions (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure the organisation keeps a clear record of every time a high-authority action is carried out on its systems. If privileged actions are misused—either by someone who should not have done it, by an insider making a mistake, or by an attacker who has taken over an account—damage can be hard to spot and even harder to investigate later. To meet this expectation, the organisation must first decide and document exactly which privileged actions must be tracked, and which types of events count as the official records to capture. The system then needs to automatically write an entry to its audit records every time one of those listed actions is executed, and keep those records available for later review. Finally, the organisation must assign responsibility for reviewing these logs to the right people, and document in its security plan how the logging and review will work.

#### Audit and centralise privileged-function execution events

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement reliable audit logging for the execution of **defined privileged functions** (high-authority actions) across the application and its supporting infrastructure, and ensure those events are **centralised, reviewable, and retained**.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-6(9)** (enhancement) requires: **"Log the execution of privileged functions."** The guidance highlights that misuse of privileged functions by authorised users, insiders, or compromised accounts is a serious and ongoing concern; logging supports detection and investigation.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create and maintain a Privileged-Function Register** for this application/system that enumerates the privileged functions to be logged (e.g., admin console actions, role/permission changes, service start/stop, configuration changes, key/certificate operations, database admin actions, IAM role assignments, and any other actions your system treats as high-authority).
2. **Map each privileged function to authoritative audit event sources** available in your environment (e.g., OS audit trails, application audit logs, database audit logs, cloud control-plane audit logs). Define the **audited event types** that constitute “execution of privileged functions”.
3. **Enable and harden auditing** so privileged-function execution generates audit records that cannot be bypassed by normal operations:
   - Ensure audit policies are enabled for the required event IDs/actions.
   - Restrict who can modify audit settings.
   - Protect audit logs from deletion/clearing without generating corresponding evidence.
   - Ensure time synchronisation (e.g., NTP) for accurate correlation.
4. **Centralise the privileged-function audit events** to the enterprise SIEM/log platform using authenticated forwarding, and configure the pipeline for **append-only/tamper-evident** storage where supported.
5. **Validate coverage with evidence-based tests**: execute each privileged function in a controlled manner in test/staging and confirm that at least one corresponding centralised audit event is generated, correctly attributed to the initiating identity (user/service account), and includes required context (timestamp, host/service, action, and result).
6. **Assign log review ownership and document it** in the system security plan: define who reviews privileged-function execution logs (e.g., SOC for triage and IAM/Platform owners for confirmation), review frequency, and escalation paths.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Owner / Platform Owner**: maintains the Privileged-Function Register and event mapping.
- **Security Engineering / SIEM Engineer**: implements central log ingestion, hardening, and tamper-evident/retention configuration.
- **System Administrators / Cloud Engineers**: enable and maintain native audit sources (OS/app/DB/cloud).
- **SOC Analyst / IAM/Platform Owner**: performs privileged-function log review and escalation.

## Acceptance criteria
- A documented **Privileged-Function Register** exists and is current for the application/system.
- For each privileged function in the register, there is a **documented mapping** to one or more authoritative audit event types.
- In test/staging, executing each privileged function results in **centralised audit events** that clearly indicate privileged-function execution and are attributable to the initiating identity.
- Audit logging is **hardened** (audit settings are restricted; log tampering/deletion is prevented or produces evidence).
- Privileged-function audit events are **retained** according to organisational requirements and are accessible for review.
- The **system security plan** documents: what is logged, where events are sent, retention, review responsibilities, and escalation workflow.


**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Create and approve a Privileged-Function Register for the application/system and keep it under change control
- [ ] Map each privileged function to authoritative audit event sources and define the audited event types that represent execution
- [ ] Enable native auditing for the mapped event types across OS/app/DB/cloud components and restrict changes to audit configuration
- [ ] Configure authenticated forwarding of privileged-function audit events to the enterprise SIEM/log platform with tamper-evident/append-only storage where available
- [ ] Run test executions of each privileged function and verify centralised audit events include initiating identity, action, result, and accurate timestamps
- [ ] Document privileged-function logging, retention, and log-review responsibilities in the system security plan and assign named reviewers

### ac-6.10 — Prohibit Non-privileged Users from Executing Privileged Functions (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This expectation is about stopping ordinary users from doing powerful, high-risk actions that can weaken the protections around your systems. Without it, someone without the right authority could disable or bypass key safeguards, create hidden system accounts, run checks that reveal tampering, or take control of the secret keys used to protect sensitive data—making it easier for fraud, theft, or undetected misuse to happen. To meet this expectation, the organisation must clearly document which actions are considered privileged and exactly which authorised people are allowed to perform each one, and it must define who counts as non-privileged. The system then needs to be set up so non-privileged users are blocked from attempting any of those privileged actions, including attempts to bypass security or malicious-code protections. The organisation must also keep evidence that the settings are in place, record attempts in system logs, include this in its security planning, and test that non-privileged attempts are consistently denied while authorised privileged users can perform the permitted actions.

#### Block non-privileged users from privileged actions via JIT elevation

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement technical enforcement so non-privileged users cannot execute privileged functions (e.g., disabling/bypassing security/privacy controls, creating system accounts, performing integrity checks, and managing cryptographic key management activities). Privileged actions must require explicit, auditable elevation rather than standing authorisation.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-6(10)** (enhancement) requires that **non-privileged users are prevented from executing privileged functions**. The guidance clarifies that privileged functions include actions that disable, circumvent, or alter implemented security/privacy controls, establish system accounts, perform system integrity checks, and administer cryptographic key management activities (and also includes bypassing intrusion detection/prevention or malicious code protection mechanisms). Enforcement is supported by **AC-3** (least privilege / access control), but AC-6(10) specifically expects the *technical block* for non-privileged execution.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create a privileged function catalogue per application/system** covering the privileged actions relevant to the environment (admin console actions, security control toggles, service account/system account creation, integrity check execution, cryptographic key management operations, and any ability to bypass IDS/IPS or malicious code protections).
2. **Map each privileged function to dedicated privileged roles** in the enterprise identity provider (IdP) and ensure non-privileged users cannot be assigned these roles.
3. **Enforce step-up/JIT elevation for every privileged function** using a PAM-style pattern (or equivalent privileged access workflow):
   - privileged roles require phishing-resistant step-up authentication;
   - privileged access is time-bound (just-in-time) and approval-gated for high-risk categories (e.g., key management, security control changes, bypass actions).
4. **Harden the execution paths** so non-privileged users cannot reach privileged execution surfaces:
   - remove local admin/system rights from standard accounts;
   - restrict admin endpoints, API routes, and operational consoles to privileged roles;
   - disable or tightly restrict mechanisms that allow privilege escalation (e.g., service installation, changing security tooling configuration, or running privileged scripts).
5. **Make denials auditable and actionable**: ensure the IdP/PAM and application/infrastructure emit logs for both successful and denied attempts, including user identity, attempted privileged function, target system/component, timestamp, and outcome; forward to SIEM.
6. **Verify with negative testing**: run scripted attempts from a non-privileged test identity against each privileged function and confirm the action is blocked and the corresponding denial event is recorded; also confirm authorised privileged users can perform only the permitted functions.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Security Lead / IAM Engineer**: define privileged function catalogue and role mappings.
- **Privileged Access Management (PAM) / Identity Team**: implement JIT elevation, step-up authentication, and approval workflows.
- **Platform/Infrastructure Engineers**: harden admin surfaces and execution paths (admin endpoints, service accounts, OS/container permissions, CI/CD controls).
- **SOC/SIEM Analyst**: validate logging, alerting, and retention for denied privileged attempts.

## Acceptance criteria
- For every privileged function in the catalogue, a non-privileged user attempt is **technically denied** (no successful execution) and produces an **audit log entry** with required fields.
- Privileged functions can be executed only by identities granted the corresponding privileged role **and** only during an approved/elevated session (JIT/step-up), with evidence retained.
- Authorised privileged users can perform permitted privileged functions without requiring standing broad privileges for non-privileged users.
- Test evidence exists showing both **denial** (non-privileged) and **success** (authorised privileged) outcomes for each privileged function.


**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Create a privileged function catalogue for the application and its integrated security/key management components
- [ ] Map each privileged function to dedicated privileged roles in the enterprise IdP and ensure non-privileged users cannot be assigned those roles
- [ ] Implement JIT/step-up elevation (PAM-style) so privileged functions require time-bound elevation and phishing-resistant authentication
- [ ] Restrict privileged execution surfaces (admin UI, API routes, operational consoles, scripts, and service endpoints) to privileged roles only
- [ ] Remove or tightly restrict privilege escalation paths (e.g., local admin rights, ability to install/modify security tooling, system account creation by non-privileged users)
- [ ] Configure logging for both successful and denied privileged attempts and forward events to SIEM with user identity, attempted function, target, and outcome
- [ ] Run negative and positive test cases for each privileged function and retain evidence of denial and authorised success

### ac-7 — Unsuccessful Logon Attempts (Control)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about stopping repeated login guessing by limiting how many times a person can get their sign-in details wrong in a short period. Without it, an attacker could keep trying different usernames and passwords until they eventually hit on the right combination, potentially gaining unauthorised access to accounts or systems. The organisation must set things so that each user is allowed no more than 5 consecutive invalid logon attempts within any rolling 15-minute period, counting both local sign-ins and sign-ins made over the network. Once that maximum is exceeded, the system must automatically lock the user account or the affected system component until an administrator explicitly releases it, and it must not allow use again until that release happens. The organisation must also document the policy and the administrator release procedures, record evidence in audit logs showing when accounts are locked and when administrators release them, include this in the system security plan, and ensure the right teams are responsible for implementing and maintaining the settings and enforcement.

#### Enforce 5 invalid logons/15 mins with admin-controlled unlock

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Configure the application’s authentication integration (and any upstream access gateways it relies on) to enforce: **no more than 5 consecutive invalid logon attempts within any rolling 15-minute period**, and when exceeded, **automatically lock the account (or affected node/session) until an administrator explicitly releases it**.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-7 (Unsuccessful Logon Attempts)** requires limiting unsuccessful logon attempts for both **local and network** logons, applying an organisation-defined **maximum number** and **time period**, and taking an organisation-defined action when exceeded. The guidance also notes that automatic lockouts are typically temporary, but **automatic unlocking is generally not permitted**; exceptions require mission/need justification. Your organisation-defined parameters specify:
- **number**: 5 (ac-07_odp.01)
- **time period**: 15 minutes (ac-07_odp.02)
- **action**: lock the account or node until released by an administrator (ac-07_odp.03)
- **lock duration**: 30 minutes (ac-07_odp.04) (only if your design uses a timed lock before admin release; otherwise treat as the lock window used for the lock state)
- **delay algorithm**: progressive delays (ac-07_odp.05) (optional if you choose throttling rather than immediate lock)
- **other action**: prompt for secret question before allowing further logon (ac-07_odp.06) (optional as a pre-lock step)

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Centralise enforcement at the identity layer used by the application**: ensure the application uses the enterprise identity provider (IdP) for interactive sign-in (SSO) and that the IdP policy applies to the relevant network logon flows (web, mobile, app client, API gateways that perform user authentication, and any reverse proxy / access gateway).
2. **Set the IdP sign-in policy to match the organisation-defined parameters**:
   - Configure throttling/lockout to enforce **5 consecutive invalid attempts within a rolling 15-minute window**.
   - Configure the post-exceed action to **lock the account until an administrator explicitly releases it** (no self-service unlock).
   - If you implement progressive delays instead of immediate lock, configure the **delay algorithm** as defined (1 min, then 5 min, then 15 min) and ensure that lock still occurs when the maximum is exceeded.
   - If you implement the optional secret-question step, configure it to occur **before** the maximum is exceeded (e.g., after attempt 4) and ensure it does not allow bypass of the lockout threshold.
3. **Ensure the application does not “mask” lockout**: when the IdP indicates the account is locked, the application must return a consistent authentication failure response and must not provide alternative authentication paths that would allow continued guessing (e.g., fallback local auth, alternate endpoints, or unauthenticated “reset” flows).
4. **Implement/validate admin unlock workflow and auditability**:
   - Use the IdP’s administrative unlock/release capability (or an integrated privileged access workflow) so that unlock is an explicit admin action.
   - Ensure audit logs capture: invalid attempt events, lock initiation, and admin release/unlock events, and forward them to the SIEM.
5. **Handle local logons on any non-federated components**: if the application has any local authentication (e.g., embedded accounts, standalone admin console, or service-specific login not federated to the IdP), configure equivalent lockout/throttling on those components or document and compensate with compensating controls (e.g., restricting access to those components to a managed admin network and enforcing strong MFA plus rate limiting).

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Owner / IAM Engineer**: configure IdP sign-in/lockout policies and ensure the application uses the correct authentication flows.
- **Security Operations / SIEM Engineer**: validate audit log forwarding, alerting, and retention.
- **Privileged Access Management (PAM) / IAM Administrator**: perform and approve explicit admin unlock/release actions.

## Acceptance criteria
- For a test user, after **5 consecutive invalid logon attempts within any rolling 15-minute window**, the account is **locked automatically**.
- The locked account **cannot authenticate** via the application’s network logon paths (web/mobile/client) until an **administrator explicitly releases/unlocks** it.
- Audit evidence exists for: (a) invalid attempt threshold reached, (b) lock initiation, and (c) admin unlock/release.
- No alternative authentication path allows continued guessing after lock (e.g., fallback login, alternate endpoints, or unauthenticated bypass).
- Lockout/throttling applies to both the application and any upstream access gateway components that perform user authentication for the application.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Configure the enterprise IdP/application sign-in policy to enforce 5 consecutive invalid attempts within a rolling 15-minute window
- [ ] Set the exceed-threshold action to lock the account or node until an administrator explicitly releases it (no automatic/self unlock)
- [ ] Enable progressive delay throttling (1/5/15 minutes) if selected, ensuring lock still triggers when the maximum is exceeded
- [ ] Verify the application and any access gateways use the IdP for authentication and do not provide fallback paths that bypass lockout
- [ ] Implement and test an admin unlock/release workflow with separation of duties and explicit approval
- [ ] Enable audit logging for invalid attempts, lock initiation, and admin release; forward events to the SIEM and retain per policy
- [ ] Run a test plan covering both local and network logon flows to confirm enforcement and audit evidence

### ac-7.2 — Purge or Wipe Mobile Device (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure a lost or wrongly accessed phone or tablet can’t keep organisation information on it after repeated failed attempts to unlock it. Without this, someone could keep trying codes until they eventually guess, or a device could be left in a state where sensitive information remains accessible even after it should have been cleared. The organisation must define which mobile devices used by staff are covered, and set them up so that after 10 consecutive unsuccessful attempts to unlock the device (not failed logins to a particular app or user account), the organisation information on that device is automatically purged or wiped. If the device uses strong full-device encryption, the organisation must use cryptographic erase; otherwise it must use an approved wipe method. A successful unlock must reset the failure count to zero. The organisation must keep documented procedures, device lists, and wipe technique rules, configure the devices accordingly, and retain audit records showing the wipe action and method used.

#### MDM device-unlock failure wipe with encryption-based sanitisation

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Configure the organisation’s Mobile Device Management (MDM) to automatically purge or wipe **in-scope mobile devices** after **10 consecutive unsuccessful device logon/unlock attempts**, using **cryptographic erase** when the device has **strong full-disk encryption**, and otherwise using an **approved NIST SP 800-88 Rev. 1 purge/wipe** method.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-7(2)** enhancement requires purging/wiping information from **mobile devices** after a defined number of **consecutive, unsuccessful device logon attempts**, where **successful device unlock resets the counter to zero**, and the sanitisation technique is selected based on the device’s **full-disk encryption strength**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define and enforce scope**: Ensure only devices in the defined set of **mobile devices used by personnel for access to organisation information** are enrolled and managed (e.g., MDM enrolment required before access to corporate resources).
2. **Implement device-level failure counting**: Configure the MDM/OS integration so the wipe trigger is based on **unsuccessful device unlock attempts** (device logon), not failed application/account logons.
3. **Set the threshold**: Configure the wipe trigger to occur after **10 consecutive unsuccessful device logon attempts**.
4. **Reset on success**: Verify that a **successful device unlock** resets the unsuccessful attempt counter to **zero** (test by attempting unlock failures up to threshold-1, then successfully unlocking, then confirming the counter resets).
5. **Select sanitisation method by encryption strength**:
   - If the device is assessed as having **strong full-disk encryption**, configure the wipe action to perform **cryptographic erase**.
   - Otherwise, configure the wipe action to perform **NIST SP 800-88 Rev. 1 purge/wipe** (purge vs wipe selection must follow the organisation’s defined technique rules).
6. **Make it auditable**: Ensure the MDM generates audit events for wipe initiation/completion including **device identifier**, **user (if applicable)**, **timestamp**, **trigger reason (unlock failure threshold)**, and **sanitisation method**; forward to SIEM/log repository with retention.
7. **Document decision logic**: Record how the system determines “strong full-disk encryption” (e.g., OS encryption status signals/attestation inputs) and how the correct sanitisation method is selected.

## WHO
- **Endpoint/MDM Administrator**: Implements MDM configuration (scope, unlock failure wipe trigger, encryption-based wipe method, logging).
- **Information Security (Compliance) Lead**: Approves sanitisation technique mapping to NIST SP 800-88 Rev. 1 and validates audit evidence.
- **Identity/Access Management (IAM) Team**: Ensures conditional access/enrolment gating so in-scope devices are actually managed.

## Acceptance criteria
- For an in-scope enrolled device, after **10 consecutive unsuccessful device unlock attempts**, the device performs the configured sanitisation action automatically.
- A **successful device unlock** resets the failure counter to **zero** (verified via test).
- Devices identified as having **strong full-disk encryption** perform **cryptographic erase**; other devices perform an **approved NIST SP 800-88 Rev. 1 purge/wipe**.
- Audit logs show, for each wipe event: device identifier, timestamp, trigger (unlock failure threshold), and sanitisation method.
- Only devices in the defined **mobile devices** scope are able to access organisation information without being subject to the MDM wipe policy.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Enrol and restrict access so that only the defined in-scope mobile devices are permitted to access organisation information
- [ ] Configure MDM/OS policy to trigger sanitisation after 10 consecutive unsuccessful device unlock attempts (device logon, not app/account failures)
- [ ] Validate that a successful device unlock resets the unsuccessful unlock attempt counter to zero
- [ ] Implement encryption-strength detection inputs and map them to cryptographic erase versus NIST SP 800-88 Rev. 1 purge/wipe per the organisation-defined technique rules
- [ ] Enable and centralise audit logging for wipe initiation/completion including device identifier, trigger reason, and sanitisation method
- [ ] Run a controlled test on representative device models to confirm correct wipe method selection and auditable evidence

### ac-7.3 — Biometric Attempt Limiting (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about stopping repeated failed biometric sign-ins from being tried over and over, by setting a hard limit of 5 unsuccessful biometric attempts. Without this, someone could keep trying different fingers, faces, or other biometric presentations until they eventually get lucky, or they could overwhelm the system and make genuine access harder to manage. To meet this expectation, the organisation must configure its biometric sign-in process so that after 5 failed tries the system prevents any further biometric sign-in attempts, using the organisation’s documented approach for what happens next (such as blocking biometric use or requiring an alternative way to sign in). The organisation must also keep clear written procedures, ensure the system settings are actually set to 5, record the outcomes in audit records (including when the limit is reached), and include the approach in its security planning and system design documentation so it can be shown and maintained over time.

#### Enforce 5 biometric failures with IdP lockout and audit evidence

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Configure the enterprise authentication flow so that **unsuccessful biometric logon attempts are limited to 5** (organisation parameter `ac-07.03_odp`), and **after the 5th failure further biometric attempts are blocked** according to documented behaviour. Ensure the system produces **audit records** for failed attempts and the point at which the limit is reached.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-7(3)** enhancement requires: *“Limit the number of unsuccessful biometric logon attempts to {{ insert: param, ac-07.03_odp }}.”* The guidance notes biometrics are probabilistic and that organisations select the appropriate number of attempts based on organisationally-defined factors.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Identify in-scope biometric entry points** for the application’s users (e.g., Windows Hello for Business, mobile biometric sign-in, biometric-capable client apps) and confirm they ultimately authenticate via the **central enterprise identity service (IdP)**.
2. **Configure the IdP authentication policy** to enforce the maximum number of unsuccessful biometric attempts to **5** (use `ac-07.03_odp` value). Ensure the enforcement is applied to the biometric factor retry behaviour (not just overall MFA attempts).
3. **Define and implement post-limit behaviour** in the authentication journey to match organisational procedures (e.g., deny biometric factor and require an alternative authentication method such as password + MFA, or trigger account lockout per existing access control policy). Ensure the user-facing outcome is consistent and documented.
4. **Enable and validate audit logging** for authentication failures, including a distinct event when the biometric attempt limit is reached/biometric is blocked. Confirm logs include at minimum: user identifier, timestamp, failure outcome, and reason/threshold-reached indicator.
5. **Make the configuration auditable and repeatable** by applying it through configuration management (e.g., policy-as-code or exported configuration snapshots) and capturing evidence of the setting value **5** across production and other relevant environments.
6. **Update documentation**: biometric logon procedures and the system security plan/system design documentation must explicitly reference the **5 unsuccessful attempts** limit and the documented post-limit behaviour.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Identity & Access Management (IAM) engineer / security architect**: implement IdP policy and ensure correct factor retry enforcement.
- **Application owner / system owner**: confirm the application’s authentication integration points are in-scope and that user experience aligns with documented post-limit behaviour.
- **Security operations / SIEM engineer**: validate audit events and ensure SIEM ingestion and alerting (if used) for threshold-reached events.
- **Compliance/GRC**: review evidence package and documentation alignment.

## Acceptance criteria
- The IdP enforces **no more than 5 unsuccessful biometric logon attempts** for the defined biometric factor(s) in the in-scope authentication flow.
- After the 5th unsuccessful biometric attempt, **further biometric attempts are blocked/denied** and the user is routed to the **documented alternative/post-limit behaviour**.
- Audit logs show **each unsuccessful biometric attempt** and a **distinct threshold-reached/blocked event** with sufficient identifiers for investigation.
- Evidence exists showing the configured value is **5** (e.g., configuration export/policy-as-code commit) and is consistent with the system security plan and biometric-device procedures.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Map and confirm all in-scope biometric logon paths that authenticate via the enterprise IdP
- [ ] Configure the IdP biometric factor retry/attempt limit to exactly 5 unsuccessful attempts (ac-07.03_odp)
- [ ] Implement and document post-limit behaviour (deny biometric and require the approved alternative) in the authentication journey
- [ ] Enable audit logging for biometric failures and verify a distinct event is generated when the limit is reached
- [ ] Capture configuration evidence (policy-as-code or exported settings) showing the limit is set to 5 in each relevant environment
- [ ] Update biometric logon procedures and the system security plan/design documentation to reference the 5-attempt limit and the post-limit response

### ac-7.4 — Use of Alternate Authentication Factor (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about giving people a safe backup way to prove who they are when they have mistyped their usual login too many times, without letting attackers keep trying endlessly. Without it, someone could lock themselves out and, at the same time, a wrongdoer could repeatedly guess passwords or codes until they get lucky, especially if the system allows unlimited retries using backup methods. The organisation must allow users to use only two approved alternate ways to sign in—either an approved hardware security key or a one-time code generated by an approved authenticator app—when those alternate methods are different from the user’s usual method, and only after the organisation’s set number of consecutive failed attempts for the usual method has been exceeded. When users are using an alternate method, the system must stop them after 5 consecutive failed attempts within any rolling 15-minute period, and it must count failures during the alternate sign-in process. The organisation must document how this works, configure the system accordingly, and keep audit records showing the failed attempts and timing so the approach can be checked.

#### IdP alternate-factor recovery with 5-in-15 rolling throttle

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Configure the enterprise identity provider (IdP) so that, after a user exceeds the organisation-defined consecutive invalid-attempt threshold for their **primary** authentication factor, the user may authenticate using only **approved alternate factors**: (1) **hardware security key (FIDO2/WebAuthn)** and (2) **OTP from an approved authenticator app**. While using alternate factors, enforce a strict limit of **5 consecutive invalid alternate-factor attempts within any rolling 15-minute period**, counting failures during the alternate-factor flow.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-7(4) enhancement guidance requires alternate authentication factors to support availability and lockout recovery, while preventing abuse by limiting invalid attempts using alternate factors. The organisation-defined parameters require: approved alternate factors, a defined number of consecutive invalid attempts for the primary factor, and a defined time period for alternate-factor attempt limiting.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define factor roles and allowed alternates** in the IdP policy model: tag the user’s configured primary factor(s) and allow only the two alternate factors specified by the organisation: **FIDO2/WebAuthn security key** and **authenticator-app OTP**.
2. **Gate alternate-factor availability**: configure the IdP so alternate-factor authentication is permitted only after the user exceeds the organisation-defined consecutive invalid attempts for the **primary** factor (use the configured threshold).
3. **Enforce rolling throttling for alternate factors**: implement a per-user alternate-factor failure counter that triggers after **5 consecutive invalid alternate-factor attempts** within a **rolling 15-minute window**; block further alternate-factor attempts until the rolling window clears.
4. **Count failures during alternate authentication**: ensure each failed alternate-factor attempt increments the alternate-factor counter (not just primary-factor failures) and that the counter resets only according to the rolling-window logic.
5. **Enforce “different from primary”**: ensure the IdP policy prevents using an alternate factor that matches the user’s primary factor type (e.g., if primary is authenticator-app OTP, alternate cannot be OTP; if primary is security key, alternate cannot be security key).
6. **Enable auditability and evidence**: configure authentication event logging to capture (a) primary-factor invalid attempts leading to alternate-factor eligibility, (b) each failed alternate-factor attempt with timestamps, and (c) block/unblock events for the alternate-factor throttle; forward to SIEM for audit verification.
7. **Document and operationalise**: maintain an auditable configuration record (policy-as-code or exported configuration) and write operational procedures for user recovery/helpdesk escalation consistent with the IdP behaviour.

## WHO
- **Identity & Access Management (IAM) engineer**: implement IdP authentication policies, factor tagging, throttling logic, and audit log configuration.
- **Security operations / SOC**: validate SIEM ingestion, alerting, and retention for authentication failure events.
- **Information security governance / compliance**: review and approve the documented behaviour and evidence package.

## Acceptance criteria
- Alternate-factor authentication is **only available after** the user exceeds the configured consecutive invalid attempts for the **primary** factor.
- Alternate factors are restricted to **FIDO2/WebAuthn security keys** and **authenticator-app OTP** only.
- The IdP blocks alternate-factor attempts after **5 consecutive invalid alternate-factor attempts within any rolling 15-minute period**, and unblocks only when the rolling condition clears.
- Failed alternate-factor attempts are **counted** towards the 5-in-15 limit.
- The IdP prevents using an alternate factor that is the **same type as the user’s primary** factor.
- Audit logs/SIEM events provide sufficient evidence to reconstruct: eligibility transition, each alternate failure with timestamps, and throttle block/unblock actions.
- Configuration and procedures are documented and traceable to the organisation-defined parameters.


**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define and configure the two approved alternate factors (FIDO2/WebAuthn security key and authenticator-app OTP) in the IdP policy
- [ ] Tag each user/app authentication method as primary versus alternate and enforce “alternate different from primary” in policy
- [ ] Configure the IdP to enable alternate-factor authentication only after the configured consecutive invalid primary-attempt threshold is exceeded
- [ ] Implement a per-user rolling throttle for alternate-factor failures: block after 5 consecutive invalid alternate attempts within 15 minutes
- [ ] Ensure alternate-factor failures increment the alternate counter and reset only according to the rolling-window logic
- [ ] Enable and export audit logs for primary invalid attempts, alternate-factor eligibility, each alternate failure timestamp, and throttle block/unblock events to SIEM
- [ ] Publish and maintain operational procedures and configuration evidence (policy-as-code/export) for helpdesk/user recovery consistent with the IdP behaviour

### ac-8 — System Use Notification (Control)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure people see a clear warning before they enter a government system, so they understand what they are agreeing to. Without this, users could unknowingly access the system without realising their activity may be monitored and recorded, or that unauthorised use is not allowed and can lead to criminal and civil penalties, increasing the risk of misuse and privacy harm. The organisation must display the warning before any user can log on through a human sign-in screen, stating that it is a U.S. Government system, that use may be monitored, recorded and reviewed, that unauthorised use is prohibited and subject to penalties, and that using the system means they consent to monitoring and recording; the message must stay on screen until the user acknowledges the conditions and takes the explicit step to log on or continue. If the system is publicly accessible, it must also show a public notice before further access stating monitoring and recording are limited to security and fraud prevention, that no content is used for unauthorised profiling, and that only authorised uses are permitted, including any privacy-consistent limits and a description of authorised uses. The organisation must have the warning text approved, configure the system so the notice appears and cannot be skipped, and keep records that users acknowledged it and proceeded.

#### IdP pre-auth system use warning with explicit acknowledgement gate

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement an approved system use notification (and, where applicable, a public access notice) at the identity provider (IdP) login stage for all human users using the system’s logon interface. The warning must be displayed before access is granted, remain visible until the user explicitly acknowledges the conditions, and require an explicit action (e.g., “I acknowledge and consent” / “Continue”) to proceed.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 AC-8 requires system use notifications to be displayed by the system to users before granting access via human logon interfaces, and to be retained until the user acknowledges the conditions and takes an explicit step to log on/continue. For publicly accessible access paths, a secondary/public notice must also be considered and implemented based on risk, including privacy- and legal-approved content.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Obtain legal/privacy-approved banner content** for:
   - the core system use notification message (US Government system identity, monitoring/recording/auditing, unauthorised use prohibition and penalties, and consent to monitoring/recording), and
   - the public access notice (security/fraud prevention limits, no unauthorised profiling, authorised uses only, plus privacy-consistent limits and an authorised uses description).
2. **Configure the IdP authentication flow** to show the banner **before** credentials are accepted and/or before any application session is issued, using an IdP feature such as a “required consent/terms” step, custom authentication step, or pre-auth landing page.
3. **Enforce an acknowledgement gate**:
   - Require an explicit user action (checkbox + “Continue”, or a dedicated “Acknowledge and proceed” button).
   - Prevent auto-redirect or bypass; the user must complete acknowledgement to proceed.
4. **Support multi-language display where needed** by rendering the approved text in the required languages (without altering meaning), selecting language based on user locale/browser settings.
5. **Record audit evidence** in the IdP transaction/logs including: user identifier, timestamp, banner version (or content hash), and a flag indicating acknowledgement completed and the explicit proceed action.
6. **Apply the configuration consistently** across all human logon entry points that use the IdP (web portal, SSO to applications, admin consoles). For publicly accessible portals, ensure the public access notice is shown at the appropriate pre-auth stage.

## WHO
- **Information Security / Compliance Lead**: owns AC-8 implementation plan and evidence requirements.
- **Identity & Access Management (IAM) Engineer**: implements IdP flow, acknowledgement gate, and logging.
- **Privacy Officer**: reviews privacy messaging for the public access notice.
- **General Counsel / Legal**: approves warning banner content.
- **SIEM/Logging Engineer**: ensures IdP acknowledgement events are forwarded and retained per logging policy.

## Acceptance criteria
- For every human login via the IdP, the approved system use notification is displayed **before** access is granted.
- The banner remains visible until the user explicitly acknowledges and performs the explicit proceed action.
- For publicly accessible access paths, the approved public access notice is displayed and includes the required privacy-consistent limits and authorised uses description.
- IdP logs (and SIEM records) show acknowledgement completion and the explicit proceed action, including banner version/hash.
- No user can bypass the acknowledgement step to reach the application.


**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Approve and version-control the system use notification and (where applicable) public access notice text with Privacy and Legal
- [ ] Implement an IdP pre-auth authentication step that displays the approved banner before any application session is created
- [ ] Configure an acknowledgement gate requiring explicit user action to proceed and block bypass/auto-redirect
- [ ] Enable multi-language rendering using the approved translations without changing meaning
