- [ ] Configure IdP logging to record user, timestamp, banner version/hash, acknowledgement completion, and proceed action
- [ ] Forward IdP acknowledgement events to SIEM and verify audit-ready retention and searchability

### ac-9 — Previous Logon Notification (Control)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure people are told when they last successfully logged in, right after they sign in. It matters because if the system never shows this information, a user may not notice something suspicious, such as an unexpected access that could indicate misuse of their account or unauthorised activity. To meet this expectation, the organisation must ensure that whenever a person logs on through a normal login screen (such as a website or desktop sign-in), the system immediately displays a message that includes both the date and the time of that person’s most recent successful login, using the organisation’s own stored records rather than failed sign-in attempts. The organisation should also document how this works, configure the system so the message appears on all supported login screens, and be able to show evidence that the correct date and time are displayed after successful sign-in.

#### Show last successful logon time in IdP sign-in success UI

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** High

## WHAT
Configure the enterprise identity provider (IdP) interactive sign-in experience to notify each human user, immediately after a successful logon, of the **date and time of their most recent successful logon**.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-9** requires: “Notify the user, upon successful logon to the system, of the date and time of the last logon.” The guidance clarifies this is intended to help users recognise inconsistencies with their last access.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Route human user authentication through the IdP** for all supported human UI access paths for this application (e.g., web/portal/mobile sign-in flows that use SSO).
2. **Enable/implement the IdP ‘previous sign-in’ / ‘last successful sign-in’ message** in the interactive sign-in success page (or post-authentication landing step), ensuring it renders **only after successful authentication**.
3. **Bind the message to authoritative last-successful-logon records** maintained by the IdP (not failed attempts, not session start time, and not client-side estimates).
4. **Standardise date/time presentation**: define the organisation’s required time zone and formatting (e.g., include year, minutes, and a clear time zone or consistent local time) and apply it to the IdP message template.
5. **Ensure coverage for all relevant human UI flows** (including any custom login pages, embedded sign-in widgets, and app landing pages) so the notification appears consistently after success.
6. **Generate audit-ready evidence**: capture configuration exports (or IaC outputs) and produce test evidence (screenshots/log correlation) showing the displayed timestamp matches the IdP’s recorded last successful logon for the same user.

## WHO
- **Identity & Access Management (IAM) Engineer**: implement IdP configuration and message template.
- **Application Owner / Engineering Lead**: ensure the application uses IdP SSO for all human UI access paths.
- **Security/Compliance Analyst**: validate evidence and acceptance criteria during audit readiness.

## Acceptance criteria
- After a **successful** human logon via the application’s supported human UI paths that use the IdP, the user is shown a message containing **both date and time** of their **most recent successful** logon.
- The displayed timestamp **matches** the IdP’s authoritative last-successful-logon record for that user (within the organisation-defined formatting/time zone rules).
- The message **does not appear** on failed logon attempts.
- Evidence exists for: (a) IdP configuration/template, and (b) test proof that the message displays the correct last successful logon time.


**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Confirm all supported human UI authentication flows for the application use the enterprise IdP (SSO) rather than local/alternate auth paths
- [ ] Enable the IdP’s last successful sign-in/previous sign-in notification feature in the interactive sign-in success experience
- [ ] Configure the notification template to display the authoritative last-successful-logon date and time (no failed-attempt data)
- [ ] Define and apply organisation-standard time zone and formatting rules for the displayed timestamp
- [ ] Test success and failure scenarios to verify the message appears only after successful logon and matches IdP records
- [ ] Collect audit evidence by exporting IdP configuration/IaC and capturing screenshot/log correlation for at least one test user across environments

### ac-9.1 — Unsuccessful Logons (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This expectation is about helping a person spot suspicious or unexpected login behaviour by showing them, right after they sign in successfully, how many times their sign-in attempts failed since their last successful sign-in. Without this, a user might not realise that someone else has been trying to access their account, or they may be unable to tell whether repeated failures are consistent with their own actions, which can delay reporting and increase the chance of unauthorised access. To meet this expectation, the organisation must keep a running count for each user of rejected sign-in attempts that happen after their last successful sign-in and before the next one, increase that count every time the system rejects incorrect details, and immediately reset it to zero after a successful sign-in. Every time the user signs in successfully, the system must show only that user the correct number for the most recent period.

#### Per-user failed-logon counter with post-success user notification

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** High

## WHAT
Implement a per-user counter that tracks the number of unsuccessful logon attempts since the user’s last successful logon, and display that number to the user immediately after each successful logon.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-9(1) enhancement requires that the system **notify the user, upon successful logon**, of **the number of unsuccessful logon attempts since the last successful logon**. This enables users to recognise inconsistencies between expected and observed login behaviour.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Track failures per user in an atomic, secure store**: On each authentication failure for a given user identifier, increment a counter keyed to that user. On each successful authentication, read the current counter value (the “since last success” value), then reset the counter to zero.
2. **Ensure correct period semantics**: The counter must represent failures occurring **after the last successful logon and before the next successful logon**. This is achieved by resetting to zero immediately after success.
3. **Inject the value into the successful session**: During the successful logon flow, pass the pre-reset counter value into the user’s session (e.g., as a session attribute, token claim, or server-side view model) so it can be rendered immediately after sign-in.
4. **Render a user-facing notification only to the authenticated user**: Display the message on the post-login landing page (or IdP-hosted success page) for the user who just authenticated. Do not expose the value to other users, administrators, or logs visible to unauthorised parties.
5. **Handle edge cases safely**: Ensure the counter update is resilient to concurrency (e.g., multiple failed attempts in parallel) and that resets occur only for successful authentications for that same user identity.
6. **Generate audit evidence**: Record (in a central audit log/SIEM) the user identifier, timestamp, success/failure outcome, and the counter value used for the notification and the reset event—without exposing the counter value in places accessible to unauthorised users.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Identity/Authentication Engineer**: Implements the counter logic, session/claim injection, and notification rendering.
- **Application Security Lead**: Reviews design for privacy, correctness, and auditability.
- **Operations/SRE**: Ensures secure deployment, monitoring, and incident response hooks for the counter service.

## Acceptance criteria
- After a user successfully logs in, the UI shows **the exact number of failed logon attempts since the user’s previous successful logon**.
- After a successful logon, the counter is reset such that subsequent notifications reflect only failures since that success.
- The notification is shown **only** to the user who just authenticated.
- Counter increments/resets are atomic and consistent under concurrent login attempts.
- Audit logs exist showing counter increment/reset and the value used for the notification for each successful logon.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Design a per-user failed-logon counter keyed to the authoritative user identifier used by the authentication system
- [ ] Implement atomic increment on each authentication failure and atomic read-then-reset on each successful authentication
- [ ] Modify the successful logon flow to pass the pre-reset counter value into the authenticated session for the same user
- [ ] Add a post-login UI component (or IdP-hosted success page) that displays the counter value immediately after sign-in
- [ ] Restrict notification rendering to the authenticated user and prevent leakage via logs, error pages, or shared caches
- [ ] Enable central audit logging for counter increment/reset and the value used for the notification, with appropriate retention and access controls
- [ ] Create automated tests that simulate sequences of failures and successes and verify the displayed value and reset behaviour

### ac-9.2 — Successful and Unsuccessful Logons (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about helping people spot unusual login behaviour by showing them, right after they sign in successfully, how many login attempts happened recently. Specifically, immediately after a successful sign-in, the system must display the number of both successful logons and unsuccessful logon attempts during the previous 24 hours. Without this, a user might not notice that their account is being targeted, or that the pattern of sign-ins does not match what they actually did, which could allow unauthorised access to go unnoticed for longer. To meet this expectation, the organisation must set up the sign-in system so that it counts both successful and failed attempts over the last 24 hours relative to the time of the user’s successful sign-in, and then shows both figures together in the notification the user sees right after they log on.

#### Post-login notification of last-24h successful/failed logons

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** High

## WHAT
Implement a user-facing notification that appears immediately after a successful logon and displays:
- the number of **successful logons** during the configured time period, and
- the number of **unsuccessful logon attempts** during the same time period.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-9(2) enhancement requires: “Notify the user, upon successful logon, of the number of {{ insert: param, ac-09.02_odp.01 }} during {{ insert: param, ac-09.02_odp.02 }}.”
With organisation-defined parameters:
- **ac-09.02_odp.01 = both** (successful and unsuccessful), and
- **ac-09.02_odp.02 = 24 hours**.
This enables users to recognise whether recent logon activity is consistent with their own behaviour.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
Implement the feature at the application’s authentication boundary (preferably via the integrated enterprise identity provider) so that it is triggered only after a successful authentication event.

1. **Define the authoritative event source**: use the enterprise identity/access management system’s sign-in/audit events as the source of truth (success and failure outcomes) and ensure events include user identifier and timestamp.
2. **Trigger on successful logon**: when a user successfully authenticates to the application, invoke a backend service (or IdP extensibility hook) to compute counts for that user.
3. **Compute rolling 24-hour window**: using the successful logon timestamp as the reference point, query sign-in events for the preceding **24 hours** and calculate:
   - `success_count_last_24h`
   - `unsuccess_count_last_24h`
   Ensure the definition of “unsuccessful” matches the IdP’s failure outcomes (e.g., bad password, denied, MFA failure) and document the mapping.
4. **Render notification immediately after login**: pass the computed counts to the post-login UI (e.g., landing page banner, first-page component, or session claim consumed by the UI) and display both counts together.
5. **Prevent information leakage**: ensure the notification is shown only to the authenticated user and does not reveal whether other accounts exist or whether specific failures occurred.
6. **Create audit evidence**: log (server-side) that the notification was generated for the user at the time of successful logon, including the time window boundaries and the computed counts (without exposing sensitive details). Retain evidence to support verification.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Application security engineer / IAM integration engineer**: implement the event query, count computation, and UI injection.
- **Identity provider administrator**: ensure sign-in event logging is enabled and accessible for the computation.
- **Security operations / compliance**: review the “unsuccessful” outcome mapping and validate audit evidence.

## Acceptance criteria
- After a successful logon, the user is shown a notification containing **both** the number of successful logons and unsuccessful logon attempts from the **preceding 24 hours**.
- The counts are computed using event timestamps and are consistent with the identity provider’s audit logs for the same user and time window.
- The notification appears immediately after successful authentication (before access to sensitive application functions, or on the first post-login page).
- The system logs evidence that the notification was generated (user, reference time, window, counts) and this evidence is retrievable for audit.
- “Unsuccessful” is mapped and documented to the IdP’s failure outcomes used in the computation.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Enable and retain identity provider sign-in audit events for both successful and unsuccessful outcomes with user identifier and timestamps
- [ ] Implement a post-success authentication trigger (IdP hook or application backend callback) to start count computation
- [ ] Query the preceding 24 hours of sign-in events for the authenticated user and compute success and failure counts using the successful logon time as the reference
- [ ] Define and document the mapping of “unsuccessful logon attempts” to the IdP’s failure/MFA/denied outcomes
- [ ] Render a post-login notification that displays both counts together immediately after successful logon
- [ ] Log notification generation evidence (user, reference time, 24-hour window boundaries, computed counts) for auditability

### ac-9.3 — Notification of Account Changes (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure people are told, right after they successfully sign in, about any important changes made to their account in the previous 24 hours. It matters because if someone changes your password, alters how you prove it is really you, or quietly adjusts what you can access, you may never notice and could keep using a compromised account without realising. To meet this expectation, the organisation must, every time a user logs on successfully, check what security-relevant changes were recorded for that user during the past 24 hours and include them in a notification. This includes changes to how multi-step sign-in is set up, any password reset activity, any changes to what the user is allowed to do or which groups or roles they belong to, and any changes to security rules or role assignments. The notification must be based on the organisation’s recorded system information, work in line with its own sign-in notification procedures, and be properly documented, configured, and verifiable using audit records and the system’s security plan.

#### IdP sign-in notification digest for security account changes (24h)

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** High

## WHAT
Implement an identity-provider (IdP) “account change digest” that is shown to the user immediately after each successful logon. The digest must list only changes to the user’s **security-related characteristics or parameters** that occurred within the configured **time period**.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-9(3) enhancement requires: **“Notify the user, upon successful logon, of changes to {{security-related characteristics or parameters}} during {{time period}}.”** The guidance explains that users can recognise changes made without their knowledge when they are informed promptly.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the notification scope** to match the organisation-defined parameter set:
   - Security-related characteristics/parameters include: **MFA status changes, password reset events, account privilege/group membership changes, and security policy/role assignments**.
   - Time period is **the past 24 hours**.
2. **Map authoritative IdP/audit events** to each required change type (e.g., MFA enable/disable/method change; password/credential reset; group/role membership changes; policy/role assignment changes that affect access). Ensure the mapping uses **recorded audit/event data** from the IdP (not inferred application state).
3. **Implement a sign-in-time hook/workflow** that triggers on **successful logon** and:
   - Determines the user and the logon timestamp.
   - Queries the IdP audit/event store for events for that user in the window **[logon_time − 24h, logon_time]**.
   - Filters to only the mapped security-related change categories.
   - Renders a concise digest (what changed, when, and—where available—who/what initiated it).
4. **Deliver the notification securely** in the post-login experience (e.g., IdP post-login page or application landing page fed by the IdP), ensuring it is visible to the user at the point of successful authentication.
5. **Make the behaviour auditable and verifiable** by recording evidence for each digest generation (e.g., event IDs included, time window used, template/version identifier, and correlation to the successful logon event). Retain IdP configuration exports and the event-mapping document.
6. **Document the control in the security plan** with the operational details: trigger condition (successful logon), time window (24h), event sources, filtering logic, and notification channel.

## WHO (roles responsible)
- **IAM/IdP Engineer**: implement event mapping and sign-in-time hook/workflow.
- **Security Operations / SIEM Engineer**: ensure audit/event retention and evidence indexing for verification.
- **Information Security Manager**: approve notification scope, ensure security plan documentation, and validate alignment with access control policy.

## Acceptance criteria
- For a test user, after a successful logon, the user receives a notification listing only the configured security-related changes that occurred in the **previous 24 hours**.
- Changes outside the 24-hour window do not appear.
- The digest content is derived from **authoritative IdP audit/event records** and is reproducible from retained audit evidence.
- Evidence exists for each notification generation (time window, included event IDs, correlation to successful logon).
- The implementation is documented in the organisation’s security plan with the configured parameters (security-related characteristics/parameters and 24-hour time period).

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define the exact security-related change categories to include (MFA status, password reset, privilege/group membership, policy/role assignments) and confirm the 24-hour window
- [ ] Create an authoritative event-to-category mapping using IdP audit/event types for each required change category
- [ ] Implement an IdP sign-in-time hook that triggers only on successful logon and queries events in [logon_time−24h, logon_time]
- [ ] Render and display the digest immediately after successful authentication via an approved post-login channel
- [ ] Record evidence per notification generation (successful logon correlation, time window, included event IDs, template/version) and ensure audit retention
- [ ] Update the security plan with the notification design, configuration, and verification approach, including how the 24-hour window is enforced

### ac-9.4 — Additional Logon Information (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure people are told, straight after they successfully log in, where their account was last used and which device they are using. It matters because if someone’s account is accessed from an unexpected place or device, the user may not notice quickly, allowing unauthorised access or fraud to go on longer than necessary. To meet this expectation, the organisation must show an immediate on-screen message after a successful logon that includes the last successful logon location, including the internet address and an approximate city or region, as well as the device identifier, using the organisation’s agreed wording and content. The organisation must also have documented procedures and system design explaining how this information is gathered and displayed, configure the system so it can reliably produce the message, keep records that can be checked later to confirm the message was generated or shown, and include this approach in its overall system security plan.

#### IdP-driven post-login notice with last successful location and device

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** High

## WHAT
Configure the enterprise identity provider (IdP) to notify users immediately after a successful logon with organisation-defined additional information: **last successful logon location (IP address and approximate city/region) and device identifier**.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-9(4)** requires that, upon successful logon, the user is notified of the additional information specified by the organisation (param **ac-09.04_odp**). The control guidance clarifies that “user location” can be determined from network logon sources such as **IP addresses** and that the notification should be based on **successful** logon context.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Trigger point**: Implement a post-authentication action that runs on **successful logon** and renders the message on the **first landing page after authentication** (before the user can access application functionality).
2. **Last successful logic**: For each user, compute the **last successful** logon (not the current attempt and not failed attempts) using the IdP’s sign-in history.
3. **Location fields**: Populate the message with:
   - **IP address** from the last successful logon source
   - **approximate city/region** resolved from that IP (IdP geolocation capability or approved geolocation service)
4. **Device identifier**: Populate the message with a **device identifier** associated with the last successful logon. Prefer an IdP/endpoint-managed, stable identifier (e.g., managed device ID/name or device registration claim). Define and document a controlled fallback for unmanaged devices (e.g., “Unmanaged device” plus an allowed identifier only if your privacy policy and IdP capabilities permit).
5. **Organisation-defined content**: Use the exact organisation-defined wording/fields for **ac-09.04_odp**: “Last successful logon location (IP address and approximate city/region) and device identifier”.
6. **Auditability**: Ensure the IdP logs evidence that the notification was generated/displayed, including the timestamp, user identifier, and the notification payload fields (IP, city/region, device identifier). Forward these logs to the SIEM for retention and audit checks.
7. **Documentation & security plan**: Document the system design/configuration showing how the last successful record is selected, how geolocation is derived, how the device identifier is determined, and where evidence is recorded. Record this approach in the system security plan.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Identity & Access Management (IAM) engineer / IdP administrator**: implement IdP policies/actions, device claim mapping, and message rendering.
- **Security architect / compliance lead**: validate that the notification content, “last successful” logic, and evidence retention meet AC-9(4) expectations.
- **SIEM/logging engineer**: ensure logs are forwarded, retained, and searchable for audit evidence.

## Acceptance criteria
- On **successful logon**, users receive an on-screen notification **immediately after authentication** (before application access).
- The notification includes **IP address + approximate city/region** and a **device identifier** corresponding to the **last successful** logon.
- The notification content matches the organisation-defined **ac-09.04_odp** fields.
- Audit evidence exists for each successful logon notification (or an equivalent verifiable record) showing the notification payload fields and timestamp.
- System design/configuration and the approach are documented in the system security plan.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Implement an IdP post-authentication action that renders the AC-9(4) message on the first post-login landing page
- [ ] Configure “last successful logon” selection logic using IdP successful sign-in history (excluding failed attempts and current attempt)
- [ ] Enable IP-to-approximate city/region resolution for the notification payload using IdP geolocation or an approved service
- [ ] Map and populate a stable device identifier claim for the last successful logon (and define a documented fallback for unmanaged devices)
- [ ] Ensure the notification payload uses the organisation-defined content for ac-09.04_odp: IP + approximate city/region and device identifier
- [ ] Configure IdP/SIEM logging so notification generation/display evidence (timestamp, user, IP, city/region, device identifier) is retained and auditable
- [ ] Update system design documentation and the system security plan to describe how the message is generated, including evidence sources

### ac-10 — Concurrent Session Control (Control)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about stopping powerful accounts from being used in too many places at the same time by limiting each privileged system account (such as domain administrators and local administrators) and each service account used by mission-critical applications to a maximum of 2 simultaneous sessions. This matters because if these accounts can be used unlimitedly, it becomes easier for mistakes or unauthorised access to spread, making it harder to detect misuse and increasing the impact of any compromise. To meet this expectation, the organisation must clearly define which accounts are covered, set the maximum to 2 concurrent sessions per covered account, and ensure the systems actively prevent a third simultaneous session from being created for those accounts. The organisation must document the rule and the day-to-day procedures for applying and checking it, describe how the system is designed to enforce it, configure the relevant settings, keep evidence that the settings are in place, include the approach in its security planning, and be able to show through testing and records that the limit is consistently enforced.

#### Enforce 2 concurrent sessions for privileged/service accounts via IdP

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement a technical concurrency/session limit so that each in-scope privileged system account and each mission-critical service account can have **no more than 2 concurrent sessions** at any time.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-10** requires limiting the number of concurrent sessions for each {{ac-10_odp.01}} (account and/or account types) to {{ac-10_odp.02}} (number). The guidance clarifies this applies to **system accounts** (e.g., privileged system accounts and service accounts), not multiple concurrent sessions created by a single user via multiple accounts.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the covered accounts** in the authoritative IAM source: privileged system accounts (e.g., domain admins/local admins equivalents) and mission-critical service accounts, tagged by account type.
2. **Create IdP-side policy objects** that map to those covered account tags (e.g., group membership or identity attributes).
3. **Configure concurrency/session limit = 2** per covered identity/account in the IdP/session management feature set.
4. **Set deterministic enforcement behaviour** for the third concurrent session attempt (e.g., deny the new session and/or terminate the oldest session) and document the chosen behaviour.
5. **Enable and centralise logging** for successful sessions and blocked/terminated concurrency attempts; forward to SIEM for auditability and alerting on repeated enforcement events.
6. **Produce audit-ready evidence** by exporting the IdP policy configuration (or using policy-as-code where available) and retaining configuration snapshots plus representative log evidence.
7. **Implement operational procedures** for exception handling (temporary increases only via approval), and for validating current session counts during incident response or access troubleshooting.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **IAM/Identity Engineer**: implement IdP concurrency policies and mapping to covered accounts.
- **Security Operations (SOC) / SIEM Engineer**: configure log forwarding, alerts, and evidence retention.
- **System Owner / Privileged Access Owner**: approve exception requests and validate that the covered account list is correct.

## Acceptance criteria
- For every covered privileged system account and mission-critical service account, attempts to create a **third concurrent session** result in the configured enforcement behaviour (deny/terminate) and are recorded in logs.
- The IdP configuration shows **concurrency limit = 2** for the defined account types/accounts.
- Evidence exists: exported IdP policy configuration (or policy-as-code artefacts) and SIEM-accessible logs demonstrating enforcement.
- Runbook/procedure exists for exception handling and for verifying current session counts.


**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Identify and tag covered privileged system accounts and mission-critical service accounts in the authoritative IAM source
- [ ] Configure IdP session/concurrency controls to enforce a maximum of 2 concurrent sessions per covered identity
- [ ] Define and document the third-session enforcement behaviour (deny new session and/or terminate an existing session) for operational consistency
- [ ] Enable logging for session creation and blocked/terminated concurrency attempts and forward events to the SIEM
- [ ] Export and retain audit-ready evidence of the IdP concurrency policy configuration (or store policy-as-code artefacts)
- [ ] Create and publish runbook procedures for validating current session counts and handling approved temporary exceptions
- [ ] Test with controlled attempts (Session A/B succeed; Session C is blocked/terminated) and retain test evidence

### ac-11 — Device Lock (Control)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure that when someone walks away from a work computer or work app, the system does not stay accessible to anyone nearby. Without this, a passer-by could open files, view sensitive information, or continue using the logged-in work session, creating a real risk of data exposure or misuse. The organisation must automatically lock the user’s device after 15 minutes of inactivity, and it must stop any further access while the device is locked. The device must remain locked until the user comes back and re-establishes access using the organisation’s normal sign-in method, such as entering their usual password or personal code. The organisation must also ensure that locking is not treated as a replacement for logging out when users are required to end their session, for example at the end of the workday.

#### Enforce 15-minute inactivity device lock with re-authentication

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Configure managed endpoint devices to automatically initiate a **device lock after 15 minutes of inactivity**, and ensure that **no further logical access is possible while the device is locked**. Require users to **re-authenticate using the organisation’s normal identification and authentication procedures** to regain access.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-11 requires that device locks are used to prevent logical access when users stop work and move away, without logging out. The organisation-defined parameters specify:
- **ac-11_odp.02**: inactivity time period is **15 minutes**
- **ac-11_odp.01**: initiate a device lock after the defined inactivity period; requiring the user to initiate a device lock before leaving unattended (where applicable)

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Set the OS inactivity lock timer to 15 minutes** on all eligible managed endpoints (e.g., via Intune/Jamf/endpoint baseline policies). Ensure the timer resets on standard user activity (keyboard/mouse/touch).
2. **Require sign-in to unlock**: configure the lock screen/unlock flow so that unlocking always triggers the **normal sign-in method** (password/PIN, smart card, or SSO-backed OS sign-in) and **does not allow “resume without sign-in”** or any unlock bypass.
3. **Disable/limit auto-unlock and trusted-device behaviours** that could weaken re-authentication (e.g., “unlock with trusted device” where it would reduce credential verification strength).
4. **Centralise and enforce configuration drift control**: manage settings through the endpoint management platform and continuously audit/report non-compliant devices (e.g., lock timer not 15 minutes; unlock without sign-in enabled).
5. **Operationalise evidence for audits**: collect configuration compliance reports and retain them per organisational retention standards.
6. **Ensure device lock is not treated as a substitute for logout**: implement complementary policy/technical guardrails for end-of-day or session-end requirements (e.g., require logout/shutdown handling for systems where policy mandates it), and train users that locking is for short absences only.

## WHO
- **Endpoint/Workplace Engineering Lead**: implements and maintains the managed endpoint configuration baseline.
- **Identity & Access Management (IAM) Lead**: confirms the unlock flow uses the organisation’s normal authentication method.
- **Security Compliance/Assurance**: validates evidence, monitors drift reports, and ensures alignment with end-of-day logout policy.

## Acceptance criteria
- All in-scope managed endpoints enforce **automatic lock after 15 minutes of inactivity**.
- Unlocking a locked device **always requires normal sign-in** (no resume/unlock without authentication).
- Devices cannot be used for further logical access while locked (lock screen/secure desktop enforced).
- Endpoint management compliance reporting shows **no drift** from the 15-minute setting and unlock-without-sign-in is disabled.
- Documented evidence (policy/configuration reports) is available for audit.
- A separate, documented requirement exists for end-of-day/session-end logout where mandated, and users are instructed that device lock is not a substitute.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Configure the endpoint OS inactivity lock timer to 15 minutes for all in-scope managed devices
- [ ] Enforce unlock requiring the organisation’s normal sign-in method and disable any unlock/resume without sign-in options
- [ ] Disable or restrict auto-unlock/trusted-device features that could bypass re-authentication
- [ ] Deploy the configuration via the endpoint management platform and enable continuous compliance reporting
- [ ] Review non-compliance reports and remediate devices that do not meet the 15-minute lock and sign-in-on-unlock requirements
- [ ] Publish and confirm user guidance that device lock is for short absences and does not replace end-of-day/session-end logout requirements

### ac-11.1 — Pattern-hiding Displays (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure that the moment someone locks their computer or phone, anything that was previously showing on the screen is hidden from view and replaced with a harmless picture. Without this, a passer-by could glance at the screen and read sensitive information that was left open, such as parts of a document, email, or other controlled material, even though the device is “locked”. To meet this expectation, the organisation must set up devices so that the lock action immediately conceals the earlier screen content and shows only an approved, publicly viewable display such as a pattern, a screen-saver style image, a photograph, a solid colour, a clock or battery indicator, or a blank screen. The organisation must document how locking works, record the relevant device settings and how the behaviour is built into the system, and be able to show evidence that the screen stays hidden and does not briefly “ghost” the old content during and after locking.

#### Enforce approved lock-screen pattern-hiding on managed endpoints

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Configure managed endpoints so that when a user session is locked, any previously visible display content is immediately concealed and replaced with an approved publicly viewable lock image (e.g., static/dynamic pattern, photographic image, solid colour, clock/battery indicator, or blank).

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-11(1) (Enhancement) requires that information previously visible on the display is concealed via the device lock with a publicly viewable image. This prevents bystanders from reading or inferring controlled unclassified information during the locked state.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Standardise endpoint lock behaviour** using the OS-native lock-screen mechanism (avoid custom overlays that may not fully clear the previous framebuffer).
2. **Enforce lock-screen image type and content** via enterprise device management (MDM/UEM) so only approved image categories are used (pattern/screen-saver style image, photograph, solid colour, clock/battery indicator, or blank).
3. **Disable lock-screen content leakage features** (e.g., lock-screen previews/status details/quick glance widgets) that could reveal sensitive information while locked.
4. **Validate lock transition and ghosting resistance** by running a repeatable test: open a representative sensitive document/view, trigger manual lock and inactivity lock, and verify the previous content is not visible at any point during/after locking (including no brief “ghosting” or bleed-through) for the entire locked state.
5. **Record and evidence configuration** by exporting the enforced device management policy settings and capturing test results/screenshots from representative endpoint builds.
6. **Continuously detect drift** by monitoring endpoint compliance for lock-screen policy adherence (approved image type, previews disabled) and alerting on non-compliance.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Endpoint Engineering / IT Operations**: implement and maintain OS/MDM lock-screen configuration baselines.
- **Security Engineering / Compliance**: define evidence requirements, review test results, and validate that no controlled unclassified information can be displayed while locked.
- **Service Desk / IT Support**: ensure users can reliably trigger lock (manual lock) and that inactivity lock settings are enforced.

## Acceptance criteria
- On a representative managed endpoint, after triggering **manual lock** and **inactivity lock**, the previously visible screen content is **not readable or recognisable** from normal viewing distance.
- The lock screen shows only an **approved publicly viewable image type** (pattern/photograph/solid/clock/battery/blank) and remains consistent for the locked state.
- No **ghosting/partial reveal/bleed-through** occurs during the lock transition.
- Evidence exists: exported device management policy settings and documented test results demonstrating the above.
- Endpoint compliance monitoring flags any drift from the approved lock-screen configuration.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Implement an OS-native lock-screen configuration baseline and enforce it via MDM/UEM for all managed endpoints
- [ ] Configure the lock screen to use only approved publicly viewable image types (pattern/photograph/solid/clock/battery/blank)
- [ ] Disable lock-screen previews/status details/quick-glance widgets that could expose sensitive content while locked
- [ ] Run a repeatable test that opens representative sensitive content, triggers manual and inactivity lock, and verifies no ghosting/bleed-through
- [ ] Export and retain device management policy evidence and the test results/screenshots for audit
- [ ] Enable endpoint compliance monitoring and alerting for lock-screen policy drift (non-approved image types or previews re-enabled)

### ac-12 — Session Termination (Control)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about automatically ending a user’s access session when it should no longer be needed, such as after 30 minutes of inactivity, right away if an account is locked or suspected of being compromised, and at the system’s defined off-hours for non-privileged users. Without this, someone could keep using an unattended or wrongly accessed account for longer than intended, increasing the chance of unauthorised actions, data exposure, or damage before anyone notices. To meet this expectation, the organisation must clearly define what counts as inactivity and what events mean “account locked” or “suspected compromise”, set the off-hours schedule for non-privileged users, and ensure the system automatically enforces these rules. When a session ends, all activity linked to that session should stop, except for any tasks the user specifically set up to continue. The organisation must also document the process, include it in its access rules, keep records of the relevant settings and design, maintain a list of the trigger events, and log when session endings happen and why.

#### Enforce AC-12 session termination via IdP policies and app session revocation

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement automatic termination of user logical sessions based on the organisation-defined triggers in **AC-12 (ac-12_odp)**: **(1) 30 minutes of user inactivity, (2) immediate termination upon account lockout, (3) immediate termination upon suspected compromise, and (4) off-hours termination for non-privileged users**.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-12 requires that the system **automatically terminate a user session after {{ insert: param, ac-12_odp }}**. Guidance clarifies this is about **logical sessions** (not network disconnects) and that termination should **end all processes associated with the user’s logical session by default**, except for processes **specifically created by the session owner to continue after termination**.

## HOW (specific approach)
Use the enterprise identity provider (IdP) as the authoritative enforcement point for logical session termination, and ensure applications honour IdP session invalidation so that app-side sessions and background user-linked processes do not persist after termination.

1. **Define and implement the trigger set (ac-12_odp) in the IdP**
   - Configure an **idle/inactivity timeout of 30 minutes** for interactive user sessions.
   - Configure **immediate session revocation/sign-out** when the account enters **lockout** state.
   - Configure **immediate session revocation/sign-out** when the account is marked for **suspected compromise** (e.g., via automated disablement, risk-based action, or SOAR-driven account state change).
   - Configure **time-of-day/off-hours session termination** for **non-privileged** users using role/group scoping (exclude privileged groups/roles).

2. **Ensure applications terminate their own logical sessions when the IdP session ends**
   - For web/API clients using OIDC/SAML, ensure the application validates tokens/sessions such that **IdP sign-out/session invalidation prevents continued access**.
   - Disable or tightly constrain long-lived application sessions/refresh behaviour so that **token/session invalidation at the IdP results in immediate user session termination at the application**.
   - For any background jobs started by the user, implement a rule: **only user-created continuation processes may continue**; otherwise, background tasks must be bound to the user session context and cancelled/terminated on session end.

3. **Wire incident/account-state events to session termination**
   - Integrate SOC/SOAR (or the account management workflow) so that **suspected compromise** results in an IdP action that triggers session revocation (e.g., account disablement/lockout/risk action that forces sign-out).

4. **Make it auditable**
   - Ensure IdP and application logs record **termination time** and **termination cause** (idle timeout vs lockout vs suspected compromise vs off-hours).
   - Forward logs to SIEM and retain according to organisational logging/retention requirements.

## WHO (roles responsible)
- **Identity & Access Management (IAM) Engineer**: implement IdP session policies (idle timeout, off-hours, lockout/compromise actions) and token/session invalidation behaviour.
- **Application Owner/Lead Developer**: ensure application session management honours IdP session termination and implements background-job continuation/cancellation rules.
- **SOC Analyst / SOAR Engineer**: ensure suspected compromise workflow triggers the required IdP account state changes.
- **Security/Compliance Officer**: verify evidence sufficiency and that privileged/non-privileged scoping matches policy.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
- A user session is **automatically terminated after 30 minutes of inactivity** (no authenticated user actions) and the termination is recorded with cause = idle timeout.
- When a user account is **locked out**, the user’s logical session is **terminated immediately** and recorded with cause = lockout.
- When a user is marked for **suspected compromise** (per organisation workflow), the user’s logical session is **terminated immediately** and recorded with cause = suspected compromise.
- During **system-defined off-hours**, **non-privileged** users are terminated automatically; **privileged** users are not terminated by this off-hours rule.
- After termination, the application does not allow continued access using existing session state/tokens, and **background processes linked to the session are stopped by default**, except explicitly session-owner-created continuation processes.
- Audit evidence exists showing **termination events, trigger cause, and timestamps** in IdP/application logs forwarded to SIEM.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Configure IdP session policies to enforce a 30-minute inactivity timeout for interactive logical sessions
- [ ] Configure IdP actions to immediately revoke/sign-out sessions on account lockout events
- [ ] Integrate suspected-compromise workflow (SOAR/SOC) to drive an IdP account state change that forces immediate session revocation
- [ ] Implement off-hours session termination for non-privileged users using role/group scoping and the organisation-defined schedule
- [ ] Update application session handling so IdP sign-out/session invalidation prevents continued access (including refresh/session lifetimes)
- [ ] Implement background-job/session binding so user-linked processes are terminated by default, allowing only explicitly session-owner-created continuation processes
- [ ] Enable and forward audit logs that record session termination timestamps and termination cause to the SIEM for evidence

### ac-12.1 — User-initiated Logouts (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure people can end their own signed-in sessions whenever they want, whether they are using a work computer, a password-protected website, or an authenticated database connection. Without an easy, user-triggered logout, someone could accidentally leave access open, or a session could remain active longer than intended, increasing the risk that unauthorised people use the same access to view or change information. To meet this expectation, the organisation must provide a clear logout option that normal users can select on demand for each of these authenticated session types, and when they do, the session must actually stop so they can no longer use it without signing in again. The organisation must also have documented procedures for how logout is handled, ensure the system is set up to support it, show a user-facing confirmation that the session has ended, and record logout/session-ending events in its audit records so the action can be verified later.

#### User-initiated logout across web sessions and workstation SSO

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** High

## WHAT
Implement a user-initiated “Log out/Sign out” capability that terminates the authenticated session for user-initiated communications sessions where authentication is used to access the organisation’s information resources (including password-protected web services and authenticated workstation access).

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 AC-12(1) (enhancement) requires: “Provide a logout capability for user-initiated communications sessions whenever authentication is used to gain access to {{ insert: param, ac-12.01_odp }}.” The control guidance clarifies that information resources include local workstations and password-protected web services. The logout must be available on demand and end the authenticated session.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Standardise authentication via the enterprise IdP** for the application’s web tier (OIDC/SAML) so that the application relies on the IdP for session state.
2. **Add a user-facing logout action** (e.g., “Log out” in the account/profile menu) that triggers the correct IdP logout flow (RP-initiated logout for OIDC, or SLO where supported for SAML) and then redirects the user to a public landing page.
3. **Ensure relying-party session termination**: on logout, clear application session cookies and server-side session state (e.g., invalidate session IDs in the app/session store) so that the user cannot continue using the application without re-authenticating.
4. **Ensure workstation sign-out behaviour**: when users sign out from the workstation/browser SSO context, ensure the IdP session is ended (or the relying-party session is cleared) so that the user cannot resume access by returning to the application.
5. **Provide user confirmation**: after logout completes, show a confirmation page/message indicating the session has ended.
6. **Audit logout/session termination**: emit audit events for logout/session termination including user identifier, session identifier (where available), relying-party/app identifier, timestamp, and source IP; ensure these events are forwarded to the SIEM.
7. **Document and test**: update the system security documentation/runbooks to describe logout behaviour, expected session termination semantics, and troubleshooting steps; test logout end-to-end in each environment (prod/test/DR) to confirm the user cannot access protected pages or APIs after logout.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Application owner / lead engineer**: implement logout UX, session invalidation, and audit event emission.
- **Identity/IAM engineer**: configure IdP logout endpoints/flows and token/session revocation semantics.
- **Security engineer**: validate audit fields, retention, and evidence generation; review documentation.

## Acceptance criteria
- A user can initiate logout from the application UI and is returned to a non-authenticated landing page.
- After logout, the user cannot access protected web pages or authenticated endpoints without re-authenticating (verified via browser back button and direct URL/API attempts).
- Application session cookies and server-side session state are cleared/inactivated on logout.
- IdP logout is invoked such that the authenticated IdP session is ended or the relying-party session is reliably terminated per the configured IdP/RP semantics.
- A user-facing confirmation is displayed indicating logout/session termination.
- Logout/session termination events are recorded in audit logs/SIEM with required identifiers and timestamps.
- End-to-end tests and documented procedures exist for production and at least one non-production environment.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Implement a user-facing “Log out/Sign out” control in the application UI that initiates the enterprise IdP logout flow
- [ ] Configure OIDC RP-initiated logout (or SAML SLO where applicable) and ensure correct redirect/landing behaviour after logout
- [ ] Invalidate server-side application sessions and clear all authentication/session cookies on logout
- [ ] Verify workstation/browser SSO sign-out behaviour so users cannot resume access after logout (including back-button and direct URL/API attempts)
- [ ] Emit and forward audit events for logout/session termination with user ID, session ID (if available), relying-party ID, timestamp, and source IP
- [ ] Create and maintain runbooks/system documentation describing logout/session termination semantics and troubleshooting steps
- [ ] Execute end-to-end logout tests across production and at least one segregated environment and record evidence for audit

### ac-12.2 — Termination Message (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure people clearly see when their login session has ended. In everyday terms, if a system simply cuts you off without an obvious message, it can be unclear whether you were properly logged out, which can lead to confusion, mistakes, or the risk that someone else could make use of an active session you thought had finished. To meet this expectation, the organisation must ensure that whenever a user’s authenticated session ends, the system shows an explicit logout message that clearly confirms the session has been terminated, not just that the connection dropped. For browser-based access, the message should appear after the session has ended. For file transfer sessions such as FTP, the message should be sent and shown as the final message before the session is closed. The organisation should also have documented procedures, defined system behaviour for different session types, the right system settings, and records showing that session termination and the logout message occurred as required.

#### Explicit logout/termination message after session end (web + FTP)

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** High

## WHAT
Implement an explicit, termination-confirming logout message that is presented to users when an authenticated communications session ends. For web access, the message must be displayed after the authenticated session has been terminated. For FTP sessions, the logout message must be sent and shown as the final message prior to terminating the session.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-12(2) (Enhancement) requires that users receive an explicit logout message indicating termination of authenticated communications sessions. Guidance clarifies that web logout messages can be shown after termination, while FTP typically sends logout messages as final messages prior to terminating sessions.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define session-type behaviour** in the application/session management design: (a) web/browser sessions render a post-logout page/message only after server-side session invalidation; (b) FTP sessions send a final 221/530-style termination response (or equivalent for the FTP server) immediately before closing the control connection.
2. **Web implementation (SSO/OIDC/SAML + app sessions):**
   - Orchestrate logout so that the IdP/app session termination occurs first (invalidate server-side session and revoke/clear tokens as applicable).
   - Render the termination message from the server after invalidation (not purely client-side), using a dedicated post-logout endpoint/page.
   - Prevent the termination message page from being reachable while the session is still valid (e.g., require a logout event identifier and verify it server-side, or verify session invalidation before rendering).
3. **FTP implementation:**
   - Configure the FTP server to emit an explicit termination response as the final message for authenticated sessions (e.g., on logout/QUIT and on forced disconnect paths that correspond to authenticated session termination).
   - Ensure the message is sent before the server closes the connection (validate ordering in integration tests).
4. **Logging and evidence:**
   - Emit audit events for (a) session termination (web: server-side session invalidation and/or IdP logout event correlation; FTP: authenticated session termination/QUIT/disconnect reason) and (b) delivery of the termination message (web: post-logout page served; FTP: response code/message sent).
   - Correlate events with a request/session identifier to support audit review.
5. **Operational documentation:**
   - Document the timing diagrams and expected behaviour per session type (web vs FTP), including edge cases (user-initiated logout, idle timeout, admin-forced termination, token expiry).
   - Include configuration locations and change-control steps for maintaining the behaviour across environments.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Application security engineer / IAM engineer:** define logout orchestration and session-type behaviour.
- **Application engineer:** implement web post-logout endpoint/page gating and server-side invalidation.
- **Infrastructure/FTP server engineer:** configure FTP final-message behaviour and disconnect ordering.
- **Compliance/audit lead:** validate evidence requirements and acceptance criteria during testing.

## Acceptance criteria
- **Web:** After logout, the user sees an explicit message confirming authenticated session termination, and the message is only rendered after server-side session invalidation (verified by integration test and server logs).
- **FTP:** For authenticated FTP sessions, the server sends an explicit termination response as the final message before closing the connection (verified by packet/response-order test).
- **Edge cases:** Idle timeout and admin-forced termination also result in the explicit termination message/response according to the defined session-type behaviour.
- **Audit evidence:** Logs show correlated session termination and message delivery (web post-logout served; FTP termination response sent) for each tested scenario.
- **Documentation:** Runbook/design notes exist describing timing and configuration for web and FTP session types.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Document session-type logout timing rules for web and FTP (including idle timeout and forced termination) in the system design/runbook
- [ ] Implement server-side web logout orchestration that invalidates the authenticated session before rendering a post-logout termination message
- [ ] Create a gated post-logout endpoint/page that verifies session invalidation (or a logout event identifier) before displaying the termination message
- [ ] Configure the FTP server to send an explicit termination response as the final message for authenticated session termination paths (QUIT/logout and forced disconnects)
- [ ] Add correlated audit logging for session termination and termination-message delivery for both web and FTP flows
- [ ] Execute integration tests to verify message ordering (web: after invalidation; FTP: final response before connection close) and record evidence for audit

### ac-12.3 — Timeout Warning Message (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about giving people a clear heads-up before their sign-in session ends, so they are not suddenly cut off. Without a specific warning, users could lose work, miss important deadlines, or be confused about why they were logged out, which can also create opportunities for mistakes or unsafe behaviour when people try to work around the problem. To meet this expectation, the organisation must set up the system so that, during an active session, it displays an explicit message stating that the session will end in 5 minutes, using the organisation’s configured 5-minute timing. The message should be shown before the logout happens, remain understandable to a typical user, and ideally prompt the user to continue the session. The organisation must also have documented procedures for how sessions are terminated and when the 5-minute warning is displayed, and it must keep records and documentation that demonstrate the warning timing and that sessions end after the expected period.

#### Show 5-minute session-end warning via IdP and app session policy

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** High

## WHAT
Implement an explicit, user-facing warning during an active authenticated session stating that the session will end in **{{ insert: param, ac-12.03_odp }}** (organisation-configured, default **5 minutes**), and ensure the warning is displayed **before** the session termination occurs.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-12(3) (enhancement) requires: **“Display an explicit message to users indicating that the session will end in {{ insert: param, ac-12.03_odp }}.”** Guidance further states this is to **increase usability** by notifying users of **pending session termination** and prompting them to **continue the session**, with the pending termination time period based on the **AC-12 base control parameters**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Centralise session lifetime and warning offset** at the enterprise identity layer (IdP) used by the application (e.g., SSO for web/mobile portals). Configure the IdP so that the warning triggers **exactly {{ ac-12.03_odp }} (5 minutes)** before the session ends, aligned to the same session lifetime that drives logout.
2. Configure the IdP (or IdP-driven front-end) to display a **clear, unambiguous message** during the authenticated session, e.g.:
   - “Your session will end in **5 minutes**.”
   - Include a **prompt to continue** (e.g., “Continue session” / “Stay signed in”).
3. Ensure the **continue** action results in a **real session extension** (fresh session/reauthentication or extension token) rather than only dismissing the warning.
4. **Instrument audit evidence**: export IdP session/sign-in and session termination events to the SIEM/logging platform, and retain logs sufficient to verify the warning-to-logout timing.
5. **Document configuration and procedure**: record where the warning offset is configured, how the message is rendered, and the operational procedure for session termination and warning behaviour (including how changes are managed).
6. **Validate with controlled testing** in the production-like environment: measure the time between the warning display and the actual session termination; confirm it is approximately **5 minutes** within an agreed tolerance (e.g., ±30 seconds) and that the message appears while the user is still authenticated.

## WHO
- **Identity & Access Management (IAM) Engineer**: configure IdP session lifetime and warning offset; implement/enable the warning UI and continue/extension behaviour.
- **Application Owner / Security Engineer**: confirm the application relies on the IdP session for termination and that the continue action extends the session.
- **SOC/SIEM Engineer**: ensure audit logs/events are exported and retained for timing verification.
- **Change Manager**: ensure configuration changes follow the organisation’s change management process.

## Acceptance criteria
- When a user is authenticated, the application/IdP displays an explicit message stating the session will end in **{{ ac-12.03_odp }} (5 minutes)**.
- The warning is shown **before** the session terminates (no late/after-logout messaging).
- The continue action **extends** the session (or triggers reauthentication/extension) and prevents immediate termination.
- Audit evidence exists showing warning display timing and subsequent session termination timing, and test results demonstrate the delta is **~5 minutes** within the agreed tolerance.
- Documentation exists describing the configuration location, message content, and operational procedure for session termination/warning behaviour.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Configure the IdP session lifetime and warning offset so the warning triggers exactly {{ ac-12.03_odp }} (5 minutes) before session termination
- [ ] Implement/enable a user-facing warning message during the authenticated session stating the session will end in 5 minutes
- [ ] Configure the warning “continue/stay signed in” action to extend the session (reauthentication or session extension) rather than only dismissing the warning
- [ ] Enable and export IdP session/sign-in and session termination audit events to the SIEM with sufficient retention for timing verification
- [ ] Document the warning mechanism, configuration parameters, and operational procedure for session termination and warning display
- [ ] Run a controlled test to measure warning-to-logout timing and record evidence that the delta matches 5 minutes within the agreed tolerance

### ac-14 — Permitted Actions Without Identification or Authentication (Control)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure only a clearly defined set of actions can happen on a system before anyone has proved who they are or logged in, such as viewing public information pages, receiving inbound calls on a mobile phone, receiving facsimiles, and carrying out emergency maintenance using a protected physical bypass switch. Without this clarity, the wrong people could try to use the system without permission, potentially exposing information, disrupting services, or abusing emergency access. To meet the expectation, the organisation must identify which specific unauthenticated actions are allowed and confirm they fit the organisation’s mission and day-to-day business needs. It must then document in the system’s security plan both the exact list of these permitted actions and a plain-language reason for why identity checks and login are not required for each one, ensuring that normal restricted activities are not included.

#### Define and document permitted unauthenticated actions per system

- **Category:** Manual
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Define a **complete, explicit list** of user actions that the application/system may perform **before identification and authentication**, limited to the organisation-defined scope (e.g., **access to public information pages; receiving inbound calls via mobile phone; receiving facsimiles; emergency maintenance via a protected physical bypass switch**). For each permitted action, document **why** identification/authentication is not required and ensure no other actions are implicitly allowed.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-14 requires that the organisation allows only those **specific user actions** that can occur **without identification or authentication**, and that any bypasses are limited to situations where identification/authentication **has not yet occurred**. The control guidance also expects the organisation to **identify actions that normally require identification/authentication but may be bypassed under certain circumstances**, and to document the permitted actions and rationale in the system security plan.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create an AC-14 unauthenticated actions register** for the specific system/service boundary, containing one entry per permitted action (including the four organisation-defined actions).
2. For each entry, record:
   - the **exact action** (e.g., which public web routes/pages; which telephony number(s)/IVR flows; which fax ingestion endpoints; what emergency maintenance function the physical switch enables),
   - the **expected outcome** (what the user can do/receive),
   - the **explicit exclusions** (what must not be possible pre-authentication, such as any data access beyond public content, any account changes, any administrative functions), and
   - a **plain-language rationale** for why identification/authentication is not required for that action.
3. **Update the system security plan** to include the AC-14 section generated from the register, ensuring it clearly states the permitted actions are those that occur **before** identification/authentication and that the value is set to **“none”** if the system truly supports no unauthenticated actions.
4. **Validate enforcement through configuration and tests** aligned to each action:
   - Web: ensure unauthenticated access is restricted to the defined public routes/pages; all other routes require authentication.
   - Mobile calls: ensure call handling is limited to receive-only workflows (e.g., call routing/recording/notification) and does not expose authenticated-only capabilities.
   - Faxes: ensure inbound fax ingestion is accepted, but unauthenticated retrieval/management is blocked; store/queue faxes for authenticated processing.
   - Emergency bypass: document the bypass procedure, ensure the bypass is **protected from accidental or unmonitored use**, and ensure the system generates **audit evidence** when bypass is used.
5. **Perform negative testing** (unauthenticated attempts) to confirm that actions outside the register are denied and do not leak information.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **System Owner / Application Owner**: accountable for the AC-14 unauthenticated actions register and security-plan updates.
- **Security Architect / IAM & Access Control Lead**: reviews rationale, exclusions, and ensures alignment with enterprise access control patterns.
- **Engineering Lead (App/Platform)**: implements and verifies technical enforcement and test evidence.
- **Operations / OT/Plant Maintenance Lead (if applicable)**: validates emergency bypass procedure and auditability.

## Acceptance criteria
- The system security plan contains an AC-14 section with an **explicit list** of permitted unauthenticated actions and a **rationale for each**.
- For each permitted action, there is an **explicit exclusion list** showing what is not allowed pre-authentication.
- Unauthenticated negative tests confirm that any action not in the register is **denied** and does not expose confidential/sensitive information.
- Emergency maintenance via the physical bypass switch is documented, protected from accidental/unmonitored use, and produces **traceable audit evidence** when used.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Create an AC-14 unauthenticated actions register for the system boundary with one entry per permitted action
- [ ] Document per action the exact allowed behaviour, explicit exclusions, and a plain-language rationale for bypassing identification/authentication
- [ ] Update the system security plan to include the AC-14 permitted unauthenticated actions list and rationale (or set to "none" if applicable)
- [ ] Implement technical enforcement so only the defined public routes/pages, receive-only call flows, and fax ingestion are reachable pre-authentication
- [ ] Configure emergency maintenance bypass to be protected from accidental/unmonitored use and to generate audit evidence when used
- [ ] Run unauthenticated positive and negative tests to prove only registered actions succeed and all others are denied

### ac-16 — Security and Privacy Attributes (Control)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure every piece of information carries the right labels about how it should be handled, who it relates to, and how long it should be kept, from the moment it is created until it is deleted. Without this, confidential or regulated information could be shared with the wrong people, kept for too long, sent without the right protection, or used without the required permission—creating legal, financial, and reputational harm. The organisation must ensure that, in enterprise email, document repositories, operational technology systems, operational control workstations, and API gateways, the system attaches and keeps permitted security and privacy labels with the information in storage, during processing, and while being sent. These labels must use only approved values for classification (Confidential or Secret), export control status (Yes/No), retention period (30–365 days), encryption requirement (Required or Optional), and access domain, and where relevant privacy labels for whether it contains personal data, whether it is a specific health identifier, whether consent/authorisation is granted, and whether the person is an employee, contractor, or customer. The organisation must record and audit any label changes, and review both security and privacy label suitability at least every 90 days.

#### Authoritative security/privacy labels bound and audited across data lifecycle

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT must be done
Implement mechanisms in the application to **bind permitted security attributes and privacy attributes** to information in **storage, processing, and transmission**, ensure the bindings **persist with the information**, and ensure **label changes are auditable**. Enforce that only **organisation-permitted attribute types and values/ranges** are used, and run **applicability reviews at least every 90 days** for both security and privacy attributes.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-16 requires that security and privacy attributes (metadata) are **associated (bound) to active and passive entities** (subjects/objects) and that this binding supports **enforcement of security and privacy policies** across the information lifecycle. The guidance also stresses that attribute values/types must be **meaningful and relevant** (permitted ranges/values), that binding strength affects assurance, and that organisations should **review applicability** of security and privacy attributes at a defined frequency.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define an authoritative attribute schema** in the application domain model for the covered systems, using the organisation-defined types/values:
   - Security attributes: *Information classification; export control (ITAR/EAR); retention category; encryption requirement; access domain*.
   - Privacy attributes: *PII indicator; HIPAA identifier flag; consent/authorisation; data subject role*.
   - Use only permitted values/ranges (e.g., *Confidential/Secret; ITAR/EAR Yes/No; retention 30–365 days; encryption Required/Optional; PII Yes/No; HIPAA Yes/No; consent Granted/Not granted*).
2. **Create a binding model** so every content item (e.g., document, message, record, event payload) has a **label envelope** persisted alongside the content (or in an associated metadata store with a strong referential link). The binding must include:
   - attribute type identifiers,
   - attribute values,
   - binding method/strength indicator (e.g., user-selected vs system-detected vs policy-assigned),
   - binding timestamp and actor.
3. **Bind at creation and preserve through workflows**:
   - On create/upload/send: require label assignment (manual selection or policy-driven prefill) and persist the binding with the item.
   - On transformation/copy/versioning: propagate the label envelope to derived artefacts unless an authorised workflow explicitly changes it.
   - On processing: ensure internal services read the label envelope from the item context and do not drop/blank it.
4. **Enforce permitted values**:
   - Implement server-side allow-list validation for each attribute type and value/range.
   - Reject or normalise invalid values (never persist unknown/blank/unauthorised labels).
   - Restrict label-change operations to authorised roles and require an explicit reason code.
5. **Audit label binding and changes**:
   - Emit structured audit events for: initial binding, label changes, and label propagation decisions.
   - Each audit record must include item identifier, attribute(s) changed, before/after values, timestamp, actor identity, and originating workflow/service.
   - Forward audit events to the enterprise SIEM/logging platform.
6. **Run 90-day applicability reviews**:
   - Implement a scheduled governance job/workflow that enumerates label usage and checks applicability against the organisation-defined attribute sets.
   - Produce review evidence (e.g., report of labels in use, exceptions, and any required remediation such as re-labelling or schema updates).

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Application owner / engineering lead**: implement label envelope persistence, propagation, validation, and audit emission.
- **Information security & privacy governance**: define/maintain permitted attribute types/values and approve review criteria.
- **Compliance/assurance analyst**: verify audit evidence and review outputs for the 90-day cycle.
- **Privileged security administrators**: approve/authorise label-change operations and exception handling.

## Acceptance criteria
- For all covered information flows (create/upload/send; internal processing; storage/versioning; transmission), the application **persists and propagates** the security/privacy label envelope with the content.
- The application **only accepts permitted attribute types and values/ranges**; invalid values are rejected/normalised and not stored.
- Every label binding and label change generates an **audit event** containing before/after values, actor, timestamp, and item identifier, and these events are visible in the SIEM.
- A **90-day applicability review** produces auditable evidence showing that security and privacy attribute applicability has been reviewed and exceptions/remediations are tracked.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define the permitted security and privacy attribute schema and allow-lists (types and values/ranges) for the application’s covered data objects
- [ ] Implement a persisted label envelope (security + privacy attributes) bound to each content item and propagated across create, transform, version, and send workflows
- [ ] Add server-side validation to reject/normalise any attribute values outside permitted ranges and prevent blank/unknown labels from being stored
- [ ] Restrict label-change operations to authorised roles and require a reason code and workflow context for any modification
- [ ] Emit structured audit events for initial binding and every label change (item ID, before/after values, actor, timestamp, service/workflow) and forward to SIEM
- [ ] Schedule and execute a 90-day applicability review workflow for security and privacy attributes and store review evidence for audit

### ac-16.1 — Dynamic Attribute Association (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This expectation is about making sure the right security and privacy rules automatically “stick” to the right people, accounts, and information as things are created, updated, combined, or moved.
