4. **Integrate with enterprise identity and session controls**: Require central identity authentication for remote access (e.g., SSO with conditional access) and ensure session establishment is tied to authenticated identity; terminate sessions on policy changes and re-authenticate on risk where supported.
5. **Generate audit evidence**: Enable logging that demonstrates encrypted remote access was used per session (e.g., VPN tunnel establishment/teardown, negotiated protocol/cipher where available, user identity, source IP, session timestamps). Forward logs to SIEM and retain per the system security plan.
6. **Document cryptographic mechanisms and configuration**: Maintain an auditable record of which cryptographic mechanisms are used for remote access and how they are configured (including exported endpoint/VPN configuration baselines and references to the approved crypto standard).

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Security Architect / IAM & Network Security Lead**: define approved remote access methods and cryptographic baselines.
- **Network/Platform Engineers**: implement and enforce VPN/TLS configurations and edge blocking.
- **Application/DevOps Engineers**: ensure any remote administration interfaces use approved TLS configurations.
- **SOC/SIEM Administrator**: ensure logs are collected, normalised, and retained.
- **System Owner / Compliance Lead**: ensure documentation and evidence align to the system security plan and change management.

## Acceptance criteria
- All remote access to the application environment is only possible via the approved encrypted mechanisms; direct unencrypted/weakly encrypted paths are blocked.
- VPN and/or TLS endpoints are configured to use the organisation-approved cryptographic baselines (no legacy/insecure protocols/ciphers enabled).
- For each remote access session, audit logs exist showing encrypted session establishment and associated identity/session metadata.
- Documentation exists mapping remote access entry points to the cryptographic mechanisms and configurations used, and is kept current via change control.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define and publish approved remote access methods (VPN and/or TLS) as the only supported paths in the access control policy
- [ ] Configure network edge controls to block direct internet access to internal resources and allow only approved VPN/TLS entry points
- [ ] Apply organisation-approved cryptographic baselines to VPN and TLS endpoints and disable legacy/insecure protocol and cipher options
- [ ] Require enterprise identity authentication for remote access and enforce session controls tied to authenticated identity
- [ ] Enable and centralise per-session audit logging for encrypted remote access (tunnel/session start-stop, negotiated crypto where available, user identity, source IP)
- [ ] Maintain an auditable configuration record (exported endpoint/VPN baselines and mapping to the approved crypto standard) under change control

### ac-17.3 — Managed Access Control Points (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure that anyone connecting to your systems from outside the office does so through approved entry points that your organisation controls and monitors, rather than finding a direct route in. Without this, attackers could bypass your safeguards, reach systems from the open internet, and potentially steal data, disrupt services, or take over accounts. To meet this expectation, your organisation must set clear written rules and step-by-step procedures for remote access that specify the approved entry points, and it must keep up-to-date design and configuration records showing how remote access is routed through those points. You also need a current, complete list of every approved entry point used for remote access, plus audit records that demonstrate the routing is actually happening, and a system security plan that describes and supports this approach.

#### Route all remote access via managed gateways with enforced routing

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT must be done
Route all remote access to the in-scope enterprise application (including administrative access) through a small set of organisation-authorised and technically enforced network access control points (managed access control points), and ensure direct paths to target systems are blocked.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-17(3) (Enhancement) requires that remote accesses are routed through authorised and managed network access control points. The guidance emphasises that limiting the number of access control points reduces attack surface.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the managed access control points set**: Select the approved entry points (e.g., VPN concentrators, ZTNA connectors, secure web/remote access proxy) and create an authoritative register including hostname/IP, purpose (admin vs user access), ownership, monitoring owner, and change-control reference.
2. **Enforce “gateway-only” connectivity to targets**: Update network security controls so target application/admin interfaces accept inbound connections only from the managed gateway networks/segments (e.g., security groups, firewall rules, NACLs) and explicitly deny direct internet-to-target paths.
3. **Integrate access control at the entry point**: Configure the gateways to use the organisation’s central identity provider with conditional access and phishing-resistant MFA where available. Ensure session establishment and authorisation decisions occur at the gateway.
4. **Make routing verifiable and continuously validated**: Implement configuration-as-code (or equivalent) for gateway and firewall/security group rules, with automated checks that “only gateway-to-target” rules exist for required ports/protocols.
5. **Centralise and retain evidence**: Forward gateway logs and network allow/deny logs (including authentication/session events) to the SIEM, and retain them per enterprise logging retention standards. Ensure the system security plan references these log sources and the expected routing behaviour.
6. **Reconcile the managed access control points register**: Periodically reconcile the register against actual gateway configuration exports and network rule inventories to ensure completeness and prevent “shadow” entry points.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Network Security Engineering**: implement and maintain gateway-only routing and firewall/security group enforcement.
- **IAM/Identity Team**: configure gateway authentication/conditional access and MFA.
- **Application Owner / Platform Team**: ensure application/admin endpoints are reachable only via approved gateway paths.
- **GRC/Compliance (or Security Assurance)**: maintain the managed access control points register, system security plan references, and evidence review cadence.

## Acceptance criteria
- All remote access paths to in-scope application administrative interfaces are reachable only via the approved managed access control points; direct internet access to target admin ports is blocked.
- The managed access control points register is complete, current, and reconciled against deployed gateway and network rule configurations.
- Gateway authentication/session logs and network routing logs are collected in the SIEM and retained; audit evidence demonstrates that sessions originate via approved control points.
- System design documentation (network diagrams / architecture records) and the system security plan explicitly describe the routing through managed access control points and the associated log sources.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Create and maintain an authoritative register of approved managed access control points (identifier, purpose, owner, monitoring owner, last reviewed date)
- [ ] Configure network security controls so target application/admin endpoints allow inbound access only from managed gateway networks/segments and deny direct internet-to-target paths
- [ ] Integrate gateways with the enterprise identity provider using conditional access and phishing-resistant MFA where available
- [ ] Implement configuration-as-code and automated validation to ensure only gateway-to-target connectivity rules exist for required ports/protocols
- [ ] Forward gateway authentication/session logs and network allow/deny routing logs to the SIEM and verify log completeness for remote access sessions
- [ ] Update the system security plan and architecture/network diagrams to document that remote access is routed via managed access control points and list the expected log sources
- [ ] Reconcile the managed access control points register against deployed gateway and network rule inventories on a defined cadence

### ac-17.4 — Privileged Commands and Access (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This expectation is about making sure that when someone needs to log in from elsewhere to do high-privilege work or look at sensitive security information, it is only done for clearly approved reasons and in a way that can be checked later. Without this, remote access could be abused to make unauthorised changes, hide wrongdoing, or steal information needed to protect the business, turning a convenient support route into a serious weakness. The organisation must allow remote access to run privileged commands and view security-relevant information only when there is explicit approval for specific purposes: incident response, vulnerability fixing, privileged maintenance, and security monitoring or audit evidence. Privileged commands must be limited to emergency maintenance, patching, configuration changes, or running security tools to produce audit evidence, while viewing security-relevant information must be limited to incident response support, vulnerability remediation, and security monitoring for Confidential and Secret data. Every remote action must produce clear records showing who did what, when, and why, and the system’s security plan must document the rationale for using remote access.

#### Purpose-bound remote privileged access with evidence-rich approvals

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement a controlled remote-access pathway for (1) executing **privileged commands** and (2) accessing **security-relevant information**, where each remote action is **explicitly authorised for allowed purposes**, **time-bounded**, and produces **assessable evidence** that links the action to the authorisation.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-17(4) enhancement guidance requires restricting remote execution of privileged commands and remote access to security-relevant information to reduce exposure of the organisation to the remote access capability. Authorisation must be limited to the organisation-defined needs requiring remote access (e.g., emergency privileged maintenance, patching, configuration changes, security tooling for audit evidence; and incident response/vulnerability remediation/security monitoring for Confidential and Secret data), with assessable evidence for both privileged command execution and security-relevant information access.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define purpose-to-entitlement mappings** aligned to the organisation-defined parameters:
   - Privileged commands via remote access only for: **emergency privileged maintenance, patching, configuration changes, and access to security tooling for audit evidence**.
   - Security-relevant information via remote access only for: **incident response support, vulnerability remediation, and security monitoring for Confidential and Secret data**.
2. **Broker remote privileged access through a PAM/JIT workflow** integrated with the enterprise identity provider and ITSM:
   - Require a **request** that selects the allowed purpose/need.
   - Require **explicit approval** (ticket/approval record) before access is granted.
   - Grant **time-bound** access (just-in-time) and scope it to the specific target system(s) and permitted actions.
3. **Enforce command and data access constraints** during the session:
   - Restrict privileged shells/remote consoles to the approved purpose.
   - Restrict access to security-relevant information (e.g., security tooling dashboards, logs, evidence stores) to the approved purpose and data classification scope (Confidential/Secret).
4. **Generate assessable evidence for every remote action**:
   - Record session metadata (user identity, target system, start/end time, purpose, approval/ticket ID).
   - Record **command-level audit** for privileged command execution.
   - Record access events for security-relevant information (e.g., view/download/open events) with the same purpose/approval linkage.
   - Centralise logs to SIEM with integrity controls and retention suitable for audit review.
5. **Document the remote access rationale in the system security plan**:
   - Reference the allowed purposes/needs and describe how the PAM workflow enforces them.
   - Ensure the plan explicitly states that remote privileged commands and security-relevant information access are only permitted under the defined purposes and are evidenced via session/command/access logs.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **System Owner / Security Owner**: maintains the system security plan rationale and purpose mappings.
- **IAM/PAM Administrator**: implements PAM/JIT policies, entitlements, and session recording.
- **SOC/Security Operations**: validates that evidence produced is sufficient for incident response and audit review.
- **ITSM/Service Desk**: manages the request/approval records that drive authorisation.

## Acceptance criteria
- Remote privileged command execution is only possible when a valid approval record exists for one of the allowed purposes (emergency privileged maintenance, patching, configuration changes, security tooling for audit evidence).
- Remote access to security-relevant information is only possible when a valid approval record exists for one of the allowed purposes (incident response support, vulnerability remediation, security monitoring for Confidential/Secret data).
- For each remote session, logs show: requester identity, target system, timestamps, purpose, and approval/ticket ID.
- Privileged command execution produces command-level audit records tied to the same approval/ticket ID.
- Security-relevant information access events (view/open/download) are logged and tied to the same approval/ticket ID.
- The system security plan contains documented rationale describing the enforcement mechanism and allowed purposes.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Create purpose-to-entitlement mappings for the allowed remote privileged command needs and allowed security-relevant information needs
- [ ] Configure PAM/JIT so remote privileged access requires an ITSM/ticket approval and is time-bounded and target-scoped
- [ ] Implement session recording and command-level auditing for all privileged remote sessions, ensuring logs include purpose and approval/ticket ID
- [ ] Implement access controls for security-relevant information so viewing/opening/downloading is permitted only under the approved purpose and Confidential/Secret scope
- [ ] Centralise and retain session/command/access logs in SIEM with integrity protections and audit-ready retention
- [ ] Update the system security plan to document the remote access rationale and how the PAM workflow enforces the allowed purposes

### ac-17.6 — Protection of Mechanism Information (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about keeping the instructions for how remote access works locked down, so only the right people can use them and only for approved purposes. Without this, someone could get hold of the details needed to connect from outside the organisation, then misuse them or share them further, potentially leading to unauthorised access to systems and sensitive information. To meet this expectation, the organisation must clearly identify what remote-access information it holds and protect it from unauthorised use or sharing. It should make sure the rules for who can access what, the step-by-step remote access procedures, the system security plan, and any relevant agreements or behaviour rules all explicitly include these protection requirements. Access to this information must be limited to authorised staff responsible for setting up or monitoring remote access, and to authorised system users who genuinely need to know, with the documentation kept up to date and usable.

#### Remote access mechanism info vault with role-based access

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Create and operate a controlled repository (“vault”) for all information that describes how remote access mechanisms work (e.g., VPN/RDP/jump-host connection instructions, configuration details, diagrams, troubleshooting steps), and ensure that only authorised, need-to-know roles can access it.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-17(6) enhancement requires protecting information about remote access mechanisms from unauthorised use and disclosure. The guidance indicates remote access requirements should be reflected in relevant governance artefacts (e.g., rules of behaviour and access agreements), so the repository must be governed and access-controlled rather than shared informally.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the scope of “remote access mechanism information”** by inventorying document types and artefacts that contain connection/usage instructions (runbooks, diagrams, firewall/NAT rules notes, broker/jump-host steps, troubleshooting playbooks, and any “how to connect” materials).
2. **Centralise the authoritative versions** of these artefacts in a dedicated repository (document management system or secrets/document vault pattern) with confidentiality labelling and restricted sharing.
3. **Enforce role-based authorisation** using the enterprise identity provider (IdP) and group-based access (e.g., remote access implementers/monitors; information security responsibilities; need-to-know system users). Require strong authentication (e.g., phishing-resistant MFA) for repository access.
4. **Restrict disclosure paths**: disable or tightly control public links, external sharing, bulk export/download, and unauthorised forwarding; require access via an approved workflow (ticketing/approval portal) that grants time-bound access to the specific artefacts.
5. **Operationalise governance and currency**: assign an owner for each artefact set, implement review/expiry for connection instructions, and ensure the repository always points to the current approved versions.
6. **Record evidence for audits**: retain access logs (who viewed/downloaded/requested), approval decisions, and periodic access review outcomes.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Information Security (or Security Governance)**: defines classification/handling rules and approves the access model.
- **IT Operations / Network & Security Engineering**: maintains the authoritative artefacts and owners.
- **Identity & Access Management (IAM) team**: implements IdP groups/roles and authentication requirements.
- **Service Management (ITSM) / Ticketing owner**: runs the approved request workflow for access.

## Acceptance criteria
- A complete inventory exists of remote access mechanism information and all authoritative artefacts are stored in the controlled repository.
- Only authorised roles can access the repository content; access is enforced via the enterprise IdP (no shared accounts).
- External sharing/public links/bulk export are disabled or require explicit approval and are demonstrably controlled.
- Access to connection instructions is granted through the approved workflow and is time-bound where applicable.
- Evidence is available: access logs, approval records, and periodic review/ownership records showing current, approved documentation.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Inventory and classify all artefacts that describe remote access mechanisms (connection instructions, configuration details, diagrams, troubleshooting runbooks)
- [ ] Create a dedicated controlled repository for the authoritative versions of those artefacts with confidentiality labelling
- [ ] Implement IdP-integrated role-based access (authorised implementers/monitors and need-to-know users) with phishing-resistant MFA for repository access
- [ ] Disable or tightly control public links, external sharing, and bulk export/download; enforce access via an approved ticket/workflow with time-bound permissions
- [ ] Assign owners and implement review/expiry so remote access mechanism information remains current and approved
- [ ] Enable and retain audit logs for repository access and workflow approvals to provide evidence of restricted use and disclosure

### ac-17.9 — Disconnect or Disable Access (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about being able to quickly cut off remote access to your systems when it is no longer safe. If remote access can’t be switched off fast enough, an attacker or an unauthorised person could keep using your systems, or access could continue after a staff member leaves or a device is compromised, causing real business disruption and financial loss. To meet this expectation, the organisation must ensure there is a working way to disconnect or disable remote access within 15 minutes of starting the action, and that this is backed by clear, written rules and step-by-step procedures. Those procedures must name who is responsible for carrying out the change, explain exactly what to do for each remote access method, and require that the organisation records who did it and the timing so it can be checked later. The organisation must also keep design and configuration records showing the system is set up to support this fast shut-off, and retain audit records proving it was actually completed within 15 minutes.

#### 15-minute remote access kill switch with auditable execution

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement a capability to **disconnect or disable remote access** to the application/system within the organisation-defined **time period** (parameter **ac-17.09_odp = within 15 minutes**), covering each remote access method (e.g., VPN/remote access gateway, ZTNA app access, SSH/RDP jump hosts, remote support tooling).

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-17(9) enhancement requires: **“Provide the capability to disconnect or disable remote access to the system within {{ insert: param, ac-17.09_odp }}.”** The guidance notes that disconnect/disablement speed varies by mission/business criticality and must be sufficient to **eliminate immediate or future remote access**.

## HOW (specific approach)
### 1) Define remote access methods and enforcement points
- Maintain an up-to-date inventory mapping each remote access path to a specific enforcement control (e.g., VPN gateway policy, firewall rule set, ZTNA application policy, bastion/jump host access policy, remote support tool allowlist).
- For each method, identify the exact configuration element that, when changed, prevents new sessions and blocks future connections.

### 2) Build an emergency “deny” kill-switch per remote access method
- Create a pre-defined, operator-triggerable **emergency deny policy** for each remote access method.
- Implement the kill switch at the earliest practical choke point (typically network/identity enforcement), so the action is not dependent on application-layer changes.
- Ensure the kill switch can be activated without ad-hoc rule editing (use pre-approved policy objects / policy-as-code templates).

### 3) Enforce authorised activation with strong authentication
- Restrict kill-switch activation to **authorised privileged operators** using RBAC/least privilege.
- Require step-up authentication for activation (e.g., phishing-resistant MFA) and record the operator identity.

### 4) Orchestrate execution and measure the 15-minute requirement
- Provide an operator workflow (runbook automation/orchestration) where the operator action is effectively “start emergency disablement”.
- Start a timer at activation and record timestamps (start/end) to demonstrate completion **within 15 minutes**.
- Include a verification step: confirm connection attempts fail (e.g., from a test account or monitoring probe) and record the result.

### 5) Produce audit-ready evidence
- Centralise logs from the enforcement points (VPN/Firewall/ZTNA/bastion/remote support) and correlate them with:
  - operator identity
  - change/runbook execution ID
  - start/end timestamps
  - policy/rule identifiers applied
  - verification outcome
- Retain evidence according to organisational logging/retention requirements.

## WHO
- **Security Operations / SOC lead**: owns the kill-switch runbook and verification approach.
- **Infrastructure/Network Engineering** (or equivalent): implements enforcement-point policies and automation.
- **IAM/Privileged Access Management owner**: ensures RBAC and step-up authentication for activation.
- **Application Owner**: confirms remote access methods relevant to the application are covered.

## Acceptance criteria
- For every identified remote access method to the application/system, the organisation can activate the kill switch and demonstrate **remote access is disconnected/disabled within 15 minutes** (measured from activation start to verified connection failure).
- Activation is restricted to authorised roles and requires step-up authentication.
- Evidence exists showing who activated it, what policy/rule identifiers were applied, and timestamps proving completion within 15 minutes.
- The kill-switch mechanism is tested (at least during implementation and then per organisational test cadence) and results are retained for audit.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Inventory all remote access methods to the application/system and map each to its enforcement point (VPN/firewall/waswo/ZTNA/bastion/remote support).
- [ ] Create pre-approved emergency deny policies for each remote access method with stable identifiers for audit correlation.
- [ ] Implement an operator-triggered kill switch workflow with RBAC/least privilege and step-up authentication for activation.
- [ ] Automate execution via runbook/orchestration so activation applies the deny policies without manual rule editing.
- [ ] Instrument and centralise logging to capture operator identity, execution ID, policy/rule identifiers, and start/end timestamps.
- [ ] Run a controlled test to verify connection attempts fail and record evidence demonstrating completion within 15 minutes.
- [ ] Document the procedure and verification steps, and ensure the runbook is included in the organisation’s change/config governance.

### ac-17.10 — Authenticate Remote Commands (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure that remote “admin” instructions sent to your on-prem servers and operational technology gateways are genuine, untampered, and only acted on once. Without these checks, an attacker could try to send unauthorised commands, alter a legitimate command while it is travelling, or resend an old command to make a system repeat an action, potentially causing serious disruption, damage, or compromise of sensitive information. To meet this expectation, the organisation must set up the approved remote management routes so that both sides prove they are trusted using certificates, and every single command must carry a per-command integrity check plus a steadily increasing sequence number. The receiving systems must verify these before executing anything, reject any command with a wrong or missing check, and refuse any command that is out of order or a repeat. The organisation must document how this works, define the procedures, configure the systems to enforce it, record the outcomes in audit logs, include it in the security plan, and assign clear responsibility for implementation and oversight.

#### mTLS + per-command HMAC + sequence replay protection for remote admin

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT must be done
Implement cryptographic authentication for **remote administrative commands** sent over **approved management channels** to **on-prem servers and OT gateways**, ensuring commands are **not tampered with** and **cannot be replayed**.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-17(10) enhancement requires implementing **mechanisms** to authenticate **remote commands**. The guidance specifies that authentication protects against **unauthorised commands** and **replay of authorised commands**, and that systems must accept commands in the **intended order**, execute only **authorised commands**, and **reject unauthorised** (including replayed/out-of-order) commands.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define and enforce approved management channels** for remote administration to on-prem servers and OT gateways (e.g., ZTNA/JIT access paths, dedicated admin network segments, or management jump hosts). Only these paths may reach the command ingestion endpoints.
2. **Implement mutual TLS (mTLS)** between the remote command issuer (or its ZTNA front-end) and the command ingestion service/agent on each target. Use enterprise PKI/workload identities; require client certificate authentication and validate server identity.
3. **Wrap each remote command in a command envelope** that includes: `command_id`, `target_id`, `payload`, `sequence_number`, and a `session/context identifier` (e.g., connection/session ID). Compute a **per-command HMAC** over a canonicalised representation of `payload || sequence_number || session/context identifier` using a key provisioned to the authorised issuer and verified by the receiver.
4. **Enforce monotonically increasing sequence numbers** per authenticated command stream (e.g., per issuer identity + per target). The receiver must maintain the last accepted sequence number and **reject** any command where `sequence_number <= last_accepted` (replay) or where ordering is violated.
5. **Verify before execution**: the receiver must validate, in order, (a) mTLS client identity, (b) HMAC correctness, and (c) sequence number acceptance. Only after all checks pass may the command be executed.
6. **Apply the same mechanism to both on-prem servers and OT gateways** by deploying an equivalent command ingestion/verification component (agent, gateway adapter, or command proxy) on each platform.
7. **Produce audit evidence**: log authentication outcomes and rejection reasons (e.g., `HMAC_MISMATCH`, `SEQUENCE_REPLAY`, `OUT_OF_ORDER`, `CERT_INVALID`) with correlation identifiers (`command_id`, issuer identity, target, timestamp) and retain per organisational logging/retention requirements.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Solution/Platform Architect**: defines the command envelope, sequence semantics, and integration pattern for on-prem servers and OT gateways.
- **Security Engineering / PKI Team**: provisions certificates/keys, manages rotation, and ensures revocation/validity checking.
- **Platform/OT Gateway Engineers**: implement the command ingestion/verification agents/adapters and integrate with existing remote management channels.
- **SOC/SIEM Engineering**: ensures logs are forwarded, searchable, and alerting is configured for repeated authentication failures.

## Acceptance criteria
- Remote administrative commands to on-prem servers and OT gateways are accepted **only** over the approved management channels.
- For every received command, the receiver performs **mTLS client authentication**, **per-command HMAC verification**, and **sequence number replay/order checks** before execution.
- Commands with **invalid HMAC** are rejected and not executed.
- Commands with **replayed or out-of-order sequence numbers** are rejected and not executed.
- Audit logs show successful and failed authentication attempts with **specific rejection reasons** and correlation to `command_id` and issuer identity.
- The mechanism is implemented consistently across **both** on-prem servers and **OT gateways** (not only generic network access).

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define the approved management channels and restrict network paths so only those routes can reach the command ingestion endpoints for on-prem servers and OT gateways
- [ ] Implement mutual TLS for remote administrative command sessions, enforcing client certificate authentication and server identity validation
- [ ] Create a command envelope format that includes command_id, payload, sequence_number, session/context identifier, and per-command HMAC
- [ ] Implement receiver-side verification to validate mTLS identity, verify HMAC before execution, and reject commands with sequence_number <= last_accepted (replay) or out-of-order values
- [ ] Deploy equivalent command ingestion/verification components on both on-prem servers and OT gateways (or adapters where native support is limited)
- [ ] Configure audit logging for each command with command_id, issuer identity, target, and explicit rejection reasons (e.g., HMAC_MISMATCH, SEQUENCE_REPLAY)
- [ ] Test replay and tampering scenarios end-to-end to confirm rejected commands are never executed and that logs capture the correct failure reason

### ac-18 — Wireless Access (Control)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure any wireless connection to your systems is set up correctly and only allowed when it has been formally approved. Without this, someone could connect using an unapproved device or wireless method, bypass intended restrictions, or gain access through a poorly configured wireless link, leading to unauthorised entry, data exposure, or disruption. To meet this expectation, the organisation must first identify every type of wireless connection it will use (such as Wi‑Fi, Bluetooth, and other wireless links), then write down clear setup rules, clear connection rules (including what is allowed and what is not), and step-by-step guidance for each type. It must also assign named people responsible for managing wireless connections and oversight. Finally, the system must only permit each wireless connection type after the organisation has recorded and approved it for the specific purpose, and it must keep records of wireless connection activity so later checks can confirm everything matched the approvals.

#### Authorise-then-enable wireless access with mutual authentication

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement an auditable wireless access approval and enforcement mechanism so that every wireless technology in use (e.g., 802.11x/Wi‑Fi, Bluetooth, and any microwave/packet radio links used for connectivity) is only enabled after formal authorisation for a specific purpose, with configuration that enforces authenticator protection and mutual authentication.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-18 requires that wireless technologies used have documented and enforced configuration/connection requirements, and that wireless networks use authentication protocols providing authenticator protection and mutual authentication. It also implies that wireless access must be controlled via formal approval and that connection activity is auditable.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create a wireless access catalogue** listing each wireless access type the environment supports (802.11x SSIDs, Bluetooth profiles, and any microwave/packet radio connectivity used for access/backhaul), including purpose, allowed user/device/service identities, and required authentication method(s).
2. **Define per-technology configuration/connection requirements** in controlled templates (e.g., for 802.11x: WPA3-Enterprise or equivalent, EAP method requiring mutual authentication such as EAP‑TLS; disable legacy/insecure modes; enforce certificate validation; client isolation where appropriate; for Bluetooth: authenticated pairing with device identity controls and prohibition of insecure pairing modes).
3. **Implement an “authorise-then-enable” workflow**: wireless access authorisations are recorded in the organisation’s GRC/ticketing system with approver identity, scope (site/zone), purpose, and validity period. The approval must generate a machine-readable policy object.
4. **Enforce at the wireless enforcement point** (wireless LAN controllers/access points, NAC/802.1X policy engine, Bluetooth management platform/gateway, and any radio gateway configuration tooling): only authorised SSIDs/profiles/policies are pushed/enabled; unauthorised wireless profiles are not broadcast or are actively blocked.
5. **Ensure mutual authentication and authenticator protection via configuration**: require mutual authentication at the protocol level (e.g., EAP‑TLS for 802.11x) and disable configurations that do not provide mutual authentication or authenticator protection.
6. **Log and retain auditable evidence**: capture association/authentication events (success/failure), policy version/authorisation reference, device/user identity (where available), and enforcement decision outcomes; forward logs to SIEM for retention and audit queries.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Wireless/Network Engineering Lead**: owns the wireless access catalogue and configuration templates.
- **Information Security (or Security Architecture) Lead**: defines authentication/mutual-auth requirements and approves security templates.
- **GRC/Compliance Owner**: operates the authorisation workflow and ensures approvals are recorded.
- **Platform/Infrastructure Operations**: implements the enforcement integration and ensures logging/retention.

## Acceptance criteria
- For each wireless access type in the catalogue, there exists a controlled configuration/connection template specifying required authentication that provides authenticator protection and mutual authentication.
- No SSID/profile/radio connectivity policy is enabled in production without a corresponding, current authorisation record (verifiable by policy object reference).
- Wireless enforcement points are configured to prevent unauthorised wireless profiles from being broadcast/accepted.
- Authentication is configured to require mutual authentication (e.g., EAP‑TLS or equivalent) and insecure/legacy modes are disabled per template.
- SIEM contains auditable logs showing wireless association/authentication attempts linked to the relevant authorisation/policy reference, with retention meeting organisational requirements.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Create a wireless access catalogue covering every wireless technology used (802.11x, Bluetooth, and any microwave/packet radio connectivity used for access).
- [ ] Publish controlled configuration/connection templates per wireless type requiring authenticator protection and mutual authentication (e.g., EAP‑TLS for 802.11x; authenticated pairing controls for Bluetooth).
- [ ] Implement an authorise-then-enable workflow that records approver, scope, purpose, and validity, and generates a machine-readable policy object.
- [ ] Integrate the policy object with wireless enforcement points so only authorised SSIDs/profiles/radio policies are enabled and unauthorised ones are blocked.
- [ ] Disable legacy/insecure wireless authentication modes and enforce certificate validation/identity checks per template.
- [ ] Configure logging to capture association/authentication events (success/failure) with policy/authorisation references and forward to SIEM for retention and audit queries.

### ac-18.1 — Authentication and Encryption (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This expectation is about making sure that anyone trying to connect to your systems over wireless is properly verified and that any information sent across that wireless link is protected from being read or tampered with. Without strong checks, an unauthorised person could join your wireless network, impersonate a legitimate user, or intercept sensitive data while it travels through the air. To meet this expectation, the organisation must set up wireless access so that both people and the devices they use must be strongly authenticated, meaning only authorised users and approved devices can connect, and it must use strong encryption so wireless data stays unreadable to outsiders while in transit. The organisation should also document how wireless access is implemented and what restrictions apply, keep the relevant design and configuration records, retain audit records showing wireless access activity, and include these wireless authentication and encryption requirements in its security planning so the right teams maintain and can demonstrate the protections over time.

#### WPA3-Enterprise (802.1X) with user+device authentication and strong encryption

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Configure all organisation wireless access to require **strong authentication** for both **users and devices** and to use **strong encryption** for all wireless traffic.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-18(1)** enhancement requires: “Protect wireless access to the system using authentication of {{ insert: param, ac-18.01_odp }} and encryption.” The organisation-defined parameter **ac-18.01_odp** specifies authentication for **users, devices**. Wireless links are a high-risk attack surface; strong authentication and strong encryption reduce unauthorised access and interception/tampering.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Standardise wireless security mode**: enable **WPA3-Enterprise** (or WPA2-Enterprise only if WPA3 is not feasible) and **disable** legacy/insecure options (e.g., open SSIDs, WEP, WPA/WPA2-Personal).
2. **Enforce strong user authentication**: implement **802.1X** with **RADIUS/AAA** against the organisation’s central identity provider (IdP). Require phishing-resistant MFA for interactive user authentication.
3. **Enforce strong device authentication**: issue and manage **device certificates** (or equivalent strong device credentials) and map them to an approved device inventory record. Authorise access in AAA based on certificate identity and device approval/compliance attributes.
4. **Bind encryption to association**: ensure the Wi-Fi controller/AP configuration enforces enterprise encryption suites appropriate to WPA3/WPA2-Enterprise and that only authenticated 802.1X sessions can pass traffic.
5. **Restrict wireless usage paths**: create SSID/VLAN mappings so only authorised SSIDs can reach the required application tiers (presentation/application/admin/data paths as per architecture), and prevent guest/unknown devices from accessing sensitive segments.
6. **Produce audit-ready evidence**: retain exported Wi-Fi controller/AP configuration, RADIUS/AAA policy configuration, and authentication logs showing successful/failed user and device authentications.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Network Security Engineer / Wi-Fi Administrator**: implement WPA3-Enterprise/802.1X configuration, SSID/VLAN segmentation, and RADIUS/AAA integration.
- **Identity & Access Management (IAM) Lead**: configure IdP authentication (including MFA) and user identity mapping.
- **Endpoint/Device Management Lead**: manage device certificate lifecycle and approval/compliance attributes.
- **Security Assurance/Compliance Lead**: verify evidence completeness and alignment to AC-18(1) for audit.

## Acceptance criteria
- All production and non-production wireless SSIDs that provide access to the system require **802.1X** and **enterprise authentication** (no open or WPA-Personal SSIDs for system access).
- Wireless associations succeed only when **both** user authentication and **device authentication** (certificate/strong device credential) are validated by AAA.
- Wireless traffic is protected using **WPA3-Enterprise** (or WPA2-Enterprise where WPA3 is not feasible) with strong cipher suites; unauthenticated clients cannot exchange application traffic.
- Wi-Fi controller/AP and RADIUS/AAA configurations are documented and retained, and authentication logs are retained to demonstrate user/device authentication outcomes.
- SSID/VLAN/route policies prevent unauthorised wireless clients from reaching sensitive application segments.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Enable WPA3-Enterprise (or WPA2-Enterprise where WPA3 is not feasible) and disable open/legacy wireless modes for all system-access SSIDs
- [ ] Configure 802.1X with RADIUS/AAA to require strong user authentication via the central IdP (including phishing-resistant MFA for interactive users)
- [ ] Implement device certificate-based authentication and map certificate identities to an approved device inventory/compliance attributes in AAA
- [ ] Enforce authorisation so only authenticated 802.1X sessions (user + device) can access the SSID’s permitted VLAN/segments
- [ ] Apply SSID/VLAN/route restrictions to ensure wireless clients can only reach required application tiers and not administrative or sensitive segments unless explicitly approved
- [ ] Export and retain Wi-Fi controller/AP configuration and RADIUS/AAA policy configuration, and retain authentication logs for audit evidence

### ac-18.3 — Disable Wireless Networking (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure any built-in wireless features in new devices are turned off before the devices are handed out and put into use, unless wireless is genuinely needed for an essential business purpose. If wireless is left switched on by default, it can give attackers an easier route to interfere with or access systems from nearby, which can lead to data loss, disruption, or unauthorised access. To meet this expectation, the organisation must first identify which devices have built-in wireless capabilities, clearly document when wireless is allowed for essential missions, and ensure that for all other cases the wireless features are disabled before deployment using the device’s built-in settings rather than temporary workarounds. The organisation must keep written rules, design and configuration records showing how wireless is disabled, keep evidence and audit records proving it was switched off before deployment, and assign named responsible staff who are trained or authorised to follow the documented process.

#### Disable embedded Wi‑Fi/Bluetooth in golden build with exception control

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Before any system component is issued and deployed, disable embedded wireless networking capabilities (e.g., Wi‑Fi and Bluetooth) unless wireless is explicitly intended for an essential organisational mission/function.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-18(3) (Enhancement) requires that wireless networking capabilities embedded within system components are disabled when not intended for use, prior to issuance and deployment. This reduces exposure to adversaries using wireless attack paths.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define an authoritative wireless baseline per device class** (e.g., laptops, desktops, rugged endpoints, embedded PCs) listing which embedded radios exist (Wi‑Fi, Bluetooth) and the supported disablement mechanisms (UEFI/BIOS, OS, endpoint management).
2. **Implement a “golden build” that disables wireless by default** at the earliest feasible stage in the provisioning pipeline:
   - Apply OS/device-management configuration to disable Wi‑Fi and Bluetooth.
   - Where supported, set firmware/UEFI provisioning defaults to disable WLAN/Bluetooth during imaging.
3. **Create an exception workflow for essential mission use**:
   - Document the exact conditions under which wireless may remain enabled (scope, device types, use cases).
   - Require approval by the system owner and information security (with an expiry date) and record the exception in the configuration management system.
4. **Enforce continuously through endpoint management** so post-build drift is corrected:
   - Apply configuration profiles that keep Wi‑Fi/Bluetooth disabled for all non-exception devices.
   - Ensure exception overrides are time-bound and limited to the approved scope.
5. **Generate audit-ready evidence tied to issuance/deployment**:
   - Capture configuration compliance reports showing Wi‑Fi/Bluetooth disabled state.
   - Record deployment/issuance logs referencing device identifiers (asset tag/serial) and the build version/policy applied.
   - Where firmware disablement is used, store firmware setting evidence (e.g., exported UEFI settings outputs or management console attestations).
6. **Add detection and alerting**:
   - Monitor for configuration drift (wireless enabled) and alert security/infrastructure teams for non-exception devices.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Endpoint/Infrastructure Engineering**: implement golden build and endpoint management enforcement.
- **Information Security**: approve and govern wireless exceptions; define essential mission criteria.
- **Configuration Management/IT Operations**: maintain build manifests, exception records, and evidence retention.

## Acceptance criteria
- For all non-exception devices, Wi‑Fi and Bluetooth are disabled **before issuance and deployment** and remain disabled after deployment.
- Exceptions are only granted for documented essential mission/function criteria, are approved by authorised roles, and have an expiry date.
- Evidence exists for each issued device showing the wireless-disabled configuration state and the build/policy version applied.
- Alerts trigger when wireless becomes enabled on devices that are not within an active exception scope.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Create a device-class wireless baseline identifying embedded radios (Wi‑Fi/Bluetooth) and supported disablement methods (UEFI/OS/endpoint management)
- [ ] Update the golden build/provisioning pipeline to disable Wi‑Fi and Bluetooth by default before imaging completes
- [ ] Configure endpoint management policies to enforce wireless disabled state for all devices except those with an active, approved exception
- [ ] Implement an exception workflow with documented essential mission/function criteria, named approvers, and expiry dates
- [ ] Record and retain audit evidence linking each issued device (asset tag/serial) to the build version and wireless-disabled configuration compliance report
- [ ] Enable monitoring/detection for wireless-enabled drift and alert when non-exception devices have Wi‑Fi/Bluetooth enabled

### ac-18.4 — Restrict Configurations by Users (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure only the right named people can make independent changes to your wireless settings, such as Wi‑Fi configuration. Without this, someone who is not meant to could quietly weaken security, disrupt services, or redirect connections, creating opportunities for unauthorised access or outages. To meet this expectation, the organisation must keep a clear, up-to-date list of the specific users who are permitted to independently configure wireless networking, and it must explicitly approve each person on that list. It should define what “independently configure” covers in practice, and then enforce it through its written rules for system access, its documented procedures for how wireless is implemented and used (including any restrictions), and the way the wireless systems are set up. The organisation must also keep supporting design and configuration records, maintain audit logs showing who made wireless changes, and include this restriction in its overall security plan so that changes can be checked and verified.

#### Authorised Wi‑Fi configurators register with RBAC enforcement

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Create an authoritative, explicitly approved list of named users permitted to independently configure wireless networking capabilities (e.g., Wi‑Fi controller settings, SSID/VLAN mappings, radio profiles, and authentication parameters), and enforce that authorisation through the wireless management plane so only those users can perform independent configuration changes.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 AC-18(4) enhancement requires the organisation to **identify and explicitly authorise users allowed to independently configure wireless networking capabilities**. The guidance notes that enforcement is achieved, in part, by the **access enforcement mechanisms** used within organisational systems.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define scope of “independently configure”** for wireless networking capabilities: document which configuration functions are in-scope (e.g., SSID/VLAN, WPA2/3 settings, RADIUS/802.1X parameters, captive portal templates, radio power/channel profiles) and which are out-of-scope (e.g., read-only viewing, vendor break-fix under controlled procedures).
2. **Establish a controlled “Wi‑Fi Independent Configurators” register** containing named users (full name, unique identity/username, employee/contractor ID, authorisation date, expiry/review date, approver, and scope tag). Treat this register as the source of truth and ensure it is reviewable.
3. **Map register entries to technical authorisation**: create/maintain IdP groups/roles (e.g., `WIFI-INDEPENDENT-CONFIG-<scope>`) and grant Wi‑Fi controller/admin portal access only to those groups. Ensure all admin access is mediated via the enterprise IdP (SSO) and uses least privilege.
4. **Enforce RBAC on the wireless management plane**: configure the Wi‑Fi controller/admin interfaces so only the authorised roles can access configuration change functions; all other users must be read-only or denied. Require strong authentication for admin roles (e.g., phishing-resistant MFA where supported).
5. **Ensure auditability and reconciliation**: configure/verify that wireless configuration change logs record the acting identity (IdP subject/username) and the specific configuration change. Implement a periodic reconciliation report: `audit changed-by` must be a subset of the authorised configurators register.
6. **Integrate into governance artefacts**: update the system security plan and wireless configuration/procedures to reference the register, the defined scope, and the RBAC enforcement mechanism.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Wireless/Network Security Owner**: defines scope and maintains the register governance.
- **IAM/Identity Engineer**: implements IdP groups/roles and SSO mappings.
- **Wireless Platform Administrator**: configures RBAC on Wi‑Fi controllers and validates audit logging.
- **GRC/Compliance Analyst**: verifies evidence (register, approvals, reconciliation outputs) for audit readiness.

## Acceptance criteria
- A controlled register exists with named users explicitly authorised for in-scope wireless configuration capabilities, including approval and review/expiry evidence.
- The Wi‑Fi management plane enforces RBAC such that only users in the authorised register (via mapped IdP roles/groups) can perform independent configuration changes.
- Wireless configuration audit logs capture the acting user identity and can be reconciled to the authorised register with no unauthorised “changed-by” identities.
- The system security plan and wireless configuration procedures reference the scope definition and the enforcement mechanism (register + RBAC).

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define and document the in-scope wireless configuration functions that qualify as “independently configure”.
- [ ] Create and maintain a controlled “Wi‑Fi Independent Configurators” register with explicit approvals and review/expiry dates.
- [ ] Implement IdP SSO group/role mappings that correspond exactly to the register scope tags.
- [ ] Configure Wi‑Fi controller/admin portals to enforce RBAC so only mapped roles can perform configuration changes (others read-only/denied).
- [ ] Verify and enable audit logging for wireless configuration changes to capture the acting IdP identity and change details.
- [ ] Run a periodic reconciliation report comparing audit “changed-by” identities against the authorised configurators register and investigate exceptions.
- [ ] Update the system security plan and wireless configuration procedures to reference the register, scope definition, and RBAC enforcement evidence.

### ac-18.5 — Antennas and Transmission Power Levels (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure your building’s wireless internet signals stay where they are meant to, by choosing the right antenna types and setting the correct transmission strength for each wireless access point. Without this, signals could leak beyond your organisation’s controlled areas, letting unauthorised people pick them up from outside and potentially gain access to your systems or data. To meet this expectation, the organisation must select antennas for each access point with the specific aim of reducing out-of-boundary reception, and record why those choices were made. It must also calibrate and document the transmission power settings so they are unlikely to travel outside the boundary, using results from periodic wireless surveys that measure the local radio environment and consider other nearby radio systems. The organisation should implement the agreed antenna and power settings consistently, keep evidence of the survey results and decisions, and ensure changes are approved and traceable.

#### Calibrate WAP antennas and transmit power using boundary RF surveys

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Select appropriate radio antennas (directional/sector/beamforming where feasible) for each wireless access point (WAP) and calibrate each WAP’s transmission power so that wireless signals are unlikely to be received outside organisation-controlled boundaries.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-18(5) enhancement requires selecting radio antennas and calibrating transmission power levels to reduce the probability that signals from wireless access points can be received outside of organisation-controlled boundaries. The guidance also expects mitigating actions to be informed by periodic wireless surveys that characterise the radio frequency profile, including other systems operating in the area.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create a WAP boundary risk inventory**: maintain an authoritative list of all WAPs with location (coordinates/floor plan), mounting details, intended coverage area, and a tag indicating whether the WAP is boundary-adjacent (e.g., near external walls, perimeter fences, car parks).
2. **Perform an initial boundary-focused wireless survey**: conduct site RF surveys (coverage mapping and spectrum analysis) at boundary-adjacent areas and include nearby radio emitters (neighbouring Wi‑Fi, licensed/unlicensed radios, microwave links, etc.) to establish the baseline RF environment.
3. **Select antennas to reduce outward reception**: for each boundary-adjacent WAP, choose antenna type/model and mounting orientation that reduce outward lobes (e.g., sector/directional antennas; beamforming-capable antennas where supported). Record the rationale linking antenna choice to survey findings and boundary risk.
4. **Calibrate transmit power per WAP using measured results**: set each WAP’s transmit power to the minimum level that achieves required in-boundary coverage while reducing the likelihood of out-of-boundary reception. Capture the exact configuration values (e.g., controller/SSID radio power settings in dBm or equivalent) and the measured basis (survey evidence).
5. **Implement and lock configuration baselines**: apply the approved antenna and power settings consistently across production WLAN controllers/APs using standard configuration management and change control. Prevent drift by enforcing a configuration baseline (e.g., via WLAN controller templates and controlled change workflows).
6. **Establish periodic re-survey and re-calibration triggers**: schedule periodic wireless surveys and require re-survey/re-calibration when significant changes occur (new WAPs, antenna replacements, building modifications, or changes to nearby RF environment).

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Network Engineering Lead / WLAN Administrator**: owns WAP inventory, antenna selection, and transmit power calibration.
- **Information Security / Compliance**: approves boundary-reduction acceptance criteria and ensures auditable evidence is retained.
- **RF Survey Vendor or Internal RF Engineering**: performs surveys and provides measured outputs used for calibration decisions.

## Acceptance criteria
- For each boundary-adjacent WAP, there is documented evidence of (a) antenna selection rationale and (b) calibrated transmit power settings.
- Survey evidence demonstrates that coverage is achieved within organisation-controlled boundaries and that out-of-boundary reception is reduced to the agreed acceptance criteria (defined by the organisation based on survey results).
- Antenna and transmit power settings match the approved configuration baseline and are traceable via change records.
- Periodic wireless surveys are scheduled and re-calibration is triggered for defined change events.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Create and maintain an authoritative WAP inventory with boundary-adjacent tagging and mounting/orientation details
- [ ] Conduct an initial boundary-focused wireless survey including spectrum analysis of nearby radio systems
- [ ] Select and document antenna types/models and mounting orientations for each boundary-adjacent WAP based on survey findings
- [ ] Calibrate and record per-WAP transmit power settings using measured in-boundary coverage results and out-of-boundary risk evidence
- [ ] Implement approved antenna and power settings via WLAN controller/AP configuration baselines under change control
- [ ] Define and schedule periodic wireless surveys and specify re-survey triggers for WAP/antenna changes and significant environmental changes
- [ ] Retain auditable evidence linking survey outputs, antenna/power decisions, and configuration change approvals

### ac-19 — Access Control for Mobile Devices (Control)

> **Overview**
>
> This expectation is about making sure company phones and tablets are set up and used in a controlled, consistent way, even when staff are working away from the office. Without clear rules and enforced connection checks, an unapproved device could gain access to company systems, or a device could be left unprotected and become a route for data theft, fraud, or disruption. To meet this expectation, the organisation must define and keep up to date clear rules for organisation-owned mobile devices, including what settings they must have and how they must be protected when they are inside controlled areas and when they are outside them. It must also set out step-by-step guidance for users and administrators, covering required protective software, regular security updates, checks for suspicious or altered software, and turning off unnecessary device features. Finally, the organisation must only allow mobile devices to connect to its systems when the organisation has explicitly approved that device’s connection, and it must be able to show this through records and audit logs.

#### MDM-enforced mobile access with device authorisation and audit evidence

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT must be done
Implement organisation-controlled mobile device access controls so that only **explicitly authorised** devices can access organisational systems, and ensure devices are securely configured and protected both **inside** and **outside** controlled areas.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-19 requires protection and control of mobile devices through **policy/behaviour** and enforceable measures, including:
- **Usage restrictions and authorisation to connect** (only authorised devices can connect)
- **Configuration management** and **device identification/authentication**
- **Mandatory protective software**, **malicious code scanning**, and **security update/patch scanning**
- **Primary OS integrity checks** (where applicable)
- **Disabling unnecessary hardware**
- Ability to demonstrate enforcement via **audit records** of connection outcomes and device authorisation.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
Use an enterprise Mobile Device Management (MDM) platform integrated with the organisation identity provider to create a single enforcement chain:
**(1) device authorisation → (2) device compliance attestation → (3) conditional access to apps/systems → (4) audit evidence**.

1. **Define device classes and off-site rules**: document which devices are in-scope (e.g., company-issued only, or BYOD only if enrolled), and specify user behaviour outside controlled areas (e.g., screen lock, encryption, no unmanaged apps, and immediate reporting of loss/theft).
2. **Create an MDM baseline (hardened configuration)** for iOS/Android that enforces required settings (passcode complexity/timeout, full-disk encryption, managed app installation, disable unnecessary hardware features, and restrict risky capabilities such as unmanaged profiles).
3. **Enrolment as the authorisation gate**: maintain an explicit device inventory/authorisation list (by device ID/serial/UDID/Android ID) and only allow access after successful MDM enrolment and assignment to the correct device group.
4. **Mandatory protective software and scanning**: enforce installation and enablement of the organisation’s mobile security agent (or MDM security features) and require malware scanning and security posture checks; block access if the agent is missing, disabled, or out of date.
5. **Update/patch posture enforcement**: configure MDM to require minimum OS/security patch levels and to trigger compliance failures when devices are behind the defined security update cadence.
6. **Integrity checks where supported**: enable MDM/app attestation signals for jailbreak/root detection and secure boot/OS integrity indicators where available; document limitations by platform.
7. **Conditional access integration**: configure the identity provider to allow access to organisational apps/APIs only when the device is compliant (MDM compliance state) and enrolled; apply stricter conditions for off-site access (e.g., VPN requirement and stronger authentication).
8. **Audit and evidence retention**: forward identity provider sign-in logs and MDM compliance/connection events to the SIEM, retaining records sufficient to prove (a) device authorisation status, (b) compliance checks performed, and (c) success/failure of connection attempts.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Security Engineering / IAM Lead**: implement conditional access rules and identity provider integration.
- **Mobile/Endpoint Management Lead**: build and maintain MDM baselines, compliance policies, and device inventory/authorisation workflow.
- **System/Application Owner**: ensure organisational apps/APIs are protected by the conditional access policy.
- **SOC / Security Operations**: validate audit log ingestion, alerting, and retention.

## Acceptance criteria
- Only devices that are **explicitly authorised** (present in the device inventory and successfully enrolled) can obtain access to organisational apps/APIs.
- Devices must meet the enforced MDM compliance baseline (protective agent enabled, malware scanning enabled, required patch level, and integrity/jailbreak/root signals where supported) to pass conditional access.
- Off-site access attempts from non-compliant or non-enrolled devices are denied and recorded.
- Audit evidence exists showing device identity, authorisation/enrolment status, compliance evaluation outcome, and access success/failure, retained in SIEM for the organisation’s required period.


**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define in-scope mobile device classes and document off-site user behaviour requirements (screen lock, encryption, reporting loss/theft).
- [ ] Create and publish MDM configuration baselines for iOS/Android enforcing encryption, passcode policy, and disabling unnecessary hardware features.
- [ ] Implement an explicit device authorisation workflow (device inventory list) and require successful MDM enrolment before any access is permitted.
- [ ] Enforce mandatory mobile protective software and enable malware scanning; configure compliance failure if the agent is missing/disabled/out of date.
- [ ] Configure MDM to require minimum OS/security patch levels and to block access when devices are behind the defined security update cadence.
- [ ] Enable platform-supported integrity/jailbreak/root attestation signals and document any limitations by device OS.
- [ ] Configure identity-provider conditional access so organisational apps/APIs only allow access from compliant, enrolled, authorised devices; apply stricter off-site conditions.
- [ ] Ingest and retain MDM compliance events and identity sign-in outcomes into the SIEM to provide audit evidence of authorisation and connection success/failure.

### ac-19.4 — Restrictions for Classified Information (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about stopping people from bringing ordinary mobile phones and tablets into secure areas in a way that could accidentally expose sensitive, classified information. Without these limits, a permitted device could connect to the wrong systems, copy or store sensitive material, or use wireless features that bypass safeguards, creating a real risk of unauthorised access or data leakage. The organisation must only allow unclassified mobile devices in classified facilities when the Authorising Official has specifically approved it, and even then the device must never be connected to any classified system, any connection to unclassified systems must be approved by the Authorising Official, and the device must not use built-in or external phone/network connections or wireless features such as Wi‑Fi, Bluetooth, or mobile data. The Information System Security Officer and the Security Operations Centre incident response team must carry out random checks of permitted devices and their stored information, and if any classified information is found, the organisation must follow its incident response plan. Connections for classified mobile devices must be restricted according to the approved classified mobile device connection policy.

#### Authorised unclassified mobile allowlist with wireless/modem lockout

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement a controlled, auditable mechanism that permits only Authorising Official-approved *unclassified* mobile devices to be used in facilities handling classified information, while enforcing strict technical prohibitions so those devices cannot connect to classified systems and cannot use wireless/modem capabilities (e.g., Wi‑Fi, Bluetooth, cellular/mobile data, hotspot/tethering). Support this with SOC/ISSO-led random inspection capability and incident handling when classified information is discovered.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-19(4) (Enhancement) requires restrictions for classified information, specifically: (1) define the security officials responsible for reviewing/inspecting permitted unclassified mobile devices and stored information; (2) define security policies restricting the connection of classified mobile devices to classified systems; and (3) enforce that only explicitly authorised unclassified mobile devices may be used in classified facilities with strict limits on connectivity and wireless/modem use, plus random inspections and incident response if classified information is found.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create and maintain an Authorising Official-approved device allowlist** for unclassified mobile devices permitted in classified facilities, keyed by device identity (e.g., serial/IMEI and/or device certificate/MDM identity). Store the approval reference per device and per facility.
2. **Enforce facility entry gating** so only allowlisted devices can enter (e.g., badge/visitor workflow integration with allowlist verification; non-allowlisted devices are denied entry or placed into a managed quarantine state).
3. **Enforce “no unclassified-to-classified connectivity” technically** using network segmentation and policy enforcement (e.g., ZTNA/SASE or firewall/SDP rules) that prevents sessions from permitted mobile devices to any classified network segments/endpoints. Ensure there is no route, no session establishment, and no DNS/egress path to classified systems.
4. **Enforce “no wireless/modem use” on permitted unclassified devices** via enterprise mobile management (MDM/UEM) configuration baselines: disable Wi‑Fi, Bluetooth, cellular/mobile data, and hotspot/tethering; remove/deny modem profiles; and block installation of tethering/Wi‑Fi sharing apps where technically feasible.
5. **Enable audit-ready evidence for random inspections** by ensuring the MDM/UEM and access-control systems log: device identity, facility entry events, configuration compliance state (wireless/modem disabled), and network access denials to classified segments. Provide SOC/ISSO with a query/report capability to select random devices for inspection.
6. **Integrate incident triggers and response workflow**: if an inspection finds classified information (or if configuration/network policy violations occur), automatically open an incident ticket routed to the SOC incident response team and execute the organisation’s incident response plan end-to-end.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Information System Security Officer (ISSO)**: owns inspection procedures, random selection criteria, and incident escalation.
- **SOC incident response team**: performs/coordinates incident handling and evidence collection.
- **Authorising Official**: approves the unclassified mobile device allowlist and any exceptions.
- **Enterprise Mobility/IT Security team**: implements MDM/UEM baselines, facility gating integration, and network policy enforcement.

## Acceptance criteria
- Only devices on the Authorising Official-approved allowlist can enter classified facilities (verified by access-control logs).
- Permitted unclassified mobile devices cannot establish any network sessions to classified systems (verified by test attempts and logged denials).
- Permitted unclassified mobile devices have Wi‑Fi, Bluetooth, cellular/mobile data, and hotspot/tethering disabled (verified by MDM/UEM compliance reports).
- SOC/ISSO can produce audit-ready evidence for randomly selected devices, including device identity, facility entry, configuration compliance, and any relevant access denials.
- When classified information is discovered during inspection (or policy violations are detected), an incident is opened and handled according to the incident response plan with documented evidence.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Register each permitted unclassified mobile device in an authoritative inventory with Authorising Official approval reference
- [ ] Integrate facility access control with the device allowlist to deny or quarantine non-approved devices
- [ ] Configure network policy (ZTNA/SASE/firewall/SDP) to block all sessions from permitted mobile devices to classified network segments/endpoints
- [ ] Apply MDM/UEM baselines to disable Wi‑Fi, Bluetooth, cellular/mobile data, and hotspot/tethering and prevent modem profile use
- [ ] Enable logging for device identity, facility entry, configuration compliance, and classified-segment access denials in SIEM
- [ ] Create SOC/ISSO random inspection queries and evidence capture workflow tied to logged device compliance and entry events
- [ ] Implement an incident trigger that opens and routes to SOC when classified information is found or when policy violations are detected

### ac-19.5 — Full Device or Container-based Encryption (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure sensitive information on work mobile devices is kept protected inside encrypted “containers” rather than left exposed. Without this, a lost or misused phone or tablet could reveal private files, records, or specific pieces of data, or allow unauthorised changes, undermining confidentiality and trust. To meet this expectation, the organisation must use container-based encryption on all DoD-managed mobile devices and endpoints, clearly define which specific data areas are covered (such as files, records, or fields), and configure the encryption so it protects both secrecy and resistance to tampering. The organisation must document how the encryption is set up and what mechanisms are used, include this in the system design and system security plan, and ensure the device management system produces audit records showing the encryption is enabled and when encryption-related changes occur. Finally, named people must be responsible for setting it up, monitoring it, and managing changes, supported by clear procedures and instructions.

#### MDM/MAM-enforced container encryption for DoD-managed mobile endpoints

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Enforce **container-based encryption** on **DoD-managed mobile devices and endpoints** so that selected application data structures (e.g., managed-app documents/records/fields) are encrypted with protections that support **confidentiality and integrity**.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-19(5)** enhancement requires: “Employ {{ param, ac-19.05_odp.01 }} to protect the confidentiality and integrity of information on {{ param, ac-19.05_odp.02 }}.” Container-based encryption provides a more fine-grained approach than full-device encryption by encrypting selected data structures.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define encrypted scope** in policy terms for the DoD-managed endpoints: identify which managed-app data containers are encrypted (e.g., managed email attachments, specific document libraries, application-specific data stores/records/fields) and explicitly exclude data that is not intended to be container-encrypted.
2. **Configure container encryption via MDM/MAM** for the defined scope, using the platform’s container/data protection capabilities (not only full-disk/full-device encryption). Configure the container encryption mode to provide **confidentiality and integrity** for the encrypted data structures.
3. **Require strong unlock and bind encryption to device compliance**: ensure the container encryption policy is enforced only when the device meets compliance requirements (e.g., device passcode/biometric with strong unlock, OS security posture), and prevent fallback to plaintext access where the platform supports it.
4. **Prevent or control plaintext export/backup paths** for the encrypted containers where supported by the MDM/MAM solution (e.g., restrict copy/export to unmanaged apps, disable unmanaged backups for managed apps, enforce managed-only sharing).
5. **Implement auditable policy deployment and change control**: version and store the exact encryption policy configuration (policy-as-code or configuration baselines) and ensure changes follow the organisation’s configuration/change management approvals.
6. **Enable and retain audit evidence**: forward MDM/MAM audit events to the SIEM/logging platform and ensure logs capture at minimum: policy assignment, encryption enabled/disabled status, and encryption-related configuration changes for the managed endpoints.
7. **Assign named operational responsibility**: designate roles for (a) encryption policy configuration, (b) monitoring encryption compliance status, and (c) approving encryption policy changes (e.g., MDM admin/security engineering + security operations).

## WHO
- **MDM/MAM Administrator (or Mobile Security Engineer):** implements and maintains container encryption policies and baselines.
