- [ ] Extend the same decision-before-enforcement flow to all access types including admin actions and async/background operations
- [ ] Implement request-scoped audit logging that records PDP decision outcome and enforcement outcome with a correlation identifier
- [ ] Create and run integration tests that prove deny decisions prevent enforcement and that decision evidence is logged

### ac-24.1 — Transmit Access Authorization Information (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about making sure that when one part of your organisation decides who is allowed to do what, the permission details it sends to the systems that actually enforce access are protected end-to-end. Without this, an attacker could intercept the permission message, change it, pretend it came from a trusted decision-maker, or redirect it to the wrong place, leading to unauthorised access or incorrect denials. To meet this expectation, the organisation must package the authorisation details as signed authorisation tokens that clearly state the requester (subject), what is being accessed (resource), what they want to do (action), and any security or privacy attributes needed for the decision. These signed tokens must be sent using a mutually authenticated encrypted connection where both sides prove they are the right parties, with extra checks that only the expected certificates are accepted, and with message protection so any tampering is detected. The enforcement points must verify the token signature before using it to allow or block access, and keep evidence through system records and documented design and settings.

#### Secure transmission of signed authorisation tokens to enforcement points

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement a mechanism to **transmit access authorization information** (including subject, resource, action, and security/privacy attributes) **using defined controls** to **defined systems** that enforce access control decisions, ensuring the information is protected so it cannot be altered, spoofed, or compromised in transit.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-24(1)** enhancement requires that the organisation **transmit the specified access authorization information** using the specified **controls** to the specified **systems** so that the receiving enforcement points can **enforce access control decisions** based on **protected, verifiable authorisation information**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the access authorization information (token payload)**
   - Create a canonical token schema that includes at minimum: **subject**, **resource**, **action**, and required **security/privacy attributes**.
   - Ensure the token issuer populates only attributes required for the decision and that each claim is strongly typed and validated.
2. **Sign the authorisation information**
   - Issue tokens as **signed authorisation tokens** (e.g., JWS-signed JWT or equivalent) where the enforcement point can verify integrity and authenticity.
   - Use keys stored and managed in an HSM/KMS; enforce short token lifetimes and include audience/issuer identifiers bound to the enforcement point(s).
3. **Transmit using mutual TLS (mTLS) with certificate pinning**
   - Configure the issuer-to-enforcement-point connection to use **mutual TLS**.
   - Apply **certificate pinning** so the client/peer only accepts the expected certificate/public key(s) for the enforcement endpoint (and/or pins the issuer identity as appropriate to your call direction).
   - Enforce strict TLS settings (no fallback to weak ciphers/protocols) and require client certificates.
4. **Add message-level integrity verification at the enforcement point**
   - At the federated application/API enforcement point, implement a mandatory verification sequence:
     1) verify mTLS client/server authentication,
     2) verify the token signature,
     3) validate token audience/issuer, expiry, and required claims,
     4) validate security/privacy attributes required for the decision,
     5) only then apply allow/deny.
   - Reject requests where verification fails and ensure failures do not default to allow.
5. **Ensure the token reaches the correct enforcement systems**
   - Route tokens only to the **federated application and API enforcement points (policy decision enforcement services)** that actually enforce access decisions.
   - Prevent token replay by using nonce/jti tracking (where feasible) and/or strict short lifetimes.
6. **Produce audit-ready evidence**
   - Log verification outcomes (success/failure reason categories), token identifiers (non-sensitive), enforcement point identity, and mTLS peer identity.
   - Maintain configuration evidence for mTLS trust stores and pinned certificate/public key values, including rotation procedures.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Solution/Platform Architect**: defines token schema, trust boundaries, and enforcement verification flow.
- **Security Engineer**: implements signing key management, mTLS/certificate pinning configuration, and verification logic.
- **API/Gateway/Policy Enforcement Team**: implements enforcement-point verification and routing to the correct enforcement services.
- **DevOps/Platform Operations**: manages deployment, certificate pin rotation, and evidence retention.

## Acceptance criteria
- The enforcement point **rejects** any token that fails **signature verification** or **mTLS authentication**.
- Tokens contain **subject, resource, action, and security/privacy attributes** and the enforcement point validates required claims before making decisions.
- The transmission path uses **mutual TLS with certificate pinning** and does not accept unexpected endpoint certificates/public keys.
- Audit logs show token verification success/failure categories and the enforcement point identity for traceability.
- A documented and tested certificate pin rotation process exists (including rollback) and is reflected in configuration evidence.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define a canonical signed authorisation token schema including subject, resource, action, and required security/privacy attributes
- [ ] Implement token issuance using a managed signing key (HSM/KMS) and enforce audience/issuer binding and short lifetimes
- [ ] Configure issuer-to-enforcement-point communication to use mutual TLS with certificate pinning for the expected enforcement endpoint certificate/public key
- [ ] Implement mandatory enforcement-point verification: mTLS authentication, token signature verification, claim validation, then allow/deny decision
- [ ] Restrict routing so tokens are transmitted only to the federated application/API enforcement points that enforce access decisions
- [ ] Enable audit logging of token verification outcomes and mTLS peer identity, and retain evidence for audits
- [ ] Document and test certificate pin rotation and rollback procedures for pinned endpoints

### ac-24.2 — No User or Process Identity (Enhancement)

> **Overview**
>
> This expectation is about making access decisions without asking who the person is, or what system process is acting on their behalf. Instead, the organisation should decide whether someone can read, write, or run something using only agreed information about the item itself, such as its resource classification label, how the data is meant to be handled, and the access purpose code, or alternatively using where it sits in the system and the type of access needed, and where privacy is involved using only the privacy purpose and the data minimisation category. Without this, the wrong people could be granted access because decisions quietly depend on personal identifiers, or privacy could be undermined by collecting and using unnecessary identity details. To meet this requirement, the organisation must define and document these identity-free decision rules, configure systems so the rules are enforced exactly as written, ensure staff and developers follow the documented approach, and keep clear records showing decisions were based on those attributes rather than on user or process identity, including in the organisation’s security and privacy plans where relevant.

#### Identity-free ABAC decision engine using purpose/classification attributes

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement an authorisation decision mechanism where every allow/deny decision is based only on organisation-defined identity-free security/privacy attributes (e.g., resource classification label, data handling category, access purpose code; and/or enclave/zone + required access mode; and/or privacy purpose + data minimisation category). The decision logic must not reference, depend on, or embed any user or acting-process identity.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-24(2) enhancement requires enforcing access control decisions based on **{{ insert: param, ac-24.2_prm_1 }}** that do **not** include the identity of the user or process acting on behalf of the user. The guidance notes that MAC/RBAC/ABAC/label-based policies may omit user identity and that identity may be expensive/difficult to transmit in distributed systems.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the identity-free decision attributes** in the application’s policy model using the organisation-defined parameters:
   - Use **ac-24.2_prm_1** as the canonical set: *resource classification label, data handling category, access purpose code (no user/process identity)*.
   - Optionally support **ac-24.02_odp.01** (*resource classification label, system enclave/zone, required access mode*) and **ac-24.02_odp.02** (*privacy purpose and data minimisation category*) as additional identity-free attribute sets.
2. **Create a trusted “access context” input** to the policy decision point (PDP) that carries only the identity-free attributes (and any required enclave/zone/access mode). Use an integrity-protected mechanism (e.g., signed JWT claims issued by an internal policy context service, or mTLS-authenticated service-to-service intent) so the PDP can trust the attribute values without needing user/process identity.
3. **Implement policy-as-code rules** (ABAC/label-based) where allow/deny conditions reference only the identity-free attributes from the access context. Ensure the policy engine is configured so that identity fields (e.g., subject/user id, actor/process id) are not used in conditions.
4. **Add automated guardrails to prevent identity leakage into decision logic**:
   - Add CI/CD checks that fail if policy code references identity-like fields (username, subject, actor, user id, process id) in allow/deny logic.
   - Add runtime validation that the PDP rejects or ignores any request context containing identity attributes for decision-making (while optionally still logging identity separately for audit/incident response).
5. **Enforce at a consistent enforcement point** (API gateway, service mesh authorisation layer, or application authorisation middleware) so all microservices use the same identity-free decision logic.
6. **Generate audit evidence of identity-free basis**:
   - Configure authorisation logs to record the exact identity-free attribute values used for the decision (classification label, data handling category, purpose code; and/or enclave/zone and access mode; and/or privacy purpose and minimisation category).
   - Ensure logs include a clear field indicating the decision basis attributes and do not present identity fields as the basis for allow/deny.
7. **Document in security (and privacy, if applicable) plans** the identity-free attribute model, the trusted access context mechanism, enforcement point, and evidence fields used for audits.

## WHO
- **Application security architect / IAM/authorisation engineer**: defines attribute model and policy-as-code rules.
- **Platform/integration engineer**: implements the trusted access context mechanism and enforcement point configuration.
- **Security operations / compliance**: validates audit evidence fields and supports audit readiness.
- **Developers**: maintain policy code and comply with CI/CD guardrails.

## Acceptance criteria
- For all tested allow/deny decisions, the policy engine evaluates only the identity-free attributes defined by **ac-24.2_prm_1** (and any selected identity-free ODP attributes), and no allow/deny rule references user or acting-process identity.
- CI/CD policy checks prevent merges that introduce identity references into decision logic.
- Runtime logs for authorisation events include the identity-free decision attribute values used and clearly show the decision basis without identity being used as an input.
- A penetration/test scenario demonstrates that altering or omitting identity fields does not change the allow/deny outcome when identity-free attributes remain constant.

**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define the canonical identity-free attribute set for authorisation using ac-24.2_prm_1 (classification label, data handling category, access purpose code).
- [ ] Optionally extend the attribute model with enclave/zone + access mode (ac-24.02_odp.01) and/or privacy purpose + data minimisation category (ac-24.02_odp.02) where required.
- [ ] Implement a trusted access-context mechanism that supplies only the identity-free attributes to the policy decision point (e.g., signed claims or mTLS service intent).
- [ ] Write policy-as-code ABAC/label-based allow/deny rules that reference only the identity-free attributes and explicitly exclude identity fields from conditions.
- [ ] Add CI/CD and runtime guardrails to fail builds or reject contexts if policy logic references identity-like fields (user/actor/process) for decision-making.
- [ ] Configure the enforcement point (API gateway/service mesh/app middleware) so all services use the same identity-free authorisation decision path.
- [ ] Enable authorisation audit logging that records the identity-free decision attribute values used as the basis for allow/deny and verify identity is not used as an input to the decision.

### ac-25 — Reference Monitor (Control)

> **Overview**
>
> This requirement is about having a single, dedicated “gatekeeper” inside the system that decides who can see or use Confidential and Secret information, every time, before anything is released. Without this, access rules can be inconsistent or accidentally skipped, or an attacker could try to change the decision-making process so unauthorised people gain access. To meet this expectation, the organisation must implement the Department of Defence access rules for Confidential and Secret data, including role- and attribute-based decisions and the principle of least privilege, through a tamper-resistant gatekeeper that is always consulted for access decisions and cannot be bypassed through other routes. The gatekeeper must be small and clearly defined enough that its behaviour can be thoroughly analysed and tested, with documented evidence showing it takes the correct allow-or-deny decisions and records the outcome, and that unauthorised attempts to interfere with it or its rules are blocked and logged.

#### Tamper-resistant always-invoked reference monitor for Conf/Secret

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical

## WHAT
Implement a dedicated reference monitor that mediates **every** access to **DoD Confidential and Secret** data using the organisation-defined access control policies (RBAC/ABAC, least privilege). The monitor must be **tamper-resistant**, **always invoked** (no bypass paths), and **small/bounded** so its behaviour can be comprehensively analysed and tested.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-25 requires a reference validation mechanism that is: (1) **always invoked** (prevents bypass), (2) **tamper-proof** (prevents compromise), and (3) **small enough to be subject to analysis and testing with assured completeness** (verifiable enforcement). The reference monitor must enforce the access control policy over all subjects and objects for the specified policy scope.

## HOW (specific approach)
### 1) Define the mediation boundary for Confidential/Secret
- Classify Confidential/Secret objects using a single, enforceable label/classification scheme (e.g., storage object labels, database row classification, or service-level object classification tags).
- Define the set of subject identities/attributes used by the policy (e.g., user/service account identity, role, clearance, project, group membership) and how they are bound to the executing workload.

### 2) Implement a single dedicated enforcement point
- Deploy an **access decision service / library** that is the only component allowed to make allow/deny decisions for Confidential/Secret objects.
- Ensure all application and data access paths for Confidential/Secret data call this enforcement point **before** any action is performed (read/write/execute/export).
- For data stores, enforce mediation at the access primitive (e.g., database access wrapper/proxy that performs the decision and only then issues the query/operation; or an OS/kernel-level MAC mediation layer where feasible).

### 3) Make the monitor tamper-resistant
- Run the reference monitor in a hardened execution context with restricted privileges (e.g., separate service account, least-privilege IAM, no direct write access to policy artefacts).
- Protect policy inputs and decision logic with integrity controls:
  - Sign policy artefacts and verify signatures at load time.
  - Store policy artefacts in a controlled location (e.g., managed secrets/config store) with access restricted to the deployment pipeline.
- Prevent bypass by removing/denying direct access routes:
  - Block database/storage direct credentials from application tiers; only the mediation component can hold the credentials required to access Confidential/Secret objects.

### 4) Keep the monitor small and verifiable
- Implement the monitor as a narrowly scoped component that performs only:
  - input normalisation (subject/object/action),
  - policy evaluation (RBAC/ABAC least-privilege),
  - allow/deny decision,
  - mandatory audit event emission.
- Keep policy evaluation deterministic and side-effect free.
- Provide a test harness that covers:
  - policy completeness (all defined policy rules/conditions are exercised),
  - negative tests (unauthorised subject/attribute combinations),
  - regression tests for policy updates.

### 5) Ensure always-invoked mediation with enforcement evidence
- Instrument the system so that every Confidential/Secret access attempt results in an audit record that includes:
  - reference monitor identifier/version,
  - subject identity/attributes used,
  - object classification/label used,
  - requested action,
  - decision (allow/deny) and reason code (policy rule reference).
- Add runtime checks that fail closed if the reference monitor is unreachable or returns an error (i.e., deny by default for Confidential/Secret).

## WHO
- **Application Security Architect**: defines mediation boundary, subject/object/action model, and policy mapping.
- **Platform/Infrastructure Engineer**: implements hardened deployment, credential isolation, and network/service controls.
- **Security Engineer**: defines test strategy, policy completeness criteria, and audit evidence requirements.
- **Developers**: integrate all Confidential/Secret access paths to call the reference monitor and remove bypass routes.

## Acceptance criteria
1. **Always invoked**: For Confidential/Secret objects, there is no operational access path that does not call the reference monitor (verified via architecture review and runtime tracing).
2. **Tamper-resistant**: Policy artefacts and decision logic are integrity-protected (signature verification) and direct data access credentials are not available to bypass components.
3. **Small/bounded**: The reference monitor codebase and responsibilities are limited to mediation and decision/audit; policy evaluation is deterministic and side-effect free.
4. **Verifiable completeness**: Automated tests demonstrate coverage of all policy rules/conditions for Confidential/Secret, including negative and regression cases.
5. **Audit evidence**: Every Confidential/Secret access attempt produces an audit event containing monitor version, inputs used, and allow/deny decision.


**Actions Required for Compliance:**

- [ ] Define the Confidential/Secret object labelling/classification scheme and the subject attribute set used for RBAC/ABAC decisions
- [ ] Implement a single dedicated reference monitor enforcement point and ensure all Confidential/Secret access paths route through it before any data operation
- [ ] Remove bypass routes by isolating Confidential/Secret data credentials so only the reference monitor (or its controlled proxy) can access the underlying stores
- [ ] Harden the reference monitor runtime and protect policy artefacts with integrity controls (e.g., signed policies with verification at load time)
- [ ] Implement fail-closed behaviour for Confidential/Secret accesses when the reference monitor is unavailable or errors
- [ ] Create an automated test harness that exercises all policy rules/conditions (positive and negative) and produces evidence of completeness
- [ ] Configure audit logging so every Confidential/Secret access attempt records the reference monitor version, inputs used, and allow/deny decision
